

Ravour book 1: 

The Null of Storms





RAVOUR’S THUNDEROUS ROAR



A classified mural, believed to have been created by a mythical beast.

  As is tradition, this passage is recited by the High Zepharian Priest to the Heir Archon before the mantle of Prime is passed.

⸻

In the beginning, Zephyrs created Ravour, a world of storms.

  From the eye of that storm, she shaped ten noble beasts of legend, each one born in her image.

  We were not mere creatures. We were divinity clad in flesh, each imbued with the purest form of power.

  We ruled the land as deities among lesser beasts, and the world bent to our breath.

  Then came the infestation. Two-legged wretches.

  Flesh too soft. No fangs. No claws. No scales. No paws.

  They did not belong, and yet they multiplied and spread.

  They called themselves ‘humans.’

  Wielding strange powers that defied the law of storms, they hurled fireballs into the sky.

  They cracked open stone and poisoned rivers.

  They hunted our kin, shattering the balance, becoming a threat that had to be eradicated.

  And so, the ten noble beasts of legend forged an alliance.

  We summoned every claw, every fang, every wing and horn from the farthest corners of the world.

  We united the beasts of land, sea, and sky with one divine purpose:

  To purge the infestation.

  But the vermin burrowed into the ground.

  They fled like cowards.

  Now, they surface only to scavenge for scraps of food, fragments of stone, and what meager remnants they manage to drag back to their burrows.

  Thus, they were named for what they truly are:

  Rodents.





Prologue



The wind howled through the jagged cliffs around Stormhaven’s gates, slicing through the cold, damp air like a blade. Godric stood at the edge of the mountain pass, his spear held firm, every fiber of his being focused on the shadows shifting in the stormy distance. His body ached, his muscles stiff from years of battle, but he knew better than to let it show.

Behind him, the gates loomed tall, carved deep into the stone, a fortress for the people below. He could feel the tension from the guards stationed at the entrance: greenhorns in borrowed armor, clutching their weapons like lifelines.

“Keep your hands steady,” Godric barked, his eyes fixed ahead. “Shake now, and I’ll have more to clean up than feathers and blood.”

One of them, braver than the rest, stammered, “We’re not Hunters, sir. We’re just—”

“Cowards, I know,” Godric growled under his breath, cutting him off.

The soldiers shifted uncomfortably. Perhaps he shouldn’t be so hard on them. They were little more than civilians.

“Do you think you can kill it?” one of them asked, his voice barely above a whisper.

Kill it? Boltrax, the mythical beast? No. Not alone. Stone me, not even with a whole hunting party. But someone has to stand here. And no one else has the spine.

That was reason enough.

“I’m not here to think,” he growled, letting the weight of his spear settle in his hands. “I’m here to hold the line.”

Another roar, closer this time. His heart pounded in his chest and he took a long deep breath, his ritual before any battle. Fear? No. Fear wouldn’t help him. It helped some stay alive, but was useless in a battle where retreat is not an option. He forced the thought away, letting the weight of the spear anchor him. The NullStone tip glinted faintly, its edges dulled from years of use.

A screech ripped through the air, sharp and grating, setting his teeth on edge. The beast was near. His grip tightened around the spear, his knuckles white against the worn wood. Come on then, you bastard.

The creature descended like a shadow, its wings spread wide, blocking out the dim light of the SolarStones lining the gate. Black feathers shimmered in the faint glow, streaked with veins of glowing blue. Its molten eyes burned with malice, and its talons gleamed like blades.

Godric planted his feet, his gaze steady. He wasn’t afraid of death—not anymore. If this was where he’d die, so be it. But he’d make bolting sure the beast remembered him.

It dove, fast and precise, its claws slicing through the air. Godric moved on instinct, twisting to the side and striking with his spear. The NullStone tip caught the edge of its face, slashing across one of its glowing eyes.

The beast roared, rearing back as blood—a strange, electric blue—dripped down its face.

“Not so pretty now, are you?” Godric muttered, circling it warily.

The beast twisted, its tail sweeping low and smashing through the stone. Godric leapt, the wind from its wings battering his frame as he brought the spear down, scraping across its shoulder. Sparks flew where the NullStone met feathers, but the creature barely flinched. Its claws raked forward, forcing Godric into a clumsy back-step that left him off balance

The creature hissed, its wings flaring as it launched itself at him again. He dodged, but not fast enough. Its tail lashed out but this time, catching him across the ribs with bone-crushing force.

The impact sent him flying.

Godric’s body hit the ground hard, skidding across the stone before slamming into the gates with a sickening thud. Pain erupted in his side—sharp and unrelenting—but he forced himself to move, slowly dragging himself toward the spear he’d dropped ten—no, fifteen—arm lengths away.

The beast stalked closer, its remaining eye blazing with fury.

Damn idiot! he thought. His arms shook with every inch. His shoulders felt ready to rip from their sockets. What a rookie mistake. Never drop your spear.

I’m… I’m not going make it.

Gritting his teeth, Godric stared into the eye of the one who bested him. This wasn’t his first time staring death in the face, although it would likely be his last.

Then, out of nowhere, a dark-skinned figure stepped into view.

The boy was no more than eight cycles, a scrap of skin and bone draped in a patched cloak. And yet, he walked like the storm bent to him, his steps too steady for a child, too sharp for anyone raised soft behind gates.

“What are you bolting doing?” Godric rasped, his voice hoarse from pain. “Get back inside!”

The boy ignored him. His dark eyes locked on the beast, unwavering.

“Kid!” Godric barked, trying to rise, but his body wouldn’t obey. He watched in disbelief as the boy stopped a few paces from the creature, his posture relaxed, his head tilted slightly.

“You know you can’t win,” the boy said, his voice cutting through the storm like a blade. “Do you really want to die?”

Godric froze. Was the boy… talking to the beast? His tone wasn’t taunting, nor was it pleading—it was something sharper, something final. For a moment, it seemed like even the creature hesitated, its wings twitching in response.

I must have hit my head harder than I thought.

The boy moved like no Hunter Godric had ever seen. In an instant, he snatched up the fallen spear, wielding it as though it had been made for him. His steps were impossibly light, his strikes unnervingly precise, each motion carrying a grace that belied his age. Godric’s jaw tightened, not in fear for the boy, but in awe. This wasn’t instinct—this was mastery.

The beast roared, unleashing a burst of lightning from the BoltStone embedded in its chest. Arcs of electricity seared through the air, but the boy was faster, twisting and leaping out of harm’s way.

The boy yelled, a sharp, guttural sound that cleaved through the storm. And then, impossibly, the lightning froze. It hung in the air, shimmering and crackling, before dissipating like mist. Godric’s breath hitched. No Hunter could command a storm. No mortal child could wield a voice that could still the chaos itself. Yet this boy—this child—had bent nature to his will.

What was he? There was no hesitation in his strikes, no wasted effort. Every step, every swing of the spear, was a calculated masterpiece, as if the boy had trained for decades. And yet, the training halls couldn’t teach this. Godric’s jaw tightened, watching the child fight as though born to wield death itself.

As he attempted to get up and join the fray, the guards rushed forward, dragging Godric inside the gates. He fought against them, his voice a growl. “Let me go! That kid—he can’t—”

The gates slammed shut, cutting off his protests.

The guards stood frozen, their whispers mingling with the storm. “Did you see that?” one breathed, his voice trembling. “He’s not human,” another whispered, clutching his chest.

Through the narrow crack, Godric saw the boy drive the spear into the beast’s leg, forcing it back. The creature shrieked, blood and sparks flying as it stumbled.

Then, with a final screech, it spread its wings and took to the skies, disappearing into the storm.

The boy stood alone, his chest heaving, the spear still clenched in his hands. How could he even lift that weapon? Let alone move like that?



***



Praew turned the page reverently, heart still racing. Though she had memorized the story ages ago, the book always pulled her in like she was Silver Wing Godric, witnessing the birth of a legend. She read on, and once again, the world fell away.



***



When the gates opened again, I was already on my feet, wincing at the ache in my ribs, my glare fixed on the boy. “Who the ten stones are you?” I demanded.

The boy turned, his sharp brown eyes meeting mine with a calmness that unnerved me. “Srevlis,” he said simply.

An odd kid, no doubt. Five gems along his face gleamed faintly, tracing a crescent around his eye like they were part of him. His hands, though scarred and worn, they belonged to a seasoned Hunter, not a boy. He didn’t look like one of ours. He couldn’t be. “You’ve got a place here if you want it,” I offered, though I wasn’t sure where the words had come from.

Srevlis shook his head. “No.”

“What do you mean, no? Why not?” I snapped, my frustration spilling over.

“Do you think you’re the only victim?” His voice cut through the night like a blade.

And then he turned, his figure disappearing into the storm and shadows beyond the gates. I wanted to call out, to stop him, but something in his tone left me frozen.

That was the last I saw of him that night.

I was left with my thoughts, battered and bruised, leaning against the closed gates of Stormhaven.

Eight years old, and that boy did what I, Godric, a veteran Hunter, couldn’t. The beast didn’t stand a chance. Srevlis chased it through the storm like a shadow on its tail. He didn’t just hunt it, he haunted it. That night, he became something more. Crescent Moon Srevlis, the youngest Mythical Beast Hunter I’ve ever seen.

At the time, I didn’t understand the weight of his words. ‘Do you think you’re the only victim?’ God, how those words gnawed at me. They carved a place in my mind and stayed there, whispering every time the aches in my ribs reminded me of my failure. Not just as a Hunter but as a man.

It took a wild-born child, just eight years old, to remind me why I fight.

This account isn’t for those behind the gates. It’s for those who will stand at the edge. Those who will pick up their spears and face the storm, even when it costs everything.

We take up our arms for those we hold dear, and for those cruelly slaughtered by beasts.

We toil and train, that there might be one less soul claimed by this endless cycle of terror.

We shall never yield whilst we tire of being naught but the prey.

We fight, for it is time that WE become the Hunters!

And I’ll die before I forget it again







Chapter 1 - The Hunter Exam





     Praew closed the book for the last time, saying goodbye to the story that raised her.

     She and her two best friends had made a pact that after the test, they would never read the story again. Because today would determine if they got to face the storm or remain stuck behind the gates forever.

     A faint hum filled the waiting hall, a steady murmur of excitement, nerves, and whispered bets between hopefuls. The walls were lit by the soft, warm glow of SolarStones embedded at even intervals along the ceiling. The stairs, the podium, the tables and desks, even the long bench they sat on had all been carved directly from the stone floor, destined to remain forever stationary, trapped and unchanging.

     Funny, she thought. Furniture has always been that way, but why does it suddenly feel so… wrong, like the rest of this dreary dungeon they masquerade as a city?

     Praew’s fingers tapped her knee, foot bouncing as she scanned the room. She wasn’t the only one tense; most of the would-be Hunters sat stiffly, whispering or staring off like they were waiting for a final meal.

She brushed a pink curl behind her ear, already feeling it frizz from the tension. With bright hair, blushing cheeks, and eyes people always said “sparkled,” whatever that meant. She apparently looked like the type to “stand aside and cheer for others.”

Yeah. Not the vibe she was going for, but she embraced it anyway. At least if she tripped over her boots, she’d look adorable doing it.

Since they’d all turned twenty this year, she couldn’t help but notice how everyone towered over her, especially the boys, with muscles they seemed to grow effortlessly. Meanwhile, tired, sick, or injured, she still trained every day without fail, only to see slower results. It wasn’t easy being a girl expected to compete with male strength.

She caught herself.

No! I will not use my height or gender as an excuse to fail. If I can’t accomplish something the others can, it’s my own fault for not training harder.

Praew looked for reassurance from her two best friends. Haldric Tosa, a pale young man with messy black hair, met her gaze with an annoyingly infectious grin. Top of the class with both sword and spear, he was easily the most likely to pass the Hunter Test.

Asher, on the other hand, was tall and lanky, with slicked-back sandy blond hair. He sat at the stone desk, still flipping through the well-worn pages of Srevlis, the Mythical Beast Hunter.

Didn’t they start reading at the same time? I hadn’t even sat down yet. So how did a muscle-head like Haldric finish before a book lover like Asher?

It took her less than a breath to figure it out. “Again, Asher?” Praew teased.

Asher didn’t even glance up. “It’s just hard to put it down. For good.”

“I know,” Praew agreed. “I almost teared up.”

“Zephyrs take me, you guys are so dramatic,” scoffed a voice behind her.

Praew recognized it instantly. Renwick lounged against the stone bench, hands tucked behind his head, long black hair tied back, his freckled nose tilted smugly toward the ceiling.

     That same condescending smirk he always wore was waiting for her.

     “Seriously? Crying over your little promise? This is why women shouldn’t be Hunters. Too emotional. It’s a kid’s book. Grow up.”

     Before Praew could reply, Asher snapped his book shut with a loud thump. “Tell me, Renwick, do you strive to be the most hated person in every room, or is that a privilege you reserve exclusively for us?” 

     Asher let out a snort. 

     “I can’t wait for Praew to become a Hunter while you get sent to the mines. Oh, and this book? It’s fourteen years old, yet it still inspires the whole bolting city. Who isn’t here because of it?”

     Renwick rolled his eyes. “It’s a bedtime story for delusional kids who want to play pretend as Hunters.” He glanced at Praew, then at Asher. “No wonder you two are obsessed with it.”

     Asher scoffed, and Haldric leaned forward, arms draped over his knees. “So what about me? Do you think I’m playing pretend as well?”

     Praew shot Renwick a victorious grin, but before the conversation could continue, the large wooden doors at the front of the chamber creaked open. 

A real Hunter stepped into the waiting hall, his presence instantly commanding the room, with a proctor in tow.

     Neither wasted time on introductions. The proctor stepped forward and spoke first.

     “You have all worked hard to reach the end of your education. For seventeen years, you trained in combat, math, science, and more. Each of you has prepared for every labor role this city might assign you. You all know this is non-negotiable. Insubordination is treason.”

“AND JUSTICE WE WILL DELIVER!” the students chanted in unison.

The proctor continued. “Stormhaven thanks you for your dedication.

“Our underground city is one of the last human sanctuaries remaining. A treacherous world lies above, where beasts, storms, and even the very stones that litter the ground can kill the strongest of men. Those who brave the surface to hunt resources for the city are called Hunters.

“Now, before your role is announced, you have the legal right to take the Hunters’ test. No matter the outcome, we thank you for your bravery and willingness to fight for your city.”

The Hunter stepped forward and cleared his throat.

“There are two parts to the test,” he announced. His voice hit like a shockwave, shaking the chamber. Dust and pebbles fell from the ceiling, pelting the hopefuls below.

How did he— but before Praew could finish the thought, the Hunter continued, quieter this time.

“The first is a knowledge exam. Pass it, and you move on to the practical. Fail, and you’re dismissed.”

Murmurs rippled through the crowd, but the Hunter ignored them and went on.

“The test will be conducted alone. Each of you will enter one at a time. If the proctor calls your name, enter the door to my right. That’s all you need to know.”

Without another word, he turned and exited through the wooden door.

A tense silence settled over the room. One by one, names were called as hopeful, would-be Hunters disappeared behind the wooden doors.

“Paew… uh… pon?” the proctor called.

Of course he got her name wrong. They always did. That’s why she went by Praew instead of Praewphan.

She rolled her eyes. What am I even doing here? This was a bad idea. I’m just going to—

Her spiral broke the moment a hand touched her shoulder. Haldric.

She turned, and both he and Asher were grinning like idiots. Without a word, they held up their hands, thumb and pinky tucked in, leaving three fingers extended.

She mirrored them. Together, they pressed their hands in a triangle of contact:

Her pointer met Haldric’s, his third touched Asher’s, and Asher’s pointer pressed against her third, all three middle fingers meeting in the center to form a triangle, the symbol of their unshakable bond and their unbreakable promise.

Haldric grinned. “No matter the weather,” he began.

“Together we reign,” they finished, voices low but a hint of resolve brewed beneath their uncertainty.

What would I do without these two?

The wooden door creaked as Praew stepped inside. Her hands were clenched into fists, but her heart was ready now.

 The room was small, suffocating. A single dim SolarStone flickered atop the desk. The Hunter sat alone with his quill poised over parchment.

“Name three Sifaralith Stones and their functions,” he commanded, barely looking up.

Praew took a deep breath. “SolarStone, EchoStone, ZephyrStone.”

The Hunter gestured for her to continue.

“SolarStones emit light or heat used for visibility, warmth, or signaling. 

“EchoStones produce loud or chaotic sounds, depending on what it recorded during its trigger. It is also how we record and to authenticate messages.

“ZephyrStones generate bursts of wind, useful for movement, propulsion, or controlling airflow.”

A small scratch of ink. The Hunter made a note.



***



Asher rolled his shoulders, keeping his posture loose. No nerves, not today.

“Three stones and their functions, list them”

“SurgeStone, BlastStone, TerraStone.”

The proctor raised an eyebrow.

“SurgeStones boosts nearby stones for a bit, then drains their charge afterwards. They’re usually saved for emergencies.

 “BlastStones generate a sonic boom with immense force. They can be used for powerful ranged attacks, stunning enemies, or breaking obstacles.

“TerraStones create tunnels or incinerate earth for shelter. It’s nearly impossible for a tunnel created this way to collapse.”

A small mark on the parchment, but nothing more.



***



Haldric stood, arms crossed, gaze unwavering. He had trained for this moment. He already knew his answer before the question left the Hunter’s lips.

“Name three-”

“ForceStone, BoltStone, NullStone.” Haldric interrupted.

The Hunter’s quill didn’t move. “Define them.”

“ForceStones can store, stack, and release impacts —enhancing strikes, breaking armor, or building traps. 

“BoltStones generate and release electricity, surging through weapons, tools, or even people.

“NullStones will dampen or deactivate the effects of other stones within range.”

The Hunter finally made a mark on the parchment.



***



“Next question”



The proctor’s eyes narrowed slightly. “Praew, was it? Explain to me how stones trigger?”

She leaned forward slightly, eager to answer.

“Every stone has a unique trigger, and they are always activated by one of the ten stone abilities. That trigger might come from another stone or an external force, but it will always align with one of the ten properties.”

The Hunter remained silent, so she continued.

“No two stones are alike. You can have two EchoStones, but their size, rarity, and trigger could be completely different. One might activate with light, another with sound. It’s why identifying a stone’s individual trigger is just as important as knowing its type.”

She hesitated briefly. “That’s also why surface-dwelling stones are the most dangerous. If a Solar-triggered EchoStone gets caught in sunlight, it could set off an entire chain reaction. A bad enough combination could take out an entire hunting party before anyone realized what was happening.”

For the first time, the Hunter’s expression shifted. Not much, but enough to note approval.

He made a mark on the parchment.



***



“Now, explain a combination technique using two of the stones you listed.”

Haldric didn’t hesitate, his voice steady.

“BoltStone and NullStone. A BoltStone triggered by pressure makes a shockingly effective spearhead.”

Silence. The Hunter’s stare was flat, unimpressed.

Haldric cleared his throat, shifting slightly. “A NullStone embedded in the weapon shaft prevents the wielder from getting electrocuted.”

The Hunter gave a short nod. “Destructive.”



***



Asher grinned, leaning back slightly in his chair. “TerraStone is too slow. If you need to dig quickly for shelter, a SurgeStone can force the TerraStone to react instantly, letting you carve a tunnel within seconds.”

The Hunter made a note. “Efficient.”



***



Praew knew the final question. She’d rehearsed her answer for months now.

The proctor’s quill hovered, waiting.

She took a deep breath. “Solar and Echo. If you have a SolarStone triggered by sound and a EchoStone triggered by light, you can make a loop. Clap once, the SolarStone flashes, which triggers the EchoStone, which makes a sound—resetting the loop. You get endless light until you cover the EchoStone.”

The Hunter’s quill hesitated before marking the parchment. “Clever”.

As Praew finished her answer, the door creaked open behind her. She turned slightly, just enough to see the proctor step inside, a black bag clutched in his hands.

Without a word, he stepped forward and placed the bag directly into the Hunter’s hands.

The Hunter accepted it without acknowledgment, undoing the clasp and peeling back the opening just enough to see inside.

A dim red glow flickered against his face.



Praew’s breath hitched. A sudden, suffocating weight pressed against her chest—unexplained emotions that weren’t hers were clawing their way inside.

Alarm. Fear. Hatred. Longing. Sadness. Defiance. 

Her vision blurred. A single tear slipped down her cheek before she could stop it.

The Hunter snapped the bag shut, his expression unreadable. He gave a small nod to the proctor, who picked the bag back up and left without a word, the door clicking softly shut behind him.

Praew blinked rapidly, dragging the sleeve of her uniform across her cheek. She opened her mouth slightly, but the Hunter didn’t give her the chance to speak.

“NEXT!”

The sudden bark of his voice made her flinch. She stood quickly, moving automatically as she walked to the door, her thoughts tangled.

It’s nothing. Just nerves.

She had built this test up in her mind for years—of course her body was shaken. That had to be it. By the time she stepped into the hall, she had already buried the thought.

The waiting hall was quieter now. The murmurs had faded, replaced by anxious, uneven breathing. A few hopefuls still whispered among themselves, but most sat still, their gazes locked onto the doors ahead. Praew clenched her hands into fists, the anticipation gnawing at her.

The heavy doors swung open.

The Hunter stepped into the hall, his presence once more demanding the attention of every person in the room. He wasted no time.

“If I call your name, you passed. The rest of you, leave.”

Silence.

Then—

“Renwick.”

The jerk beside her let out an exaggerated sigh of relief, stretching his arms. “As if there was ever a doubt,” he muttered, casting a smug glance at the others.

“Haldric.”

Haldric exhaled sharply, nodding once. His posture was stiff, but the tension in his shoulders eased slightly.

“Korven.”

A tall, broad-shouldered boy at the far end of the room straightened. His expression remained stoic, but Praew caught the flicker of satisfaction in his eyes.

“Edrik.”

Another boy, thinner, wiry, grinned, jabbing his friend in the ribs. “Told you,” he muttered, shoving to his feet.

A pause.

“Praew.”

Her breath caught.

For a moment, she didn’t move. Didn’t react. Then, slowly, she let out the breath she didn’t realize she’d been holding.

She’d done it. 

She was still in the running.

Finally—

“Asher.”

Praew glanced toward her friend. He didn’t react immediately. Just nodded, his easy confidence more subdued than usual.

The Hunter’s gaze swept the remaining hopefuls.

“You were given a chance. You failed.” His tone was impassive. “There are no second attempts.”

The weight of his words settled over the room. The ones not called slowly stood, some with their heads bowed, others with clenched jaws, holding back frustration or disappointment.

Praew didn’t watch them leave.

She couldn’t.

She was too busy processing the reality of it.

Six left.

Only six.

The Hunter wasted no time. “On your feet,” he commanded.

A few shuffled awkwardly, but everyone obeyed.

“The first test is done. You passed.” His gaze flicked between them, assessing. Measuring. “Now we see if you’re truly worth sending to the surface.”

 He turned sharply, gesturing for them to follow.

No further explanation.

No hesitation.

The six of them moved, stepping into the unknown.

The corridor leading deeper underground was long and winding, the air thick with damp stone and flickering SolarStone light. Shadows stretched along the uneven walls, and the further they went, the heavier the silence became.

Renwick, of course, was the first to break the silence. “Six left, huh? Guess that means fewer idiots to slow me down.”

Praew didn’t reply. He wasn’t talking to her anyway. He just enjoyed hearing his own voice.

The path narrowed, the ceiling pressing lower as they moved deeper into the mountain’s core. A presence settled beside her. Asher.

For a moment, neither of them spoke until Praew finally asked, “You okay?”

It took him too long to answer. “Yeah,” he muttered.

Liar.

She didn’t press further. Not now.

Ahead, the corridor widened again. The Hunter leading them came to an abrupt halt. Then, without a word, he stepped aside.

Praew barely had time to process the sudden shift before she saw it. The space beyond them was massive.

The underground training hall was enormous, far larger than she had expected. The ceiling stretched high above, rough-hewn stone arching over the expanse. SolarStones flickered overhead, their golden light barely reaching the depths of the battlefield below.

And the battlefield was nothing like she imagined.  It was a maze.

Twisting stone formations, jagged outcrops, unstable platforms. Paths wound between sharp inclines and sudden drops, the entire course built for combat, survival, and calculated brutality.

A stone platform overlooked the field with seating carved into the rock as the waiting area.

Above them all in the highest seat, watching with quiet authority was The Prime Archon, the leader of the city.

Praew swallowed hard.

I can do this!

The candidates took their seats. Praew sat alone. Haldric and Asher sat in front. Her hands clenched into fists in her lap. Her heartbeat thundered in her ears. As she tried to steady her nerves, Renwick sat next to her.  Of course it had to be the most insufferable person she had ever met. She always hated the way he would stare at her. 

This was it. The second and final test. Fail here, and you were out. Forever. 

The Hunter stepped forward, his sharp gaze sweeping the room. His voice rang out, crisp and final. “There are times when you will run into Hunters from other cities.”



A ripple of unease spread through the gathered hopefuls as the Hunter continued, “This test will simulate that.”

Praew glanced at Renwick. The Hunter’s next words made her stomach twist. “The person next to you is a Hunter from another city.”

She swallowed. She hadn’t realized she’d been holding her breath.

“There is a stone for each of you,” the Hunter continued. “Work together or independently, I don’t care. Just retrieve them both.” No further explanation or rules were given.

Praew’s stomach churned. Why does it have to be this guy?

The first pair rose, Korven and Edrik. They shared a glance, then strode down the steps into the training field.

A horn sounded. The test began.

Praew watched intently.

The two hopefuls moved as one, sprinting into the maze-like course, ducking between towering stone pillars with wordless understanding. They scanned the field, assessing threats, working in tandem. Edrik leapt onto a ledge and spotted a BlastStone embedded in the wall. He gestured to Korven, signaling caution.

Korven kicked a EchoStone into a ditch, triggering a distant explosion. A distraction. Edrik used a ZephyrStone to propel them both onto a high ridge, where he scanned the battlefield and directed their next move. They reached the pedestals together and, without hesitation, each took a stone.

Praew narrowed her eyes. They were avoiding the dangers quite effortlessly.

The Hunter gave a small nod. “Impressive communication.”

Praew exhaled slowly. This was doable.

The two returned, victorious.

Then, Asher and Haldric stood, striding side by side down the steps into the arena.

Praew felt proud knowing her two friends would pass. They were the most skilled in her graduating class.

Why does no one think I’ll succeed? Not my parents, not even my friends.

I’m skilled too! When I focus, I’m just as good as Asher.

She was sick of being teased for being clumsy. Bump into a few things, hit a few corners, get a few bruises, and suddenly everyone forgot she scored second-highest in both hand-to-hand and spear combat training.

The horn sounded, pulling her back to the test.

The two bolted forward, sprinting into the maze. Their teamwork was unmatched. Asher called out hazards, Haldric led the charge. A SolarStone embedded in a wall flared to life, reacting to their movement. Haldric threw up an arm, shielding his eyes. Asher, quicker, shoved a piece of cloth over the stone, killing its glow before it could trigger something worse.

Praew released a breath. They were doing it. Making even quicker progress than the first group.

Then they hit the obstacle.

A wide gap stretched before them, a pit of jagged stone and unstable terrain below. The stones were on the other side.

Asher made the jump first, barely catching the edge, pulling himself up. Haldric leapt next. He didn’t make it. His fingers scraped against the rock. He slipped.

“Stone me!” His legs dangled, boots kicking uselessly against the wall. “Asher—”

Their eyes met. Haldric reached out.

Asher hesitated.

His fingers twitched at his side, half-reaching, half-still. His gaze flicked to The Prime Archon watching from above, then back to Haldric. His friend’s hand was open, waiting. Asher looked torn. He could pull him up. He should pull him up.

Then his eyes darted to the stones.

And he turned away.

Praew froze. What? No. No, no, no. What are you doing, Asher!?

Haldric’s grip faltered. He scrambled, his expression shifting from shock to fury. “Asher—Asher!”

Asher grabbed both stones.

Praew’s stomach dropped.

Haldric’s arms trembled as he pulled himself up, rage in his eyes, breath coming fast and sharp. Asher wouldn’t meet his gaze.

The Hunter’s voice echoed across the chamber. “The test has ended.”

Praew’s pulse roared in her ears. She saw it—the moment it settled in Haldric’s mind. The moment he realized his friend had chosen to leave him behind.

The air was heavy.

Haldric didn’t move. His breath came in sharp, shallow bursts. His fingers curled against the rock, pressing so hard they turned white. He looked up. Asher still wouldn’t meet his gaze.

Haldric’s nails dug into his palms. His breath was ragged, shoulders rising and falling with barely contained rage. His jaw clenched so tight it ached. His fists trembled.

“That’s not fair!” His voice cracked, half-roar, half-wound. “He took my stone!”

The Hunter dismissed Haldric with a lazy flick of the hand. “It counts,” he said, using his other fist to cover a yawn.

A muscle ticked in Haldric’s jaw. His whole body was coiled, every inch of him screaming to hit something. “That’s bolting convenient. Wouldn't you agree, Asher?" He was barely holding back the urge to make Asher bleed.

Asher didn’t say a word. Didn’t look at Haldric. Didn’t even glance her way. Praew felt sick.

Her name was called. She stood. The stone steps felt heavier beneath her feet.

Renwick chuckled beside her. “Looks like teamwork’s off the table, sweetheart.”

She didn’t respond. She was too focused on not thinking about Asher, not thinking about Haldric’s face.

The horn sounded.

Renwick lunged ahead, grinning. “Try to keep up!”

Praew bolted after him. The course was a battlefield. SolarStones flared unpredictably, BlastStones trembled, waiting for a trigger. She had to move fast, move smart.

Renwick wasn’t fast, but he was cruel. “Wow,” he said, panting, “seems like you got a lot on your chest.”

She ignored him.

“Bet it’s hard running with all that weight.”

Her teeth clenched. He was too close. She needed to get ahead.

She spotted a half-buried ZephyrStone. Perfect. She leapt onto it, triggering a gust of wind, and launched forward.

For a moment, she was winning. Actually winning. A thrill shot through her. No one would ever doubt or mock her again. 

Renwick’s voice came smooth, mocking. “Damn. Not a bad view from back here, either.”

Praew hesitated. Just for a second, but that’s all it took.

She faltered. Her foot caught a stone. She stumbled.

Zephyrs take me!

Renwick flew past her, laughing. The pedestals were in sight.

She scrambled, lunged, and collided with him. They tumbled across the stone floor.

Panicked, she reached for something, anything to subdue him.

The BlastStone triggered. A shockwave tore through the field, sending her flying.

She barely registered Renwick laughing. Barely saw him snatch both stones.

He turned, smirked, and winked. “Better luck next life, sweetheart.”

Praew stumbled back into the risen seats. She barely heard the Hunter’s voice. “You must beat your competition. By any means necessary.”

She barely heard Haldric’s fists trembling. Barely noticed Asher, staring at the ground.

Her mind roared.

“Two actually managed to pass this year,” the Hunter said. “Renwick and Asher.”

Wait… just two? Why not four?

“One person had to take both stones. No city can afford to let another claim what they need to survive. We do not share. We win.”

The others looked exactly how she felt, horrified.

Praew stared at the ground in shock.

I… failed. No second chances. No appeals.

The Hunter’s words rang in her skull as he asked the students, “Could you live with yourself, knowing you only received a Common Stone, and let another city walk away with something more rare? That’s why a Hunter must always aim to claim every stone for himself.”

The weight of it settled in. Praew’s hands curled into fists. Her heart was still racing. 

And Renwick just smiled. 











Chapter 2 - Acclimation



The hall was silent.

No more tests. No more proving themselves. The choices had been made.

Praew sat stiffly on the stone bench, surrounded by the weight of what had happened. Haldric was deathly quiet beside her, jaw clenched so tight she could hear his teeth grind. Asher, a few steps away, hadn’t spoken a word since the trial ended.

The Hunter stepped forward, holding a roll of parchment in one hand. The gathered hopefuls—those who had passed and those who had failed—held their breaths as they watched him.

“This is the final stage of the selection process,” the Hunter announced, his voice carrying across the stone chamber. “For those of you who failed, your paths do not end here. The city still has use for you. You will be assigned a labor role according to your strengths or the current needs of Stormhaven.”

A murmur of unease rippled through the crowd. This was, singlehandedly, the most important moment of their lives. It determined their labor role until retirement, their lodging, their clothing—even how much food they’d get each day. 

Food had always been scarce, but in recent years? If a Ratrod slipped into an upper-class stew, they’d probably dance to the nearest temple and thank Zephyrs for the blessing. The shortage had become nearly unbearable. And while students received the largest rations in the city, The Prime administration forbade them from sharing any of it with their families.

The Hunter’s eyes swept across them. Then, without further delay, he began.

“Haldric.”

Haldric straightened, shoulders rolling back as if bracing for impact.

“You are assigned to government administration in The Prime Archon’s office.”

A flicker of something—hope? Relief?—crossed his face. At least he wouldn’t be shoveling waste like some of the others who failed. But Praew could still see the tension in his shoulders. He wasn’t happy.

“Praew.”

Her stomach twisted.

The Hunter studied the parchment, then her. “You have been assigned to enforcement under the city’s constable Special Forces”

A hush fell over the room. Special Forces? That was no small position.

Praew swallowed hard. That was… unexpected. She had braced herself for something far worse, something lowly. But the way the Hunter looked at her, the way his gaze lingered just a moment too long—it unsettled her.

She didn’t thank him. Just nodded and sat back down, heart pounding.

He continued. Edrik and Korven were sent to labor assignments: one to the mines, the other to forge work. They both cried, begging for a reconsideration.

Then, only two remained.

The Hunter took a slow breath before speaking. “Two of you passed the trial.”

Renwick sat back, smug, hands folded behind his head. Asher remained stiff, staring at the floor.

The Hunter’s gaze settled on them. “You are no longer civilians. From this day forth, you are Hunters.”

Praew felt Haldric’s body lock beside her, felt a blazing hatred radiating from him like heat from a fire.

The Hunter continued.

“Renwick. You will be a Stone Hunter.”

A proud smirk stretched across his face. He leaned back as if he already knew. Of course he did. Renwick had been aiming for this since day one—hunting and extracting Noble Stones, working closely with the city’s elite.

Then, the final name.

The one that changed everything.

The Hunter straightened, rolling the parchment tight in his hands.

“Asher.”

Asher didn’t look up.

“You will be a Beast Hunter.”

The hall gasped.

For a second, the entire room was frozen.

A Beast Hunter.

Not just any Hunter. The most prestigious, the most feared, role any Hunter could obtain aside from a Mythical Beast Hunter like Srevlis.

Praew’s breath caught in her throat.

She knew what would happen next. .

Haldric’s fists clenched so tightly his knuckles turned white. His chest rose and fell in sharp, shaking breaths. And then—a low, guttural growl rumbled from his throat.

“That should have been me,” he whispered.

Praew turned to him, startled by the sheer hatred in his voice. “We’re going to be okay, Haldric. We were not assigned poor jobs. This may be the end of our hunting story, but realistically, we should be proud that we made it this far.”

Haldric was shaking. He wasn’t looking at Asher. He was staring through him.﻿﻿

Asher, to his credit, didn’t move. He didn’t acknowledge the gasps, the murmurs, the stunned expressions. His shoulders squared, his chin lifted slightly, but his hands were trembling at his sides.

The Hunter continued, “Beast Hunters are our first and last line of defense. You will leave the city, track down threats before they reach our walls, and ensure our survival.”

Then, his tone softened. He wasn’t talking down anymore; he began speaking as though to a friend.

“Brother Asher, Brother Renwick—my name is Hunter Steel. You have the choice to change your names so as to not implicate your families when you’re above surface. Hunters can be petty. If they learn your name and the city you hail from, they may bribe someone from your own city to harm your family. You can discuss this further with your team lead. But first…”

He gestured toward the crowd. “Say your goodbyes. You may not come back alive.”

Silence.

Praew turned to Asher.

He hesitated.

For the first time since the test, Asher looked at them. First at Haldric, still sitting rigid, shaking with fury. Then at her. 

Why is he looking at me like that? As if I hurt him. I’m not the one who broke their promise and betrayed their best friend.

She stared back, then nodded toward Haldric. She was staying out of their drama, but that was low. How could she trust a guy like that?

It was the same thing she told Hal and Asher years ago: If either of you ever get a girlfriend and cheat, we can’t be friends anymore. If someone can betray the person they love the most, what would stop them from betraying their friends?

Asher’s lips parted.

Zephyrs take you, this better be a bolting good apology, Praew thought.

But no, he looked away instead.

Asher’s hands curled into fists. Then, without a word, that bolting Ratrod literally turned his back on them and walked away. No apology or goodbye. The coward didn’t even glance back.

Renwick, on the other hand, grinned.

“Hey, Praew!” he called out. “Looks like I was half right, ehh? Don’t look so heartbroken. I’ll always come back for you.” Renwick flashed her a wink. “You can keep my bed warm until I do.”

Praew barely resisted the urge to throw something at his face. Haldric, however, didn’t hold back. His hands slammed against the bench, his voice a raw snarl. “You—”

Renwick only laughed as he turned and strutted toward the Hunter, joining Asher at the threshold.

The doors slammed shut behind them.

Praew sat there, staring at the empty space where Asher had stood. Haldric’s breath came ragged beside her.

No one spoke. The silence was deafening.



***



The corridor was dark, damp with the scent of stone and metal. Asher stepped forward, his boots echoing sharply against the stone.

     Renwick whistled beside him, hands laced behind his head like he didn’t have a care in the world.

“You sure it’s okay to leave like that? Not even a word to Praew?”

     “Why do you care?” Asher snapped. “Who are you to lecture me? You belittle and harass her constantly. She’s just trying her best. Don’t pretend you care now.”

     Renwick looked down in embarrassment—no, mocked embarrassment. A wide grin crept across his face.

“Lecture you?” He let out a chuckle, nudging Asher’s arm. “I’m just impressed. Never knew you had it in you.”

Asher clenched his jaw, ignoring him. Then, just ahead, someone shifted in the dim light.

“Asher,” a voice called out. It stopped them. Just for a breath.

The Prime Archon stood in their path, hands clasped behind his back, expression as stoic as ever. He was tall and narrow-framed, with neatly combed sandy blond hair streaked faintly with silver. His face was carved with patience and precision—high cheekbones, a blade-straight nose, and unreadable eyes that gave nothing away. He looked like a man built for stillness, each movement measured, each word sharpened before use.

     “Son, before you go, I just want to say—” The Prime trailed off as Asher walked right past him, then stopped.

     “You should know better than to call me that in public, Prime Archon. I had wondered if you would still try to sabotage me, or if what you said last night was true—that you had finally given up. But lo and behold… who else in this Zephyrs-taken city could twist people, rules, and words that masterfully but you?

   “Pitting me against my best friend?” Asher chuckled softly, fists clenched. “You’re not as clever as you think you are, if you thought that would stop me. After all, a Hunter mustn’t hesitate.” Asher resumed walking.

     The Prime Archon sputtered, as if to continue, but the words never came. Behind him, Asher heard Renwick greet The Prime.

     “Prime Archon. It’s an honor, sir.”

The Prime didn’t respond, likely still caught off guard. Not to be discouraged, Renwick continued:

“Must be difficult, maintaining the city’s approval. But who knows what the air may bring. Perhaps I’ll return with a prime contribution.”

Asher stopped in place. Wait, what?

  “Young man, what—was that you just said?” The Prime said sharply, his voice low and dangerous.

“Hmm? I won’t let you down,” Renwick said smoothly.

Then, without another glance, he turned and jogged to catch up to Asher.

“Damn,” Renwick drawled, falling in step beside him. “And you said I—strive to be the most hated? Guess it’s true what they say.”

Asher stopped in place, staring daggers at him. “And what’s that?”

Renwick just smiled, like he’d been hoping Asher would ask.

“That all Rivers run cold.”

Asher gritted his teeth.

That bolting Ratrod. Though… he’s right, isn’t he? Asher’s fists clenched at his sides. I’m sorry, Haldric. Praew.

But he stayed quiet. Letting Renwick rile him up would only prove him right.

Renwick only laughed. “Oh, this is gonna be fun.”

Asher followed in silence, breath steady, fists tight. The sound of their boots echoed against the stone as Hunter Steel led them through the heart of the city. 



***



     The path was a familiar one—one that every citizen had passed a hundred times but never truly seen. Two guards stood at the end of the corridor, their spears crossed over the entrance to a reinforced gate.

     The only passage to the surface. No one was ever allowed through. The moment the guards saw them approaching, their stance shifted. Their expressions hardened—not with suspicion, but with awe.

     Hunter Steel’s voice rang with quiet authority. “Hunter Steel, Apprentice Hunter Asher, and Apprentice Hunter Renwick requesting surface gate access.”

The air felt thick.

The guards hesitated for only a breath before snapping to attention. In near-perfect unison, they withdrew their spears and stepped aside.

The lock unlatched.

The heavy mechanisms groaned as the doors slowly swung open.

Renwick, for once, was silent.

Asher barely heard his own footsteps as he stepped forward, his heartbeat roaring in his ears. Every eye in the city would be on his back if they could see him now. The weight of it pressed against his shoulders.

And then—

The door fully opened.

Asher stepped through and the door shut behind them.

     The tunnel stretched ahead, splitting into two paths. To the left, a downward spiral vanished into endless darkness. But their path led upward—a steady, sloping incline carved into the stone. It was wide, yet somehow felt suffocating, the walls pressing inward the higher they climbed. No railings, no markers—just a path of smooth, worn stone leading into the unknown.

Asher and Renwick followed in silence, their boots scuffing against the floor. The only sound was the faint, steady rhythm of their steps and the occasional soft drip of water from somewhere unseen. The higher they went, the thicker the air became, charged with something unseen—something humming just beyond reach.

Asher rolled his shoulders, trying to shake the unease creeping into his bones.

The incline was gradual, but each step forward felt heavier than the last.

He exhaled, only to realize his breath came out shakier than before.

Renwick was quiet. Too quiet.

Asher flicked a glance at him.

The usual smirk was gone. The cocky swagger, absent. His shoulders were rigid, his eyes darting to the walls, the floor, then up ahead where the tunnel curved just out of sight.

For once, the bastard looked just as nervous as Asher felt.

The further they went, the more the air changed. It wasn’t something he could see—it was something he could feel.

The air moved. Not like the stale, recycled drafts of the underground, but something alive. It curled around him, slipping past his collar, gliding over his skin like unseen fingers tracing every inch of exposed flesh. It wasn’t harsh—it was gentle, almost soothing, yet it sent a shiver down his spine. It touched his face, his arms, his chest, an invisible tide washing over him, and for the first time in his life. It was eerie and intimate, all at once. Asher felt violated. He clenched his fists, resisting the urge to shudder. He had imagined his feet first touching the surface, but he had never imagined it would touch him first.

A strange, tingling sensation crept up Asher’s spine, buzzing at the back of his skull. He flexed his fingers, then frowned.

His skin prickled.

The hair on his arms stood on end.

He stopped mid-step, watching as his own forearm bristled—every fine strand of hair rising like needles, as if pulled by some invisible force.

A few paces ahead, Renwick cursed under his breath.

Asher turned to him.

Renwick’s arms were the same. Every strand of hair spiked upward, standing in jagged, unnatural rows along his forearm. His blonde hair, normally messy, now seemed to lift slightly at the edges, the static charge pulling at the very air around him.

“What in the ten stonesl…?” Renwick muttered, running a hand through his hair only for it to frizz back into place.

Asher swallowed hard, rubbing his arms, but the sensation didn’t fade.

The world felt wrong.

A pressure weighed against his chest—not enough to choke him, but enough to make breathing feel unnatural. He wasn’t sure if it was the incline or the sheer weight of what lay ahead, but his heart was hammering harder than it should.

He looked back at Renwick, who met his gaze.

Neither of them spoke.

They didn’t need to.

They were standing on the edge of something immense. Something terrifying.

And they knew it.

Asher clenched his fists, pressing his nails into his palms to ground himself.

Was Steel smirking ahead? That bastard was enjoying this!

They kept walking.

Each step felt heavier, like trudging through water. The incline curved again, drawing them closer, closer to whatever lay at the end of this path. The dim SolarStones embedded in the walls flickered, their glow paling against the overwhelming pull of the world above.

They passed another massive set of doors, their heavy iron frames groaning as they swung open. The moment the seals broke, a powerful gust of air blasted through the passage, slamming into them like a living force.

The moment the fresh air hit their lungs, both Asher and Renwick doubled over, coughing violently. It was as if they had taken a breath after nearly drowning—too much, too sudden, too real. Their chests heaved, their bodies instinctively rejecting the unfamiliar sensation. The air was richer, heavier, carrying a depth they had never known underground.

Hunter Steel stood unfazed, watching them with quiet amusement. “The air above is cleaner, thicker,” he said. “You’ll get used to it. Your lungs will stretch, expand. More air means more stamina. More stamina means more strength. That’s why Hunters are stronger than civilians.”

Asher forced himself to steady his breathing, although his lungs still burned. He exchanged a look with Renwick, whose usual smirk was nowhere to be found. For the first time since they’d stepped onto this path, the weight of what it meant to be here truly settled in.

Renwick clutched his knees, shaking his head.

Seeing that jerk suffer made Asher feel the whole experience was worth it.

Asher glanced at him, then back to the path ahead. This was only the beginning.

A strange mix of fear and exhilaration twisted in Asher’s gut.

He’d spent his whole life underground. Imagining the surface. Dreaming of it.

“This is the last gate! Prepare yourselves!” Steel called out.

He was finally here!

One last door

One final step.

His heart slammed against his ribs.

The doors crept open.

Then—he saw it.

He saw—saw—

The beautiful… bright light?

No. Not beautiful. NOT AT ALL.

BLINDING. ALL-CONSUMING. UNRELENTING.

A searing, golden-white radiance consumed everything, swallowing the world in an unbearable glare. Asher flinched, squeezing his eyes shut as pain lanced through his skull. Tears welled instantly, stinging as they streaked down his face. His hands shot up to shield his eyes, but it was useless—the light pierced through his lids, burned into his vision. Why didn’t anyone warn us?

Somewhere beside him, Renwick let out a strangled curse. “No one ever said it would be like this!” he shouted, voice panicked.”

Asher’s breath came fast, disoriented. “I—I can’t see! What’s happening!?”

Hunter Steel exhaled, unfazed. “It’s just the sun,” he said simply. “You’ll adjust.”

Adjust? Asher fought against the sharp throbbing behind his eyes. He had imagined this moment a thousand times—stepping onto the surface, seeing the endless skies, the vast lands beyond the city’s walls. But not this. Not blindness.

They were led forward, but they couldn’t see where. Their steps were unsteady, guided only by the firm grip of the Hunter pulling them along. Time stretched. The burning never stopped. Time lost all meaning. Flickers, Flashes, what was the difference? The burning in his eyes had dulled to something worse, a deep, pulsing ache that throbbed in sync with his skull. The world was still nothing but white, endless and merciless. No edges. No depth. No escape. He squeezed his eyes shut, but even that didn’t help. The afterimage was seared into his mind, haunting the darkness behind his lids.

Renwick yelled a string of curses beside him. “How are we supposed to bolting hunt if we’re blind!?”

No answer. Steel let them suffer. No reassurances —just silence. He wanted them to sit with it. To learn.

Asher exhaled sharply, his breath ragged. He could feel it now, the change creeping in at the edges. The air wasn’t as heavy as before. Cooler, maybe. His skin, which had prickled under the heat, now felt…normal? Had the temperature dropped? He frowned, trying to piece it together. Shadows—there had to be shadows now. Or maybe his mind was playing tricks on him, grasping for something, anything, that wasn’t the overwhelming brightness.

How long had it been? A whole day? More? He didn’t know. His body ached from standing, from waiting. From the sheer exhaustion of it all. He felt hollow. Heavy. Miserable.

Night fell, and with it, his vision returned—sort of.

The burning light faded, replaced by a deep, endless dark. For the first time since stepping onto the surface, Asher could make out shapes, vague and formless. The shadows of trees swayed in the breeze. The rocky terrain stretched in uneven ridges, disappearing into the distance. But beyond that? Nothing.

No sprawling landscapes. No endless sky. Just a void of ink-black emptiness.

He let out a slow breath. He could see again… but there was nothing to see.

Hunter Steel led them back inside the gates—thick, reinforced walls that loomed behind them, standing as the last barrier between the city and whatever lay beyond. It was still the surface, but it wasn’t the wild. Not yet.

A crude shelter had been carved into the stone, a resting space for newly inducted Hunters. Asher was too exhausted to argue. He sank onto the makeshift bed, muscles aching, mind heavy with the weight of the day. Renwick muttered something under his breath before rolling onto his side, already half-asleep.

The absence of silence felt unnatural—distant rumbling, nearby booms, and the eerie cries of beasts too close for comfort. Asher closed his eyes and forced himself to sleep.

When morning came, it wasn’t the harsh, blinding white of before.

Asher stirred, groggy, noticing first how much easier it was to breathe. His chest didn’t feel as tight when his lungs expanded fully.

The light was bearable now, filtering dimly through the thick gates. He sat up slowly, blinking against the golden hues spilling through the cracks.

Asher frowned, scanning the room. No sign of Renwick. No sign of anyone.

Except—

A figure leaned against the far wall, arms crossed, golden eyes glinting in the dim morning light. His spiky gray hair stood in wild, jagged tufts, like a storm permanently frozen in place. His armor—jet black scales, Tarnox hide, maybe?—reflected faint glimmers of light. A Hunter’s grin stretched across his face—sharp, knowing, amused.

Asher’s pulse quickened. He didn’t recognize the man.

“Where’s Renwick? And who are you bolting supposed to be?” The words slipped out before he could stop them.

     The stranger’s grin widened. “The name’s Azel. If you’re asking about that kid that was with you, I’m sure he already left with his team lead. But is that any way to speak to your team lead?”

Asher stiffened like he’d been struck. His stomach lurched.

“N-No, sir! Sorry, sir!”

Azel pushed off the wall, stretching his arms behind his head with a slow, deliberate roll of his shoulders—relaxed, but watching him like a wolf sizing up prey.

“You sleep well, kid?” His voice was smooth, casual.

Asher swallowed. “U-Um… yes, sir?”

Azel tilted his head, like he was giving it some serious thought.

“That’s good. I was worried you had trouble falling asleep. You seemed pretty exhausted… sleeping past the tenth flash. I figured maybe I should let you sleep in a little more, then let you find your own way to my base.”

 Asher’s stomach dropped. “No, sir!”

Azel clapped his hands together, the sharp sound like a BlastStone in the empty room.

“Then perhaps—if it’s convenient for you—you might want to GET OUT OF BED AND GET READY TO LEAVE!”

     Asher practically threw himself out of bed. “Y-Yes, sir!” He scrambled to pull on his boots, nearly tripping over himself as he stood. His heart pounded. He wasn’t sure if he was more afraid of Azel or of being left behind.

     Azel just chuckled, watching him with that bolting grin.

     “Have your eyes adjusted? Can’t have you screaming and crying again—” Azel put on an exaggerated, mocking voice. “I—I can’t see! What’s happening?—like a baby bird.”

     Asher’s face burned. “I—I didn’t—you saw that?”

     Azel smirked. “Don’t worry, kid. A lot of Hunters did. It’s tradition: hazing the greenhorns. You’re not the first, and you won’t be the last.”

     Then the grin faded.

     “But out there?” Azel’s voice turned hard, flat. “You won’t get the luxury of panicking.”

     His eyes locked onto Asher’s,

     “Out there, hesitation gets you killed.”

     The weight of those words sank into Asher’s chest like a stone.

     Azel took a step back, jerking his head toward the door. “Get dressed. We move now.”

“OPEN THE GATE!” Azel barked, his voice carrying over the grinding metal.

Asher stood rigid beside him, heart pounding as the final gate loomed ahead. The iron behemoth towered before them—thick, reinforced, lined with NullStone to keep the outside world at bay. Every groan of the gears sent a fresh spike of tension through his chest.

Azel turned to him, his golden eyes hard as steel.

“This is your first time on the surface.” His voice was gravel—rough, final. “You’ve heard stories. You’ve seen glimpses. You might think you understand it.”

Asher nodded stiffly.

Azel took a step closer. “You don’t.”

Asher’s breath caught.

“The surface isn’t the city. It’s not safe. It’s not predictable.” Azel’s voice was calm—too calm. “Every step is a risk. Every turn could kill you. And the second you stop paying attention, you die.”

Asher swallowed hard.

Without warning, Azel jabbed a finger against his chest—hard—forcing him to take a step back.

“You will do exactly as I say, when I say it, how I say it.” His tone left no room for argument. “If I tell you to stop, you stop. If I tell you to move, you move. If I tell you to drop, you drop. No hesitation. No questions. Do you understand me, son?”

Asher clenched his jaw. “Yes, sir.”

Azel held his gaze for a long moment, then gave a single nod.

“Good.”









Chapter 3 - To Scratch the Surface



The gate groaned, gears screeching as the slabs of iron parted. The widening crack revealed nothing at first—only a sliver of blinding gold. The air thickened. The world beyond beckoned.

Then the gates yawned open.

A gust of wind hit Asher’s face.

His breath hitched.

Before him stretched a world he had never truly seen.

The sky yawned wide above them, endless and terrible in its beauty, swirling with deep blues and violet hues, lightning crackling across the distance like veins of raw energy. Dark clouds twisted above distant peaks, rolling over mountains that stabbed toward the heavens.

The ocean shimmered on the horizon, vast and unknowable, its surface broken by jagged spires of rock and ancient wreckage. A storm brewed in the distance, flashes of light igniting the waves like fire.

And the land—rolling hills, thick forests, crumbling ruins of an older time—breathed with life. Strange birds with iridescent feathers swooped through the trees, their cries echoing in the wind. Giant beasts moved in the far distance, barely silhouettes against the horizon. It was beautiful. It was terrifying.

  Asher stepped forward, awestruck, eyes wide—

  A hand clamped onto his shoulder. Before he could react, he was yanked back hard. A sharp click echoed beneath his foot. Then—CRACK.

  A BoltStone erupted, jagged tendrils of electricity lashing through the air, scorching the ground where he had stood a second earlier. The scent of burning earth filled his lungs. Asher’s heart slammed into his ribs. He’d almost died. Just like that. No battle. No beast. Just one bad step.

  Azel’s grip didn’t budge. The older Hunter didn’t say anything at first. He just stared at him. Then his voice cut through the air like a blade.

  “I don’t remember telling you to move.”

  Asher panted, trying to steady his breathing.

  Azel shoved him back. “This is what I meant. You get distracted, you’re dead. You admire the view, you’re dead. You think for one second that this world isn’t trying to kill you?” He pointed to the scorched crater at Asher’s feet. “This place will rip you apart.”

  Asher’s awe shattered. His hands trembled. The surface wasn’t just beautiful. It was a graveyard waiting to happen.

  “Don’t make me save you again.”

  Asher nodded, then asked, “But why is there a BoltStone right in front of the gates?”

     “Wind picks them up and scatters the stones randomly,” Azel said. “This is why the surface is so unpredictable. Now stay close!”

     Asher followed.

     They moved in silence, navigating the twisting pathways of the ruined world.

     The ruins loomed like the bones of an old kingdom: shattered walls and broken towers swallowed by vines and time. The forest whispered, branches swaying unnaturally, the distant howls of beasts rising in the wind.

     Asher paid attention now. Every step was calculated, every shadow watched. At one point, a low, guttural growl rumbled nearby.

  Asher froze, his hand shooting to his weapon.

  Azel did not react. He only murmured, “Don’t… move. Slowly… exhale.”

  Asher did.

  The growl eventually faded.

  Only later did he learn he’d nearly gotten them both killed—almost drawing his blade on a non-aggressive beast. One that, according to Azel, “can wipe out entire Hunter parties when threatened.”

     As they continued their trek, Asher made sure to follow every one of Azel’s directions without hesitation, and after two terrifying days, they finally reached their destination.

    The base was built beneath the ruins of an old fortress—high stone walls still visible above ground, their decay mirrored in the cracked stonework lining the corridors below.

     Azel gestured for Asher to follow. He did, tension curling in his chest.

     They passed through a narrow corridor, then entered a nearly empty, dimly lit hall with just three figures scattered around the room. One was drinking. One was working on a broken weapon. The last lounged with a book in hand, barely acknowledging the new arrival.

     Azel clapped his hands together, the sharp sound echoing through the hall. “Alright, let’s get introductions out of the way. You’ll be training under each of them eventually, so listen up.”

     Azel gestured first to a broad-shouldered man sprawled lazily on his back, his face half-shadowed by a tattered hood. “Durian, our tracker. If something’s hiding, he’ll find it.”

     Durian raised one finger without looking, finished the last line of a page, then finally glanced up—light brown eyes sharp beneath the hood. He gave Asher a once-over, said nothing, licked his finger, and turned the page to continue reading.

     Next, Azel pointed at a woman with cropped hair and sharp eyes, sitting on a stool at a wooden table, one leg crossed over the other and a drink in hand. “Kyra, our runner. Best legs in the fortress.”

     Kyra rolled her eyes and gave a lazy two-finger salute. “What he means,” she said, glaring at Azel, “is if you need a message or supplies moved fast, I’m your best bet.”

     Azel pointed next to a broad-shouldered man hunched over a broken weapon. “Nico, our tinker. He builds, repairs, and occasionally makes things explode when they shouldn’t.”

     Nico didn’t look up from his work. “They explode when I want them to,” he corrected.

     Azel smirked, then finally gestured to himself. “And me? I’m Azel. I run this operation, keep these lot in line, and make sure no one dies too soon.”

     The group chuckled, but Azel’s gaze flicked back to Asher, sharp again.

     “Now, son, your turn. What’s your name?”

     Before Asher could answer, Durian snorted. “You mean you don’t already know? Two-day trek to get here and you never asked the kid his name?”

     A few chuckles followed.

     Azel rolled his eyes. “Figured I’d let the kid introduce himself before you lot started making bets on whether he’d survive.”

     “That’s rich,” Durian said. “You’re in for a surprise. We’ve got quite the honored guest gracing us today.”

     Azel looked at Asher, confused. “Go on.”

     Asher hated this part. Hated being in that bolting man’s shadow. He just wanted to be seen for himself. So he squared his shoulders, lifted his chin, and spoke in a voice just a little deeper than it normally was. “Asher Rivers.”

     Silence.

     The air in the room shifted. Kyra blinked. “As in . . .  The Prime Archon’s son?”

     Asher swallowed hard. “Yeah. Why?”

     The group exchanged glances. Something unspoken passed between them—something Asher didn’t understand.

     Azel’s jovial demeanor vanished. Then, finally, he exhaled through his nose.

     “No reason,” he said.

     But Asher knew that was a lie.

     Azel turned. “Come on.” He led Asher deeper into the base, Durian and Kyra following close behind, until the narrow corridor widened into a vast holding chamber. That’s when Asher saw them. The beasts.

     They were kept inside giant enclosures, reinforced with massive slabs of stone. Many of them. Beasts he was taught could never be farmed, only hunted. Yet here they were. Eating. Breeding. Existing.

     “Beautiful, aren’t they?” Azel said with a smirk.

     Asher stared, his mouth going dry. “Impossible.”

     Durian leaned against the fence, arms crossed. “Not impossible. Just inconvenient.”

     Asher turned to him, throat tight. “We were always told these creatures couldn’t be farmed. That they’d kill each other.”

     Durian grinned, but there was something too sharp in it, too knowing. “They will. If they’re starving. Keep them fed? They’re as docile as cattle.”

     Asher’s world tilted. He looked toward Azel for reassurance.

     Azel gave a short nod.

     Asher felt his pulse throb in his skull. “Then why is there a food shortage?”

     “Wait,” Kyra cut in. “You really don’t know?”

     Asher turned to her. “Know what?”

     Kyra opened her mouth again. “There—”

     “Isn’t a way to preserve the meat,” Azel said, shooting her a glance as he cut her off.

     “If you look closely, you’ll notice the Tarnox and Faelhens here don’t look like they do in the books you read in school. They change in captivity. We don’t fully understand it, but we call it de-acclimation.

     “De-acclimated beast meat starts to rot in a flash.”

     Kyra chimed in, glancing at Azel as she spoke. “After three days, the rotten meat becomes completely poisonous to humans. One bad shipment could kill a whole Floor in Stormhaven. That’s why we don’t risk it.”

     Azel nodded. “We study ’em. Breed ’em. Eat ’em ourselves. But for Stormhaven? We can only risk bringing a few live ones close to the city, butcher them, and have the meat served at ration stations within three days.”

     Asher didn’t respond. He couldn’t.

     Azel stepped out, leaving Asher alone with his thoughts.

     He returned a flicker later with a wide grin.

     “Your face’ll get stuck that way if you keep frowning. Relax, kid. I don’t think you’ll have to worry about that any time soon.”

     Then he tossed a shovel into Asher’s hands.

     “Congratulations,” he said, mocking. “You’re a Beast Hunter now. I believe the fearsome beasts have left you some droppings to hunt.”

     The ‘droppings’ were massive, steaming piles of manure. His job? Shoveling them out of the base.

     Asher didn’t know whether to scream, or run, or vomit.

     He stared at the tool, clenched in his hands. He thought he’d won. Thought he’d beaten the test and escaped The Prime’s shadow. But he was just a glorified farmhand. The other Hunters laughed. Only then did Asher realize his father had gotten the last laugh after all.

Was this really worth it?

Asher thought back to the moment on the platform during the Hunter Test. They’d been so close. With one final pull, he and Haldric would both become Hunters. And yet Asher hesitated. 

     His father’s voice crept in: ‘You and your little friends will never become Hunters. I forbid it.’

      Asher couldn’t help but glance up. The smug grin on his father’s face told him everything he needed to know. Asher realized the ploy—they were both about to fail.

     But the three of them could’ve failed together. That wouldn’t have been so bad, would it? Twelve years ago, didn’t he decide to become a Hunter just so he could remain by Haldric and Praew's side? 

     No… that’s not exactly right, is it?



***



Twelve years ago.

     “You guys look so stupid,” Asher Rivers, an eight-year-old boy with tousled blond hair, called out as he watched his two friends of the same age, flail around in the training yard.

     He endured them, mostly. Suffered their noise, their play-acting, their endless games of pretend swordplay. But they were the only kids who weren’t scared of him or his father. Praew and Haldric didn’t care that the Rivers family had practically ran the city since their city ruler, The Prime Archon’s health declined. They didn’t care that Asher’s father was the Heir Archon, The Prime’s successor of Stormhaven. Maybe that made them stupid. Or maybe it made them the only real friends he had.

     Praew and Haldric were swinging wooden swords at each other with the grace of drunken soldiers after a long shift. No stance. No control. Haldric had already tripped twice. Praew held her stick with both hands like a club. Asher winced every time they struck each other—it was chaos.

     Asher didn’t play with sticks anymore. He didn’t have to. His father had arranged private sword lessons for Asher, shortly after becoming the Heir Archon last year. Real blades. Real teachers. When his father became Prime, he would name Asher as the new Heir Archon. That was how it worked. Legacy, bloodline, and purpose. Asher wasn’t a child. Not like them.

     “Hah! I win again!” Praew cheered, stepping over a winded Haldric.

     “That’s not fair!” Haldric groaned from the ground, clutching his ribs. “I tripped! Zephyrs take you—I want a rematch!”

     “Language, Haldric.” The familiar voice came from behind them.

     They all turned to see Arthur Rivers approaching, hands clasped behind his back, posture as straight as a soldier’s spear. The Heir Archon. Asher’s father.

     “Sorry, sir,” Haldric mumbled, his face turning red.

     Arthur chuckled while shaking his finger. “Now, no need to take the Lord’s name in vain, is there?”

     Then something shifted.

     A female Zephyrian Acolyte emerged from the shadow of the corridor and leaned in to whisper to Arthur, whose face tightened instantly. “WHO is coming?” he said too loudly, glancing back at the children. The acolyte leaned closer, but Asher had already started walking. Quiet steps. Curious ears. He was close enough now.

     “Then, escort the children to the sanctuary,” his father whispered. “Asher’s friends must be kept safe.”

     The acolyte nodded. “Uhh, hey, kids,” she said too lightly. “How about we go on a little adventure?”

     Praew and Haldric didn’t move at first, but Asher kept close behind, listening.

     “What? Then where is Bresdin?” Arthur hissed as they walked. “He already killed one of those beasts. Can’t he handle another?”

     “He’s still recovering from that battle,” the acolyte replied. “He’s, umm, in no condition to face another one.”

     “Blast me backwards,” Arthur cursed under his breath. “Then who do we have?”

     “Umm only Godric was willing,” she said.

     “No!” Arthur snapped. “Absolutely not! I will not have my—” he stopped, looked over his shoulder, at Asher, and lowered his voice again. “I won’t have him sacrifice himself.”

     “I’m sorry, sir,” the acolyte said. “Godric was already seen heading to the surface. We, uhh, we can’t call him back.”

     “That bolting man!” The Heir spat. Asher never heard his father curse like this before. “How is The Prime doing?” 

     “He’s already safe in the bunker. But, umm, he is not likely to last long,” the acolyte said.  “We believe it’s best that you mentally prepare. Tonight, you will likely become the next Prime Archon.”

     Arthur said nothing. No celebration or pride. He just walked in silence until they eventually arrived. The bunker was on the Third Floor—a level Asher had never seen before. Its stone corridors were colder, the SolarStones lined the walls less frequently here. It held a large shrine in the center of the room with unlit SolarStones surrounding it.

      Praew and Haldric chased each other ahead, still laughing, still play-fighting. Asher watched them, shaking his head. Children. That’s all they were. He would never let himself be seen that way. He had a name to uphold, and soon, a title.

     Asher sat in the corner while they wrestled, mind racing with what he’d overheard. What beast? What sacrifice? Bresdin, his father’s greatest rival, injured? Godric going to the surface to be sacrificed? 

None of it made sense.

     An acolyte stepped into the room and cleared his throat, loudly. “The Ex Prime, Libur Cali, has passed away peacefully.”

     Arthur Rivers stood still for a moment, then nodded.

     “You are now The Prime Archon. Please come with us. There is information that only you may know.”

     Arthur turned to leave. Asher got up to follow, only for one of the acolytes to block his path. “Not you, child. Only The Prime may enter.”

     “But I’m the next Heir Archon,” Asher argued. “Can’t I come?”

     The acolytes chuckled.

     Arthur didn’t. He turned to his son, voice suddenly sharp. “Silence! You must earn that title, Asher. You need the approval of the city and a prime contribution. Then we’ll talk.”

     “But—” Asher started only to be cut off.

     “That, is enough,” Arthur snapped. “We will speak later.” Then he was gone.

     Praew and Haldric walked up beside Asher, quiet for once. 

     “Who wants to be a bolting Prime Archon anyway?” Haldric muttered.

     “Yeah,” Praew added. “Zephyrs take him! You really wanna sit behind a desk being boring all day? Or,” she said, nudging him. “You could be a Hunter with us. That’d be way more fun, wouldn’t it?”

     A nearby acolyte turned sharply. “You mustn’t speak ill of The Prime Archon. You should know—that’s treason.”

     Everyone in the room erupted into the chant:

     “AND JUSTICE WE WILL DELIVER!”

     “I’m sorry,” Praew and Haldric both said quickly.

     But Asher smiled. No one had ever dared say anything bad about his father before. Not even once. Maybe being a Hunter wouldn’t be so bad after all. At least… it would be entertaining.

     Praew nudged his arm with a sleepy grin. “Told you, you’re one of us now.”

     Haldric shifted against the wall, stifling a yawn. “Just don’t make me have to save you too many times.”

     Asher huffed through his nose—somewhere between a laugh and a sigh.

     No one said much after that. Praew and Haldric drifted toward sleep, slumped against the wall with half-lidded eyes.

     Asher stayed seated, hands clasped in his lap, gaze lingering on the SolarStones along the walls, dim but even, barely lighting the bunker’s stonework. Then, without warning, every SolarStone in the room faded out as he inhaled.

     By the time he exhaled, the room was in total darkness. The SolarStones stayed dark for another breath. Then, just as he began to inhale again, they faded back in—gentle, like their trigger had been a soft voice filling the room.

     That was a flicker. Everyone knew the term, but Asher had never felt it like this before. In school, they’d learned that every triggered SolarStone across the world, regardless of when it activated, pulsed together, all at once, in the same rhythm: faded out, stayed dark, faded back in.

     Three breaths. That was a flicker. It happened every sixty breaths—a natural measurement of time, as the world breathed.

     He began counting. At first, to see if he could time the next one. Then, to pass the time. But as he reached the sixtieth breath again, the room dipped into darkness once more. Another flicker.

     Then another. And another. By the twelfth or thirteenth flicker, something strange began to happen.

     He didn’t need to count anymore. Asher instinctively knew how many flickers had passed. His breathing lined up on its own, moving in time with the pattern. He could feel when the next flicker was coming. Like the space between waves. Like a heartbeat.

     This was it, wasn’t it? The Flickering Rhythm? A skill all children eventually gained—when they could instinctively feel the rhythm of time?

     The light dimmed again.

     Then, on what he knew was the twentieth flicker—everything changed.
