     The large, untriggered SolarStones near the shrine flared to life in a rhythmic pulse every half a breath. Sixteen times—eight breaths—where a full wash of color filled the room.

     That was a flash.

     Twenty flickers in a flash.

     Twenty flashes in a day.

     Each flash was unique, marking how late it was. One pulse for the first flash, twenty for the twentieth—signaling the start of a new day. The world’s way of marking a new phase.

     Asher closed his eyes and breathed. Time seemed to slow since gaining the RhythmStone. It no longer slipped by while he was unaware. He could instinctively count every breath, and every pause the world took. Most families saw gaining the RhythmStone as a cause for celebration. But why did he feel so… empty?

     He sat still, eyes on the wall, watching the flickers come and go. Then came the second flash. Then the third. Twelve flickers later, the door creaked open—and his father returned.

      There was nothing still about him. His robes were misaligned, his hands shaking slightly at his sides. His face was pale, save for his eyes—bloodshot red like he hadn’t blinked in a flash. Sweat clung to his brow, soaking into his collar. Whatever he’d learned behind those locked doors had shaken him to his core.

     “You, take the children home,” Arthur told the Zephyrian Acolyte from earlier, his voice shaking.

     Then he grabbed Asher’s arm and marched him through the long corridor that led to the Floor above. His steps were too fast. Asher had to half-jog just to keep pace, his shorter legs struggling to match the stride of a man unraveling.

     As they passed between Floors, Asher noticed how little he’d seen of the city before. The real city. Not just the First Floor, with its clean walls and polished halls, but the way Stormhaven truly sprawled out beneath the surface. A hollowed-out cylinder of layered stone, carved like a massive inverted tower. Three Floors deep. Each Floor wrapped in three concentric rings—stone walls so high and thick you could not hear the people on the other side. Each ring was its own sector, sealed off from the rest. Only one path in or out, one gate per wall. Each level felt like its own prison. Its own kingdom.

     The First Floor, where they were headed, was the most pristine. Home to the ruling class, the government offices, and Asher’s own quarters. The Second Floor held the schools and the Hunter office. The Third was for the market and laborers. And somewhere on the Second Floor, sealed with thick iron gates, was the one true exit to the surface.

     They had nearly reached the First Floor, nearing their home, when Asher finally spoke. He’d held the question in for as long as he could—walking in silence beside his father’s quick, purposeful stride. Asher watched as sweat beaded along The Prime’s brow, his father’s jaw clenched as he kept glancing over his shoulder, scanning corners, eyes darting as if tracking something Asher couldn’t see. 

     He’d never seen his father this shaken before.

     “Father… is something wrong?” Asher asked, just above a whisper. “You don’t seem… well.”

     Arthur stopped walking.

     He turned with a sharpness that made Asher flinch. Without a word, squeezing Asher’s arm hard enough to hurt, his father squatted low until their eyes were level. Asher could see everything now: the raw red veins in his eyes, the sweat on his temples, and something else, his breath was terrible.

     “Don’t you ever call me that in public again,” Arthur hissed. “Never again! Do you understand me?”

     Asher opened his mouth, but no words came. He just nodded, stiffly, barely able to meet his father’s stare.

    “Better yet,” Arthur said, “from now on, you address me as The Prime Archon. No matter where we are. No exceptions.”

     Asher had never heard his father speak like that before. Who was this man?

     Asher blinked back the sting in his eyes and stood straighter. A true leader doesn’t cry. A true Leader never shows weakness.

     Neither spoke as they continued walking. Asher felt something in him crack. And even then he knew, some cracks wouldn’t mend.

When they finally arrived back home, Arthur—no, The Prime Archon—said nothing at first. He walked into his quarters, dropped his robe, and changed into something more formal for the announcement he’d have to make soon. 

     A perfectly tailored white suit. The back bore a red trim that formed an intricate design of three majestic trees, each embedded with ten rubies carved into the shape of leaves.

The trees had deep roots that curled across the fabric, winding into the shape of a doorway beneath each trunk.

     Asher stood in the doorway, watching in silence, wondering what it all meant.

     That was when he saw it.

     A faint glimmer. A slight bulge beneath The Prime’s chest. The Prime Archon’s undershirt slipped for a moment as he buttoned up his tunic, and Asher saw a stone embedded in the left side of his father’s pectoral. A Sifaralith Stone. Asher didn’t know which kind, nor what it meant.

     “What is that?” he asked, before he could stop himself.

     Arthur’s hands froze on the last button. “You will never speak of this,” he said, voice low and final. “Not to anyone.”

     Asher nodded, but the image had already burned into him.

     They sat facing each other in the study. A thick circular stone desk separated the two while Arthur tried to explain why Asher couldn’t just inherit the title of Heir Archon. “It doesn’t work that way. Titles must be earned. The people have to see you. Believe in you.”

     But Asher wasn’t listening. Not anymore. He was still thinking about the stone. About his friends and how much his father had changed. “Maybe I don’t want to be the Heir Archon,” he said. “If becoming The Prime means ending up like you, I would rather be a Hunter.”

     Arthur slammed his flat palm on the stone table. “You! You foolish boy! You don’t know what you’re saying.”

     Asher and The Prime Archon both flinched, staring at the permanent palm print indented into the thick stone. The Prime only hesitated for a moment. “Do you even understand why we live underground?” Arthur barked, rising from his chair. “Do you know what’s up there right now?”

     Asher stepped back, shocked, but a leader— no, a Hunter doesn’t cower in fear. Matching his father’s outbursts, he yelled back, “Do you?! Or are you too busy hiding down here like a coward while real heroes like Godric sacrifice themselves!?

     The Prime stood there for a long moment, chest rising and falling like he’d just run through every Floor of the city. Through gritted teeth, he seethed, “You and your little friends will never become Hunters. It's too dangerous. I forbid it.”

     “You know you can’t do that,” Asher said, lowering his voice, “Once we graduate school, everyone gets to take the Hunter test. Not even The Prime Archon can stop that.”

     Arthur said nothing.

     Asher turned, heart pounding, and walked out of the room. He didn’t look back. After all, a Hunter mustn't hesitate.





Chapter 4 - Stone Face

Present Day – Ravtra, Second Week of Ferta

Four days after the Hunter Test



Praew wouldn’t hesitate again. It had only cost her—oh, nothing major. Just all her hopes and dreams. Still, her new job wasn’t terrible.

It was way worse.

“Focus, Praew.” Her new bestie, drill instructor Stone Face, spoke in a low, emotionless growl, like metal grating stone.

     She held the squat. Legs burning. Back straight. There were no chairs in this class, only pain. He paced the room, and they were required to follow him with their heads. A test of focus, apparently. Turn the wrong way, and he’d single you out. Lucky her.

     Who did this guy think he was? The Prime Archon?

     He stood at the front of the training hall now, arms behind his back. When had he even gotten there?

     “You have a six-week training camp,” he said. “There are three classes but you will attend a lecture on only one subject per day. Today is Sifaralith combat, tomorrow will be Shadow Intelligence, the following day will be on Ghost Operation. That’s how you will spend the first three days of every week.”

     The room wasn’t quiet. Groans, grunts, and panting escaped the lips of the ten recruits in waves. Muscles shook but no one dared complain.

     “The remaining three days are reserved for tests. If no test is scheduled, you will use that time for personal training.” He paused. “I advise you to use that time wisely.”

     Stone Face let the words hang.

     “To qualify for Special Forces, you must pass two of the three classes. If you fail two…” His gaze moved across the room. “You’ll be reassigned as a soldier, guard, or sent to the mines.”

     Great, as if this wasn’t stressful enough already!

     “Any questions?” Stone Face asked.

     Sweat slipped into Praew’s eyes. She didn’t blink.

     Behind her, Junya whispered, “I want to die.”

     Bpen spoke up. “To be honest, sir, I can’t feel my legs. Can we take a break?”

     The instructor nodded, then barked loud enough to wake the surface: “Stand at attention!”

     Everyone stood. Sighs of relief rolled across the room like a wave.

     “Free! Finally free!” Wannii shouted.

     The instructor’s eyes swept over the class shaking his head in obvious disappointment. 

     That— “bolting Stone Face! If you’re going to torture us, you should at least have the decency to tell us your name!”

     The room went silent as the whole class stared at Praew jaws agape.

     “What?”

     The instructor loomed over her. “Stone faced? How… original.”

     Praew finally understood her mistake. “I’m Sorry sir, I…I didn’t mean to say that out loud.”

     “You meant to only think it?” The instructor asked.

     “I—didn’t mean— “ she started to protest but the instructor cut her off.

     “I don’t give a bolting horn what you Ratrods call me,” he bellowed, loud enough to shake the walls—although his eyes never left Praew. “So long as you don’t mind joining her after class for an extra 5-flicker squat.” He let that sink in, then turned to the rest of the room. “If the rest of you want to leave on time, you’ll call me… let’s see…” He scratched his chin, voice dipping to a murmur. “Professor Stone has a nice ring to it.”

     Then, louder again: “You lot can call me Professor Stone.”

     “If there are no more interruptions… let’s begin,” he said, lowering himself into the only chair and desk stationed at the front of the class. He placed the tips of his fingers on some kind of stack, but from where Praew stood, she couldn’t make it out.

     “Each of you, come up and take one paper. Then return to your spot and get back in a squat formation.”

     A chorus of sighs and groans followed—frustration, exasperation, and more than a little reluctance.

     Praew stepped forward with the others, took her paper, and read it as she walked back. To her surprise, it was neatly laid out—a simple breakdown of each week’s lesson, with notes on whether there’d be a test.



Week 1 – Sifaralith Classifications. No test.

Week 2 – Stone Durability & Spiral Decay. No test.

Week 3 – SurgeStone Mechanics. No test.

Week 4 – NullStone Mechanics.

Test – Combat display to be fitted for a weapon

Week 5 – Combat Applications. No test.

Week 6 – Final Evaluation: Combat against me, a Hunter.



“As you can see,” he continued, “we begin today with classifications.	

“You all know of Sifaraliths. Smooth, circular stones with a crystal embedded into a carved design on both faces. That design and its color let us identify the type. But the trigger? Good luck.”

     He tapped the stone desk. “Everything you know about stones falls under what we call Core Stones. And that’s only one of three kinds. Trust that you’ll learn the other two.”

     “Beast Stones come next. They come in all shapes and sizes—literally. These stones are Embedded into the bodies of surface Beasts. That’s what gives them power. The embedding can usually be found in a specific appendage, depending on the species. Take Ratrods. Every single one has a SolarStone Embedded into its horn. Their trigger? ZephyrStones. Wind. One breeze and those horns light up.”

     “When a Ratrod dies, its body—flesh, bones, everything—crumbles to dust exactly one day later. But not the Embedded part. That remains.”

     He glanced around. “We call that crumbling. You’ll understand why soon.”

     “Some of you are probably wondering how we get food from beasts like that. Simple. If the Embedded appendage—like the horn—is removed within that one-day window, the rest of the body decomposes normally. Same rate as a human.”

     He stood and pulled something from beneath the desk, a Ratrod horn and some kind of claw to show the class. “That’s why Beast Stones look different. You might find a horn, a claw, a leg bone, a fang, a beak—whatever part the beast was Embedded with. These things litter the surface. And that’s part of what makes it so dangerous.”

     “They’re one and a half times stronger than Core Stones. But even they break. When they do, they dissolve and reform into a circular Core Stone of the same type, rarity, and trigger. This is also called crumbling.”

     Praew raised her hand. “Wait… if Beast Stones are stronger, why don’t we craft weapons out of those instead?”

     Stone didn’t miss a beat. “Would you rather have a metal spear where you can replace Core Stones as needed, or a one-trick bestial spear that crumbles without warning? Plus, it’s nearly impossible to find a craftsman skilled enough—and willing enough—to waste time on a temporary weapon.”

     He paused. “Now, Nature Stones.”

     “They look like Core Stones. But they shimmer like glass. Never try to use them.”

     “Nature is alive. And it doesn’t accept our control. These stones are Embedded into plants, trees, metals, stone, and more. They usually trigger on instinct. But if an external force tries to activate them? If someone tries to manipulate the trigger? Somehow, they always know. And they’ll either refuse—or trigger at you instead.”

     “No one understands how they know. But they do. When a plant—”

     A sharp voice cut through the room as Wannii stood up. “Zephyrs take me! We’re all stuck down here! What’s the point in learning any of this? It’s not like we’re ever gonna see the surface anyways!”

     “Says who? Your previous teachers?” Instructor Stone asked curtly. “You’re not children anymore. Your emotional outbursts will not tolerated. You will join Praew after class.” He stepped out from behind the desk. “Everyone, stand up while I address the Tarnox in the room.”

     The room fell quiet as he slowly locked eyes with each student before speaking again. “You all wanted to be Hunters, right? Grew up knowing you were going to be the one. You dedicated your lives to train for it. You did everything you could to prepare for that special day—the Hunter test—and still, you failed. Guess what? You’re not special. That’s the same story as every other foolish kid who failed this year. Time to get over it.”

     He let the silence linger before continuing. “I want you all to ask yourselves—why did you want to be a Hunter? Was it for the adventure?” He looked at Wannii. “To protect the ones you loved?” He looked at Praew. “Because someone inspired you?” He looked at Bpen. “Or was it for the fame and glory?” He turned to Junya. 

     “You’ll never have the title of Hunter. But that’s all it is—a bolting title. Here, you’ll earn something far more valuable: purpose. And the chance to truly protect this city. There’ll be times you go to the surface, visit other cities, collect resources, even fight beasts—anonymously. It’s not about the glory. It’s about what you do with the opportunity you’ve been given.”

     He paused one last time. “Out of everyone who failed the Hunter test, you ten showed the most promise. Make no mistake, you’ve been given a second chance. It’d be wise not to squander it.”

     I can… still see the surface? Praew blinked. Emotions she’d buried started to crawl their way out—hope, freedom, the thought of breathing real air. A single tear slid down her cheek. She wiped it away before anyone noticed… hopefully.

     Instructor Stone’s voice cut through again. “The next person to have an outburst will fail my class. If you understand, get back into a squat position.”

     Everyone dropped back down—no groans, no complaints, no gloom. Just silence. 

     “Now, where was I?” Professor Stone asked.

     “When a plant… sir,” Wannii said, surprisingly attentive.

     “Ah, yes.” He nodded. “When a plant or structure Embedded with a Nature Stone is destroyed, the stone remains. These stones can be nullified—but never surged. You can carry them, but that’s it. The only real use is planting them—giving them a chance to grow back into what they were.”

     He stepped behind the desk. “We won’t go into planting. That’s advanced. But I will say this—” His voice dropped. “Embedded nature remembers. If you try to manipulate it, or harm it, the thing might hold a grudge. And nature’s wrath can be dangerous enough that the only way to earn its forgiveness…” He leaned forward. “Is to destroy it.” He paused. “Once it’s nothing but a Nature Stone again… it forgets. Even after it’s replanted.

     “That will be all for today. I want each of you to seriously think about everything you learned—and more importantly, think about your future. Meet back here, same time tomorrow to begin your Shadow Intelligence training. Your instructor will be waiting for you.”

     He turned toward the door. “Class dismissed.”

     Praew stood up and stretched her legs, groaning under her breath. Junya rolled her ankles with a wince. “Ugh… my legs are killing me.”

     “I know,” Praew said, giving a half-laugh. “I thought we were gonna be stuck like that forever.”

     Bpen let out a low whistle. “I still can’t believe what he said. That we’re actually going to the surface.”

     Wannii gave a short nod as they walked. “Didn’t think we’d ever hear that.”

     Praew smiled. “Right? Maybe this class won’t be so bad after all.”

     “Praew. Wannii.” Instructor Stone’s voice cut across the room like a blade. They froze. “I don’t recall dismissing either of you.” He didn’t look up from the Beast Stones on his desk. “Get back into a squat position.”

     Praew sighed and muttered “Well, I’ve been wrong before,” 

     Junya and Bpen chuckled, then waved as they headed out. “See you at the ration station.”

Looks like her new best friend, Drill Instructor Stone-Face, had an unfortunately good memory.



***



   Praew’s legs trembled as she continued to squat. She was going to collapse. She was sure of it. Beside her, Wannii grunted quietly through clenched teeth. Her knees shook just as badly.

“Enough,” the instructor said at last. “You’re dismissed.”

They both groaned in relief and dragged themselves to their feet.

Wannii leaned on Praew’s shoulder as they hobbled toward the hallway. “Stone me,” she muttered. “My legs are gone.”

Praew gave a weak laugh. “If this is day one, we’re not gonna make it to day six.”

They followed the scent of warm food toward the nearest ration station. It wasn’t large—just a squared-off space with stone walls, simple benches, and a single narrow window carved into the far wall where trays were handed out.

Each citizen received an identification card every year—name, age, occupation, and their ration portion etched into it. Every meal required a ration slip, a thin strip of paper matching the info on their card. No slip, no food—unless you had enough standing to have someone collect it for you.

This was Praew’s first time in a private ration station. Only ten people were there—the students, a quiet distributor waiting behind the window with hands outstretched for her and Wannii’s slips. Trays of room-temperature portions sat behind the distributor, clearly cooled off in their tardiness.

Junya waved them over. She sat with Bpen at the far table, already halfway through her meal.

Wannii, getting her food first, slumped onto the bench with a sigh. Praew followed, grabbing a tray before joining the others. Grain mash, bitter-looking vegetables, and a tough strip of something that might have been meat, littered her tray. Rations were determined by role—and they were all trainees, no matter how sore their muscles felt.

“Oh! you two are still alive?” Junya asked.

“Barely,” Wannii groaned. “He made us squat for, like, forever.”

“I can’t feel my knees,” Praew added, poking at her mash. “I think they liquefied.”

Junya grinned. “Well, at least you gave us a head start.”

There was a short pause as they all started eating.

Then Junya spoke again. “But seriously… who is that guy?”

“I don’t know,” Bpen said, setting his spoon down. “But I’ve seen that face before.”

Praew froze, spoon halfway to her mouth.

“It was about six years ago,” he said. “Do you guys remember that incident? When a beast broke into the city?”

The rest of the table went still. Not waiting for a response, he continued.

“Do you guys remember that incident from six years ago? When that beast broke into the city’s Second Floor?”

Junya blinked. “Wait… that was real?”

“It was real, all right,” Bpen said. “It had black scales, long horns that curved forward, a thick bushy tail. It was lean—muscular. Steam poured from its snout every time it breathed. The thing moved like it was carved out of rage.”

Praew tilted her head. “That was a Tarnox, wasn’t it?”

“Yeah. That’s what they said.”

He looked down at his tray, voice steady but tense. “It rampaged through everything. And for some reason, it charged straight toward a child. Everyone ran—even the kid’s own parents abandoned him. But then… suddenly, he was there.”

Bpen glanced up, eyes locked.

“Our instructor. He stepped in, calm as anything. As the beast charged, a blue sphere of light appeared between its horns. The sphere grew as it charged forward but Professor Stone reached out with one hand, grabbing it by the horn, twisted, and redirected it. Sent the thing charging headfirst into the bolting wall. It slammed hard and dropped—knocked itself out cold.”

He paused.

“Then the professor looked back at the kid, asked if he was okay, and just… grabbed the beast by the horn and dragged it back up to the surface. Like it weighed nothing.”

Wannii stared. “That can’t be real. How could anyone be that strong?”

Bpen’s voice dropped, softer but clearer than before. “I don’t know. But I will never forget that face… because I was that kid. And that’s the uncarved truth.”

The table stared at him in silence. Only then did Praew realize—the whole room was quiet. All ten students were watching Bpen, eavesdropping on the story.

“What?” Praew asked loudly, causing everyone to look away, suddenly whispering to each other and pretending they hadn’t been listening.

Her friends still stared at Bpen in silence, so Praew spoke first. “You must’ve been so scared.”

“Well, I—” Bpen fidgeted. “You know, I mean… it is what it is, ya know? …I’m fine.”

Noticing his discomfort, Praew shifted the topic just slightly. “Just think—one day, you’ll be the one standing between a beast and a civilian. I have to admit… I admire your bravery.”

Bpen couldn’t hide his smile, holding his head a little higher. “Yes, well—I mean, we all will soon, don’t you think?”

The others nodded. They continued to eat in silence for a while, until Junya whispered, “The portions are way smaller than they were in general education.”

The others agreed. Wannii added, “They announced the food shortage got worse recently.”

Praew looked up. “If that’s true, then why not accept more Hunters each year to get more food?”

The table went quiet. No one answered. Then Junya leaned in and whispered, “Because the food shortage is fake.”

Wannii instantly hushed her, hissing, Are you crazy?” She looked toward the distributor’s window. “Saying something like that could get you killed!”

Junya just rolled her eyes. The rest of dinner passed in silence, heavy with thoughts of Professor Stone, the Tarnox, and the food shortage.

They eventually said their goodbyes and went their separate ways. Praew waited a little longer before leaving—just so she could walk home alone.

She lived on the Third Floor, in the slums of the outer ring. It was something she’d never told any of her friends except for Haldric. It wasn’t that she was ashamed. She just knew they would pity her. And there was nothing she hated more than pity.

Her parents worked hard, and she couldn’t have been prouder of them. The best part about her new job was knowing that, one day soon, she could move them to the First Floor. From the slums to upper class. It was a privilege granted to all Special Forces and Hunters. That alone was reason enough to give this everything she had.

As Praew walked, her thoughts circled back to the food shortage. It wasn’t just the daily rations that had been reduced lately. It was the supplementary slips too, the ones people used to buy what were considered non-essentials: everything from snacks to clothes, tools, bedding, and furniture.

Regardless of what The Prime thought, those slips mattered. Not just for comfort, but for survival. And for Praew’s parents, they meant everything.

Her father worked as a stone sculptor, smoothing out walkways, shaping doorframes, sometimes carving benches or storage blocks when someone could afford them. Her mother was a woodworker. She crafted smaller things—handles, shelves, utensils, and, once in a while, something decorative if someone had slips to spare. But lately, fewer people did.

When supplementary rations dried up, so did the orders. And no matter how hard her parents worked, they couldn’t flourish. Eventually, her family had to move from the Second Floor middle ring to the Third Floor slums.

She reached the door of their home and pressed her hand to the stone, letting the stress of the day slip off her skin. From the outside, the house looked like nothing—just another shallow-cut home along the outer cave wall. But inside? Inside, it felt like stepping into a different world.

The floor was smooth, shaped by her father’s careful hands. The walls were carved with delicate patterns—winding plants, leafwork, and soft curves he called “flowers.” Real wooden chairs with soft cushions surrounded a table her mother had built, its edges curved and inlaid with darker wood in swirling patterns.

She’d been inside Asher’s home before. The Prime Archon may have had a larger home, but not even he had a more beautiful one.

This was the most beautiful place in the city, and it was her little secret.

Her father, Raksa, looked up from his spot near the wall, dust still clinging to his arms from work. “There’s our little pebble,” he said with a smile. “Well? Did they finally put you in charge yet, or are they still figuring out how brilliant you are?”

Her mother, Malai, stepped in from the other room, brushing her hands against her skirt. “Why are your shoes still on? And how was your first day?”

Praew chuckled as she leaned down to unlace her boots. “It’s not really a job yet. Six weeks of classes and tests first. They call it ‘Specialized training.’”

“Oho,” Raksa said, raising his brows. “So you’ve got six weeks of being yelled at by angry old men, then?”

“Basically,” she muttered, stretching her shoulders.

Malai studied her face, then softened. “You look ready to collapse. Go wash up and get some sleep, little pebble.”

Praew turned toward her room, then paused. “Hey Mae, Papa… I heard the rations were reduced,” she said quietly. “What are yours like?”

Her parents exchanged a look before Malai answered. “Oh? We didn’t even notice,” she said lightly. “Nothing to worry about. We’re still getting plenty.”

Raksa nodded beside her. “Don’t you have enough on your plate? Focus on your studies,” he said with a quiet chuckle.

Praew hesitated—just a moment—then nodded and got ready for bed.

Feeling clean and refreshed, she picked up the wooden handle of a device shaped like a hairbrush. She’d come up with the idea when she was ten, after studying Sifaraliths for the first time. Her parents surprised her with a working model on her eleventh birthday.

It had a smooth wooden grip and a stone head, inlaid with two carefully embedded Sifaraliths—stacked one behind the other. A SolarStone triggered by force, just strong enough to create heat from the light. And behind it, a ZephyrStone, triggered by the SolarStone, calibrated to blow a steady stream of warm air.

She smacked the side of the handle a few times against her palm—just enough to activate the trigger.

The device sizzled gently in her hand. Heat built across the surface. A moment later, a soft stream of warm air flowed through her hair.

Praew never understood why her parents refused to sell the design to the engineering or research branches of Stormhaven. Surely a device like this would earn them a mountain of supplementary rations. Every time she brought it up, they’d just smile and say, “It’s not ours to sell.”

 Once she stepped into her room and shut the curtain behind her, the quiet settled around her.

Her bedroom was even larger than her parent’s. The bed stood in the center like a work of art on display—an elegant blend of stone and wood, its arched frame carved with smooth precision. Thick bedding rested on top, layered with soft blankets and perfectly fluffed pillows. It looked impossibly luxurious compared to anything outside these walls.

The frame displayed carvings of winding floral patterns, gentle vines and petals etched in perfect symmetry. On the walls, her father had shaped an entire mural of the surface world—trees rising toward a carved sky, gentle hills marked with grass and water, and in the center, a radiant SolarStone etched into the ceiling like a sun frozen in time.

Just above her headboard, tucked into the curve of the arch, was her favorite part, a small carving that read, “We love you always -  Mae & Papa.”

Praew kneeled on the bed, her fingers brushing the words—her nightly ritual. After all, her parents had sacrificed so much to give her this life.

She slipped beneath the covers and closed her eyes, dreaming of the day she could do the same for them.









Chapter 5 - Deafening Silence



Praew’s eyes fluttered open in the quiet, pre-morning stillness of her home. The plush embrace of her luxurious bed tempted her to linger, but she slipped out with practiced coordination.

“Ouch,” she breathed through gritted teeth, rubbing her leg where she bumped it on the corner of the bed.

Coordinated or not, without the natural dawn they spoke of in storybooks about the surface, this hour still belonged to her. No one else was crazy enough to start their day this early by choice. After quietly getting ready, careful not to wake her parents, she slipped out of the house.

As Praew moved through the familiar streets, she encountered a girl whose bubbly chatter broke the silence. The girl had a warm, inviting smile, and her dark almond-shaped eyes sparkled with an infectious energy. Her jet-black hair bounced as she walked, framing her golden-brown skin that seemed to glow even in the dim corridor light.

“Hi! You’re up early too! What’s your name? I love these quiet mornings, don’t you?” the girl chattered cheerfully, whether Praew responded or not.

Praew offered a few grunts when absolutely necessary, silently wishing the girl would take a different path. There was nothing wrong with her—it’s just that one of the main reasons Praew got up this early was specifically to avoid talking to people before breakfast.

Still, she had to admit—there was a flicker of curiosity, even amusement, at the girl’s boundless energy. Zephyrs take me, who had this much enthusiasm so early?

They reached the cafeteria, which stood empty and quiet. The girl declined breakfast with a quick wave and a bright smile, disappearing down another hallway. Praew grabbed a portable grain bar, swiftly collecting her breakfast and continuing on to class.

She stepped into the empty classroom from yesterday. In the doorway to her left sat an embedded stone desk, positioned at the front of the class for the professor. A  large slate board for writing was embedded in the wall behind the desk, and still no chairs for the students. Zephyrs take me! Are we expected to squat for every class? Praew silently complained as she walked to her spot. Rounding the desk, she yelped and jumped back in alarm. She wasn’t alone. Near the other side of the desk, dressed entirely in black shoes, shirt, socks, gloves, and a featureless mask, a figure was seated cross-legged on the floor, motionless. The mask had no eye holes or features, only a strange symbol painted on it. Could they even see out of that creepy thing?

“Um, good morning,” Praew ventured, her voice soft. The figure didn’t respond, remaining perfectly still. What was with this person? Feeling a bit awkward, Praew quietly sat down, mirroring the figure’s posture, and waited as the rest of the class began to arrive.

     Assuming it might be some kind of test, Praew decided to mimic the figure’s silence, crossing her legs and closing her eyes. 

     After what felt like forever, another student entered. They—no, she greeted the silent figure first and received no response. Was that Junya? She asked Praew what they were supposed to do. Praew simply kept her eyes closed, offering no answer. 

     Praew peeked her eye open just enough to see Junya mimicking her, sitting down to meditate. Praew and Junya caused a ripple effect as students trickled in. One by one, they sat down to meditate in silence, until the entire class was seated quietly. A chain reaction of stillness spread through the room.

     Praew’s eyes snapped open to a piercing, high-pitched screech. As she blinked away the shock, she saw the masked figure at the front, dragging a dagger across the slate board to produce that grating noise. The sound jolted everyone out of their meditation.

     The figure picked up a slender stone from a ledge beside the board and began to write. As they pressed it against the slate, the stone crumbled slightly, leaving a trail of white streaks that formed the letters. “Name: Instructor”—there was a noticeable hesitation—then they added “Shadow” in a deliberate, bold script. Below that, they wrote “Shadow Recon Instructor.” The room remained completely silent as everyone took in the name and title, the figure’s presence commanding an eerie, authoritative aura.

The masked figure’s hand moved swiftly, inscribing on the board: “From now on, any speaking during class time will result in immediate failure of this class.”

Praew glanced around, noting the wide eyes and tense postures of her classmates. Some exchanged anxious glances, while others sat perfectly still, absorbing the gravity of the rule.

The figure then inscribed the second rule: “No questions will be tolerated in this class. If there’s anything you do not understand, figure it out by any means necessary or prepare to fail the class.”

By any means necessary? Praew thought. How were they supposed to learn anything if they couldn’t even ask questions?

She shifted her weight, arms crossed, as she began tapping her fingers against her bicep, slowly at first, then faster. She wasn’t sure if this was exciting or terrifying. Probably both.

Instructor Shadow never stopped writing. Snapping back into focus, Praew continued to read.

Week 1 – Silent Movement

Week 2 – Tailing

Week 3 – Information Gathering

Week 4 – Infiltration

Each word was carved out with the same crisp clarity, no hesitation. The stone the instructor held clicked once more, then stopped.

Praew felt sweat bead on her forehead. Though she wasn’t squatting this time, her legs began to shake from the weight of something even worse—dread.

Everyone knows I’m clumsy. Why in the bolting seven stars am I in a class that specializes in everything I’m bad at? I can’t talk. I can’t ask questions. And I’m supposed to just figure it all out on my own? Zephyrs take me, well, you might as well fail me now and save yourself the trou—

A click snapped her back into focus as a hidden stone door scraped open along the far-right wall, directly beside the spot where the instructor had been meditating earlier.

The instructor beckoned for them to follow, then turned without a word and stepped into the hidden passageway. One by one, they moved after her, disappearing into the narrow tunnel beyond.

The room was massive. Dimly lit, built in the same style as the one before, just stretched out to impossible size. On the left wall stood another slate board, embedded clean into the stone like before. But it was the right side that drew her attention.

Two narrow pathways looped along the far edges of the room, but in the center was an obstacle course unlike anything Praew had ever seen.

Directly ahead, a stretch of stone ran forward, lined on both sides by unlit, embedded, SolarStones. The path began with the kind of worn, lightly textured stone she was used to seeing in the city—solid, dependable, with just enough traction underfoot. Then it shifted abruptly into rough terrain, jagged and uneven, with chunks of rock embedded at awkward angles. Past that, the stone smoothed into something dangerously polished—like the finish of a well-kept blade.

Just beyond that lay a patch of what looked like sand. She only recognized it because of her father’s work, though she’d never seen this kind of brown sand before.

The rest of the room faded into shadow. Whatever came next was swallowed by the dark.

Another ear-breaking screech snapped her focus back to the instructor.

The dagger scraped across the slate board again—just like before—pulling every eye toward it. While they were distracted, the instructor had already filled the board with a detailed outline of their upcoming test. Or perhaps it was already there.

Praew’s eyes scanned the writing:



FINAL TEST: SILENT COURSE + INFILTRATION

Objective:

Move silently through the course. If you complete it, you will be given a clue to identify a target and a specific item they carry. You must obtain this item without being caught. Completion of both tasks is required to pass this class.

Terrain Test: Four Stages

You may attempt this course once per week. You are allowed six attempts total. The sooner you pass, the more time you will have to complete the second half of the test. Time matters.

Stage 1 – Stone Path

A stretch of stone lined with embedded SolarStones—triggered by sound. It begins with the kind of rough-textured stone common in the city, shifts into jagged, uneven rock, and ends with smooth, polished stone.

Walk without making a sound. If any stone glows…Simple. Just pretend she wasn’t born with thunder in her feet.



Stage 2 – Sand Track

A narrow stretch of brown sand with hidden ZephyrStones buried throughout. The trigger is still sound—too much noise, and a gust of wind will lash through the path, throwing sand like needles across your skin.

Praew imagined coughing up grit before she even stepped in.



Stage 3 – Grass Field

Tall grass covers the area. Hidden beneath are BlastStones, control your sound. If triggered, the stones will explode beneath your feet and launch you backward. Severe injury is likely.

Great! How many bones am I going to break before I pass?



Stage 4 – Water Crossing

A shallow stream scattered with uneven stones. BoltStones lurk below the surface. One loud sound, and they’ll discharge—while you’re submerged.

That one pulled her attention. She swallowed hard. Was this a second chance or a death sentence? Only a lunatic would stay. 

She should’ve walked away. Should’ve bolted. Yet her feet just stood there—quietly defiant, like they knew something she didn’t. They didn’t speak, of course. But somehow, they still managed to say, “we’ve got this.”

And the ridiculous part? She almost believed it.



Final Test- Treason

You are being tested on your courage and your ability to move, observe, and adapt in total silence. If you pass, you will be given a clue. That clue will point you to your target, a government official or a Hunter.

You must identify the person, study their behavior, find the right moment, and steal the correct item. You may only steal once. If you are caught, just like the surface, failure may result in imprisonment—or death. The Shadow Recon Department will never protect an operative from the repercussions of their own failure. That said, they will never allow you to face judgement from a mission well done.

Though you have six weeks to complete the test, don’t forget—the sooner you succeed, the more time you’ll have to trail your assigned target. The more time you spend observing them, the better your chances of retrieving the correct item. If you wait until the sixth week to pass, you may only have days—maybe a few flashes—to study your target. That is nearly impossible—and has only ever been accomplished by one Shadow before.

“This has to be a joke.”

Jorpen, a usually quiet student with eyes that looked like they’d never known sleep, blurted out without remorse.

“You want me to risk my life walking through water? You expect me to steal from a bolting Hunter, or a government agent? And if we get caught, you won’t even protect us? You’re out of your bolting mind!”

The screech of the instructor’s knife against the slate board was louder than ever. A response was already written out for Jorpen: “You spoke. You have failed this class. Leave immediately.”

Jorpen ignored the words, turning to the others for support.

“You guys can’t seriously be considering this. Look at this! This is treason!”

He raised a clenched fist.

“And justice we will deliver!” he chanted—alone.

No one joined him.

He scanned the room slowly, locking eyes with each traitor.

The silence told him everything.

In this class, speaking meant failure. And every one of them had already made their decision. None would waste their second chance.

Jorpen took a breath, stepped forward, and froze in place. 

In the blink of an eye, the same dagger that had scraped the slate just moments ago now pressed against Jorpen’s neck.

Its tip pierced just enough to draw a single drop of blood. The class stared in shock as the blood slipped down his skin.

Silently, he raised his arms and allowed  the professor to march him out of the room.

No one dared speak while Instructor Shadow was gone. The nine students stood in awkward silence, exchanging glances while they waited.

Ten flickers later, the instructor returned in their usual silence. They entered the room and pinched the dagger between their forearm and bicep, wiping it clean across the crease of their inner elbow. Then they turned and began clearing the board.

Jorpen’s okay… right? Unease twisted in Praew's gut as she watched what looked like something a warrior might do after a kill. Glancing around at the others, she could tell—she wasn’t the only one thinking it.

The professor began writing again.

“If you wish to move silently, you must first choose the correct clothing.”

Another line followed immediately.

“Your goal is balance, clothing tight enough to muffle sound, but loose enough to hide your shape. Too tight, and it reveals your gender. Too loose, and it betrays your position.”

More scratches echoed through the room.

“People wear clothes to be seen. You wear them to disappear.”

Something about that line resonated with Praew. She paused, absorbing the meaning before continuing to read.

“Black is ideal: subtle, stylish, and it vanishes into darkness. Otherwise, choose colors that blend with your surroundings like gray or brown for cavern walls. Matching tones without drawing attention makes you harder to remember.

“If I point to you, go to the center of the stone test, stand in place, and flap your arms until I signal you to return to your spot.”

They turned and pointed at a student wearing layered, oversized wraps.

The student stepped into the center of the SolarStone path. As they began to move, lifting and lowering their arms with casual motion, the stones lit up on both sides, flaring in response to the rustling fabric.

Then, without a word, the professor stepped forward and took the same position.

They flailed their arms with exaggerated, almost mocking motion—far more reckless than the student had been, yet the stones didn’t flicker once.

Without pausing, the professor returned to the board. “Your shoes matter just as much. Soft soles on the inside aren’t enough. You need cushioning on the outside. Sound-absorbing outsoles are often the difference between life and death.”

They circled the final sentence twice 

“Silence begins before you take a step.

“We will now go over how to step. When I point at you, run to the other side of the stone test through the SolarStone path and back, as quickly and quietly as you can. Observe how much the SolarStones light up when you move.”

Professor Shadow turned and pointed at Praew.

She took off, doing her best to stay light on her feet. It didn’t help. The SolarStones flared beneath her with every step. Bright, unforgiving pulses that chased her across the path. When she reached the far side, the professor pointed again. She ran back. The stones lit up just as harshly, if not worse.

Each student followed one by one. Some sprinted, some hesitated, but the result didn’t change. Flash after flash lit up the path. Not one of them crossed without drawing light.

Once the class returned to the starting side, the professor turned back to the board.

“To run silently, lift your heel slightly, then roll your foot smoothly toward it—but never let the heel touch the ground. Each step should gently rock your foot from toe to mid-foot. Lift your toes at the end of each step, then place them down first to begin the next. This cushions impact and absorbs sound. Don’t lift your heel too high, or others may notice. It may feel awkward at first. Practice until it becomes second nature.”

The professor pointed again. Praew stepped forward, adjusting her form. She tried to focus—lifting her heel, rolling her foot just as instructed. The result was… slightly better. The stones still lit, not as explosively—until she tripped, collapsing hard in front of her classmates.

A few could barely contain their laughter, too scared to let it out in case it counted as speaking.

Good, she thought as she returned to her spot. Let them laugh. It’ll be that much more satisfying when I’m the first to pass every test.

The others followed. Some fumbled the rhythm entirely, others managed to dull the glow, but none crossed clean. Even the best attempts left a trail of flickering stone behind them.

The professor returned to the board.

“Walk to the other side and back when I point at you.”

Praew moved naturally—unadjusted—and the SolarStones lit with each step. The rest of the students were no better. The stones responded like a heartbeat, echoing every thump on the ground.

Another note followed. “Now walk on your toes. Raise your heels slightly—not enough to be noticed, just enough to reduce sound.”

She tried again, heel slightly lifted, careful to balance—but the stones still flared, just more lazily now. The others mirrored her. Some improved. Some didn’t. Not one made it across in silence.

Just when Praew began to think it was impossible, the professor stepped onto the path. They ran—quick and fluid. It looked like ordinary movement, yet not a single SolarStone triggered. As if NullStones were tied to their feet.

How did they make it look so easy?

Excitement stirred in her chest at the thought of mastering that kind of control.

The final message appeared on the slate.

“To pass each test, you must run through each path and walk back without triggering a single Sifaralith. Your first chance to attempt the test is in three days. Practice well. Class dismissed.”

By the time Praew finished reading the board, the professor was already gone.

None of the nine students said a word as they left the secret chamber. Bpen was the first to speak once they reached the hallway. “Stone me, that was intense! You guys think Jorpen’s still, you know… kicking?” No one answered. Praew just shrugged. Too nauseous to eat, she went straight home.









Chapter 6 - To Kill or Die



In the blink of an eye, Praew was heading to class again. Yesterday was a blur—from going home, to crashing into sleep, to getting her ear talked off again this morning.

“I’m sooo excited! Today is basically my first class ever! I’m so nervous I feel like I could hurl!”

That chatty girl from yesterday was walking beside her again.

Zephyrs take me.

Praew had even left earlier than usual just to enjoy a silent morning. What was this girl doing—waiting for her?

“What do you think? Any advice?” the girl asked, snapping Praew out of her thoughts.

“Oh—um, have you tried meditation before class? It might help,” Praew offered.

“Yeah, I tried that yesterday. Helped a little but… now I’m even more nervous today. By the way, are you okay? You seem like you’re limping a little.”

Praew hadn’t even realized it. Her fall from yesterday already felt like a distant memory.

“I’m fine,” she snapped, a little too quickly.

The girl seemed to get the hint. “Oh, uh… right. Well, I better not hold you up. I’ll see you later, Praew!” She took off running, her footsteps sluggishly loud down the corridor.

Who was that girl? She still hasn’t even told me her name.

As Praew grabbed her food and headed to class, something else occurred to her.

How did she know my name? Did I ever introduce myself?

Whatever. It was too early for this.



***



When Praew stepped inside the classroom, another masked figure sat meditating—shorter than the one from yesterday, but dressed in the same all-black uniform. This one wore a different kind of lifeless mask.

What stood out most wasn’t the mask. It was the fact that the figure was meditating on top of the desk.

The indecency of it made every student hesitate in silent, obvious discomfort. No one said a word—still scarred from yesterday’s silence rule, still unsure if it was in effect. Nine students. No sign of Jorpen.

Oh no. He’s not actually dead… is he?

The masked figure climbed down without a word, turned their back to the class, and wrote on the slate board:

Ghost Operation

A muffled female voice came from behind the mask. “My name is—ughh, I hate these stupid things. Like seriously, how are we supposed to talk with these on when you can barely breathe?”

Praew’s brow twitched. The voice irritated her instantly, though she couldn’t explain why.

The figure pulled off the mask and head covering in one motion, revealing that same warm smile, those dark almond-shaped eyes, jet-black hair, and golden-brown skin that somehow still caught the light in this dim room—along with the same over-talkative energy that had haunted Praew’s mornings.

“You!?” Praew blurted.

“Me!” the girl beamed, all giddy and glowing.

The insufferable, walking EchoStone who had annoyed her two mornings in a row now stood at the front of the class.

“My name is Mai Azadi,” she said, hands on hips. “And in this class, you beautiful rays of SolarStones are going to learn how to assassinate people.”

She clapped once—loud and sharp. “Now I know what you’re thinking. Me? Kill someone? But I’m just a sweet little Faelhen trying to get through life mimicking everyone else—without clawing anyone!”

She spun around, twirled a writing stone between her fingers, and underlined the words on the slate.

Ghost Operation.

“That’s adorable,” she said brightly. “Truly. But no. If you’re in this class, you’re going to learn how to stab people in exactly the right place to make sure they don’t scream. Or better yet—how to make it look like they died in their sleep. Or tripped on their own stupidity.”

She turned back to the class, eyes twinkling. “Fun, right?”

No one spoke.

“Oh, still doing the no-talking thing?” she said, mock-whispering. “That was Instructor Shadow. So mysterious. I bet they even taught you to breathe quietly.”

She leaned over the desk. “Not me. Breathe loud. Ask questions. Or don’t. I’ll just keep talking anyway.”

Right on cue, the room flickered—one full breath of darkness.

Zzip. Zzip. Zzip.

Air split in rapid succession.

“Ow—what the—”

“Something hit me!”

Praew flinched at the sting. Her whole body tensed—something had definitely struck her ear. Her hand shot up on instinct. A thin needle pierced clean through her right lobe, warm and smooth, embedded like a forged earring.

The light slowly returned.

Mai stood at the front, clapping. “Beautiful form! Not a single miss.”

Praew looked around—every student had one. Perfect placement. Gleaming tips. Not a drop of blood.

Mai beamed. “Oh, and those were soaked in a very deadly poison, by the way. But don’t worry—it’s very slow-acting. You’ll be just fine… if you finish class and do your part. Otherwise, well… no antidote for you.”

A hand shot up. Bpen looked nervous and flushed, his voice cracking as he stood halfway.

“Wait, what? You poisoned us? That’s insane! And assassinating people? That’s not heroic, it’s cowardly!”

Mai tilted her head, eyes narrowing just a touch. Her smile didn’t move.

He tried to go on. “You’re clearly the same age as us, how can you be our instructor? You’re just some—”

Thip.  

As Mai covered her eyes with her right hand, a metal needle flicked from her left, piercing his other ear mid-sentence.

She sighed, still smiling but her voice grew sharp. “That was a larger dose of poison. Any more and you won’t make it to the end of class. Consider this a mercy. If it hit your eye, you would be blind. Lips or tongue? You wouldn't have survived another breath.”

She looked around at the rest of them, voice chipper again.

“To learn how to assassinate is also to learn how not to be assassinated. Cowardly or not… you will learn. Or you will die.”

Mai twirled her writing stone, then spun toward the board. “Now, arteries!“

With a few quick strokes, she drew a sloppy stick figure and jabbed three dots onto it—neck, inner thigh, and wrist.

“There are lots of ways to kill someone,” Mai said brightly, “but arteries are your best friends. Veins take too long, guts are too messy, and bones? Don’t even get me started.”

She tapped the neck dot with the stone. “This one’s your carotid. Slice it clean, and they’ll pass out in half a flicker. Dead in one if they don’t get help. It’s fast, flashy, and nice and quiet if you don’t hesitate.”

She moved to the thigh. “Femoral artery. Big one. You’ll know you hit it when the blood arcs. It’s dramatic. They’ll bleed out in a flicker, maybe two. Not subtle, but fun at parties.”

She pointed to the wrist. “This works if you’re patient. Slowest of the three. Might take three flickers or more, but if you’re trying to make it look like an accident? This one’s your girl.”

She spun back to face them, eyes bright. “There’s plenty more, but these are the classics. Stab here—” she jabbed her own neck lightly, “and it’s over before the breath’s even finished.”

Mai spun her writing stone once more, then walked to the back of the classroom. She pressed her hand against a panel that didn’t look like a door—until it slid open with a low hiss.

Behind it was a narrow, dim hallway.

“Closest to the door,” she said sweetly. “Come with me.”

The student nearest the back stepped forward, stiff and hesitant, then followed her through. The door slid shut behind them.

Three flickers passed.

Only Mai returned.

She was still smiling. “Next.”

And so it went. One by one, students were called. One by one, they entered the hallway. And only Mai returned. Her cheer never faded. Her voice never changed.

“Next.”

Soon, only four students remained—Junya, Wannii, Bpen, and Praew. They’d huddled together without even realizing it, like prey flocking tight. None of them spoke above a whisper. No one laughed.

Wannii was called. She swallowed hard, nodded once, and left without looking back.

“Next.”

Bpen stood slower. His legs shook. He looked like he might cry. He didn’t say a word as he followed Mai into the dark.

Now it was just Praew and Junya.

They stood in silence until Junya slowly turned toward her, her voice barely audible.

“If I don’t come back,” she whispered, “please look for a man named Note on the Third Floor. The food shortages aren’t real.”

Then Mai returned.

“Next.”

Junya stood. Her eyes met Praew’s for a heartbeat. Then she stepped into the hallway, and the door sealed behind her.

Praew was alone.

Her heart thudded in her chest—louder than the silence.

Finally, the door opened once more.

Mai stood there, glowing as ever. “Your turn, Praew.”

The door sealed behind her with a quiet hiss.

Praew stepped into a dim, narrow room lit only by a single SolarStone embedded in the ceiling. The air felt damp and wrong, like it had been still for too long. At the center of the room sat a man bound to a heavy wooden chair. His legs were tied down, his wrists strapped together in front of him and anchored to his knees. He wasn’t struggling but his breathing was so frantic, shaking with wide, terrified eyes.

When he saw Praew, his voice cracked. “Please. I have a wife… a daughter. She’s only four. They need me. I haven’t done anything wrong!”

Mai stepped forward, her voice disturbingly calm. “Don’t worry,” she said. “I already paid them a visit. Don’t you want to join them?”

The man broke. His sobs tore through the room, wild and unrestrained. He begged louder, blubbering nonsense, his words lost beneath panic.

Praew froze. Her limbs felt heavier than stone. There was nothing threatening about the man—not in his voice, not in his eyes. He looked like a man who’d been broken long before today.

Mai stepped in behind her and let the door close. She walked over to a small stone table without a word, picked up a narrow, polished blade, then held it out to Praew. “One of three arteries,” she said brightly. “Neck, thigh, or wrist. Clean or messy, doesn’t matter to me. Just end him.”

Praew didn’t move. Her hands clenched at her sides as the man began to sob, whispering his daughter’s name.

“I’m not a killer,” she said, barely audible.

Mai tilted her head, her smile still fixed. “You will be if you want to live. This is an order from The Prime Archon himself.”

Praew’s stomach churned. The man was pleading louder now, saying things she didn’t want to hear. Saying he was innocent. That he didn’t deserve to die. Her heart pounded, each thud louder than the last. Still, she didn’t move.

Mai lowered the blade slightly, her voice dropping to something flat and cold. “If you don’t do it, you won’t need to worry about the antidote. I’ll kill you right here and now. Then he will join you. Treason will not be tolerated.”

Praew turned toward her. She wanted to speak, to argue, but the words stuck. Mai took a step forward. Her gaze no longer playful, no longer forgiving. Just a blade with legs and a smile.

“I said no,” Praew whispered, then louder, trembling, “I won’t do it.”

Mai kept coming.

If this is what it takes to live… then maybe I’m not meant to survive this place. 

Backed into a wall of panic, Praew shut her eyes. Her thoughts darted wildly—until something Haldric once told her surfaced, clear as a breath between heartbeats:

“Every good Hunter knows to be careful when you corner a beast. Most will cower, becoming easy prey, but some bare their teeth. Cornered beasts are the most unpredictable—‘cause they have nothing left to lose.”

No! She would not cower in her final moments.

Praew’s eyes snapped open and locked onto Mai’s. “Then get it over with,” she said with a sneer, raising her chin like a blade ready to clash with the one in Mai’s hand.

Praew braced herself—body tense, breath held, heart wild.

Mai Azadi hesitated.

Praew felt it—a flurry of emotion breaking through her perfect mask. Mai’s eyes narrowed. She swallowed. Her fingers fidgeted around the hilt of the blade. 

Maybe she wouldn’t do it. Maybe the blade will lower.

But then—for just a blink—Mai’s gaze darted to the man. And when her eyes returned to Praew, they were set.

“No!” Praew shouted, lunging forward. But it was too late. Praew felt the warmth, wet and thick.

Shlick.

Blood sprayed across her face.

Praew gasped. Hands shaking, she wiped at her face and snapped her eyes open. The man was slumped in the chair, his throat neatly cut open. Blood streamed down his chest and pooled around his feet. His eyes were still open, but there was nothing left behind them.

“What are you doing!?” Praew cried out.

Mai rushed forward and clamped a hand over Praew’s mouth. “I’m saving your life!” Mai hissed. “Now eat a stone and listen.”

Praew froze. Her breath came in short, ragged bursts.

Mai turned and pointed to a side door near the back wall. “The others are waiting. Walk through like you did the job. Pretend you slit his throat. Don’t say a word. I’ll figure out what to do with you later.”

Praew nodded slowly and walked out the door. The blood on her face felt hotter than before.

The hallway was longer than before. Each step landed like stone. Her fingers twitched, slick with dried blood that had already begun to flake. She rubbed them against her pants but couldn’t get rid of the warmth, or the stain.

She hadn’t killed anyone.

And yet she couldn’t stop shaking.

The waiting room was smaller than Praew expected—and far too quiet. No one spoke. No one moved.

The air was thick with the metallic stench of blood. It clung to everything—skin, uniforms, mouths. Every student was drenched in it. A few trembled. Most didn’t blink. Wide eyes fixed to nothing. As empty as the dead man tied to the chair.

Praew spotted Junya near the far wall, her jaw clenched so tight it looked painful. “We need to talk,” Praew said quietly, stepping toward her. “After class.”

Junya gave a small nod. That was all.

The door behind them scraped open.

Mai Azadi entered like nothing was wrong, her smile intact—but not reaching her eyes.

“Congratulations,” she said brightly, clapping once. “You all passed.”

Not a single student reacted.

Her smile wavered. When Mai spoke again, her voice lowered in an unusual moment of solemnity.

“Look, I know what you just went through was traumatic. You’ll find no comfort in hearing that it gets easier. Honestly, that might just make it worse.” She paused, glancing at a student near the corner who wouldn’t stop shaking. “But The Prime Archon makes this test mandatory in the first class so we can get it over with.”

A long silence stretched. Then her gaze swept across the room.

“There will be times on the surface,” she said slowly, “when you’ll have to kill another human. Trust me. The worst beasts I’ve ever fought… the ones that haunt me the most… never had claws or horns. They were human.”

No one responded. No one dared to protest.

But then the moment passed, and with a flick of her hand, her voice returned to its usual springy rhythm.

“Anyway! You’ll be surprised what you can do with a little pressure! I mean, I wasn’t even acclimated yet when I almost killed your other teacher. Mr. Stone-Face himself, Silverwing Godric!”

That broke the silence. A ripple moved through the group. A few heads turned. A murmur rose.

Junya raised a hand, the movement stiff. “Wait… Professor Stone is the Silverwing Godric, Mythical Beast Hunter? The one who wrote Crescent Moon Srevlis?”

“Yup! Is that book any good? I’ve never read it,” Mai said cheerfully, like she hadn’t just dropped a boulder into the room. “You guys didn’t know that? Huh. I guess that’s why he told me not to use his real name around you. Oh well!”

The room said nothing. A few students shifted. One coughed. No one looked at anyone else.

She shrugged, then clapped again, a little off the beat this time. “Alright! Door to your right leads to two wash stations—men’s and women’s. Can’t exactly have you walking the halls covered in blood, can we? You’ll find fresh uniforms in your sizes inside.”

She grinned. “And if anyone enters the wrong wash station,” she added sweetly, “I’ll kill them where they stand.”

It was probably a joke, but no one laughed.

Mai gave the group one last sweeping glance. “Oh yeah, and Praew?” Her gaze stopped on her. “I’d like you to stay behind and speak with me. Everyone else, class dismissed.”

“Oh, one last thing,” Mai added, raising a finger like she’d just remembered something mildly important, just before anyone had the chance to leave.

“Don’t get too distracted, by the way. Forgetting little details can get you killed.”

The students froze. Several hands darted to their ears.

“The poison,” someone whispered.

Mai beamed. “Relax. I stabbed you with a second needle while you were busy executing your targets. That one had the antidote.”

“So congrats! You passed, you didn’t die, and now you smell like a birthing clinic.” She shrugged. “Still counts as a win. Now go clean up.”

Mai waited until the last of the class had shuffled out toward the wash stations. Only then did she speak. “Praew! I have great news. I failed you!”

Praew blinked. The words barely registered before Mai was already rambling on.

“I marked you down for successfully completing the kill—but I wrote that you hesitated too much and recommended expulsion from this class. So now you won’t have to assassinate anyone else!”

“Anyone else?” Praew echoed. The words felt loose in her mouth.

“Yeah! Isn’t that awesome? That means you’ll have to pass both of the other classes, though. Otherwise, you’ll get reassigned to the mines or something much worse, andm ugh, you don’t want that, trust me.” She grinned wide. “Ha! I see that stunned look on your face. It’s okay. You don’t have to thank me.”

This bolting woman.

“Thank you?!—I—” Praew began to protest, but Mai cut her off with a cheerful, “You’re welcome!”

Then she leaned in slightly, voice dropping to a conspiratorial whisper. “So, how’d I do? Not bad for my first day? I took your advice and meditated before class—totally helped, but not gonna lie, I was still pretty nervous. And you! I was just about to kill you, but where did you get that strength? The way you looked at me and told me to get it over with… it gave me shivers!”

Praew didn’t respond. She couldn’t.

Mai’s voice softened. “You may have failed my class, but you’ve earned my respect, Praew. So I’ve decided, you can be my friend. I’m glad I met you. I can’t wait to get to know you better. I already feel like—”

“Can I go now?” Praew asked.

Mai paused. “ Uhh. Sure.” She sounded dejected.

Good, Praew thought as she turned and left. The dried blood made her skin tighten, but Mai’s words made her skin crawl.

What a farce. Who did that woman think she was—playing friend, pretending this was normal? Thank her? Not in this bolting lifetime. At least she’d failed this class. With luck, she’d never have to see Mai Azadi again.

Praew washed up and changed, fast and careless. No point fussing with the rest. 

When she stepped out, her hair still dripping, Junya was waiting for her in the empty hall.

Praew didn’t even slow her steps. “Talk.”

Junya followed, a little sheepish. Her long black ponytail swayed with every hurried step, the rhythm at odds with the tension in her shoulders. Pale skin, rose-kissed cheeks, and just a splash of freckles along her cheekbones—she looked far too soft for the weight she carried. And she carried something. Praew felt it.

“I was just scared, okay?” Junya mumbled. “I didn’t mean anything by it. Just nonsense, y’know? Thought I was gonna die…”

“Twice,” Praew said flatly. “You’ve mentioned it twice now. That wasn’t nonsense. The look in your eyes was too serious for it to be a joke. Especially as your final words.”

She kept walking. “Who is Note? What do you know about the food shortage?”

“I can’t,” Junya whispered. “I can’t get you involved. It’s too dangerous.”

Praew’s patience snapped. “You involved me the moment you brought it up in a classroom that’s probably being monitored by hidden EchoStones. Or did you really think they wouldn’t supervise an over-talkative assassin teacher, that couldn’t even keep Godric’s name a secret, just in case she said something she shouldn’t?”

The way Mai acted during the test… the way she covered Praew’s mouth and whispered about it after. She acted like they were being monitored.

Behind her, Junya stopped. Praew glanced back—and froze.

Her friend stood shaking, eyes wide, lips trembling. Zephyrs take me, was she… crying? Just what did I get dragged into?

“Fine, but not here,” Junya said, dabbing her eyes with the edge of her shirt. “If you’re right about the room being compromised, there’s no telling if the halls are as well. I don’t live far—let’s go to my place.”

Praew gave a small nod and let her lead. The concern felt a little dramatic, honestly. The classroom? Sure—plenty of places to hide Sifaraliths. But the hallway? The walls were smooth, bright, and clean. No cracks, no clutter, no shadows. Nothing that could mask an embedded EchoStone. But still… if this was what it took for Junya to talk, then fine. Let her feel safe. Safe people talked.

They walked in silence. Not awkward, just… heavy.

In less than four flickers, they arrived.

Before they even stepped inside, one thing stopped Praew cold.

The size.

Praew gasped. “You live alone already? How?!”

“Yeah,” Junya muttered, shrinking a little. “Both of my parents died in the Sifarilith mines earlier this year. I don’t have any other relatives, but since I was about to graduate, they didn’t put me in the orphanage. They gave me a single unit—the same they give all unmarried independents.”

Praew felt like the wind had been knocked out of her. She gasped again, hand flying to her mouth. “I’m so sorry. I couldn’t imagine going through that alone.”

Junya looked away, jaw tight. “It’s fine.”

But Praew could tell it wasn’t. Not even close.

Junya wouldn’t even look at her. She just opened the door and welcomed Praew into her home.

Well, Junya’s place barely qualified as a home—just a single-room unit buried in the Third Floor’s outer ring. A short step divided where she slept from where she lounged, like that somehow made it two rooms. The kitchen sat untouched. Praew never understood why every house had one when no one was allowed to eat at home until they’d had their first child. Another rule no one questioned.

“Sorry,” Junya said, fidgeting with the hem of her pants. Her fingers tugged at the fabric like it might unravel the tension in her chest. “Besides Wannii, you’re the only other person I’ve ever brought home.”

She glanced around the room, avoiding Praew’s eyes. “I—I know it’s not much. Being so empty and all. If it wasn’t for the killing, I would be so excited to actually finish this training so I can finally get supplementary slips and decorate the place. If I survive until then, this place will look a lot—”

Praew didn’t let her finish. She stepped forward and wrapped her arms around her.

Junya froze.

The silence stretched for half a flicker. Then her shoulders slumped, and Praew felt her start to shake. When she looked up, the mask was gone. Tears rolled freely down Junya’s pale cheeks.

“They didn’t care at all,” she choked out. “Someone from The Prime Archon’s office just showed up one day with two soldiers. Said they’re sorry—said my parents were dead—told me to gather my things. I had one flash to move out. One flash!”

Her voice cracked.

    “I only had time to make two trips. A handful of clothes and this protection bracelet my parents gave me.” She raised her arm to reveal a crude bracelet of braided cord with a stone in the center, carved to look like a BoltStone. “That’s all I got to keep.” Her body trembled harder, her voice breaking into hiccups. “How can they be so heartless?” 

The hiccups became sobs. Deep, guttural ones. The kind that rattled out of the ribs and left the throat raw.

Praew said nothing. Just held her tighter.

She didn’t know how long they stood there—three flickers, maybe more—but eventually the cries softened. Junya was still sniffling when she pulled back slightly, wiping at her eyes with the sleeve of her shirt.

“How do you do that?” Junya asked, voice still hoarse. “First with Bpen, now me. You always seem to know just what to say… or do.”

Praew gave a dry laugh. “I wish that were true. I catch myself blurting out ridiculous things at the worst times. I’m not as bad as my friend Haldric, but—stupid me, remember? I called a Mythical Beast Hunter ‘stone-faced.’”

Junya let out a snort. Then a laugh. Then a full-bodied, red-eyed, messy laugh that sounded far too rare.

They laughed together, the tension bleeding out of the room one breath at a time.

“But seriously,” Junya said again, more quietly now, “how do you do it?”

Praew shrugged. “I don’t know. I just speak my mind and act on instinct. Then worry about the consequences later. I may come off super nice or like the meanest person you’ve ever met, but at least I’ll never be known as fake.”

Praew glanced at Junya. “As for the hug… if I were in your shoes, I’d need one. So I gave you one. I’d rather you think I’m weird for hugging you than overthink it and miss the chance to be there.”

“Wow,” Junya whispered. “I hope I can be like that someday.”

“You can,” Praew said, her tone steady now. “Just stop worrying what other people think.”

Praew let that settle. Then, gently, “So… about this food shortage.”





Chapter 7 -  Stormhaven Mysteries





Junya sat with her knees pulled up, thumb tracing invisible lines across the seam of her pants. Praew joined her sitting on the stone furniture in the living room. Junya hadn’t spoken in a while, not since the food shortage came up again. Praew waited, watching the tension gather in her shoulders like a storm.

Then, finally, a breath.

“I was looking into my parents’ deaths. Something about it just felt… off.”

“Off how?” Praew asked, leaning in.

“Well, for starters,” Junya said, fingers fidgeting faster now, “they just didn’t come home. No warning, no message. I skipped school the next day and searched all three Floors of the city.”

Praew blinked. “Wait—you actually skipped general education?”

Junya gave a small, guilty smile. “Yeah. I know. Pretty bold, right?”

“How did you not get caught?”

“Oh, that part was easy,” Junya said with a weak laugh. “I stole an administrative uniform. No one questions someone who looks like they belong.”

Praew raised an eyebrow but didn’t interrupt.

“I spent two days searching,” Junya continued. “Eventually I found the mine entrance on the Third Floor, but they wouldn’t let me in. Said I needed clearance papers. Whatever, I didn’t have any.”

Junya sighed.

“Luckily, I knew a few of my parents’ friends who worked down there. I went to four different homes. None of them had heard about a cave-in. Nothing. When they asked their supervisors, they were told my parents were just home sick.”

Praew’s expression darkened. “Sick?”

Junya nodded. “For ten days, that was the story. Then… administration showed up. But even after that, my parents’ friends still hadn’t heard anything. That’s when Joel—one of their closest friends—told me something weird.”

She shifted forward, voice low now. “He worked the same shaft. He said there was no cave-in. No rescue efforts. No bodies. Just a sudden order to block off the shaft completely. The last thing he remembered was hearing my dad complain about getting new orders to dig straight down. Then a foreman burst into the area, cursing under his breath, and ran toward that area.”

Praew sat still, jaw clenched.

“After that? Everything went quiet,” Junya said. “The shaft was sealed. The rest of the crew was told my parents were sick and sent home. But no one ever saw them again.”

She looked down at her hands. “Joel told me, ‘Why would they say your parents are home sick if they’re trapped in a mine?’ He said it didn’t add up.”

Praew nodded slowly. “Because it doesn’t.”

“Joel said he might know someone who could help,” Junya added. “His wife didn’t want him to tell me anything. She was scared. They argued—right in front of me. But Joel told her I had a right to know the truth and ‘that man’ was likely the only one who could give me that.”

“Note,” Praew said flatly.

“Note,” Junya repeated, nodding slowly. “He didn’t know anything about my parents—not really. Said he’d heard nothing specific about the mines. I felt like the whole trip was a waste. Just another dead end.”

She paused, arms crossed now, eyes low.

“I was about to leave when he said something… strange. Out of nowhere. Told me I was right to question everything. Said even our ration slips are a lie. And that digging down on the Third Floor is just as dangerous as doing it on the First or Second. Whatever that means.”

“Did you ask for a clearer answer?” Praew asked, her annoyance with this Note person starting to show.

“Of course I did,” Junya muttered. “But he just laughed and said he won’t tell me more until I figure out what any of that actually means first.”

She cleared her throat.

Praew could tell Junya was trying not to crumble again, trying to hold back her tears and frustration, even though her voice kept cracking.

She exhaled shakily. “So yeah. That’s where I’m at. A reason to believe the food shortage might be fake, one sealed shaft, zero answers… and a lot of questions.”

“That doesn’t tell me a lot,” Praew said, standing up and stretching. “I guess we will just have to get more answers together.”

Junya began to tear up again. “Thank you,” she choked out. “When I told Wannii, she… well, she keeps acting like I’m crazy and keeps telling me to forget about Note, everything I heard. She told me to let my parents rest. She’s been my friend since early childhood. My parents even secretly fed her before. How can she be so heartless?”

“I don’t know,” Praew said thoughtfully. “It may seem that way, but don’t you think she’s saying all that because she knows it’s probably true? Secrets like this are dangerous. She’s probably just trying to keep you safe.”

“Yeah,” Junya said, a little sheepish now. “You’re probably right.”

Praew placed a hand on Junya’s shoulder. “Wannii’s a good friend. Me, not so much. I’m too reckless to give you common sense. I know someone close who works in government administration. I think it’s time I visit an old friend to see what we can dig up for you.”

Junya nodded, forcing a brave smile as Praew walked out the door and headed home. 

Praew had the next day off. That meant she could drop by the ration station near The Prime Archon’s office—the one Haldric would go to for breakfast. And honestly, she probably should. It had been seven days since they’d failed the Hunter test. Time to see how he was holding up.

The following day, Praew was already seated, waiting for Haldric at the ration station. It was nearly identical to the one she normally visited, just larger. Rows of identical stone tables filled the space, all lined with the same slumped shoulders and lowered gazes. The SolarStones overhead gave off a weak, even light—just enough to see your food, not enough to feel awake.

The air hung heavy with the stench of overcooked rice and chewy intestines. Nutrient-packed, sure, but lifeless. The kind of meal you only enjoyed if you didn’t have to eat it nearly every day. A thick, flavorless mush that made teeth optional—except for the strips of beast intestine, which made them work overtime. It was the most confusing meal Praew had ever eaten. Was it a drink, a soup, or a punishment?
