She spotted Haldric before he noticed her. He looked better than she expected. Getting betrayed by his best friend and being denied the chance to ever see the surface could have broken him.

But the smile he gave her the moment their eyes met…

Some smiles just couldn’t be faked.

Haldric got his food and made his way over, the wooden tray balanced neatly in one hand. He dropped into the seat across from her at the stone table with practiced ease.

Praew’s eyes drifted to his tray. 

It wasn’t just smaller than hers. It was barely enough to count as a full meal.

Her brows drew together. She was still in classes, technically. Was she still getting double portions compared to everyone else? Administration was notoriously known for their generous rations. It was probably part of why they were so hated by the rest of the city.

But if this was what Haldric got… she couldn’t imagine what everyone else was living on.

Her appetite flickered.

“What seems to be the problem, constable?” Haldric asked, his voice light with amusement. “Am I under arrest?”

Praew chuckled softly. “Not this time,” she said. “I just wanted to check on you.”

“Right,” Haldric replied. “Well, I’m not giving you my ration slips, if that’s what you’re after, so don’t bother.”

Praew didn’t join Haldric’s laughter this time. She pushed at her rice with the edge of her utensils, sifting through it as if it might rearrange itself into something better. Her fingers moved absently, the food forgotten.

Then she finally spoke.

“Haldric, have you noticed anything… odd at your job?”

He glanced at her, pausing mid-bite.

“Odd?” he repeated. “I work in administration for The bolting Prime Archon. Every document I go through seems odd to me. You’re gonna have to be more specific.”

He looked around the room, probably to make sure no one had heard him use The Prime’s name in vain.

Praew sighed, setting down her utensils and rubbing her temple.

“I mean… look at us. Look at everyone.”

She gestured at his tray, at the thinning portions he’d somehow grown used to.

Haldric didn’t need to look. He wasn’t stupid. It was already impossible to ignore. The quiet fatigue hanging over the cafeteria wasn’t just exhaustion—it was hunger.

Praew continued.

“Something doesn’t add up about the food shortage. If our Hunters are having this much trouble getting food, why not accept more Hunters?”

“I don’t know what to tell you,” Haldric said. His eyes darted around the room again before he leaned in slightly.

“I’ve been thinking the same thing. But I haven’t heard anything out of the ordinary.”

“I don’t care if I eat less. I can handle it. But my parents? They’re older. They need more. And they’re getting weaker.”

She didn’t look at Haldric as she spoke. She didn’t have to. He had to have seen it too. His Grayparents, a generation older than her parents, were likely faring even worse. Like the thinner faces and heavier movements she saw in her own parents.

She stared down at her tray, pushing a lump of rice to the side with the edge of her utensils.

“They used to fuss over me when I got home. Always asking if I ate enough, if I had clean clothes, if I was sleeping okay.”

She set the utensils down gently.

“But lately… they just smile. Say they’re proud of me. And then they sit back down like even that took too much energy.”

She trailed off. The words stuck somewhere she couldn’t reach.

They were too tired to even fuss. She felt her eyes start to water.

She came here for Junya. But perhaps this had always been on her mind.

There was a strange power in voicing her worries. It was like dislodging a single stone, only to feel the weight of the whole mountain shift behind it.

“Okay, I get it,” Haldric said. “I’ll do some digging and see what I can find. Just… don’t do anything stupid.”

“What? Me?” Praew said with feigned innocence, pushing down her emotions and forcing a smile.

“I thought I was supposed to be the impulsive and emotional one,” Haldric said, eyeing her. “There’s something you’re not telling me, and that worries me.”

“I—” Praew looked away, the word catching in her throat.

Haldric didn’t let up.

“I may not be as smart as you, but I can tell when something’s off. What is it? What’s going on?”

Praew took a deep breath, steadying herself before meeting his eyes.

“I was going to tell you anyway,” she said. “I have a friend who needs you to look into a suspicious cave-in and the mysterious disappearance of two individuals.”

Haldric choked on his drink, tray already empty. When had he finished it?

“Praew, could you please stop being so nosy? Why are you always getting involved in everyone else’s problems?” He threw his hands slightly. “This sounds even more dangerous than the food shortage!”

“Come on,” she said, leaning in. “Don’t tell me you already forgot. A Hunter never ignores—”

“We’re not Hunters, Praew!” Haldric snapped, voice rising.

Yeah… it still bothered him. That was a stupid thing to say. She made a mental note.

“You’re right. I’m sorry,” Praew said quietly. She took a bite of rice, letting the silence settle before swallowing and continuing.

“It’s too bad, though. She needed someone who could hunt down what happened to her parents. But you’re right, Haldric. It would be way too dangerous. Someone would have to risk their life becoming her hero.”

She took another bite of rice, chewed slowly, then swallowed.

“Such a shame…” She tilted her head, then continued.

“She’s so pretty. Seeing her cry nearly broke me.”

“I know what you’re doing, Praew,” Haldric said flatly.

“Is it working?” she asked, adding a wink.

“Stone me. You’re suggesting I just… snoop around, digging through sensitive or classified incident reports?”

Praew smirked. “I prefer to call it an investigation.”

Haldric shook his head. “This is a terrible idea.”

“But you’re considering it,” Praew said, that look on his face making it obvious he was.

She leaned forward, her expression serious. Haldric tapped his fingers against the edge of his tray. He was mulling it over. It wasn’t just curiosity anymore. Like her, he would need to learn the truth by any means necessary.

“Alright,” he said finally. “You handle your end. Train, ask questions, see if you hear anything. I’ll start looking into storage and incident reports.”

Praew grinned. “And every six days, we compare notes.”

Haldric rolled his eyes. “You can just say a week, Praew.”

She gasped, putting on her most innocent voice. “I just didn’t want you to get confused!”

Her smirk widened, mischievous.

Haldric frowned. “What was that supposed to mean?”

Praew’s smile only grew. “I mean, after all… I remember a certain someone mixing up the seven planets with the six-day week once.” She tapped her chin dramatically. “Remind me, who was that again?”

Haldric stiffened. “Oh stone me, I was a child!"

“Of course you were!” Praew beamed, her voice dripping with false sympathy. “And an actual grown adult would have to be incredibly stupid to assume a week was seven days.”

Haldric scowled, but Praew was already laughing.

Her laughter cut through the dull murmur of the cafeteria.

Haldric didn’t answer. Just watched her for a moment, shaking his head as her laughter spilled out, unburdened by the world around them.

She caught the look in his eyes before he turned away. Like he was trying not to smile. Like he almost forgot to guard whatever it was he was holding back.

“Just don’t do anything too reckless,” he said, leveling his gaze.

She smirked, the last trace of laughter still warming her cheeks. “No promises.”

  Haldric rolled his eyes. “We meet in three weeks. I need time to actually look into things. What are their names, and when did they disappear?”



***



After going over the details of Junya’s case, Praew and Haldric said their goodbyes. She knew she’d made the right choice going to him. When he put his mind to something, no one was more capable.

Praew found herself in a rare good mood as she walked home. She was happy. Happy to see her best friend again, happy to have made progress helping Junya, and happy to be spending the rest of the day with her parents, enjoying some much-needed, stress-free relaxation.

She walked inside—and cursed in front of her parents.

Not a light one, like “Zephyrs take me.” No. A full-throated, blasphemous belt of a slur involving a mother’s private part falling on the floor.

Her parents gasped. She ignored them.

Because sitting in her living room, casually chatting with them, was Mai Azadi. Smiling. Dagger at her hip. 

The worst person Praew could imagine had somehow found where she lived.

A psychopathic assassin who was inches away from her parents.

“What are you doing here?” Praew yelled, eyes widening as she inched forward, trying to position herself between Mai and her parents without drawing attention to it.

“Oh, nothing. I just had time to kill,” Mai said casually.

Was that a threat or a joke? Praew couldn’t tell. She glanced around for anything she could use as a weapon, but there was nothing. Everything not lodged in stone was either too bulky or too flimsy to stop a blade.

“Oh yeah?” Praew said, now fully between them. A plan began to form. “Well, that’s perfect. There are some things I’ve been just dying to know. How about we go for a walk?”

“Well, actually—” Mai began.

“Mae, Papa, we’ll be back,” Praew called over her shoulder, not letting Mai finish.

Praew ‘clumsily’ bumped Mai’s side. She didn’t notice, right? Praew used her other hand to grab Mai’s before she had a chance to notice anything missing. 

She ran as hard as she could, Mai in tow, until they reached an abandoned home carved entirely from stone. It hadn’t been repurposed yet. Thin cracks ran through the walls and ceiling, as if the structure itself had grown tired of holding on. The floor was littered with gravel-like dust, loose debris shifting beneath her boots.

Praew slammed the door shut behind them, panting. The sudden impact stirred the air, sending tiny motes of dust spiraling into the light.

She looked up, hand on her hips as she tried to catch her breath. A single SolarStone glowed overhead, fractured with a spiral-shaped crack. The beam it cast broke apart, splitting across the ruined walls. It looked ready to crumble—just like her life right now. Spiraling, splintering, falling apart.

She glanced over at Mai. That bolting monster wasn’t even a little out of breath. Mai just smiled beneath the cracked SolarStone overhead. The shadow hiding her eyes painted her more menacingly than Praew could have ever imagined.

“Wow, that was fun,” Mai said brightly. “Nothing like a good midday jog! Thank you for carrying my dagger for me, by the way. Made the run easier. Very thoughtful of you. Now, how do you plan to return it? My hand? My throat? Ooh, my wrist? I love guessing games. Do I get a prize if I—”

“Zephyrs take me!” Praew snapped, brandishing the dagger. “Shut your bolting mouth unless I ask you a question!”

Mai tilted her head. “Oh, interrogation. Hmm. No.” She stepped forward. “You’re holding the dagger wrong. And your stance is awful.”

Then, without hesitation, she adjusted Praew’s arms like a teacher correcting a child.

Was this girl serious right now?

“How did you find my home?” Praew demanded.

“Oh, I just followed you yesterday,” Mai said, like it was the most normal thing in the world. “You almost fooled me, too. For a moment, I thought Junya’s place was yours. I’m glad I waited until one of you left to be sure. Rookie mistake, am I right?”

Praew said nothing at first. She just stared, jaw slack. That must be what this was about. Mai must’ve overheard them. So she is here to kill me.

Her voice came out flat. “Why? If you planned to kill me from the start, why stalk me? Why come to my house? You could’ve done it any time. Why bring my parents into it?”

“Kill you?” Mai laughed. “Silly. I’m not here to kill you. I’m here to keep you!”

Praew must have made a face because even Mai looked flustered.

“I mean, not like that! I meant your friendship. I’m here to keep your friendship! I feel like we got off on the wrong foot yesterday and I—”

Praew laughed bitterly, lowering the knife. Who was she kidding? If Mai wanted her dead, Praew couldn’t stop her.

“My friendship?” Praew snapped. “You can’t be serious. We were never friends, Mai. And I would never be friends with a psychopathic murderer like you. I’d rather die than kill someone. You get off on blood. What makes you think we could ever be friends?”

Mai opened her mouth to speak, but Praew wasn’t finished.

“No! Don’t even bother answering that. You’re sick. And I want you nowhere near my parents.”

She tossed the dagger at Mai’s feet.

“Just say away from me.”

The assassin dropped her head. For once, the smile vanished.

Praew turned to leave.

“What do you know?” Mai’s voice cracked.

Praew froze. She turned back. Tears ran down Mai’s face.

Was I too harsh? But… my parents. I have to keep them safe. And … Mai was far from stable.

Mai raised her voice, nearly shouting. “What do you know?! I am Stormhaven’s best assassin. The only person to pass all the Shadow Intelligence tests on the first day. But that’s all I want to be seen as, a ‘person’.”

Her voice caught. She swallowed hard, fists clenched.

 “I’m only the best because I was raised to be the best. While you sat in classrooms, I trained to kill. You think I’m good at it because I like it?”

She stepped forward.

“I stay positive because if I don’t… if I don’t—” She shook her head, hard. “I would have killed myself long ago. Do you know what they promise us if we keep a flawless kill record for five years? We’re allowed to make a friend. That’s it. After five years of being… being perfect. Of killing whoever they pointed to. And only if we’ve proven we’re so deadly that no one can distract us, we’re allowed one friend. I’m the first to ever earn that.”

Her voice broke.

“Yeah, I’m awful at it. I don’t know what I’m doing. Yeah, maybe I am sick. But don’t pretend you know me. If we’re being honest, let’s really be honest.”

She pointed at Praew.

“This is really about that man you tried to save. The one I killed.”

“Yeah, well maybe it is!” Praew snapped back “Look, I’m sorry to hear about your messed-up life, but that doesn’t change the fact that you killed an innocent man. Right in front of me! I still feel his blood on me. I still saw his eyes when I slept last night.  How could you do that?”

“You don’t know everything, Praew!” Mai shouted, arms flailing.

Then her voice dropped low. Too low. Drained.

“That man? His wife and daughter were reported sick. No one had seen them in weeks. Meanwhile, he was flaunting his rations. Sharing meat with coworkers. Smiling while they ate.”

Praew’s stomach twisted.

“Eventually people got suspicious. His neighbors reported a smell. When they broke in… they found his wife and four-year-old daughter gutted. Partially cooked. He’d been eating them. Feeding them to others. Without telling them.”

Praew nearly collapsed. Her hand reached out, steadying herself against the wall. Her legs wouldn’t stop shaking.

“That’s the kind of man you mourn.” Mai shook her head. “That man had the audacity to beg for his life, saying his wife and daughter needed him. But I went to his house myself. I saw their remains… You condemn me but protect people like that?”

Mai bent down, picked up the dagger, and sheathed it.

She walked past Praew toward the door.

Her voice, barely more than a whisper, sliced through the air behind her.

“You don’t have to like what I do. But before you judge someone… don’t ever assume you know the whole story.”

Without another word, she was gone. Praew stared at the fissures in the wall as Mai’s words echoed in her head. 

“You condemn me but protect people like that?” 

Why had she been so hard on Mai?

Praew wasn’t stupid. She knew executioners existed. Knew what their job meant. And yet, she had acted like Mai was the only monster in the room. Everyone in her class had killed someone that day. Junya. The others. So why had she only cared when it was Mai? Was it really that simple? Just because she saw it?

She leaned her head back, jaw tight. That man said he was innocent, and she believed him without question. All because she didn’t want to hurt him.

And she still hadn’t thanked Mai. Not for saving her life. Not for failing her from the class so she wouldn’t be forced to kill anyone else. Instead, she called her a psychopath.

Zephyrs take me… way to show appreciation, Praew.

She did not move. Just sat in that cold, dim room, letting the guilt twist in her chest until the fifteenth flash, the hour most people got off work.

When she finally returned home, she was too exhausted to explain anything to her parents. After successfully reassuring them she was fine, she went straight to bed. Praew had a feeling she wouldn’t see that man in her dreams again.







Chapter 8 - Letting Stones Slip



The fifteenth flash signaled a brief escape from the monotony of endless paperwork.

Haldric was mentally drained. Whether it was from Praew's request this morning or his endless office duties, he couldn’t tell. 

Another day of work done. Only a million more to go, he thought, as he finally reached home.

Haldric rubbed his hand against the rough stone of the doorframe before stepping inside. He was told it was a ritual his father used to do for good luck.

The house, carved directly from the cavern wall, had a way of swallowing sound, softening it into something warmer than the harsh streets outside. The dim glow from a single embedded SolarStone flickered in the kitchen. The fading of light made their small home feel almost alive, shifting like breathing rock.

Both his parents had been Hunters, but they were killed fifteen years ago fighting the mythical beast Fendire. Since the age of five, his Grayparents on his mother’s side had taken him in. Their eldest child had died in a mining accident, and Graypa had volunteered for the Sterilization Program after Haldric’s mother was born.

After losing both children, his Grayparents had become a little… well, overprotective. They didn’t even try to hide their excitement when he failed the Hunter’s test. They’d always feared he would suffer the same fate as their daughter.

Ironically, that was one of the only times they ever talked about his mother.

Then came their voices.

“Haldric! Oh, Haldric, you’re home!” Graymother Wintrid’s voice, always soft yet bright, carried from the kitchen. He barely had time to step forward before she shuffled into view, thin hands wiping on a threadbare cloth, face lighting up like he was a hero returning from war.

Graypa Cenrick wasn’t far behind, moving slower but still wearing that same eager smile. His back was bent from years of work, his hands rough from a life that had never once been easy, but his eyes still held warmth.

“Are you alright, lad? No injuries, sprains, or broken bones?”

“Graypa, I sit at a desk sorting paper. The greatest risk at my job is a paper cut. I’m fine.”

Wintrid ignored him, already fussing, hands fluttering near his shoulders and arms, checking him over like he was made of fragile glass.

“You must be starving! Come, sit. We got you something special today.” She turned away, too fast, too eager. It was only when he noticed her thin wrist trembling slightly as she reached for a small cloth bundle that a flicker of unease settled in his gut.

The unease vanished the moment the scent hit him like a slap. She set the bundle on the table, unfolded the fabric, and revealed a small pile of rice with thin cuts of tenderized meat. Glorious, juicy, mouthwatering meat.

Not the criminally thin slices of dried beast intestine he had grown used to. Not the rice they mashed with water to stretch their portions. Real meat. With real rice.

Haldric hesitated. Too long. “They’re selling whole meals for supplementary slips now?”

He glanced at his Grayma just in time to see her shift uncomfortably.

“Are you two crazy?!” he asked, eyes still locked on the meat. “How much did this cost?”

Wintrid’s smile faltered.

“Wait.” His voice came out sharper than intended. “Didn’t they just cut back on our supplementary slips? How did you even—”

His voice trailed off as he watched Wintrid’s fingers twitch against the cloth. Cenrick looked away.

The answer settled before either of them could speak. Stone me, he thought. This food wasn’t from their supplementary slips like they always claimed. 

They hadn’t finished all their rations.

Haldric’s stomach twisted, guilt settling in his chest like a slow-building storm. They were already too thin. How had he never seen it until now? The way Wintrid’s cheeks looked hollower than before. The tremor in Cenrick’s fingers. And still, they had starved themselves—for him? 

How long? How many meals had they skipped, just in case he ever came home hungry?

“Don’t do this.” His voice was quieter now, but still firm. “I get food at work. I don’t need this.”

Wintrid’s hands clutched at her apron. Cenrick sighed, rubbing a hand across his wrinkled forehead. “We just wanted to make sure, lad. You’re workin’ hard, and—”

“You need your strength!” Haldric snapped, fists clenching at his sides. Frustration bled into something deeper. He swallowed, forcing his voice down. “You’re all I have. You need your strength so we can all stay together as long as possible. I’ve already lost my parents. I don’t want to lose you too.”

Wintrid’s fingers trembled slightly against the cloth before she let out a quiet, soft laugh. Cenrick exhaled through his nose, a tired but warm sound.

Haldric pulled them both into a quick, firm hug.

Wintrid’s arms wrapped around him, thin but still warm, still gentle. Cenrick chuckled, clapping his back once before stepping away. “Alright, alright, before you start makin’ your old Graypa cry, off to bed with you.”

Haldric smirked, rolling his eyes as he turned for the stairs.

The house felt quiet again.

He sat on his small bed, staring at the ceiling and the faint cracks in the stone where moisture had settled. He thought of Praew. Of what she had said about the food shortage. Of the quiet acceptance in his Grayparents’ voices.

He thought of how Grayma Wintrid had smiled despite everything.

And for the first time since starting his job, he felt uneasy. 

It took too long to fall asleep. It felt like his eyes had only just closed when the giant SolarStone on the First Floor outer ring triggered, flooding his room with the harsher light of day. 



***



The next two days flew by in a blur of whispered questions and dead ends. Praew and Junya spent them digging for answers wherever they could. At one point, Praew met with Joel, Junya’s parents’ friend, to hear his account firsthand, hoping something—anything—useful would slip through. But Joel was tight-lipped, his nerves barely concealed, and most of what he said left her with more questions than answers. 

When she pressed him on what paperwork was required to access the mines, he shifted uncomfortably and muttered something under his breath.

An investigation letter. Sealed with The Prime Archon’s stamp.

Not even Haldric could help them with that. So now what?

“Focus, Praew,” Instructor Stone barked from the front of the room. “Unless you want the whole class squatting for the rest of the session instead.”

She straightened fast, pulse thudding. Right, right. Let’s focus, she thought. Sitting was much more comfortable.

Godric didn’t miss a beat.

“All common and Noble CoreStones deteriorate with use,” he said, pacing between rows. “As you use it, a small crack appears. The more it’s used, the more that crack grows, always in a perfect spiral. Once the crack reaches the center, the stone crumbles into a grainy sand. We call this crumbling. The same thing happens to Beast Stones.”

He scanned the class, watching for flickers of recognition.

“I’m sure most of you have seen a cracked SolarStone, maybe even watched one crumble. But none of you have seen what happens next up on the surface. Something we call reverse crumbling.”

The class erupted into chatter, voices overlapping

Reverse? Praew blinked. How could that even be possible?

She raised her hand, but her words came out before he called on her.

“Instructor… uhhh, Stone,” she said, still embarrassed to call him that. “If we can uncrumble stones, why doesn’t the city ever have enough Sifaraliths? Why do Hunters compete with other cities? Why do they even worry about inventory? Isn’t most of the surface covered in the sand of crumbled Sifaraliths? Something doesn’t add up.”

“You’re right,” Godric said. “That’s because we can’t uncrumble them. Not normally.”

He stepped to the front of the class, voice dropping into something heavier.

“There are only two things that can reverse the process. One is Terravoris—one of the Ten Beasts of Legend. It eats metals, gems, stones, and Sifaralith sand. When it eats enough, it somehow reforms stones inside its body.”

A few students exchanged uneasy glances.

“You can find stones in its droppings. The waste protects them from being triggered. But once the feces dries and blows away, the stones remain behind.”

“That’s revolting,” Junya muttered.

“Yes,” Godric said flatly. “That’s what it means to be a Stone Hunter. Hunters who follow Terravoris and fight each other over its droppings.”

“So they’re just feces Hunters?” Wannii asked.

Someone made a noise that might’ve been a laugh, but it cut off fast. No one wanted to be the loudest person in the room.

“Make no mistake,” Godric said. “It can be just as dangerous as being a Beast Hunter. If you ever injure a Beast of Legend, they can mark you. A tattoo appears. Each one is unique to the beast that marked you.”

He paused, his tone shifting.

“Since your other teacher took it upon herself to reveal my real name, there’s no point in hiding any longer.”

Instructor Stone—no, Silverwing Godric, Mythical Beast Hunter, rolled up his right sleeve. A glyph tattoo was etched into his skin. The ink shimmered with an impossible, ruby-like color that could only be found on the surface of a SurgeStone, as if one had been melted down and turned to ink.

 Two distinct rows of inked marks covered his right forearm, curving perfectly in tandem, mirroring the powerful bite of some fierce animal. Each tooth imprint appeared precise and sharp, arranged like a crescent. The design gave a feeling of strength and permanence, as if the jaws had clamped down on skin and never quite let go.

His left arm was not to be outdone. Rolling up his other sleeve revealed a pair of sapphire-blue horns curving gracefully upward. They mirrored the formidable shape of a Tarnox’s horns, yet held an elegance no Tarnox from any picture book Praew had read could ever match. The rich, gemstone hue shimmered subtly beneath the skin, giving the inked design an almost regal quality.

The classroom was silent in worship and shock. All eyes were fixed on his arms.

Two!? He bolting faced two Beasts of Legend and survived!? Praew couldn’t even breathe in his presence—especially when he began unbuttoning his shirt, turned around, and lowered it to his waist.

A massive silver and yellow glyph of a wing stretched across his back, from just above his left hip to his right shoulder. The detail was stunning. The wing shimmered as if the surface of the ink caught light with every breath.

Praew recognized it instantly.

“Isn’t that the wing of Boltrax?” she asked.

Godric buttoned his shirt without answering.

No wonder they call him Silverwing Godric.

“We call these territory marks,” Godric said. “Because they let other Beasts of Legend know… this is their prey to be hunted.”

He let the weight of that settle before continuing.

“These glyphs act as a form of tracking. They can find you anywhere, anytime. The tattoo doesn’t disappear. Not even if the beast dies.”

The room was stunned silent as Godric continued.

“There are only two ways to remove one. 

“First, you die. It’ll disappear from your body the moment you do. Second… is highly unlikely, so don’t count on it.”

He crossed his arms.

“It’s theorized that Beasts of Legend can only mark so many Hunters at once. There are rare reports of a mark vanishing, but only if someone else becomes a higher priority. Someone who angers them more than you did. But don’t get hopeful. Hunters die all the time. The beast just replaces them without even trying.”

No one spoke, not because they had no questions, but because they were terrified of the answers.

“Your only guaranteed way to hide is to be underground. A cave. A tunnel. Deep enough to lose them.”

He let that sit.

“Terravoris is the exception. Make too much noise, get in his way, or catch him on a bad day, and a dark brown mark appears on the back of your hand. Congratulations. You’re a Mythical Stone Hunter. Now try not to die.”

No one moved or  interrupted.

“Terravoris holds a grudge worse than any other beast. He can find you anywhere. Even underground. Other beasts might ignore you if it’s inconvenient. Terravoris and the ones he commands will track you to the other side of the planet just for being too loud.”

His voice dropped further.

“That’s why no one marked by Terravoris is ever allowed back into the city. They could bring disaster to us all and…”

His eyes swept the class.

“It’s been too long since someone interrupted me. Are you all paying attention?”

“Sir,” Bpen said slowly. “You just hit us with a boulder. I think I can speak for everyone when I say we need time to process this. You just confirmed you’re one of only two mythical Beast Hunters in the city, you faced three Beasts of Legend and lived, plus you met Srevlis. And don’t forget the Tarnox that got into the city… sir.”

“Oh, would you all quit that bolting look in your eyes?” Godric snapped. “I can already see it. You’re all itching to get your first mark. Well, I’m telling you now, that’s bolting stupid. There’s no glory in death. I did what needed to be done. That’s all I’m trying to teach you. Do what needs to be done.”

The room stayed quiet.

“Now, if you’re all done processing, we still need to go over the other way reverse crumbling can happen. And sometimes, it’s even more dangerous than Terravoris.”

He resumed pacing.

“As you know, nature can be Embedded with Nature Stones. We believe a sentient tornado is Embedded with a powerful TerraStone, much like the beast Terravoris. We call it Haiyana. It’s a giant force of nature.”

He glanced at the walls like he could feel it even now.

“It seems to choose what it pulls in. Two Hunters could be hit—only one might die, while the other walks away untouched. If it chooses you, your death is certain. It sucks in Sifaralith sand and spits out CoreStones.”

He let the words hang a moment before continuing.

“There’s no use chasing it. Haiyana is completely unpredictable. We can’t predict its trajectory, nor where the stones will land. They could be launched to the other side of the planet, the ocean, or two feet in front of it.”

He met their eyes again.

“If you see it in the distance, don’t bother running. Use every TerraStone you have to dig as deep as possible and hope it doesn’t feel like taking your life.”

A long silence followed.

“That’s all for today’s lecture. Any questions?”

Praew had one, but so did everyone else. Every hand in the room went up. Godric pointed to a guy in the back, someone Praew had never bothered to learn the name of.

“Yeah, uhh… Instructor Stone Godric, sir?”

Godric sighed. “Spit it out, kid. You’re holding up the class.”

The student sat up straighter. “Sorry, sir. Uhh… What's an ocean?”

All of the other hands dropped in unison. Praew’s included.

Godric gave them all a flat stare, clearly annoyed “Class dismissed,” he barked.



***



Praew’s early morning trek to class felt blissfully serene, although admittedly, she felt a little guilty for enjoying the silence.  

     Not once had Mai Azadi shown up since their argument. She was probably taking a different route to avoid her. She did tell Mai to stay away. She just didn’t expect someone who thought it was okay to stalk her friends to actually listen. 

     Still, Praew had been wanting to talk to her. About—well, everything. And soon, she just might get her chance.

     Ghost Operation was scheduled for tomorrow morning, but not for her. Mai had pulled her from the class so she wouldn’t have to cross a line she didn’t believe in. It wouldn’t be treasonous if she stopped by, would it? If she went early enough, she could probably catch Mai alone.

But first, she just had to survive another Shadow Recon class.



***



That proved to be more difficult than she thought. It was week two and she couldn’t even take a single step without triggering the SolarStones in the first silent movement course. She didn’t even bother attempting to test out last week, choosing to spend the time investigating with Junya over making a complete fool of herself. 

     Everyone somehow passed but Junya and her. Surprising, but fair. That’s what they got for skipping. What she found unfair, however, was that somehow Junya crossed the path today perfectly, like it was nothing.

     That’s just not fair! Praew almost yelled but stopped herself. No one has seen Jorpen since his outburst in this class last week. So no one else dared to defy their instructor’s no speaking rule.

     Praew’s right eye started to twitch. She patted it three times before throwing away the bad omen. Praew believed if her right eye twitched, she was going to get in an argument or something really bad was going to happen.

     As the class concluded, Praew stepped outside the room, waved goodbye to Junya and Bpen, and waited for Wannii. She’d noticed that Wannii has been cold and distant the last few days. Praew occasionally caught Wannii glaring at her, rolling her eyes and shooting dirty looks. Praew was going to get to the bottom of it today.

     Concentrating on the ground as she practiced silent step, Wannii stumbled in the doorframe. Clearly surprised to see Praew waiting for her. Wannii brushed a hand through her short black hair, slicking it back slightly. Her recent haircut was cute. It rested only halfway down the ears revealing piercings Praew hadn’t noticed before. With her small nose, scrunched brow, and naturally intense expression, Wannii always looked like she was scowling. Honestly these days, she probably was.

“Hey, Wannii! I love the haircut. It’s so cute!”

“Thanks,” Wannii said, arms folding across her chest, voice flat and uninviting.

Really? That’s it? Ugh, this woman.

“Of course!” Praew pushed on, forcing lightness into her voice. “Have you always had your ears pierced? I have six piercings myself.”

     “Yeah,” Wannii said with an exaggerated sigh.

     That was on purpose. It had to be. Girl, if you’re jealous just say so. You obviously have a problem with me—“So just spit it out already!”

  Wannii stopped mid-step.

  Great. Not again, Praew.

  “Fine!” Wannii snapped, raising her voice to match the tension in the air. “Stay away from Junya! You’re way too dangerous!”

  “What?”

  “You know what she’s looking into! I know she told you! And the funny thing is—I was glad she did! You’re so smart, logical, considerate. Obviously, you’d tell her to stop poking her nose around an obvious trap before she gets herself killed, right?”

  “Wannii, I—” Praew didn’t get the chance to finish.

  “No! No, you did not. Instead, you thought it was a good idea to encourage her and tell her story to someone from The Prime Archon’s office?! You might as well kill her yourself!”

  “ No!” Praew argued. “He’s my best friend. We can trust him!”

  “You… can trust him.” Wannii clarified “We don’t even know him. And you really expect me to be okay with you putting my best friend’s life in the hands of someone who works for the very place that executes inquisitive people like her? Haven’t you heard the saying? ‘What faith is owed to a stranger when betrayal may protect them from danger?’”

     “He wouldn’t do that,” Praew’s fists tightened until her knuckles went white. She was sick of being scolded. By Haldric, Mai, Godric, and now Wannii too? The irony wasn’t lost on her. Wannii found her dangerous… just like Praew felt about Mai.

  Her head dropped. She couldn’t blame Wannii. What they were doing was irresponsible, stupid, and dangerous… and Wannii was just worried about Junya. Wait, I may not know Junya as long, but I’m worried about her too!

  “Is that so bad?” Praew asked calmly, lifting her head and locking eyes with Wannii. “Maybe you’re right. But I think it’s better not to pretend. We both know she’ll never give up. It’s her parents we’re talking about. Can you honestly say you wouldn’t do the same?”

  She stepped forward, voice unwavering. “Do you really think scolding her and ignoring her tears will stop her? If you do, then I’ve severely overestimated your intelligence. Hide behind your words all you want, Wannii. But no shadow you cast will ever stretch far enough to hide your cowardice. If you really cared about Junya, you would never leave her side. At least with me, if we get caught, I won’t let her suffer alone again.”

  Wannii looked around the hallway, then leaned in, her voice quieter now, but no less sharp. “Do you even know what she’s planning, Praew? Junya plans to sneak into the mines at night and investigate the area alone. She’s making sure no one goes with her so no one else gets implicated. I only found out because she forgot to hide a few maps. In her room”

  Wannii shook her head. “So much for not letting her suffer alone again, huh? If you really cared about Junya, you would never leave her side. That’s what you said, right?”

  Her voice broke slightly as she went on. “You have no idea how much I’ve given up to keep her safe.

     “The Hunter test was supposed to be our defining moment. The final test was one Junya and I could’ve passed with our eyes closed. We had to identify the triggers of a set of weak Sifaraliths, one by one, and place them gently into a Hunter pouch without setting off a chain reaction. The pouches were embedded with an EchoStone. If even one trigger fired inside, the bag would scream out loud: You failed.

     “I was on my last one, holding my breath like it might help, when the scream came. For a second, I thought I’d blown it. That I’d ruined everything. But then I realized it came from my right.

     “I turned to see Junya’s face, frozen, hollow. She looked like she hadn’t slept in days. And I’d been so caught up in the test, I didn’t even notice. Some friend, right?”

     Wannii’s voice cracked, but she pushed on.

     “Of course she made a mistake. I would’ve too, if I were mourning and still trying to hold it together.

     “But Stormhaven doesn’t care. They already took her parents, her home, her belongings. Now they were taking her dream of reaching the surface.

     “I just stood there, final Sifaralith still in hand. If I placed it in the correct pouch, I would become a Hunter. But who knows when—or if—I’d be there when she needed me most.

“I wasn’t going to let them take away her only friend too.

“So I made my choice. I let the final stone slip. It landed in the wrong pouch, and it screamed for me too.”

  Her eyes burned. Wannii had the look of someone who stood by their decision with no regret. “I would give my life for her without a second thought. You don’t know me.”

     Wannii stepped closer now, fists trembling. “You think I haven’t tried it your way? Junya’s been doing this for a while now—taking stupid risks, sneaking around, skipping classes, stealing from the Archon and not clueing me in until after the fact. She says it’s to protect me, but really? She just doesn’t want anyone stopping her. I tried talking her down. Backing off. Discouraging her. And it was working. Zephyrs take you, it was working!”

  Her whole body shook now. “Then you came and ruined it. I wanted to believe things could still change. But I know now—I can’t stop her. So I’m asking you… can you at least make sure she doesn’t go alone?”

  Praew took a breath, then slowly extended the back of her hand.

  “Let’s make sure together, Wannii. We’ll both keep an eye on her. Make sure she knows how dangerous this is, and that she can’t be impulsive.”

     Wannii nodded once, slapped the back of Praew’s hand with her own. “Thank you, Praew. Let’s do this.”

     Praew smiled. “Junya’s lucky to have a friend like you.”

    “Thanks,” Wannii said. “She’s lucky to have you too.”

    “You both do,” Praew replied, still smiling as they walked forward and turned the corner.

     Wannii hesitated, about to say something else—but froze.

     Praew froze too. “Zephyrs take me, she whispered.”

    Godric was waiting in the hallway, arms crossed, leaning against the wall.

    “Well,” he muttered, pushing off the wall and stepping toward them. “That was quite the argument you two were having near a government-run facility.”

     His eyes narrowed. “Now, I didn’t hear what you bolting emotional wrecks were screaming about, but it sounded like something I should’ve. Would either of you care to fill me in?”

This is bad, I should have been more cautious.

    “Oh, nothing,” Praew said, holding Godric’s gaze without flinching. “Just arguing about whose Hunter Exam was harder.”

“Yeah,” Wannii added flatly.

 “I see,” he muttered. “Just be mindful of where you decide to have your mental breakdowns. You’re dismissed.”

Praew shared a look with Wannii. They both started walking quickly down the hall—

“Dangerous habit… letting stones slip.” Godric’s voice cut through the silence behind them.  “It’s not uncommon for one to end up missing.”

     Praew and Wannii shared a wide-eyed look, fear rising like static between them. Then they bolted in opposite directions, not daring to speak.







Chapter 9 - The Breakup



The next morning came far too fast.

     Praew found herself in an empty classroom standing in front of Mai Azadi. Just as she hoped.  Unfortunately, everything she had planned to say had fled her.

Mai spoke first, not even bothering to look up from her book.

“You don’t look like one of my students.”

“Hey Mai,” Praew began, “I just —“

“If you are not one of my students,” Mai interrupted, “You are not permitted to enter the classroom at this time. Please leave to avoid being forcefully escorted off the premises.” Mai stood up, likely to escort her.

“You didn’t deserve what I said,” Praew blurted out, her voice low and shaken. Mai froze in place. “I was scared. Angry. I thought I knew what kind of person you were, but I didn’t. And you didn’t deserve that. I’m sorry.”

Mai turned, tilted her head. Sideways looking confused.

“I still don’t know how to feel about you,” Praew said. “I’m not like you, Mai. I don’t know if we ever could be friends. But… I’m willing to try if you still want to…but there needs to be boundaries.”

Mai studied her in silence. No teasing, no smile.

Then finally, a soft one surfaced. Not her usual grin. Something smaller. Realer.

“That’s fair,” Mai said. “First rule: no more calling me a psychopath.”

Praew almost smiled. “Deal. Second rule: no more showing up at my house.”

Mai’s head tilted to the opposite side. “Even if I bring gifts?”

“Rule 3,” Praew continued, “No stalking. No threats, and no talk of murder.”

Mai sighed, dramatically. “Fine. But you’re no fun. Then… rule four: you let me train you in silent movement. At this rate, you’re going to fail your final.”

Praew awkwardly stepped forward in an attempt at quieter movement.

“What? How would you know?”

Mai looked at her sympathetically.

“The same way I knew it was you in the hallway without ever looking up from my book. Praew, my sweet porridge, I’ve seen blind children move with more grace than you.”

     “Fine,” Praew said sharply. “But I’m not doing it because it’s your condition. I’m only doing it because I never turn down a chance to train.”

     Mai blinked. “Sure thing. Then we start today. When I finish class, let’s meet here to start your training.”



***



After agreeing, Praew left before any students got to class. Spending the day training, she didn’t return until the students were starting to file out. 

“Praew! What are you doing here?” Junya asked cheerfully as Praew entered the room. Right behind her, Wannii gave Praew a nod. 

      Praew nodded back before responding to Junya. “Oh I’m going to do a little sparring with your teacher.” 

“You’re going to do what?!” Bpen asked walking up to them

“Don’t worry,” Praew said. “I’ll try not to hurt her. 

Mai near the front of the class snorted loud enough for them to hear. 

“Right,” Bpen said, putting a hand on her shoulder. “You will be missed, old friend.” He shook his head as he left the room.

“So…” Junya started, “are you going to be busy all night tonight?” 

That seemed a little too excited

 Praew glanced towards Wannii, who had the same look on her face. That question felt off to both of them. Wannii shook her head quickly, then pointed to herself. 

Good. Praew nodded. Wannii would keep an eye on her tonight while Praew trained.

“Yeah… unfortunately,” Praew said, realizing she still hadn’t responded.

“Well, that’s too bad” Junya said.  “I guess we can hang out some other time.”  Before Praew could reply, Junya was gone. Wannii had to break into a jog to chase after her.

     The classroom emptied, leaving just Mai and Praew.

     “Doesn’t it ever get uncomfortable being so popular?” Mai asked curiously. “I mean, anytime you walk into a room, you can’t even blend in. What if you have to assassinate someone?”

Praew raised an eyebrow.

“So…” Mai bounced on her heels. “We made up now, right? Like officially made-up, not a fake make-up, because there’s this book I’m reading that says your first argument is, like, the most important moment in a relationship. It said this argument sets the tone for how she’ll treat you from then on, and determines whether she’ll respect you, neglect you, or completely reject you.”

Mai blinked. Totally serious.

“You’re not gonna break up with me, are you?”

Break up with…” Praew rubbed her temple. “Mai, what exactly was that book called?”

 “Oh! Uh…” Mai scrambled to the front. “I have it with me.”

She reached under her desk and pulled out a black book with a white sketch on the cover. It showed a pair of eyes winking and a mouth biting its own lip.

Mai held it up proudly. “Her Smile, Your Success: Satisfy Her and Keep the Relationship Strong.”

Praew stared. “We’re not courting, Mai.”

Mai blinked. “What’s courting?”

“What do you mean, what’s courting?” Praew was aghast. She was no expert by any means. In fact, she was probably the worst person to ask altogether. But to not even know what it was? Poor girl, Praew thought, taking the back of Mai’s hand and patting it pitifully.

     Mai tilted her head like a confused Faelhen. “Like… I know I was trained on it, but I just can’t remember. I think it involves people and… emotions? Or dancing maybe? I never did any missions that required it. My handler always said I’m too young.”

     She fidgeted with the book’s corner. “Anyhow, when we argued, you made it pretty clear I’m not very good at this friendship thing, and I might’ve made you a little uncomfortable with my eagerness and spying.”

“A little?” Praew asked incredulously.

“Okay, so a lot uncomfortable,” Mai admitted sheepishly. “Well, I figured I should learn how to be a better friend. The book said that when you test her patience and she gets angry, it’s best to give her space, don’t pester her, and give her time. I didn’t think it made much sense, because how is it a test if it was an accident in the first place?”

     She perked up. “But we made up, so I guess that means we passed the test!” She clapped once. “Yay us!”

     Praew couldn’t help but smile. How could she ever think Mai was a bad person? She was so painfully innocent—and trying so hard. Misguided, sure. But she was trying.

      Mai clapped again. “Well, we better start training, eh? Partner?”

     Praew rolled her eyes as Mai opened the secret passageway leading to the testing area. Mai led the way, and Praew followed a step behind through the dim corridor.

     “Hey, Mai,” she said, her voice a little softer, “can you do me a favor?”

     “Anything! What do you need?” Mai responded, eyes bright.

     “Next time you get a chance… can you burn that book?”

     Mai paused mid-step. “It’s not the right book, is it?”

     Praew shook her head.

     “Yeah, I guess that makes sense,” Mai said with a sigh. “Chapter Eight was really weird. It seemed very unsanitary, so I skipped most of it.”

     That finally did it, Praew burst out laughing. Mai joined in a beat later, giggling as the hidden door creaked shut behind them.

     Praew followed Mai into the training room, their giggles mingling with the sound of their footsteps. Mai led her through a narrow passage until they reached the familiar testing path, a strip of polished stone flanked by SolarStones. Each one was triggered by EchoStones or sound.

     Praew shook out her arms behind her. “Now don’t go easy on me. I need to get better—fast. I’m sure you’re really good at this, so train me the way you were.”

     Mai glanced back over her shoulder and nodded. Her eyes seemed… distant, somehow.

Mai glanced back again. Her gaze held, focused and unusually still. “You’ve been practicing silent movement in Shadow intelligence, right?”

     “Yeah,” Praew said, a bit hesitant.

     Mai nodded toward the path. “Show me.”

     Praew stepped forward, took a breath, and moved. Her foot scraped slightly, and one of the SolarStones flickered in response. She winced, kept going, then stumbled just enough to throw off her rhythm. Another faint glow pulsed along the edge. She stopped halfway and turned back, her return just as uneven.

     “I’m not very good at it yet,” Praew said, walking back.

     Mai was already smiling when Praew reached her again. Or trying to. One corner of her mouth tugged upward in a stiff, uneven stretch, like it was embedded in place. The way her eye twitched uncontrollably, Praew first thought a BoltStone had shocked her mid-blink. It was the first time Praew had ever seen her struggle to smile.

     Is she trying to reassure me? Zephyrs take me, that looks painful!

     “I can tell,” Mai eventually said. “Your overthinking makes you stiff. Take a deep breath and slowly exhale while relaxing your shoulders.”

     Praew complied.

     “Good,” Mai said, a genuine smile returning. “But there’s still too much tension in your hips.” She stepped in front of Praew. “Loosen here—yeah. Now don’t look down, but when you step, roll your foot from the tips of your toes to the ball. No heel. No slap. Quiet like blinking, not a scuffling boot.”

     Praew nodded, shifting her weight, trying to roll her foot like Mai said. But halfway through the motion, her heel clipped the floor with a sharp clack. She froze.

     Then she sighed, eyes dropping to her boots.

     That’s when Mai moved.

     Praew glanced up just in time to see Mai’s shoulder twist—just enough warning to catch the blur of a fist shooting toward her gut. Praew gasped, stumbling back as her arms flew out and caught the strike just in time.

     “What are you doing?” she snapped.

     Mai blinked. “What am I—?” Her fist still pressed against Praew’s palm. “What are you doing? Why did you block it?”

     “Why wouldn’t I?” Praew bellowed. “And answer the question. Why did you try to hit me?”

     “Because you asked me to!” Mai snapped. “Didn’t you tell me to train you how I was?”

     “Zephyrs take me. That’s how you were trained?”

     “Well, yeah,” Mai admitted, “my handler said not to train students like this, but… is it really that bad? Wait—did I mess up again? Oh no. Did we just have another fight?”

     Praew exhaled, lowering her guard. “Fine, no, we’re fine. But prepare yourself, Mai. If you’re swinging, I’m blocking. If I’m taking hits, I’m hitting back. I learn by standing my ground, not by standing down.”

     The next three days with Mai made Praew question her life choices. Repeatedly.



***



With all that bravado, Praew failed to land a single hit. It felt like she spent more time sparring than actually practicing silent movement. Eventually, they agreed to just one swing each, per mistake. Still, hopefully the bruises weren’t too noticeable.

     “Focus, Praew,” Godric barked in class afterwards. “Then maybe you’ll actually win your next match with whatever boulder ran you over.”

     Zephyrs take this bolting man. If this is how he talks to women, it’s no wonder he’s never been married.

     “Sorry, sir,” Praew muttered. One look at Godric was enough to keep her from saying what was really on her mind.

     “Don’t be sorry, be better,” Godric growled. “I asked where Junya and Wannii are. They do not have permission to skip class.”

     Praew was so tired and sore, she hadn’t even noticed they were gone.

     “I don’t know, sir. I’m sure they have a good reason,” she said.

     Like staying up all night investigating and failing to wake up on time, she thought, but wisely kept that to herself.

     Godric grunted and turned to the rest of the class. “Today we’re going over the uses and limitations of SurgeStones.”

Godric didn’t pace around the room like usual. He stomped. Back and forth like a riled Tarnox, boots hammering the stone.

     “SurgeStones,” he barked, “can keep you alive, if you know what you’re doing. But one mistake, and you’ll be staring death in the face. You want to stay alive? Then remember these rules.”

He slammed a fist on his desk. Every student jumped.

“Rule one. A SurgeStone doubles the power of a Sifaralith Stone and raises its rarity rank by one. Temporarily. But it also shuts it down afterward, for exactly the same length of time. You surge for five flickers, both stones are deadweight for five more. You surge for a flash?” He locked eyes with Praew. “You’d better hope you have more stones, or you’ll be left with nothing but your fists and regret.”

     Praew’s stomach turned. What’s going on? He seems more grouchy than usual today.

     “Be mindful of how long you surge,” Godric warned, stomping back toward his desk. “Whatever stone you’re surging, it’ll spiral and crumble twice as fast. Doesn’t matter if it’s Common or Noble. If you forget to check on it or time your surge, it could spiral straight to dust. That’s how many Hunters lose their lives.

     Bpen, off to Praew’s right, raised his hand. “But sir—”

     Godric stopped mid-stomp and shot him a look sharp enough to silence a Faelhen mid-mimic.

     “Now pay attention. This is where most rookies screw up. SurgeStones aren’t one-size-fits-all. A Noble SurgeStone can override another Noble or a Common Stone. But try using a Common SurgeStone on a Noble, and you’ll be standing there looking stupid with a stone that does nothing.”

     Yeah, something is definitely off, Praew thought. He wasn’t letting anyone interrupt today.

     “You’ve probably guessed by now. This is how we identify rarity. You want to know what kind of stone you’re holding?” Godric asked. “Test it. If it doesn’t react, it’s stronger than your surge. If it connects, congratulations. You just found your limit.” He spat the next words like bile.

     “Unfortunately, just like in life, one’s rank isn’t measured by success, but by their failure.”

     He turned to the slate board and scraped three shapes: a circle, a bolt, and a fang. “Core. Nature. Beast.”

     He stabbed the stone into each, making the slate board click. “Core and Beast Stones can work on each other, no problem. But like I told you before, don’t mess with Nature Stones. They’re too bolting unpredictable. I had one nullify every stone I carried once. It’s safe to assume there’s one out there that could surge them too.”

     He let the silence settle.

    “If you ever sense Nature acting suspicious, hide your stones at all costs. Maybe you’ll live to see another day.”

     A few students began to whisper to each other. But Godric’s growl cut through the room and silenced it immediately.

     “You can surge your own body, too. Strength. Speed. Stamina. Even reflexes. Makes you sharper for a flicker or superhuman for a flash. But when the surge ends?” He slapped his chest. “Your body crashes. Hard. That weakness lasts twice as long. You surge for ten flickers, you’ll be crawling for twenty. Surge too long, and your veins turn black, your skin starts to crack, and eventually your body crumbles like a beast’s corpse. I’ve seen it, and it ain’t pretty.”

     Godric stopped, closed his eyes, and shook his head like he was trying to shake off a memory.

     “One of the most dangerous things on the surface, second only to Nature itself, is other humans,” Godric said, not even trying to hide his disgust. “And make no mistake, you will have to fight them.”

     He began to pace again. “You can surge other people’s stones. If you make contact. Just like with NullStone contact requirements, your surge must trigger on your body or weapon while in contact with the effect of their stone. Weapon-to-weapon contact counts, as long as the embedded stones are both active.

     “When successful, your SurgeStone links to theirs. Their stone temporarily increases one rank in rarity, becoming more powerful, but your surge takes over as their trigger. Even if they cut theirs, you can keep their stone active for as long as yours is.”

     He sliced the air with his hand.

     “And when you cut yours off? Their stone times out. Useless. Perfect moment to strike. Just remember to keep that stone in your sight.

     “There are three ways to lose control.”

     He raised a finger. “First and most common, they hide their stone. Tossing a cloth over it or putting it away long enough will break the link.”

     “Second, they can use a NullStone with equal or greater rarity than your SurgeStone. That severs the connection instantly.”

     He gave a short nod, walking back toward his desk.

    “Both of those will trigger cooldown. Your stone and theirs are unable to trigger for the same length of the surge. That’s why surges work best on embedded weapons. Makes it easier to throw off their rhythm and harder for them to break contact.”

     Reaching under the desk, Godric pulled out five wooden baskets and gently placed them on top with a tenderness Praew was surprised he was capable of.

     “Listen up, Ratrods. Split into pairs. One attacker, one defender. Each of you will take two stones: one SurgeStone, and one other of your choice.”

     He scanned the room. “We’re heading to the training room to practice surging and cutting surge links. After that, I’ll test each of you on your body surge limits. You need to feel, not guess, how long you can push yourself before you crumble.”

     Godric opened the secret door.

     “If we get through everyone without any major injuries, I’ll tell you the third method. The only true counter to a surge.”

     His voice echoed without emotion.



***



     A sharp light cut through Haldric’s window, searing into his eyes even through closed lids.

     That’s how it happened every morning. One giant SolarStone in each district, triggered at the fifth flash, acted as both alarm and signal—the day had begun. At the fifteenth flash, the trigger would stop, and a thick stone box would be hoisted over the SolarStone to dim its flashes throughout the night.

     They said the surface had a SolarStone as big as the planet hanging in the sky. That made no sense, though. What could possibly trigger a Sifaralith that massive?

     Haldric rolled onto his back, staring up at the uneven rock above him, half-listening to the muffled sounds of Cenrick and Wintrid moving downstairs. The faint scent of boiled blood cubes and rice paste lingered in the air—their breakfast, if it could even be called that. His would be distributed at the ration station near the administration building.

He wasn’t hungry, though. His appetite had vanished the day he found out his Grayparents had been quietly skipping meals, just so he could have a little more.

He dressed without thinking, pulling on the stiff, standard-issue uniform they had given him on his first day. Muted gray, like everything else in this city. Nothing about it marked him as anything special—no insignia, or indicator of status.

Haldric said goodbye to his Grayparents and rushed out the door. The middle ring district of the Third Floor was already awake, but barely.

The slums never felt truly alive—just a dull, exhausted shuffle of bodies, the sound of footsteps against uneven stone. The air was thick with the faint sourness of unwashed clothes, the ever-present dampness of underground life. A few traders set up makeshift stalls, their eyes darting around, watching for enforcers. The last thing anyone needed was to be caught selling without permission.

     Haldric kept walking.

     The Second Floor was quieter in a different way—less desperation, more resignation. Here, the roads widened, not much, but enough to show the difference. The homes had doors. Real wooden ones, not the thick stone slab he lived behind or the cloth-draped entryways of the less fortunate. People moved with purpose, but without hope. It wasn’t hunger that slowed them so much as it was acceptance.

And then, as always, the First Floor was something else entirely.

The shift was immediate, buildings carved with precision, the streets perfectly aligned. Enforcers stood at key intersections, motionless as statues, helmets reflecting the glow of SolarStones overhead. The people here moved calmly, orderly, as if they knew eyes were always watching.

Haldric exhaled as the administrative building came into view—its tall, imposing entrance flanked by two guards who barely even looked at him as he stepped inside.

By the time he entered the building, his mind had already begun shutting down in preparation for the tedium ahead.



The records hall was a monument to routine. A vast, windowless chamber, its tall, domed ceiling pressing down like the weight of the cavern itself. The desks stretched in perfect, unmoving rows, each identical, each occupied by another clerk just like him. No marks of ownership, no sign of individuality, just worn stone and endless parchment. The air smelled of old ink and dust, heavy and unmoving. The only sounds were the scratch of Faelhen quills, the shuffle of parchment, and the occasional clearing of a throat. 

Somewhere in the distance, a slow, deliberate flicker of light was swallowed by six flashes—the unlucky number that marked the start of his shift.

Start at the sixth flash, work six more, six until curfew, seven to snore.

For the next six flashes, he’d do nothing but sort, stamp, and record data. Hunter reports: Mostly filled with exaggerated kill counts, half of them barely legible. Resource distribution forms: He wasn’t allowed to question why certain districts got more than others. Incident logs: Minor disputes, market altercations, someone caught trying to barter with ration slips.

Stamp. Sign. Sort. Repeat.

It was enough to make his brain crumble.

The worst part was the redundancy. Every form had to be checked, verified, logged, then sent to another department for a second verification. As if they didn’t trust their own system.

He flipped through a stack of Hunter evaluations, eyes glazing over the familiar categories:



Kill Efficiency – How fast they eliminated threats.

Resource Retrieval – How many usable parts they recovered from beasts.

Behavioral Stability – A vague but important column. Too reckless? Too independent? Too many violations? Hunters who failed this category usually disappeared.



Haldric’s fingers hovered over that last section for a moment before he forced himself to keep moving. 

Stamp. Sign. Sort.

Across from him, Gerrik, a man in his mid-thirties with a permanent slouch and an unfortunate mustache, yawned loudly. “You ever feel like we’re just moving numbers around to make it seem like something’s happening?”

     Haldric didn’t look up. “We are.”

     Gerrik hummed in agreement. “I spent an hour yesterday logging a report about another report being misplaced.”

     Haldric flipped another page.

     “Then I had to write a report about the process of recovering that report.”

     He finally looked up, narrowing his eyes. “You’re making that up.”

     Gerrik raised his hands. “I swear on my ration card.”

     Haldric sighed and went back to work.



     When the midday break finally came, Haldric stretched his aching hands and stepped out onto the upper walkway, overlooking the lower levels of the administrative sector.

     Below, clerks and officers hurried between offices, voices blending into a low murmur. It was all so structured. So controlled. No one questioned anything. No one resisted.

He found himself wondering—did it really have to be this way? Had the people of Stormhaven always been forced into their roles? What was so wrong with choosing your own path?

He shook his head. That sounded an awful lot like treason.

And justice we will deliver, he thought. 

His gaze flickered toward the hallway leading to The Prime Archon’s office. Sealed doors, guarded at all times. Behind them, decisions were being made that people like him would never hear.

He exhaled sharply, turning away. This wasn’t his concern.

He had work to do.



The afternoon was worse than the morning.

The monotony stretched endlessly. His hand was cramping from stamping documents. His back ached from sitting in the same position for multiple flashes. The words on the pages started blurring together, names and numbers losing all meaning.

Occasionally, he’d hear footsteps—someone important passing by. But he never looked up.

Until just before the end of the day, when he arrived.

Haldric didn’t see him at first. He only felt the difference.

The way the guards near The Prime’s office straightened slightly, how clerks at nearby desks suddenly paid just a bit more attention to their work, avoiding glances upward.

Silver Wing Godric.

The Mythical Beast Hunter wasn’t here for pleasantries.

He stood near the entrance of The Prime Archon’s office, arms stiff at his sides but his posture was sharp, wound tight.

Haldric had seen Godric from a distance before. A disciplined, structured man. A model soldier in every way. A true hero.

But now…

Godric’s normally neat hair was slightly unkempt, a few strands falling loose from where they should have been spiked back. His uniform, usually crisp, had a slight misalignment at the collar, the fabric wrinkled like he had been gripping it too tightly.

He stepped forward. Spoke low. “I have questions for The Prime.”

The guards exchanged a glance but didn’t argue. One of them pushed open the door, and Godric stepped inside.

The door clicked shut behind him.



And that was when Haldric moved.

He pushed back from his desk, stretched as if casually shaking off fatigue, then made his way toward the same guards.

One of them barely looked at him before speaking. “What do you need?”

Haldric kept his expression neutral. Relaxed. “I need to pass along a report. The Archon’s office requested it this morning.”

That wasn’t entirely a lie. The Prime had requested it. Just… not today.

The guards didn’t bother verifying. One of them gestured to the chair against the stone wall. “Wait here. He’s in a meeting.”

Perfect.

Haldric nodded, sat, and waited.

Or rather—listened.

At first, everything was muffled.

A steady murmur of voices, broken by occasional pauses. The stone walls did their job well.

But then—a shift.

A sharper voice. Raised. Not The Prime’s

Haldric’s back straightened slightly, feet firm against the floor. His heart kicked up just a fraction.

Then, through the thick wooden door— “. . . are you bolting doing!?” Godric’s voice, rough, edged with barely restrained anger.

Muffled response. Too low to make out.

Another pause. Longer this time.

Then—sharper, more direct— “You asked me to take over this position!”

A murmur of words followed, but still too quiet.

Haldric’s fingers tightened against his knee.

Then the words that made his stomach drop. “…DOING TO THEM?!”

A spike of cold unease. Them? He wished he could hear what The Prime was saying. Only Godric spoke loud enough to catch.

The pause that followed was tense—even through the wall, Haldric could feel it.

Then, one last snap— “IF I FIND OUT YOU—”

The door creaked open.

Haldric’s pulse jumped. He immediately hunched forward, feigning disinterest, flipping open his stack of papers as if the reports actually mattered.

From his lowered gaze, he saw Godric exit. He didn’t slam the door but the way he walked—sharp, fast, his hands curled into fists at his sides, said everything.

The moment the door closed behind him, he didn’t even glance at the guards. Didn’t look at anyone.

He just left.

Haldric forced himself to stay still, resisting the urge to watch Godric disappear down the hall. Instead, he let his gaze drop to the floor, mind racing.

What had The Prime said? What was happening? And most importantly—who was ‘them’?

A voice snapped him from his thoughts.

“Haldric.”

One of The Prime’s attendants addressed him. “The Prime Archon will see you now.”

Haldric swallowed, schooling his expression into neutrality. No hesitation. No visible nerves. He stood, adjusting the paper in his hands like it was the only thing that mattered, and stepped inside.

The silence in the office felt different.

Not the usual, weighted kind of silence that accompanied The Prime’s presence. This was cracked—uneven, like something had shaken the room just before Haldric stepped in.

And maybe it had.

     The Prime Archon’s office was Stormhaven in miniature: pristine, controlled, and cold. The desk, carved from a single slab of dark stone, was polished to perfection, its surface nearly bare, as if clutter itself was beneath him. Behind him, a massive carved relief of the city loomed, a precise map etched into the rock, every district accounted for, every tunnel controlled.

     And yet, for the first time, the man sitting at the desk did not match his domain.

     His fingers pressed too firmly against the papers before him. His collar was slightly askew—not enough to be immediately noticeable, but just uneven enough to feel off. A set of deep creases ran down the center of his tunic, not the usual folds from wear, but bunched in a way that didn’t seem natural. His sleeve bore a faint wrinkle near the elbow, as if it had been shifted, pushed, or gripped at some point.

Haldric wasn’t supposed to notice these things.

But he did.

And before anything else, The Prime exhaled sharply, his upper lip curling slightly in disgust.

“Blasted Hunters,” he muttered, almost spitting the word before catching himself.

Haldric’s pulse jumped.

The Prime never did that.

For all his control, all his public-facing image, the words came out like a slur. Haldric had never seen The Prime display open disdain like this before. 

But just as fast as the slip happened, it was gone. The Prime Archon straightened, his expression smoothing back into its usual, unreadable mask.

Haldric hadn’t realized he was staring until he felt his own throat tighten. He forced himself to keep his posture steady. No reaction. Nothing out of the ordinary.



But something of his reaction must have shown, because The Prime’s gaze flickered up, just for a second, sharp, assessing.

Don’t acknowledge it, Haldric thought. Move on.

He swallowed and opened his mouth, only to realize that the reason he had come here sounded much weaker now than it had in his head.

He had an excuse. Something about a report. But standing here, with the tension still thick in the air, it suddenly felt ridiculous.

The moment stretched.

Too long.

And before Haldric could stop himself, before he could pull together the excuse he had planned—the words that came out were not the ones he intended.

“Are you okay?”

The Prime stiffened.

Then, almost amused—“Ahh, you heard that, did you?”

Haldric’s stomach dropped. 

Idiot.

He had just drawn attention to something he should have pretended not to notice. The first rule of working in the system: don’t acknowledge things above your station.

He clenched his jaw, already kicking himself internally.

Stupid, stupid, stupid. Fix it. Now.

He straightened, forcing a quick breath through his nose before shaking his head. “I… I didn’t hear anything, sir.”

Too fast, stupid!

The Prime Archon let out a short chuckle, his composure snapping back into place. He adjusted his sleeve, smoothing out a crease as if that was the only thing worth fixing.

  “Son, it is quite all right. I won’t tell your superior.”

  The way he said it was almost amused, like he was indulging a joke only he understood. He leaned back slightly, tilting his head.

  “Hmm, how long has it been since the two of us spoke like this?”

  Haldric shrugged, too nervous to speak.

  The Prime gave a slow, thoughtful nod in return.

  “Funny, you might very well be the first person to worry about my well-being since the previous Prime Archon named me his Heir. Thank you, Haldric.”

  Haldric hesitated.

  The Prime Archon wasn’t as untouchable as he had always seemed. There was something in his voice, not just amusement, but… something.

  “Right, I apologize for my son and what happened during your Testing. It wasn’t supposed to be that way,” The Prime said, bowing his head bitterly.

  Maybe The Prime wasn’t as in control as people thought. Maybe he was being pushed around in his own position.

The thought had barely formed when Haldric blurted out, “Are all Hunters that much of an asshole, or is Godric a special case?”

Wait.

Did I just say that out loud? Inner thoughts, idiot! Inner. Thoughts.

A slow blink from the leader of the city.

Then—a smirk.

“Ahh, yes. Godric. Mythical Beast Hunter.” He exhaled through his nose, shaking his head. “They say never meet your heroes, lest you find out how much of a fraud they really are.”

A pause.

“That, was certainly the case for me.”

His eyes flickered, not just toward Haldric, but through him, past him, as if recalling something long buried.

Then, just as quickly, he sat back in his chair, his expression smoothing over, settling into something unbothered.

As if none of it had happened at all.

“Regardless, what is it that you wish to speak to me about?”

Haldric clenched his fists for a fraction of a second, then forced himself back into control.

This was his moment to regain composure.

Focus. Stick to the plan. Ignore what just happened.

He shifted his weight slightly, adjusting his stance into something professional, something that felt like he hadn’t just embarrassed himself.

And then, as if the moment before hadn’t happened at all, he finally got to the reason he came. After receiving The Prime’s stamps, he returned to his desk and spent the rest of the day sorting meaningless reports.

By the time the workday ended, Haldric’s mind was buzzing. Not with reports—but with the image of Godric’s face. The tension in his shoulders. The way his hands had curled into fists at his sides.

Something had happened in that meeting.

Something big.

But Haldric was just a clerk. A paper-pusher. Whatever this was, it wasn’t his concern.

At least, that’s what he told himself.

Still…

As he stepped out onto the streets, the weight of the day pressing against his shoulders, he felt something stir—a sensation he hadn’t realized had been missing. A quiet hum beneath his skin, an old instinct waking up.

Three weeks ago, it had slipped away, buried under routine and obligation.



But now?

Now, the pieces weren’t adding up. The whispers in the records, the tension in The Prime’s office, the missing people—it was all leading somewhere.

The truth was out there, hidden in the dark. Waiting.

And for the first time since taking this job, he felt the pull of something greater.

The thrill of the hunt.













Chapter 10 - War Claw Bresdin





“I stare down the crucible that defines my fate.”



Mettra, Fourth Week of Ferta

Fifteen days since beginning job assignment



Three uneventful work shifts came and went. As soon as Haldric got home, he bathed and cleaned his room. She was coming. They’d picked tonight weeks ago—before curfew, to share what they’d learned. 

But why did it have to be my house? He shook his head. He’d given up trying to understand Praew’s thought process long ago.

     Still, when the knock came, he felt a twinge of irritation. Not at her. At himself. Because this time, he was looking forward to it.

He opened the door.

Praew stood on the threshold, arms crossed, in the dim glow of the embedded SolarStone. She stepped inside without waiting for an invitation, already smirking. “Did you forget how to answer a door, or were you hoping I’d just leave?”



Haldric sighed. “No, I was just preparing myself for whatever nonsense you’re about to drag me into.”

“Good,” she said. “Because I’ve got plenty.”

Before she could say more, a voice rang out from the hallway. “Oh my!”

Haldric’s stomach sank.

Wintrid. She appeared with a delighted expression, her eyes already sparkling. Cenrick wasn’t far behind, moving slower but just as amused.

Praew blinked. “Uh… hi?”

Cenrick stroked his chin, looking between them. “Now this is a sight.”

“No, it isn’t,” Haldric said immediately.

Wintrid clasped her hands together. “She’s here at night, dear. What else could it be?”

“Nothing,” Haldric said flatly.

Cenrick hummed. “You know, lad, there was a time when we thought this day would never come.”

Praew smirked. “You doubted me?”

“Oh, we doubted him.” Cenrick nodded sagely. “You, my dear, always had more sense.”

Haldric rubbed his temple. “You’re both reading into things that aren’t there.”

Wintrid sighed dramatically. “Oh, let me have this, Haldric. I need some joy in my old age.”

Praew elbowed him lightly, tilting her head. “Dear… I don’t think I can lie to my future Grayparents like this.” She placed a hand over her heart, feigning guilt. “It just wouldn’t feel right.”

Haldric whipped his head toward her, eyes wide. “You!—”

Wintrid beamed. “Oh, I knew it!”

Cenrick chuckled. “Took you long enough, boy.”



Haldric exhaled sharply through his nose, his fists clenching. “You’re feeding them, Praew.”

She smiled innocently. “Just letting nature take its course.”

Wintrid patted Haldric’s shoulder fondly. “Well, I suppose we should leave you two lovebirds to talk.”

Cenrick grinned as he turned away. “Just don’t make me a Grand-Grayfather yet, boy.”

Haldric nearly choked. “What—? No—! That’s—”

But they were already gone.

Praew snorted. “They really have no faith in you, huh?”

Haldric exhaled, dragging a hand down his face as the last echoes of his Grayparents’ laughter faded into the next room. 

Then he turned to Praew, arms crossed. “Why did we have to meet at my place?”

Praew didn’t miss a beat. “Would you rather get interrogated by my father?”

Haldric hesitated. Just for a second. Then he shivered.

Praew grinned. “Thought so.”

Haldric scowled. “That still doesn’t explain why you had to lead my Grayparents into—whatever that was.”

Praew shrugged, entirely unbothered. “Now we have an excuse.”

Haldric narrowed his eyes. “An excuse for what?”

She smirked. “To meet, late at night.”

Haldric exhaled sharply, rubbing his temples. “You really think ahead, don’t you?”

Praew beamed. “Of course. I plan for everything.”

Haldric gave her a flat look. “Except how to avoid embarrassing me in my own home.”

She tapped her chin, mock-thoughtful. “Yeah, that part was just a bonus.”
