Haldric sighed. “Let’s just get on with it.”

Haldric and Praew headed up to his bedroom.

Has my room always been this small? It doesn’t smell bad… right?

Not that he cared what Praew thought or anything. But still…this was the first time he’d ever had a girl in his room.

Don’t make this weird, he told himself.

Below, his Grayparents were whispering and giggling on the stairs. He tried to block it out, setting aside the embarrassment as he began turning over everything he’d gathered.

A few days ago, he told her, he’d witnessed something far above his ration allocation.

“Godric, the Mythical beast Hunter, met with The Prime Archon last Ravtra… if you can even call it that.”

Praew’s brows furrowed. “What do you mean?”

Haldric’s grip tightened against his forearm. “I was outside the office. I didn’t hear everything, but what I did hear?” He shook his head. “It wasn’t a meeting—it was a fight.”

Praew straightened. “A fight?”

Haldric nodded. “Godric was furious. I couldn’t make out The Prime Archon’s words, but Godric? He wasn’t holding back.” He hesitated, then recited the words burned into his memory:



“Are you bolting doing?!”

“You asked me to take over this position!”

“…DOING TO THEM?!”

“IF I FIND OUT YOU—”



He stopped there, watching as Praew’s expression darkened.

She leaned forward. “Them?”

Haldric nodded. “That’s the part I can’t stop thinking about.” His fingers drummed against the table. “Whatever this is, it’s big. Godric was ready to rip The Prime Archon apart.”

Praew narrowed her eyes. “And The Prime?”

“Shaken,” Haldric said. “Not openly, but… I noticed the little things. His sleeve was wrinkled, like someone had grabbed him. His collar was slightly off-center. And for the first time, I saw him lose control.”

 Praew absorbed that, her fingers tapping against her arm. “If those two are arguing, something serious is going on.” She frowned. “You know, that’s not the only weird thing about Godric.”

Haldric raised an eyebrow. “What do you mean?”

She leaned forward slightly. “He’s my drill instructor.”

His jaw dropped. “You’re getting trained by Silver Wing Godric?!”

She nodded. “At first, I thought it’d be exciting. He’s a legend, after all. But he’s always on edge. Either angry or irritated, nothing in between. I swear, there’s something off about him.”

Haldric straightened. “Off how? I heard he’s grumpy, but—”

“No, it’s more than that,” Praew cut in, lowering her voice. “Wannii, Junya’s best friend, and I were talking about the investigation and how Junya planned to slip away and enter the mines. Wannii told me she purposely let a stone slip during her Hunter test just to keep an eye on her. I’m pretty sure Godric was eavesdropping. He told us it’s dangerous to let stones slip. Said it’s not uncommon for someone to end up missing.

“And then, after Junya and Wannii snuck into the mines… they disappeared.”

Haldric sat back, an uneasy weight settling in his chest. “So you think he knew something? Or maybe he made them disappear on purpose?”

Praew nodded. “I can’t shake the feeling he was trying to scare us off. Like it was a final warning not to dig any deeper.”

Haldric stilled. He didn’t want to believe it. Godric was still one of his heroes. One of the two Mythical Beast Hunters of Stormhaven.

Praew’s eyes were sharp. “And do you know how Godric reacted when Bpen and I asked about Junya and Wannii?”

 She didn’t give him a chance to respond. “He didn’t,” she said flatly. “He didn’t acknowledge it at all. Just told us to get back to training. Like they never existed.”

Haldric absorbed that, his stomach twisting.

“You think he knows what happened?” he asked.

Praew clenched her jaw. “I don’t know. But The Prime and Godric are both acting suspicious right now. Something’s happening, Haldric. Something big.”

Haldric exhaled. “Maybe. But… I don’t know if The Prime Archon is in control of it.”

 “What do you mean?” she asked, tilting her head.

Haldric hesitated. “He wasn’t acting like someone who’s pulling the strings. More like someone barely hanging on.”

Praew considered that. “So, you think he’s a puppet?”

Haldric shrugged. “I think someone’s pushing him into a corner.”

She hummed, turning the thought over.

“Then we find out who’s pulling the strings.”

Haldric nodded. “And we do it without getting caught.”

Praew tapped her fingers against the table, thinking. Then, she met his gaze.

“You have a way in.”

Haldric frowned. “What?”

 “The Prime likes you.”

He stiffened. “He doesn’t like me.”

        Praew smirked. “You don’t see it, do you? Ever since you tried to convince Asher to make peace with his father, The Prime’s been treating you differently. He wouldn’t have let you stay in his office otherwise.”

  Haldric clenched his jaw. “That doesn’t mean I can just—”

  “It means you should use it,” Praew cut in. “Get close to him. Earn his trust.”

  He looked away, something uneasy curling in his stomach.

  “He already trusts me,” he muttered.

  “Then get him to tell you things,” Praew said. “Not just what he wants you to hear. Find out what he’s really up to.

     Haldric exhaled sharply, rubbing his temple. This was a bad idea. A really bad idea. And yet…

“If I do this,” he said slowly, “then you stay out of trouble. No stupid risks. No sneaking around on your own.”

Praew grinned. “No promises.”

Haldric gave her a flat look.

“Fine,” she relented. “I won’t do anything reckless. Happy?”

“Not even remotely.”

She just smirked. “Then we have a plan.” 

Haldric exhaled, resting his elbows on the table. They had a plan. He just hoped it wouldn’t get them killed.



***



Morning found Haldric back in the records hall, where everything carried on as usual: silent, orderly, stifling. He had just settled into the mindless rhythm of sorting when the door swung open.

A supervisor entered, his presence neither hurried nor casual, but with purpose. He made his way past the rows of clerks and stopped beside a mid-level officer—a man Haldric recognized but had never spoken to.

“You won’t be handling your task today,” the supervisor said, handing the officer a slip of parchment.

The clerk barely glanced at it. “What? Why?”

“It’s been reassigned.”

The clerk sighed, shaking his head. “To who?”

The supervisor’s eyes flicked toward Haldric.

“Haldric?” the clerk said, frowning slightly. “He’s too new for this.”

The supervisor’s voice remained steady. “It came from higher up.”

That ended the discussion. Without another word, the supervisor turned and walked away.

The clerk exhaled, stretching his neck.

“Fine. Guess it’s yours now,” the clerk muttered, pushing a stack of papers toward Haldric.



Haldric took them, still processing what just happened while breaking the wax seal. The parchment was thick, heavier than standard notices. Official. Haldric skimmed the text.

Then his fingers tightened around the edges.



RETIREMENT NOTICE

For: War Claw Bresdin, Mythical Beast Hunter



Retirement. Honorable discharge.



Not exile but grounded. The city didn’t want Bresdin to die out there. But he wasn’t allowed on the surface anymore. Effective immediately.

And the one assigned to make it official? Haldric himself. 

     Stone me.

He had to strip a legend of his active-duty sash. The weight of it settled in his stomach, cold and uneasy. He looked up—

The clerk, who had looked so irritated a moment ago, was grinning ear to ear. Now that the supervisor was gone, the clerk had no reason to feign disappointment. That Ratrod was faking the whole time!

Haldric said nothing. Just exhaled sharply and stood. He had a job to do.

And something told him… this wouldn’t go well.

He took the parchment, folding it carefully before slipping it inside his uniform. His fingers clenched slightly at the feel of it. Honorable discharge. A legend forced into retirement. This is just a formality, he told himself. I’m sure they’ve already briefed Bresdin. He is probably just waiting for the official notice.

Haldric sighed loudly and turned toward the exit.



The Hunters’ Office was technically just one Floor down from The Prime Archon’s Office, but getting there was another story entirely. The administrative sector and the Hunters’ sector didn’t connect directly.

By design.

To reach the Hunters’ Office, he had to exit into the outer ring, descend through the crowded lower tunnels, then cut back through the city’s winding pathways to reach the center again.

It was a long, inconvenient detour—a deliberate obstacle.

A reminder that The Prime Archon’s people and the Hunters were not meant to cross paths.

As he stepped into the streets, the air changed. The polished stone of the inner ring gave way to rougher, more uneven pathways. The slums, still waking from the night, pulsed with slow-moving bodies—workers heading to shifts, traders setting up in whatever spaces they could carve out.

Haldric moved quickly, efficiently, blending into the flow of traffic.

No one paid attention to a clerk.

By the time he reached the entrance to the Second Floor’s outer ring, the crowd had shifted. The atmosphere here was different from the quiet order of the administrative halls—it felt more alive, but also more dangerous.

No enforcers patrolled here. Not unless they wanted trouble.

Hunters policed themselves.

As he climbed the narrow, uneven steps, the stone walls seemed to close in, the passage roughly carved and less refined than the polished walkways above.

And then, at last, reaching the innermost ring of the Second Floor, Haldric stood before the entrance to the Hunter’s Office: a heavy iron door, slightly rusted around the edges, embedded deep into the cavern wall.

The weight of what lay beyond settled in his chest.

This was a bad idea.

The moment he stepped inside, they’d see the uniform. They’d know where he came from.

And they wouldn’t welcome him.

The iron door groaned as Haldric pushed it open, its weight cold against his fingers.

The air here was different—charged, heavier. Conversations dulled, movements slowed, and heads turned.



They had noticed him.

A clerk in a room full of Hunters.

Haldric kept his steps measured as he approached the central desk. The Hunters lounging nearby didn’t hide their stares. Some muttered under their breath, others openly watched.

The man at the front desk barely looked up. Older. Scarred. Unimpressed. His armor bore fresh claw marks—a Hunter still active in the field.

Haldric cleared his throat. “I need to speak with War Claw Bresdin.”

The man didn’t react, just kept flipping through a logbook. “He’s busy.”

Haldric’s fingers twitched. “It’s official business.”

A beat. Then, the man let out a slow exhale, finally lifting his gaze. Flat. Unimpressed.

“Official business, huh?” His lip curled. “From The Prime?”

The room stilled.

Haldric ignored the tension climbing up his spine. “Yes.”

The man started at Haldric, then called out over his shoulder. “Bresdin!”

Nothing.

He called again. “Oi, War Claw! You got a fan.”

Silence.

Haldric exhaled sharply. “I’ve been sent to conduct an Honorable Discharge.”

That got a reaction.

A few Hunters straightened. A handful exchanged glances.

This was a REALLY bad idea.

Near the back of the room, a figure slipped out the door. Quiet. Quick. Gone before anyone could react.

Bresdin.



Haldric’s stomach sank.

A low chuckle broke the silence. One of the Hunters—tall, wiry, jagged scars across his jaw -- grinned. “Did you just say ‘Honorable’?”

Another snorted. “You came to put War Claw Bresdin out to pasture? Stormhaven’s first Mythical Beast Hunter in the last two centuries?”

A third Hunter let out a sharp breath. “Is that a joke?”

Haldric’s jaw tightened. Here it comes.

The room shifted.

A few Hunters stood up. Slow, deliberate.

Haldric forced himself to stay still as three of them closed the distance, their expressions outright hostile.

The tall, scarred Hunter spoke first. “Tell me, clerk. Do you even know how a Beast Hunter earns the Mythical title?”

Haldric exhaled through his nose. “Of course. Who doesn’t? You have to injure a mythical beast solo, with at least three witnesses.”

A rough, older voice cut in. “Yet you want to retire the only man in this city’s history to not just injure, but kill one?”

The scarred one shook his head. “The bolting Prime thinks he can force a legend into retirement?” He leaned in slightly. Too close. “You think Bresdin’s gonna let that happen?”

Another Hunter scoffed. “You ever seen a real fight, boy? Ever watched your team get torn apart? Ever stood drenched in blood that ain’t yours?”

Haldric’s jaw tightened. “No.”

“Then don’t talk to us about ‘honor.’” The last word was spat.

A hand shoved Haldric’s shoulder. Not hard. But not gentle, either.

He stayed firm. Didn’t react. Didn’t flinch.



Another Hunter scoffed. “We answer to one thing, clerk.” His fingers tapped the handle of a long knife at his hip. “And it sure as stones ain’t some bolting Prime underling.”

He took a step towards Haldric, but Haldric didn’t move.

Another step. Then—a voice.

“Enough.”

The Hunters stopped, their attention snapping toward the front desk. The old Hunter behind it hadn’t moved. But his tone had changed. The scarred one held Haldric’s gaze a moment longer, then exhaled sharply. “Fine.” He rolled his shoulders. “Guess you get to leave in one piece.”

Haldric took that as his exit. He turned, keeping his steps measured, controlled. Never rushing. Never looking back. And the moment he stepped past the threshold and back into the tunnels, his breath finally left his lungs.

He didn’t go far. He ducked into the nearest side path, pressing himself against the stone wall, exhaling slowly. His heart was still racing. They hadn’t hurt him but they’d bolting wanted to.

He had been sent to take the sash from one of the only two Mythical Beast Hunters in Stormhaven. A true Hunter.

And that man?

Had slipped away the moment he heard. That wasn’t the reaction of someone accepting their fate. That was the reaction of someone who had already made their decision.

Haldric pushed off the wall, his feet already moving. There was only one place Bresdin could be going. And if Haldric was right—if Bresdin was planning what it looked like—then this wasn’t over. 

Not even close. Haldric could catch up before Bresdin reached the surface.

Haldric reached another set of gates—no, doors. Massive, looming structures of reinforced stone and iron. Their sheer size made him feel small. His breath came ragged, lungs burning from the unfamiliar air. Static prickled across his skin, his body still struggling to adapt. But he refused to show weakness.

“Give up, Bresdin!” Haldric called out.

Bresdin stood just ahead, waiting before the gates.

He stepped forward with a smirk. “Do you really think you’re in control, boy? Do you even know what’s behind this door? How close you are to the surface?”

Haldric’s fists clenched. He wasn’t in the mood for taunts.

“Why do you chase me? You want a fight?” Bresdin spread his arms wide, his voice thick with amusement. “Very well. If it’s a fight you want, it’s a fight I’ll give you.”

 He gave a subtle signal, and the door swung open.

The light struck Haldric like a hammer, searing through his skull. His vision shattered into a storm of burning white, blotting out everything. His breath hitched. He was blind.

His body tensed, but his mind reached for something steady: training.

Since childhood, he and Praew had drilled for moments like this. At first, it had been a game. A test of skill. Blindfolded sparring sessions that started with clumsy swipes and uncontrollable laughter. But as they got older, the laughter faded. The training became real. Because one day, they knew, they might have to fight blind—mud in their eyes, blood dripping down their face, the dirty tricks of a dishonorable Hunter.

He never thought he’d use it against one of his heroes.

The light from the surface was so bright, he could feel heat on his skin, the glare rendering his eyes useless. But that didn’t mean he was defenseless. He slowed his breathing, stretching his awareness outward. The tunnel was narrow, the space between them small. The air shifted—Bresdin was moving. His boots scraped against the stone, the slight shift of weight betraying his stance.

  Haldric heard the faintest rustle of armor. A punch cut through the air. He tilted his head just enough for it to graze past his cheek. Another step, another shift in weight—he twisted, ducking under a second blow. He could hear the faint rustle of armor, the drag of fabric against skin. Bresdin swung again, but Haldric sidestepped, each dodge fueling Bresdin’s aggravation. This Hunter, this so-called legend, had resorted to blinding a young, unarmed man, but it wasn’t working.

  His attacks grew sharper, faster, but they weren’t landing. Haldric could hear his growing frustration.

  “You think you can dance around forever, boy?” Bresdin’s voice held a growl now.

  Haldric didn’t answer. Words wouldn’t help him here.

  Then, the sound he had been dreading: a long, deliberate scrape of metal leaving its sheath.

  A weapon.

     His heart kicked against his ribs. This was different. He could dodge fists, predict movements. But a blade? That was an entirely different kind of fight. Too many possibilities. Too many ways for this to end badly.

  The whoosh of steel came fast.

  Haldric twisted on instinct, feeling the air part where the blade should have cut him in two. He barely had time to reset before it came again, each swing faster, more precise. Bresdin wasn’t slashing wildly—he was testing him, seeing how long he could hold out. The air burned in his lungs, his vision still nothing but white light, but he gritted his teeth and focused.

  Another shift. The blade lifted. A downward swing.

  Haldric dropped low.

  And then, the unmistakable sound of metal colliding with stone. A loud, sharp clang.

  Bresdin’s breath hitched. A hesitation.

  Then, a scream.

  “My hand! Arthur! You cut off my bolting hand!”

  Haldric braced for another attack—only, none came. The air shifted. Boots shuffled.

     The Prime Archon’s voice cut through the tension, smooth and laced with disdain. “Come now, War Claw, did you truly think your dealings with the rebels would go unnoticed?” He gave a quiet chuckle. “You’re late, Bresdin. We’ve been expecting you.”

  “Take him away,” The Prime Archon ordered, and Haldric could hear the shuffling of feet as guards moved in.

  “Prime Archon? Sir?” Haldric called out, still blinded.

  “Yes, son, I’m here,” The Prime answered, his voice unusually soft. “Are you hurt anywhere?”

  Haldric felt hands on his shoulders—steady, firm, checking him over. Not like a superior evaluating an officer. Like a father making sure his son was still in one piece. It was quick, efficient, but there was something else beneath the movement. Tension. Relief.

  “I’m okay,” Haldric managed.

  The Prime Archon’s grip lingered for a second too long. Then, just as quickly as it had come, the moment snapped shut.

  “Of course you are.” His tone had changed—back to the sharp, composed authority of Arthur Rivers. A controlled breath. “That was impressive, how you managed to dodge while blind. I didn’t know you could fight like that.” 

He hesitated, then added with quiet satisfaction, “The Hunter administration was foolish to fail someone like you.”

  Haldric was still trying to process everything. His thoughts blurred together, but one rose to the surface.

  “How long have you been here?” he asked The Prime.

  “For quite some time,” The Prime Archon said smoothly. “I knew Bresdin might attempt to flee.”

  Haldric, with a hint of accusation, asked, “Why didn’t you just have the guards block the first gate?”

  The Prime Archon explained, “For the guards’ safety. They can’t stop a Hunter. If a group of them came, it could start a war. It’s better to handle it in an area with no guards around, nowhere for him to escape.”

  Haldric hesitated before responding, “I suppose that makes sense, sir.”

  The Prime Archon nudged him. “I’m sure you still can’t see, but I believe your audience wishes to speak with you.”

  Haldric blinked in surprise. “Audience?”

  The Prime Archon smiled. “It seems quite a few Hunters followed you from the Headquarters. They watched your fight.”

  Haldric’s surprise grew. He hadn’t heard them at all. The realization revealed just how focused he’d been on chasing down Bresdin. He had thought he was chasing prey, but all along, a group of Hunters had been tracking him.



***

Arthur Rivers watched with a keen eye as the Hunters surrounded Haldric, bombarding him with questions. “How did you do that? Someone from The Prime’s office fighting a Mythical Beast Hunter while unarmed and blind? Have you been acclimated to the surface before?”

  Their admiration was palpable, and Arthur felt a surge of satisfaction. This young man could be the key to bridging the gap between his administration and the Hunters.

  Arthur observed how the Hunters shook Haldric’s hand, their awe evident. He recognized the potential of this moment. Haldric’s performance today had shown not just skill but also resilience and adaptability, qualities Arthur highly valued.

  Standing beside Haldric, Arthur felt a sense of excitement, tinged with disgust. This young man could be the bridge he needed. The bridge that Asher was supposed to be.

  This must be the reason they insisted I reassign the task to Haldric. Just how far ahead can they predict? …How unsettling.













Chapter 11 - Fired



"Once again, I stand as the final wall between two opposing ideals."



The first thing Haldric noticed was the air—crisper, richer, almost electric in his lungs. He took a slow breath, deeper than usual, and the difference was immediate. His chest didn’t burn anymore. His muscles no longer ached. 

He blinked, adjusting to the dim lighting.

He was in a bed—stone-carved, embedded into the wall like all Hunter bunks. The room smelled of rock-dust and sweat, the unmistakable scent of those who lived their lives in battle. His vision was clearer than it had ever been, sharper.

The blinding pain from the surface light was gone. He could see perfectly again. And standing just a few paces away, waiting patiently, was The Prime Archon.

Haldric sat up immediately. “Sir?”

The Prime Archon seemed troubled at first. Then his lips curled into a smile. “Ahh, you’re awake!”

Haldric swung his legs over the edge of the bed, straightening his posture. “How long was I out?”

“Not for terribly long. Your body was in dire need of recuperation.” The Prime Archon folded his hands behind his back. “Shall we take a walk?”

Haldric hesitated for only a second. “Of course, sir.”

He stood, testing his weight. His balance felt different. His steps were lighter. His strength more… present. As if his entire body had been recalibrated overnight.

The Prime Archon watched Haldric with a smirk.

“What’s so funny?” Haldric asked.

The Prime turned away, as if to hide his amusement. “Come,” he said, nodding toward the hall. “Let’s talk.”



The tunnels were quieter than usual at this hour. The faint hum of distant movement echoed through the stone, but for the most part, it was just the two of them.

The Prime Archon led him through the winding pathways, slow and deliberate. “Tell me, Haldric. How do you feel?”

Haldric considered. “Different.”

The Prime Archon hummed. “Good. That’s how it should be.”

He gestured around them, as if motioning to the walls, the stone, the very air itself.

“Your body has acclimated. Your lungs have expanded. Your muscles are more efficient. Your stamina, speed, and strength are all enhanced. Every Hunter experiences this after spending time near the surface. It’s an evolution—a natural mutation.”

Haldric frowned slightly. “Mutation?”

The Prime Archon nodded. “A refinement. A necessary adaptation. The surface is harsh, but in return, it strengthens us. Hunters—true Hunters—are not the same as other people. They aren’t just trained for this life, Haldric. They’re built for it.”

Something shifted in The Prime’s voice. His usual stoicism softened into a kind smile—one Haldric hadn’t seen in twelve years. Not since he was a child playing with Asher. But that was before Mr. Rivers became The Prime Archon.

“When I acclimated, the hardest part was getting used to my new strength,” The Prime said with a chuckle. “You don’t want to know how many doors and tables I accidentally destroyed.”

Haldric stiffened slightly.

He had never questioned The Prime Archon’s strength before. He was feared, respected, but in an administrative sense, in his control, his tactics, his mind. But now, standing beside him, hearing the weight behind his words, Haldric was seeing him in an entirely different way. The Prime Archon was enhanced. Haldric exhaled, steadying himself. He wasn’t sure what to say to that, but he had the distinct feeling that today would change everything.

At first, neither of them spoke. Then, The Prime Archon finally broke the silence. “How have you been, Haldric? Since failing the test?”

Haldric blinked. That was unexpected. No one had asked him that before. He let out a slow breath, thinking.

“If I’m being honest, sir… I hated Asher. And for some reason, I hated you too.” He sighed. “But then I realized, if Asher didn’t betray me, neither of us would have succeeded. And you know what? I’m glad one of us made it.”

The words surprised even him. Saying it out loud felt… final.

The Prime Archon hummed, nodding. Then, quietly:

“No, you were right to hate me.”

Haldric frowned, glancing at him.

The Prime Archon’s voice was regretful. “It’s my fault after all.”

He kept walking, slower now.

“I don’t know where I went wrong with Asher. Maybe I pushed him too hard. Maybe I shouldn’t have tried to make him take after me. After all, who in their right mind would want to be the most hated man in the city?”

A dry chuckle, self-deprecating. Then his voice softened.

 “But he had everything, Haldric. He was honest. Good. Caring. Exactly what the city needs. And instead of rising to that, he betrayed his closest friends and ran off to be a Hunter.”

  He exhaled. “I’m sorry, Haldric. It should have been you.”

  Haldric gasped. He should have been a Hunter. That wasn’t something he had ever expected to hear from The Prime Archon. His fingers curled at his sides, but before he could even begin to process what that meant, The Prime Archon stopped walking, turned to him, and met his eyes.

“I guess what I’m trying to tell you is… you’re fired.”

Silence followed. The words didn’t make sense at first. Fired? …What?

When it clicked, Haldric snapped. “I’M BOLTING WHAT?”

The Prime only smiled. And in that moment, Haldric realized, it was a joke.

It WAS a joke… Right? Panic crept up His spine.

The Prime raised his hand, fingers flicking in a subtle but deliberate gesture.

A low rumble shook the cavern.

Haldric’s breath hitched as the groan of shifting stone filled the air. The final gate was opening.

Wind rushed in, crisp and unfiltered, carrying a scent so new it sent a shiver down his spine. The air felt different. Lighter, freer, charged with something alive.

The Prime’s voice cut through the hum of the wind. Calm. Absolute.

“You can’t go back to your old life, Haldric. Your days in administration are over.”

Haldric barely processed the words.

His eyes were locked forward as the gate continued to part, unveiling the last barrier between him and everything he’d ever wanted.

Footsteps echoed behind him. Someone was approaching.

The man was tall, broad-shouldered, his presence heavy with an unspoken authority. His gaze flicked over Haldric, sharp and assessing. Familiar.

The Hunter from the exam.

Haldric felt a single short-lived spike of hatred. He hadn’t seen this Hunter since the day he failed.

The Prime motioned toward the man. “I believe you’ve met Hunter Steel.”

Steel.

A name to match the man.

He gave Haldric a slight emotionless nod.

The Prime Archon’s voice was commanding again. “You have two choices.”

Haldric’s fingers curled into fists.

“You can begin your adventure as Hunter Steel’s apprentice. Or…” The Prime Archon turned fully toward him, eyes sharp. “You can become mine.”

Haldric barely heard him.

His gaze had locked onto something else entirely.

The surface.

The world stretched before him—wild, untamed, waiting.

A vast sky unfolded, a masterpiece of color unlike anything he had ever seen. Purple and red hues melted together as a giant SolarStone peeked over the horizon, its light refracting through stormy clouds. Swirls of deep blue churned in the distance, lightning crackling within.

And the land—it was nothing like the stories.

Less green than he had expected. Less… alive.

The trees stood hunched, their trunks an unnatural gray, nothing like the brown depicted in books. Thick vines coiled around them like veins, stretching toward the light. The dirt itself seemed to shift ever so slightly, as if it breathed alongside the wind.

It was beautiful. It was dangerous. It was an adventure calling.

But it would have to call for someone else.

He turned away.

“I’m no Hunter,” Haldric muttered. “What’s next, sir?”

The walk back down to the Administration Office was filled with awkward, nerve-racking silence. Why does The Prime look so troubled? Did… he want me to go? 

Haldric was lost in his thoughts. Did I make the wrong choice? It was right there—freedom. So why did it feel so… wrong? 

Like if he had taken it, he would regret it for the rest of his life.

He didn’t even realize when they arrived. The Administrative Office was silent—not the usual silence of routine work or quiet tension, but something else entirely. He snapped out of his thoughts to find every worker in the office staring at him.

Dozens of officials and office workers stood frozen, mid-task, mid-conversation, mid-motion. Waiting. Watching. They must be shocked to see him entering with The Prime.

Haldric kept his posture straight, stepping forward with The Prime Archon beside him. He didn’t speak. Neither did The Prime. The sound of their boots on stone was the only thing filling the space until The Prime finally stopped.

“Go to your desk and grab your things,” he said.

Ah, right. I was fired today, wasn’t I? They couldn’t have an acclimated sorting paper. Haldric’s exciting adventure hadn’t come to an end just yet—but what now? Guard duty? That made the most sense.

He gathered his things and returned to The Prime, waiting for direction.

The Archon’s voice rang out, firm and absolute. “Effective immediately, Haldric is no longer a member of Administration. He is my chosen successor.” The Prime Archon straightened as he raised his voice “The Heir Archon has been selected.”

No one moved. For a moment, the silence held. Then came a sharp slam. It took Haldric a few breaths to realize the sound had come from him. He’d dropped his supplies in shock.

Did The Prime just announce me as… as his Heir?

His mouth hung open. He shut it quickly, steeling himself. The stack of books and booklets remained perfectly upright, undisturbed. He placed one foot on top of it and leaned forward, resting an arm on his thigh in a majestic pose, as if this had all been on purpose.

Hopefully no one noticed his leg shaking uncontrollably.

Then a voice—hushed, uncertain.

“Sir… is it true? What they’re saying about him?”

Haldric’s pulse thrummed as he turned toward the speaker—a senior administrator near the front of the gathered staff. What about me? What did I do? It couldn’t be about what happened yesterday, could it? Only Hunters and soldiers were there, and it’s not like a Hunter would ever want news like that to spread. 

But there was something in the clerk’s eyes. Something in all their eyes.

The Prime Archon took his time. He glanced at Haldric once before turning back to the room, then gave a small, knowing smile.

“Yes. The Heir Archon defeated an armed War Claw—Bresdin, Mythical Beast Hunter—in a duel. Blindfolded and unarmed.”

A breath—

A pause—

Then the room erupted. Officials and clerks alike shot to their feet, cheering and stomping. Applause rang out, hands striking stone and cloth, maybe because someone had finally stood up to the Hunters.

Some saluted immediately, their motions crisp and full of approval, perhaps because the Heir had finally been announced.

A whistle pierced the air from the back of the room.

Haldric barely had time to react before the crowd surged toward him. Hands clapped his back, shook his shoulders, gripped his arms. Someone ruffled his hair—he wasn’t sure who. A younger official grinned wide. “About time one of ours made history.”

History.

That’s what he was now, wasn’t he?

A story. A legend.

And he loved it.

He squared his shoulders, lifted his chin. He was exactly where he was meant to be.

The Prime Archon let them celebrate. He stood off to the side, watching, letting the energy settle before speaking again. His voice cut through the noise—firm, yet pleased.

“Then you should have no doubt why I’ve chosen him.”

The cheers didn’t stop. If anything, they grew louder.

And for the first time in his life, Haldric felt something he never thought he would in this building: belonging.

After shaking hands with everyone, The Prime signaled for the Heir to follow him.



***



When the door to the office shut behind them, it muffled the celebration surprisingly well. For a moment, Haldric let himself enjoy the quiet. The stillness was a welcome contrast to the chaos outside.

The Prime moved behind his desk and paused as his hand hovered over a drawer as if weighing a decision. His shoulders lowered, not with weariness, but with a kind of resolve Haldric had never seen on him in public. He opened a drawer and drew out two objects, setting them on the polished desk with a deliberate care that made Haldric straighten instinctively.

A small leather-bound book, its corners worn soft with handling. And beside it, a key unlike any Haldric had ever seen. It bore stone teeth and a gray wooden handle. Scales were etched into the handle, forming a repeating pattern accompanied by a bolt and a set of fangs.

Haldric’s mouth went dry. “Sir?”

The Prime rested a hand on the book first. “This belonged to the Archon before me. Notes, philosophies, warnings, mistakes. A record not of rulership, but of burden.” His fingers tapped the worn cover. “You will add your own pages when the time comes.”

He slid the book a little closer to Haldric. “Do not take these words as law, Haldric. Let this be a guide. Learn from what came before you. An Archon who ignores the past does not spare his Heir from failure; he condemns him to inherit it.”

Haldric hesitated before reaching out. The leather was warm from The Prime’s hand. He slid his fingers beneath it to lift, but his hand shook, and the book hardly moved. It felt heavier than it should have, as if the weight came from what was written inside it rather than the book itself. He let it settle back onto the desk.

Then The Prime lifted the key.

“And this,” he continued, “is the Master Key.” He turned it once between his fingers, the faint lines within it catching the light of the chamber. “It opens every lock in Stormhaven. Gates, archives, vaults, homes. If anything in this city has ever been denied to you, this key can grant it.”

The weight of it settled into Haldric’s palm when The Prime placed it there. It was colder than it looked. Colder than it should have been. His fingers closed around it on instinct, as if afraid it might fall straight through his hand and crack the floor.

“You will not use it lightly,” The Prime said. “You will carry it always. As proof of your station as well as a reminder of the responsibilities it bears.”

Haldric’s throat tightened. He nodded, though he was no longer sure what he was agreeing to.

The Prime reached into the drawer once more and produced a thin cord. With a simple motion, he threaded it through the hollowed center of the key and stepped closer.

“May I?”

Haldric dipped his head. The cord brushed the back of his neck as The Prime looped it around and tied it off. The key dropped against his chest with a solid, unforgiving thud.

“With this key,” The Prime said, his voice barely above a murmur, “the city can no longer question your judgement. However, if Stormhaven falls, it will fall on the shoulders of its Archon.” The Prime then lifted the book from the desk and held it between them. “With this book, swear that you will always question your own judgement and only act with Stormhaven’s best interest in mind.”

“I… I swear,” Haldric answered, the words leaving him before he realized he’d said them.

The Prime placed the book into his hands. It was still heavy, unbelievably so. He could already feel the spine digging into his palm. Into his grip. Into his life. And yet it was lighter than it had been earlier. Haldric was a little more confident he had the strength to carry this burden.

He slipped the book into his pocket. The spine pressed against his thigh, while the chill of the key brushed his chest as it swayed. Every movement reminding him of their presence.

“Thank you, sir,” he managed.

The Prime searched his face for a moment. As if sensing Haldric’s new resolve, he nodded, then walked off, tracing his hand against the far wall until—

Click.

A deep, rumbling shift of stone filled the space.

Haldric’s breath hitched as a massive section of the wall slid open, revealing something hidden behind it.

A mural.

Not just any mural—this one felt ancient, older than the city itself, and more beautiful than even the surface. The figures were breathtaking, all inlaid with stunning precision. Thousands of tiny gemstones had been set directly into the stone, the colors shimmering beneath the chamber’s light, giving the mural an almost living quality.

It didn’t just tell a story. It breathed one. 

A figure at the center—a woman, radiant and full of light. Her form was distorted, her stomach round like an expectant mother.

Surrounding her were shards of something broken—something powerful.

The Prime Archon stood back, letting Haldric take it in.

Then his voice broke the silence.

“Do you believe in God, Haldric?”

Haldric didn’t look away from the mural. “No. I believe in what I see. And I don’t see Zephyr making our lives any better. No matter how much we pray, we struggle to survive.”

The Prime hummed. “Well spoken. I didn’t either—not until the night I became The Prime and was shown an ancient city abandoned by history… among other things. It was filled with murals like this one.”

He stepped forward, fingers gliding across the mural’s surface as if tracing a memory etched in stone.

“I heard it took years,” he said, voice low with reverence. “But the Zephyrian Acolytes managed to decipher the ancient language and translate the inscriptions. Would you like to hear what this one says, Heir? It is your right.”

Haldric didn’t answer. His gaze was already being pulled—past The Prime, toward a section of the mural he hadn’t noticed before.

He stepped closer.

A single figure stood apart from the others, tall and still, his gaze seeming to meet Haldric’s. There was no mistaking it—those eyes weren’t part of the stone anymore. They were looking straight at him.

“Hal,” the mural seemed to whisper.

That had to be his imagination… right? 

The figure wore a tunic woven from what looked like living roots and hanging moss. His long brown hair draped over his shoulders, and in one hand, he held a staff glowing faintly with amber light. Perched on his shoulder was a small, four-legged beast, draped in—

“Heir?”

The Prime’s voice pulled him back. Only then did Haldric realize how close he’d gotten to the wall.

He stepped back, turning to The Prime, pulse still racing. “Sorry. It just… pulled me in. Who is this guy supposed to be, anyway? Zephyrs?”

“Who, exactly, are you referring to?” The Prime asked.

“The guy right—” Haldric looked back at the mural of the Deity , only to find… it was no longer there. 

Praew would say he’s crazy.

“Nothing. Never mind,” he said, recovering quickly. “Just a trick of the light. Anyway, what does the inscription say?”

The Prime picked up a thick leather-bound book that had been hidden beneath the mural. After flipping through a few pages, he began to read aloud.

And Haldric listened.

“In the first days, when the heavens were young and the stones yet gleamed with the breath of divinity, there was but one who watched over mankind.”

Even translated, the words felt ancient and foreign to Haldric—the cadence of The Prime’s voice steeped in archaic speech, each syllable drifting past him like a language meant for someone else.

“She had no name, for none were needed. She was only known as Mother.

“Boundless was her love, unshaken her mercy. If her children hungered, she filled their tables. If they thirsted, she broke the clouds and wept rain upon the fields. If they shivered in the dark, she bid the sun to rise and hold them in its warmth.

“Yet power begets envy. And envy begets ruin.

“For the other creatures of the world had no Mother, no voice to answer their cries. They saw the gifts mankind had been given, and in their hearts, a question took root.

“Why them? Why not us?

“Their whispers turned to jealousy. Jealousy to anger. And anger to war.

“And so it was that ten great species raised claw and fang, stone and fire, against she who had given mankind life.

“They struck her low. Tore her open.

“Her womb—the cradle of creation—was sundered, its power spilled like blood upon the ground.

“And from its ruin, the great Deformite was shattered. Scattered. Its pieces carried by thieves and traitors alike. 

“For it is known: to wield two Heavenly Deformites is to stand among the True Gods.

“Thus, the great hunt began.

“And among the Hunters, there was one unlike the rest.

“A creature of shadows and silk, silent as breath, with eyes that gleamed in the dark.

“Sly. Greedy. Cunning.

“With quick hands and quicker wit, she slunk through the chaos, her steps softer than whispers. She needed no claws, no weapons—only patience, only guile.

“And in the dead of night, when war had left the world in silence, she stole away two Heavily Deformites, cradling them like baubles.

“And with them, she wove a world of her own.

“But folly is the sister of pride, and in her arrogance, she sought not only to shape a realm—but to fill it.

“To prove herself greater than Mother.

“She sought to create.

“But she could not.

“She had no gift of breath, no power of birth.

“And so, she stole.

“A city. A people.

“She ripped them from their home and cast them into her hollow world, watching as they floundered in the storms of her making.

“She did not guide them. She did not shield them.

“She left them to toil in a land never meant for them, to survive where survival was not promised.

“She was careless. Reckless.

“She abandoned them.

“And it is by her whim, her folly, her failure, that mankind now suffers.

“She was no Mother.

“Only a thief.”

The air was thick with dust and time, the scent of old stone settling deep in his lungs. Haldric stepped forward, once again drawn to the mural. The symbols carved into the rock seemed to hum with something ancient—something weighty.

His eyes traced the etched figures, the script of a long-dead tongue. Sharp lines. Curving strokes. A language he could not read, yet somehow felt.

His fingers hovered just over the stone, not quite touching, but close. Close enough to sense the weight of history pressing against his skin.

Behind him, The Prime Archon’s voice was steady. “That was but one fragment of a truth long buried… the true history of our people.”

Haldric nodded slightly. He had heard the story.

The Mother, the ten who tore her open. The thief who stole two Heavenly Deformites and wove a world of her own.

His gaze moved to the etched figures, lingering on the thief—the one who had cast them into this world.

The figure’s eyes had been carved with exacting precision, slanted just slightly, giving the illusion of something clever and unbothered.

“Do you have any questions from what I have read so far?”

The room held its silence. The weight of The Prime’s words settled into the stone, into the air, into Haldric’s skin.

Then—

“Hah. Well, that’s quite the dramatic retelling.”

Haldric flinched. The words … didn’t come from The Prime. The voice was too melodic and feminine.

“What’s next?” she continued. “Are you going to accuse her of devouring the sun every night as well?”

When had someone snuck in? Where were they?

His breath caught as he scanned the room.

There!

Leaning against the far wall, arms crossed, weight settled lazily into her hip—like she had always been there.

His mind reeled. Who—?

His gut twisted. How—?

She didn’t belong. That much was obvious.

Not in the way a stranger shouldn’t be here, but in the way she looked too clean.

The underground left its mark on everyone. The damp air clung to skin. Dust settled in hair, on clothes. Even The Prime Archon, meticulous as he was, bore the faint signs of someone who lived in stone corridors.

But her?

Nothing.

No dust on her sleeves. No dampness weighing down her hair. Not a smudge of grit on her skin.

Her hair was pale—not faded, not silver, but the kind of white that never touched grime. It should have looked dull in the dim torchlight, but somehow, it didn’t. Somehow, it caught the flickering glow just enough to seem like mist curling at the edges.

Her skin was smooth, too smooth, untouched by the roughness of this world. Like polished stone shaped by wind and time, yet never broken.

Her eyes—sharp, too sharp. There was an intelligence behind them, something calculating. Like she wasn’t just looking at him, but through him.

Even the way she stood was wrong.

Relaxed, unconcerned. Like the walls around her weren’t closing in with silence. Like she had all the time in the world.

Then, as if sensing his stare, she smirked.

She flicked her gaze toward The Prime, the corner of her lips twitching in an almost-smile.

“Tell me, did you stretch the truth on purpose, or is this just the best your little translators could come up with?”

Haldric’s eyes snapped to The Prime Archon, expecting a reaction.

But The Prime didn’t move.

Didn’t flinch. Didn’t shift, he just stared at Haldric expectantly.

He didn’t so much as acknowledge her.

Like she wasn’t there.

Haldric swallowed, throat suddenly dry.

He turned back to the woman. “Who—”

She cut him off with a flick of her fingers.

“No, no, let me guess.” She tilted her head, her expression caught somewhere between amusement and boredom. “You’re going to ask who I am, how I got in here, what I want. Blah, blah. But really, aren’t those such boring questions, Hal?”

Haldric clenched his jaw.

Everything about her was wrong but not in a way he could name.

She let out a short laugh, shaking her head.

“I’ll admit,” she mused, flicking a glance back at the mural, “I didn’t think anyone was still telling this story.” She smirked. “But since we’re all sharing stories today…” She gestured lazily toward the mural. “Tell me, child. Do you believe the story?”

Haldric tried to swallow, but his mouth was too dry. Did he believe it? He wanted to, but did he, really? When he finally found the courage, his voice came quieter than expected. “I… I don’t know.”

The woman nodded. “Looks like there’s hope for you little rodents yet.” 

Then, without another word, she strode toward The Prime’s door and—

…she—

Did she just walk through it? Gone.

No sound of footsteps. No lingering presence. The door hadn’t even moved. Haldric stared. The air felt different, wrong in a way he couldn’t place.

The Prime Archon sighed audibly. “I think this will be a good stopping point for the day.”

Haldric blinked, barely processing the words. A stopping point? That’s it?

Did The Prime really see nothing? Could he truly not hear her, even as she openly scorned him? What… what is happening to me? First that Nature Deity vanished from the wall, and now this?

The Prime turned away, but the sigh he left behind clung to Haldric’s thoughts like dust in the air.

Did he seem… disappointed?

The thought sat uneasily in Haldric’s chest. 

What was the last thing The Prime said before she appeared? He asked if I had any questions about what he’d read… right? And if he couldn’t see or hear the girl, then my reply was…

“Who—” …and later… “I don’t know.”

Stone me, he thinks I wasn’t paying attention at all!

Before Haldric could reassure The Prime, before he could even try to seem more engaged, the man spoke first.

“You’ll need time to digest what you’ve heard.”  Then with a sharper tone, The Prime continued “This goes without saying, but this isn’t information you can share. No friends. No family.”

His gaze settled on Haldric. “This must stay between us.”

Haldric nodded automatically. “I understand.”

The Prime turned away. Taking it as a dismissal, Haldric stepped out of the office, and the door clicked shut behind him.

And only then, alone in the quiet, did the weight of it all finally settle.

Haldric barely remembered the walk back to his home. His mind churned with everything: The Prime’s words, the mural, the woman who shouldn’t exist.

His Grayparents were waiting. Worry lined their faces, but before they could ask, he cut them off with a tired smile.

“I’m alright.”

They didn’t seem convinced, but they let it be. Wintrid muttered something going to bed, while Cenrick gave his shoulder a squeeze—wordless, but firm. 

Haldric exhaled, stepping into his room.

He was ready to collapse, sleep early for once. The last few days had been… too much.

Then, the knock.

Haldric groaned. He didn’t even need to guess.

When he opened the door, Praew grinned up at him.

“I’m honored!” she gasped dramatically, clutching her chest like she’d been blessed by a deity . “I get to spend time with such a famous person!”

Haldric pinched the bridge of his nose. “Alright, alright, that’s enough.”

But she wasn’t done. “You’re all anyone’s talking about! An unarmed administrator from The Prime’s office took on a Mythical Beast Hunter while blindfolded. In a sword fight. And won.” She laughed, shaking her head. “I do want to know how exactly you cut off his hand without a weapon, though.”

She raised a brow. “What did you do? Skip our meeting last night to run around spreading false rumors about yourself?”

Haldric sighed. The truth was… worse.

With the Grayparents in bed, Haldric and Praew sat at the living room table as he recounted the last two days. Praew listened, and the more he spoke, the wider her eyes got.

By the time he finished, she was staring. Jaw slack.

Then—

“ARE YOU INSANE?!”

Haldric winced. “Praew—”

“You did WHAT?!”

She looked ready to shake him, like somehow that would make the last two days sound less ridiculous.

He’d kept certain details to himself—the blinding sun, the mural, that woman. No one acclimated was allowed to talk about the first light. No one spoke about the strength they gained.

And the mural? That woman? There was no point.

Praew exhaled, rubbing her temple. “Wow.”

Then, more slowly, “So… you’re really going to be the next Prime Archon?”

She paused, then grinned. “This is good! This is really good!” She smacked his arm. “Now we can finally see what The Prime’s really up to!”

Haldric began tapping the table. “He’s not the enemy.”

She scoffed. “You don’t know that. He’s too suspicious—”

The Prime had saved him. Had believed in him. Had given him everything he lost.

“HE IS NOT AN ENEMY, PRAEW!”

The words burst out of him, louder, harsher than intended.

Startled, Praew jumped out of her seat into a defensive stance Haldric didn’t recognize.

Wow. Someone’s jumpy, he thought—until he looked down. The side of his fist was still pressed to the stone table—or what was left of it. The surface had caved under his touch, shattered into jagged pieces.

Praew took a half-step back. “Whoa. Okay. Relax.” She eyed the broken stone, then him. “How’d you even do that?”

Haldric swallowed. His pulse pounded. Ah, right. I’m a bolting idiot. Now that I’ve acclimated, I’ve gotta get better at controlling my strength. “It was already cracked.”

Too quick. Too defensive.

Praew raised a brow. She didn’t believe that, but she let it go. For now. After a beat, Praew spoke again. “That brings me to something else.”

Haldric frowned. “What now?”

She leaned in. “I made contact with someone. A group of people, actually. Worried citizens who think something’s wrong with the city.”

Haldric rubbed his temple.

“And their leader?” she continued. “His name is Note.”

Haldric blinked, then frowned. “Note?” He crossed his arms. “That’s the most suspicious name I’ve ever heard.”

“I know,” she admitted. “But they’re the only people actually talking about this.”

Haldric’s fingers curled against his leg. Something about this didn’t sit right. “You’re seriously trusting a guy who calls himself ‘Note’?” His voice was flat.

Praew shrugged. “It’s just a name.”

“It’s a fake name,” Haldric countered. “Which means he’s hiding something.”

She rolled her eyes. “Or it just means he doesn’t want enforcers tracking him down for asking questions.”

Haldric wasn’t buying it. “And what exactly is he asking questions about?”

Praew’s smirk faltered slightly. “The missing food. The city’s leadership. The people who’ve been disappearing.”

Haldric’s jaw tightened. “Sounds a lot like someone looking for trouble.”

She scoffed. “Or someone who actually gives a stone about this city.”

He couldn’t argue that, but it didn’t make him feel any better. “Tell me you didn’t agree to anything.”

Praew hesitated. “I… agreed to meet with them tomorrow night.”

Haldric groaned, dragging a hand down his face. “Of course you did.”

She frowned, arms crossing. “And what am I supposed to do, Haldric? Just forget about my missing friends? Pretend like I…” she hesitated, voice shaking, “like I didn’t fail them?”

Why didn’t she understand? He felt bad for her, sure, but people who asked too many questions didn’t last long in Stormhaven. Either they disappeared… or they were baiting someone else to.

Stone me. Haldric sighed, pushing past his frustration. “Okay I’m sorry, but I need to meet this guy first. Before you get too involved, before you start making promises and put yourself at risk. Understood?”

She sighed but nodded. “Alright, fine. You can meet him.”

Haldric wasn’t convinced. No matter what Praew thought, he’d be watching this ‘Note’ guy carefully.

Because even the best Hunters forget—sometimes, the real predator isn’t the one leading the prey.

It’s the one prowling just behind.

Waiting to strike.











Chapter 12 - Trigger Identification



"Some may call my death an unnecessary sacrifice—"



The slums of the Third Floor outer ring were always damp, always dim, and always humming with an undercurrent of quiet desperation. The SolarStones overhead flickered, their uneven glow fading in and out of darkness. Every inch of space was crammed with makeshift stalls, piles of salvaged goods, and the hunched figures of unauthorized traders who knew better than to call attention to themselves.

Where was she? Haldric scanned the depressing street. This was the only part of the city where a black cloak that hid your face wouldn’t draw suspicion. People would just assume it was a trick of the dim light.

It’s not like Praew to be late.

They’d agreed to meet right here at the seventeenth flash—just one before curfew. As the Heir Archon, Haldric was curfew-exempt, but this Note person was way too suspicious. Meeting him past curfew would only draw the attention of the curfew patrol, even with clearance. But meeting too early could attract even more eyes. Someone was bound to recognize the second-most powerful person in the city.

Most traders and beggars were packing up, but a few still lingered.

  Haldric’s nose curled at the sour scent of sweat and unwashed bodies that wafted toward him.

  And waiting for them in this filth was Note.

  Haldric spotted him immediately. It wasn’t hard.

  Never in his life had he wanted to punch someone more. Not Renwick after what he said to Praew. Not even Asher after his betrayal.

  What annoyed him—no, what infuriated him—was how absurd this guy looked.

  Does he even know what discreet means? Even The Prime would stand out less than this freak.

  Note looked like he belonged in the statue garden on the First Floor. He stood with effortless stillness, arms crossed over a chest so broad it made Haldric’s own shoulders feel inadequate. His dark skin was streaked with soot, but his posture remained perfect, his presence unwavering. A shadow of a perfectly trimmed beard framed his sharp jawline, and those piercing light blue eyes burned with a confidence that made Haldric’s stomach twist.

     And then there was the muscle.

 Veins coiled over his arms like tree roots, his biceps bulging beneath a torn, sweat-streaked shirt. His legs were massive—grotesquely overgrown. It was comical, really.

 No one actually liked that much muscle.

 Right?

 Haldric exhaled sharply through his nose.

 And then he saw it.

 Just the tiniest flicker of movement beside him.

 He flinched as Praew stepped up, eyes scanning the street.

               Stone me, how did she—? I didn’t hear her at all!

 When Note approached, Praew’s mouth dropped open.

  A yawn. That’s all. Just a yawn. Not admiration. Not attraction.

  Just exhaustion.

  Right?

  Right?!

  Haldric squared his shoulders, forcing his expression into something neutral. This fraud was going down.

  “So, you’re Note.” His tone was as flat as the damp stone beneath their feet.

       The man smirked. Like he had already won something. “And you’re The Prime’s new pet.”

Haldric stiffened. His fingers twitched at his sides. You win this round… fraud.

Praew, oblivious or choosing to ignore the tension, stepped forward. “You’re the one asking questions about the city.”

Note’s smirk remained, but his gaze turned serious. “I ask nothing but for people to question more.”

Haldric scoffed. “Oh, well. Thank the Stones, then. A poet is here to save us all.”

Note ignored him. His gaze stayed on Praew. “You’ve lived on the Second Floor, sheltered from the third your whole life, haven’t you?”

“Yes,” she lied. She always kept that part of her life quiet. Haldric only knew because their families were close. She wasn’t about to tell some grotesque stranger, no matter how much his muscles rippled.

Note nodded, like he had already expected the answer. His eyes flicked toward Haldric. “And you?”

Haldric stifled a grin, pleased she was thinking cautiously. “Same.”

Note sized him up and down, then glanced away. “I doubt that.” He exhaled, shaking his head. “No matter, neither of you know how this city really works.”

Haldric bristled. “I know enough.”

     “I doubt that as well.” Note’s voice cut through the noise of the slums, low and steady. Not mocking but certain.

     And somehow, that was worse.

    Something in the weight of those words made Haldric’s skin crawl.

    For the first time, he wasn’t sure if he wanted to argue.

    Note’s piercing blue eyes swept over the market, then flicked toward a narrow alley. “Not here,” he said. “Too many ears.”

   Praew hesitated only a moment before following. Haldric sighed, dragging a hand down his face before trailing after her. This was already a waste of time.

The alley twisted into a hidden passage between two crumbling buildings, the stone walls damp with condensation. The deeper they went, the quieter it became. Finally, Note ducked through a doorway barely visible in the dim light.

Inside, the space was small but reinforced—an old storage room, maybe. Stacked crates lined the walls, and a table sat at the center, surrounded by mismatched chairs. A SolarStone embedded in the ceiling cast an uneven glow over the dust-coated floor.

Note gestured toward the seats. “Sit.”

Haldric didn’t move. Praew, ever the more trusting one, sat down first, watching Note carefully.

Note pulled a chair for himself, the wood groaning under his weight. He folded his arms, leaning forward slightly.

“Tell me,” he said, “what do you two know about government administration?”

Haldric narrowed his eyes. “I used to work in it.”

Note nodded. “And what have you learned?”

Haldric exhaled through his nose. “That everything is categorized, controlled, and precise. That every citizen has a role to play, and that’s what keeps the city running.”

Note’s lips twitched. “A good answer. The kind of answer they drill into your head. But let’s go a little deeper.” He leaned back, exhaling sharply. “Let’s talk about the sterilization program.”

Haldric frowned. “It’s voluntary.”

Note snorted. “Sure. Every man just happens to volunteer for sterilization after having two kids.”

His gaze flicked toward Praew. “Tell me, have you ever met a single man who refused it?”

She hesitated. “I… I don’t think so.”

Haldric’s brow furrowed. His mind raced through every conversation, every offhand remark from co-workers, from neighbors. But, no, he couldn’t recall a single one.

Note watched their reactions carefully. “Strange, isn’t it? A choice that no one has ever made?”

Haldric clenched his jaw. “Maybe they all just agreed.”

Note smirked. “Sure. And while we’re at it, let’s believe every single worker in the city just happens to love their assigned job.”

Praew straightened. “What are you saying?”

“I’m saying it’s not a choice,” Note said confidently.

Haldric’s arms crossed. “They don’t force it. No one is held down against their will.”

 “They don’t have to,” Note said. “The system does it for them.” His voice lowered. “Now, tell me something else. Have either of you ever heard of a family with more than two children?”

Silence.

Praew’s fingers curled against the edge of the table.

Haldric opened his mouth. Stopped. Because the answer was obvious. No, they had never heard of a third child. Never seen one. Never questioned why.

Note’s lips pressed into a thin line. “If it were truly a choice, wouldn’t someone have chosen differently?”

The words sat heavy in the air.

Praew swallowed. “What happens if… if someone has a third child?”

Note’s gaze darkened. “The third child receives no food ration.”

Praew inhaled sharply.

Haldric stiffened. “No way,” he muttered.

Note’s voice remained steady. “Both parents lose their job assignments. The family is reassigned roles.”

Praew’s breath hitched.

“And the worst part?” Note’s expression was unreadable. “The entire family is relocated to the Fourth Floor.”

Haldric let out a sharp laugh. “The Fourth Floor? That’s a myth. Everyone knows there are only three Floors in our city.”

Note didn’t argue. He didn’t push back. Instead, he stood up, walking slowly to the far side of the room. With deliberate slowness, he crouched down and rolled up a ragged old carpet. 

Beneath it—A trapdoor.

The wood was old but sturdy, a metal handle embedded in its surface. A chill ran down Haldric’s spine.

Praew was already moving. “What’s down there?” she whispered.

Note gestured. “See for yourself.”

She took a step forward, but Haldric’s hand shot out, gripping her wrist. “Praew, wait.”

Her voice was quiet but resolute. “I have to know, Haldric.” She pulled free.

Her fingers wrapped around the handle—and she tugged on it.



***



       The hatch creaked open beneath Praew’s hands, a thick breath of stale air rising from the depths below. The woodenl rung of a ladder was damp as she swung over the edge, her grip tightening. She had no idea how deep this went.

      Step by step, the world above shrank into a distant glow, then vanished entirely.

  Praew heard Haldric curse under his breath as he began his climb down. Somewhere farther up the ladder, Note chuckled and muttered, “Coward.”

  She rolled her eyes. Note may have had a not-so-terrible look, but she couldn’t feel any attraction to boys like that. The kind who couldn’t go five flickers without comparing the size of their Sifaralith Stones.

  The air grew staler the further she descended. The silence began to stretch. There was no telling how much time passed before her boots met solid ground.

       And when they did—she froze.

  Haldric and Note dropped down beside her a moment later.

  What lay before them wasn’t a city. It was something much worse. The cavern stretched outward, a gaping wound in the earth. Buildings slumped together like bones stripped of flesh, their walls brittle and crumbling. This place was cold—not just in temperature, but in something deeper. Something stripped and abandoned. Yet, people moved through it. Shadow-thin figures, barely more than silhouettes against the gloom. No conversations. No haggling. No life.

     And the only light? Ratrods.

     The rodents varied in size as they scurried along the broken streets, each with a single horn embedded with a SolarStone, pulsing with dim, dying light. Their trigger was a ZephyrStone, meaning each breath they took activated the glow. But the moment they sensed danger, they held their breath and darted away, their horns fading into darkness. The sight alone made her skin crawl.

A shiver ran down her spine. She opened her mouth—to ask, to demand an explanation—but something stopped her. Something crouched in the dirt, barely visible in the dim glow. A child. Praew blinked.

They couldn’t be older than six. Maybe younger. Their tangled hair clung in thick, matted knots, their rags stiff with filth. They were so thin, their limbs barely more than bones wrapped in skin.

Then she saw what they were doing and her stomach turned. A freshly killed Ratrod was still twitching as the child sank their teeth into it.

It was massive, easily the size of a newborn, its thick body nearly the size of the child’s own torso. 

Praew stopped breathing.

The child showed no signs of hesitation or disgust. Just raw hunger. Blood smeared across their chin as they ripped into the flesh, teeth tearing, throat swallowing. The noise—the wet crunch, the low growl—sent something sharp down her spine. And then the child’s head snapped up.

Praew staggered back.

Those eyes. Wild, feral, red eyes. The kid tensed, hunching lower over their kill. A thin arm curled around the carcass, as if Praew was about to steal it from them.

A low sound rattled in their throat. A growl.

She couldn’t move. She couldn’t speak. Her voice barely came out. “Where are your parents?”

The child didn’t answer. Didn’t blink.

Note scoffed. “Probably dead.”

The words were too flat. Praew turned sharply, staring at him. “You say that like it doesn’t matter.”



Note shrugged, unbothered. “It doesn’t.”

Something inside her twisted. How could he be so—The child was still watching her.

Still baring their teeth. Still guarding the only food they had. Praew felt sick. She’d thought she understood Stormhaven. Thought she had seen suffering. 

But this? She hadn’t known anything at all. And the worst part? Haldric hadn’t said a word yet. 

She turned toward him.

His fists were curled tight. His jaw locked. His eyes stayed on the child, on the feral look in their eyes.

He was absorbing it, wasn’t he?

The air in the Fourth Floor felt different —stifling, almost suffocating. It wasn’t just the humidity or the scent of damp stone. It was something heavier. Something she could feel pressing down on her ribs, sinking into her skin.

Praew followed closely as Note led them deeper into the hidden passageways, her ears straining for any movement. Haldric was right behind her, his steps just as careful. 

Once they turned a corner, Note finally spoke. “Did you know other cities let their citizens choose their own career paths?”

  Praew blinked. “Which… other cities?”

  “All of them. Is it that surprising?” Note shrugged, as if it were the most normal thing in the world. “If they’re good at their jobs, they get promoted. If they struggle, they’re reassigned or trained for something else. And if they hate their job, they quit and try something new.”

  Praew stared. That was… ridiculous.

  Haldric’s voice was quiet but firm. “That sounds like a mess.”

  Note triggered a weak SolarStone so they could see, then tilted his head. “Does it?” He leaned back slightly. “Because from what I’ve seen, it works quite well.”

  Praew frowned. It was hard to believe. But before she could argue, Note continued.

  “But here, in Stormhaven?” He gestured around them. “To be fired is the same as breaking the law.”

  Praew felt her stomach tighten.

  Haldric glanced toward him. “Fine. You obviously want me to ask, so what happens when someone loses their job?”

  Note let out a slow breath. “If they’re lucky, they’re demoted. If not? They’re sent here. To the Fourth Floor.”

  Haldric didn’t bother hiding his suspicion. “And how exactly do you know all of this?”

  Note’s bravado vanished. “As you’ve probably guessed, I was a Hunter. That’s how I’ve visited another city. I confronted The Prime about the fake food shortage. Since I had no family for him to hold hostage, he had no way to control me. Before I knew it, I was discharged and sent down here.”

     Haldric scoffed. “You expect me to believe that?”

  “No,” Note said. “Not yet, I don’t. But you will, Haldric. Maybe once you see for yourself that there are only two career paths on the Fourth Floor.”

  He lowered his voice. “You either become a beast… or livestock, preyed on by something even worse.”

  He gestured toward the dark. “That child you saw? That was the first path. The second is slaving away as a Sifaralith trigger identifier.”

  Haldric frowned. “Livestock? Sifaralith trigger identifier?”

  Note nodded. “Someone has to figure out what makes a stone activate. And the only way to do that… is to test it.”

  “Think of it this way,” Note said. “Junya’s parents were miners on the Third Floor, right? Lived in the outer ring, probably knew nothing about the Fourth. That’s how it works for most.”

  He glanced back at them. “But some people get reassigned or relocated to work in the mines. Those people are never seen again, because they’re sent here.”

  The words landed like a punch in her gut.

  She turned sharply toward Haldric. “Wait—our classmate Korven! He was relocated for mining, wasn’t he?”

  When the flicker of realization hit, Haldric looked like he was about to be sick. They had all assumed Korven had been… relocated. Moved somewhere for labor. But they had never thought to ask where.

  Her hands curled into fists. How many others had vanished? How many names had they stopped saying because they believed they’d just been ‘reassigned’?

  Note gestured for them to keep moving. Praew swallowed hard and pressed forward, following a long corridor that narrowed as it stretched on. As they neared a bend, a faint light began to rise in the distance.

  Note stopped abruptly, crouching behind the remnants of a stone pillar broken off at a height that nearly matched Praew’s own. She and Haldric dropped beside him. The rest of the pillar lay toppled along the ground, its length reaching toward the mouth of the enormous chamber ahead.

  A massive underground pit opened before them, the cavern stretching farther than any part of Stormhaven she had ever seen. It was the only place on this Floor where they’d found actual SolarStones, casting their glow across the stone and lighting the space in full.

     And the people—the hundreds of workers, if you could even call them that -- were treated like absolute waste. Lashings if they made a mistake. Lashings if they worked too recklessly. Lashings just for existing too long. Praew’s breath caught. Wasn’t slavery banned over a hundred years ago?

     Men, women, and children moved in slow, mechanical patterns. Some were hunched over stone tables, testing Sifaraliths with trembling hands. Others were dragging carts filled with jagged rocks, their bodies thin, their faces hollow with exhaustion.

     Praew noticed an enforcer grin as they whipped an old man, and her blood turned cold. They were enjoying it—every moment of hurting these poor people.

 They were always watching. Waiting for the next one to make a mistake or hesitate. Like a beast stalking prey.

 Then—a shockwave of sound. A crack, a blast. Praew flinched.

     One of the workers, a young man around the same age as Praew, staggered back from a table, clutching his hand. His fingers were gone. Blood spattered the stone.

  The people around him barely reacted. An enforcer stepped forward, calm and expressionless, and grabbed the man by the collar. He didn’t even resist as he was dragged away.

  Praew’s breath caught. That face. I know that face!

  “Zephyrs take me,” Haldric cursed.

  “I know him,” Praew whispered.

  “Stone me! Praew, don’t,” Haldric hissed.

  It was Jorpen. Her classmate. The one with the strongest moral compass. The one who disappeared after speaking out in Shadow Recon. They said he had dropped out. That he’d requested reassignment rather than commit treason.

  But he wasn’t the one bleeding.

  He was the one dragging the injured man away.

  Hot tears slid down her cheek. There would be no rescue today.

  There was something on the back of Jorpen’s neck. A burn or a band. Maybe he was little more than a slave himself.

  Mai had said something when failing Praew from her class. That Praew had to pass both her other classes, otherwise she could be—

  Zephyrs take me, she thought. Didn’t Mai say I could be “reassigned to the mines or something much worse”?

  At the time, it sounded like a threat. But she was just trying to warn me, wasn’t she? No wonder she insisted on training me.

  Praew was starting to realize just how little she really knew. But one thing was certain, Mai Azadi knew about this place. And when Praew got back, she was going to find out exactly how much.

     “This is Sifaralith trigger identification.” Note’s voice was steady, but Praew could hear the simmering anger beneath it. “The lowest career in Stormhaven. The fate of all who don’t fit The Prime’s ideal for a perfect city.”

     Haldric tensed beside her. “I… I can’t believe this.” His fists clenched at his sides. His voice wavered, like he was trying to convince himself as much as them. “The Prime Archon treats me like his own son. He’s like the father I never had… He cares. He cares about this city, about everyone.” His breath hitched. His fingers curled tighter. “I . . . he wouldn’t allow this. He wouldn’t.”

     Praew turned toward Haldric, watching.

     This would be even harder for him. She had already been introduced to the darker side of Stormhaven over the past few weeks. This was atrocious—yes—but she had begun mentally preparing long for something like this before arranging the meeting with Note.

  Haldric, on the other hand, was only just beginning to see it. The image of his new father figure was starting to fall apart.

     His gaze flickered over the brutal scene before him. The lashes. The lifeless stares. The silence of the forgotten. A hollow pit formed in his chest. She could see it. “The Prime,” Haldric continued, "probably doesn’t even know this is happening.” His voice cracked. Just a little.

Praew swallowed, the words weighing in her throat.

His eyes betrayed his own words. Moisture lined his lashes, catching in the flickering glow of the SolarStones. He hadn’t wiped it away. Maybe he hadn’t noticed. Or maybe he refused to. Instead, he pulled a small book out of his pocket.

He gripped the book tightly, as though someone would confiscate it.

She didn’t say anything. Consoling him would have to wait. They had bigger problems now. They would likely be attacked at their next destination.

Note exhaled through his nose. “I can see you’re not ready to accept the truth.” He turned away, his broad back cutting through the damp air like a wall. “We’re leaving.”

Praew turned to follow Note when a shadow moved in the corner of her eye. She looked over to see Haldric sneaking up to a pile of Sifaralith stones, grabbing a ledger from a table nearby. 

 He examined it, looked at one pile, then back at the ledger—then looked again at a different pile. And just like that, he was off. He grabbed a stone from two separate piles and returned before Note even noticed he was gone.

“Are you crazy!?” Praew hissed at him.

He glanced in her direction, then looked away.

They followed Note until they entered another lifeless chamber. The ceiling vanished into shadow, swallowed by a height too vast for light to reach.

 The shimmering debris littering the ground began to dance as their only source of illumination scattered and scurried. Praew never thought she’d miss the company of Ratrods, yet Mai Azadi’s training screamed in her head.

     She was too exposed and unfamiliar with the terrain in a way Note clearly wasn’t. He moved like someone accustomed to the dark, more so than either Haldric or herself. An ambush wouldn’t be difficult here.

     So this is it? It was likely. Haldric could be so dense sometimes. Since they failed to see eye to eye, anyone with a brain would know to eliminate the Heir before he runs off to The Prime.

     Couldn’t you just pretend to condemn The Prime?

     Tense, alert, Praew flinched when Note finally spoke.

     “I believe I know what happened to Junya’s parents.”

     Praew felt a flicker of hope. He’s giving me a clue? Perhaps I won’t have to kill him after all!

 An unsettling realization struck. Where did that come from? Since when had the idea of killing come so effortlessly? Zephyrs take me, Mai is rubbing off on me.

     Praew abandoned her stance, letting down her guard. “What did you find?” 

     “According to what Junya told me,” Note said, “their last assignment would’ve placed them right—here, one Floor up.” He pointed up while keeping his eyes locked with Praew.

     Praew and Haldric looked up to find a giant gaping hole in the ceiling. With how crude and misshapen it was, the hole didn’t look intentional.

     Praew gasped. If Junya’s parents fell… if Junya and Wannii fell, they wouldn’t be walking away uninjured. Praew started to search the debris desperately.

     “There’s no bodies. But there’s plenty of blood. And gore. You don’t want to see what the Ratrods were eating.”

     Note’s voice was steady, but his eyes kept flicking toward the dark edges of the chamber.

     “I used to be a tracker, Praew. A location will always tell the story behind a death.”

     He stepped carefully over a slab of fractured stone and motioned to the collapsed pile behind them.

     “Two bodies fell. Both died on impact. Not unusual for a pitfall—except for where they landed. Perfectly on top of all this rubble.”

     He waited a beat before continuing.

      “When a floor gives out, most people get buried. Pinned, crushed, maybe even thrown to the lower ground. Best case, you bounce off the rubble and land beside it. But to hit and stay right on top?” He looked at her. “That only happens if you fall after the collapse.”

     “Because of this, I have reason to believe Junya’s parents were murdered for discovering the existence of the Fourth Floor.”

     “I’ve heard no word of Wannii or Junya down here. Vanishing without an arrest or deceased report is concerning. Either they got caught investigating, or someone caught wind of what they were up to. Does anyone come to mind?”

     Godric and Mai both seemed to know something… but Mai wouldn’t do this.

     “No,” Praew said.
