     “I understand. Then this is where I stop my investigation,” Note continued. “My condolences. But they were likely taken out. Professionally. I believe it’s best if you stop looking into this, or you may disappear next.”

     Haldric snorted. “We finally agree on something.”

     Note, never one to let a quip go, shot back, “What’s the matter, Haldric? You’ve been so quiet, I forgot you were even here.”

     Haldric didn’t bother replying.

     The climb back up felt longer than the descent. Each rung of the ladder, each damp, suffocating passage, weighed heavier than the last.



     Praew’s mind churned. She wasn’t stupid. She’d known their city had its problems, but this? People worked to survive, sure. But enslaved? Brutalized? Dying for the city’s convenience?

     The images wouldn’t leave her. The lash marks on those who had worked too slow. The empty, resigned eyes of children who hadn’t been children in a long time. She forced herself to focus on the rungs beneath her hands. On breathing. On not looking at Haldric, because she knew what he was thinking. She could feel it. The heat of his frustration, his doubt, his denial.









Chapter 13 - A Question’s Consequence



“But I will meet the Creator knowing I remained faithful to my code.”



     They had barely reached the outskirts of the Third Floor when Note spoke again. “Do you ever wonder,” he asked, his voice casual but sharp, “why Hunters hate The Prime Archon so much, yet do nothing about it?”

     Haldric scoffed. “They’re Hunters. They don’t care about anyone but themselves.”

     Note gave him a pointed look. “You really believe that?”

     Haldric hesitated.

     The question seemed to linger, scratching at something in the back of his mind. Did something happen with Bresdin? Praew wondered.

     “They can live above the surface,” Note continued. “They have power, strength, autonomy. So why stay here?”

     Praew crossed her arms. “They have families.”

     “Exactly.” Note turned to face them fully. “Stormhaven’s upper class.”

     Haldric stopped walking.

     Praew’s stomach clenched.



     Note’s usual grin faded. He placed a reassuring hand on Haldric’s shoulder and spoke in a surprisingly gentle voice. “Haldric, I know we got off on the wrong foot. But please, don’t let that be the reason you deny the truth about The Prime.”

  “Oh… and what truth is that?” Haldric demanded, his voice simmering with barely contained rage. He slapped Note’s hand away, refusing to be placated.

     Note withdrew his hand, along with any trace of empathy. “You may have replaced Arthur’s son. But not everyone gets to be the Heir Archon.”

      He took a breath.

  “Haven’t you ever wondered how one man keeps control over forty thousand people, and seventy active Hunters, each capable of wiping out a small army?

  “He holds their families hostage. I wasn’t lying earlier. The best hostages are the ones who don’t even realize they became one.

  “Every Hunter, every Special Forces member, every government official… they’re allowed to move their families to the First Floor. Lower crime. Better food. Nicer homes. More guards. More patrols. Who wouldn’t trade a little privacy for peace of mind?”

  “But that peace comes at a cost: constant surveillance and quiet obedience. It makes it impossible for even a group of Hunters to get their families to the surface without being executed first.”

     The flickering SolarStones above barely chased away the damp chill clinging to Praew’s skin.

     Haldric’s breathing quickened, his fists clenching and unclenching at his sides. Praew recognized that look. He was reaching his limit.

  Suddenly, he turned on Note. “You seem to know a lot for just a ‘worried citizen.’” His voice dripped with sarcasm and spite.

  He stepped forward. “You’re one of them, aren’t you?”

  Praew had come to the same conclusion some time ago.

  “A rebel,” Note said with a nod.

      Haldric spun and seized her arm. “Praew! We can be executed just for being seen with this guy! We need to leave, NOW!”

  Praew felt her eyes begin to water. 

  Note’s smirk vanished. He stepped forward, eyes blazing. “The greatest threat to her right now is you, Haldric!”

  Haldric let out a snort as he began to march her away. “Really? That’s the best you could come up with?” He wouldn’t even let her face the direction he was leading her.

  Praew watched as Note’s hand slid behind his back, fingers likely curling around a weapon. He didn’t draw it. He just held there, steady, as he spoke in a calmer voice.

  “Regular citizens can’t withstand the superior strength of an acclimated human. So how about you let her go?”

  “Enough!” Haldric yelled, his grip tightening.

  “Ow!” she hissed. His fingers bit into her skin with a monstrous grip. Like the jaws of a beast, ready to snap bone.

     Praew moved on instinct. She straightened her left arm against his grip, creating just enough slack to lean forward, shifting her weight onto her right foot. Then she drove her heel into the crook of his knee. His leg buckled. He reeled backward, off-balance. She planted her foot and pivoted sharply left. To build momentum, she twisted back, swung her free arm across her body, striking his throat.

     Haldric released her, stumbling back, coughing. His necklace slipped from beneath his shirt and struck the stone with a sharp clink.

     A key?

 Praew huffed, rubbing her wrist. Superior strength? Acclimated human? What did that even mean? Her mind reeled, but before she could speak—

  “Well blast me silly,” Note said under his breath. “You alright, Praew?”

  She nodded, still rubbing her wrist.

  They both looked at Haldric just as he straightened, a rasp in his throat. “I’m fine too,” he grumbled. “Thanks for asking.”

  “I may be part of the resistance, but does that make me wrong? The Prime must have records. Truth about the so-called food shortage, the Fourth Floor, or something that will open your eyes. See for yourself, Haldric. I’m not asking you to betray The Prime. I’m asking you to become a better one. When it’s your reign, only you will have the power to stop this.”

  Note’s eyes locked onto hers.

  “Thanks to your friend here, I’ll be leaving Stormhaven immediately. I would rather not give him the chance to have me executed.”

  “Good instincts,” Haldric sneered. “Though every time you speak, I’m more tempted to do it myself.”

  Note ignored him, gaze fixed on Praew. “If Wannii or Junya were captured, you’re in danger too. Come with me, Praew. Join the Song of Freedom.”

  Freedom? That’s all she’d ever wanted. To escape this dungeon. To go on adventures. To protect those too weak to lift a spear. Wasn’t that worth fighting for?

  “You can’t seriously be considering this, Praew!” Haldric snapped.

  “No, I—I can’t, Note,” she said hesitantly. “I can’t give up on my friends and family. Not when I have the power to help.”

  “Our loss,” Note said with a nod. “Follow the music if you change your mind.”

     Note crossed his arms, eyeing them both.

  “In Ravour, power is never created. It’s taken. So take heed, the more you claim, the more dangerous you become to those you may take it from.”

     Praew and Haldric turned to each other. And when they turned back, Note was gone.

  Vanished like a whisper in the dark. 

     “I believe it’s time you and I had a talk.” Her anger bled into every word.



     Haldric let out a slow breath through his nose, his fists clenched at his sides. “I couldn’t agree more.”

     She blinked. That wasn’t the reaction she expected. His voice was tight, seething, his stance rigid. Praew narrowed her eyes. “Wait, why are you angry?”

     Haldric stepped closer, there was something burning in his gaze.

     “You knew, didn’t you?” he asked, voice low and trembling with restrained fury.

     She frowned. “What?”

     “You knew that bolting Ratrod was a rebel, didn’t you!?” Haldric accused.

     Praew didn’t respond.

     “Stone you, Praew! I’m the bolting Heir Archon! What do you think would happen if I was caught talking with a rebel!? Not just you and I—our whole families would be executed!”

     The words cracked through the air like a whip.

     Praew inhaled sharply.

     “I only started to suspect when he spoke about a Fourth Floor, but I had to know!” Her frustration flared, matching his. “And what are you yelling at me for?! You’re the one keeping secrets!”

     Haldric’s eyes flashed. “What are you talking about?”

     “What’s this about an acclimated human and superior strength?” she snapped.

     Haldric’s lips parted like he was about to say something, but then—nothing. His throat bobbed.

     Praew folded her arms, waiting. Daring him to lie to her.

 “It’s none of your concern,” he finally muttered.

    Praew let out a bitter laugh. “Oh, really?” She held up her arm, the faint light catching on the swelling bruises already forming around her wrist. Courtesy of his unnatural strength. “My arm would disagree.”

     Haldric stiffened. His gaze flicked to her wrist, just for a second. Then he looked away.

     “What is going on with you!?” She took a step closer, forcing him to look at her. “You’re getting so aggressive. You defend The Prime Archon more than anyone. And now you’re able to crush my wrist like it’s a ration cracker?”

     She jabbed a finger at his chest. “Talk. Now.”

     Haldric’s gaze was cold. He didn’t answer immediately. Instead, he exhaled, slow and measured, before speaking. “There’s more going on than you could ever understand, Praew.”

     His voice wasn’t angry anymore. “You always think you’re so smart,” he continued, “So bolting sure of yourself. But look at what you just did.”

     She blinked. “What—?”

     “You just made your closest friend commit treason.”

     “And justice we will deliver,” Haldric said alone, looking even more disgusted with her. She hadn’t said the words.

     She’d stopped reciting that bolting chant every time she heard the word treason weeks ago. Though the guilt still lingered, she refused to show it. “I didn’t—”

     “You met with a rebel,” Haldric interrupted. “Talked with him. Listened to him!” He sneered. “If anyone saw us, we would be dead, Praew. And for what? Some conspiracy theories and a good story?”

     She felt herself blink rapidly as her eyes began to water. Not now Praew, she thought, compose yourself!

     “There’s a reason you’re in Special Forces and not Administration.”  The words dripped with something ugly. Something cruel. “You’re not as smart as you pretend to be.”

     Heat flared behind her eyes. She felt it building in her chest, a pressure that was too much, too sharp. Praew clenched her fists.

     She could have fought back. Could have told him how blind he was, how stupid he sounded. But she didn’t.

     Because she knew—deep down, she agreed with him.

     Instead, she turned on her heel and stormed off without another word. She didn’t look back. Not once. She headed toward the upper Floors. Away from him. Away from his arrogance.

     Because if she didn’t leave right now—

     She might stoop to his level and say something she couldn’t take back. 





***





Praew walked home in silence, her thoughts still tangled from the fight with Haldric. The raw emotion had ebbed, but dried tears clung to her cheeks, leaving her face stiff and tight. 

     Only now did she register how unnaturally quiet the street was. This was her first time using her new clearance to bypass curfew. Shadows shifted across the walls, and the echo of her breathing made the corridor feel narrower than usual. 

     A faint breeze brushed past, likely the drift of a distant ZephyrStone triggered somewhere to circulate oxygen. The twentieth flash lit the path just as her home came into view.

     She slowed.

     Mai Azadi was waiting out front.

     Praew froze for a moment, then remembered—she hadn’t shown up for training. The guilt hit her instantly.

     “I’m sorry,” she said. “I forgot to tell you I had plans today. I didn’t mean to skip.”

     Mai didn’t ask what the plans were. She just looked at her carefully.

     “Are you okay?” she asked.

     Praew gave a weak nod. “I’m fine.”

     Mai’s eyes narrowed faintly, unconvinced. But she didn’t push.

     “When I’m worked up, it helps to blow off some steam,” Mai said. Then paused. “Come on. Let’s go train. It’s not too late.”

     Praew hesitated, but only for a moment. It was the middle of the night, but she didn’t have classes tomorrow. She could sleep in.

     She nodded. “Alright.”

    They walked in silence, cutting through the halls toward the empty classroom. From there, Mai led her through the hidden passage into the training chamber.

     The space no longer felt foreign.

     Praew had grown.

     She could now cross all four terrain paths, even water, without triggering a single stone. Those drills were behind her. Her feet knew the rhythm. Her breaths matched the stone.

     Now, she and Mai trained for real. No tricks. No tests. Just hand-to-hand combat.

     Tonight, they didn’t speak. No commands. No corrections. Just movement. Precision. A silent, unchoreographed dance between them. Each strike and each block carried by a rhythm that neither of them heard but both instinctively followed. There was no music, yet something deeper played beneath their feet, guiding each motion like the undertow of a song.

     Praew moved with more force than usual. More weight behind each swing. More heat in every advance. She needed the chance to burn through her frustration. She could feel it. And Mai could too.

     Mai broke the silence.

     “Do you want to talk about it?” she asked, breath steady.

     Praew hesitated, just long enough to defend Mai’s strike. Between movements, Praew told her everything. About Note. About the Fourth Floor. The disappearances. The Sifaralith identification program.

     Mai didn’t respond. Her eyes glazed over, staring through Praew, through the room, like she was watching something only she could see. Her movements flowed by habit alone.

     Praew’s elbow slipped past her guard and struck clean. A solid hit. Mai didn’t flinch.

     Praew hesitated, stunned by the opening, but her instinct took over. She stepped forward and raised her hand for another strike.

     This time, Mai caught her wrist.

     Her grip was firm. Her eyes locked on Praew’s.

     “Don’t go back there,” she said.

     She stepped in and grabbed both of Praew’s arms, holding her in place.

     “Promise me. Promise me you’ll never go back there!”

     Her grip was urgent, her voice shaken, and her eyes were—

     Zephyrs take me. Her eyes were terrified.

     “I knew it!” Praew exclaimed. “You know about the Fourth Floor!”

     Mai didn’t answer. Her body stiffened as she let go, eyes refusing to meet Praew’s.

     “You need to tell me everything,” Praew demanded.

     Mai still didn’t respond. Instead, she turned and took a few slow steps away from her.

     “I—” Her voice caught. “I can’t, Praew.”

     Praew stood in place, refusing to give up. She wasn’t angry, not anymore, but she needed to know what Mai knew.

     “I thought we were friends,” Praew said, softer now. “Friends don’t keep secrets from each other. Please… just tell me.”

     Mai stopped. She didn’t turn right away. Her hands hung loose at her sides, but Praew could see the slight tremble in her fingers.

     When she finally turned, her voice came quiet. Not fragile, but tired.

     “I was born on the Fourth Floor, Praew.”

     The words lingered in the air like dust refusing to settle.

     “My whole family worked in the Sifaralith Identification Program. My father died before I was born. I don’t even know what he looked like.”

     Mai stepped toward the far wall, eyes fixed on nothing.

     “My mom and my two older brothers did the dangerous work—testing reactions, moving volatile stones. I wasn’t allowed near the tables. I just carried stones to the correct bins when they called out the placement.”

     Praew frowned. “Wait… two older brothers? That means your family had three kids?”

     Mai nodded faintly. “Yeah, I think that’s why my family was sent there.”

     She hesitated.

     “I loved them. Even when they were cruel. My brothers blamed me for everything. Said I shouldn’t have been born. That my dad died because of me.”

     Her face didn’t crack, but something in her voice tightened.

     “I never understood it. But my mom, she never blamed me. She told me I was special. That I was hers.”

     Her gaze dropped.

     “One day, everything changed. My mom was handling a BlastStone and a BoltStone. A strong ZephyrStone triggered nearby. It triggered both stones in her hands.”

     Mai raised her own hands as if remembering, then slowly let them fall.

     “She lost all her fingers so the guards came. We knew what that meant. If you’re no longer useful, you’re removed. Disposed of. I’d seen it happen before.”

     “Yeah, me too,” Praew nodded.

     Mai paused, swallowing.

     “My brothers lost it. They fought back. Screaming, throwing tools. I tried to help, but a guard hit me—hard. I flew into a pile of unprocessed stones. I landed right next to the ZephyrStone that tore our lives apart.”

     She closed her eyes.

     “I blacked out. But just before everything went dark… I saw them. My mom. My brothers. All three of them being dragged away.”

     Her arms hung limp now. Her voice thinned. “I never saw them again. But when I woke in the same location, there was something next to me. A red…” She stopped.

     Praew stepped closer, gentler now. “A red what?”

     Mai didn’t answer. Her lips parted, then closed. She turned her head, chest rising and falling. “I’m sorry,” she whispered. “I… I really can’t say.”

     The silence held. Mai looked down at her hands again before continuing.

     “But I never forgot the three guards’ faces. Not one.”

     Her voice hardened.

     “When I was alone, I found a broken chunk of stone. I remembered something—something my brothers used to do to pass the time. They’d sharpen rocks with other stones. Said it kept their hands busy when they weren’t. So I did the same. I sharpened it. For days.”

     Mai smiled, looking up and locking eyes with Praew. Those eyes held a hatred Praew had never seen before.

     How can someone hide so much pain behind a smile? Instead of pity, Praew’s admiration for her new best friend only grew.

     “I waited. I watched. I was ready,” Mai continued. “Then the day finally came.”

     Praew could feel the weight behind every word.

     “The Prime Archon came down. Four guards flanked him. And standing in formation to greet him were the three men who destroyed my family.”

     Praew’s stomach turned.

     “They stood at attention. Clean uniforms. Eyes straight. Like nothing had happened.” Mai’s hands clenched slightly. “That’s when I struck.”

     She stared past Praew, not really seeing her.

     “I snuck up behind them. Quiet. So quiet. No one noticed. Not even The Prime. Not until it was too late.”

     “I drove the blade into the backs of their knees. All three. Dropped them screaming. And before they hit the ground, I slit their throats.”

     Praew’s breath caught.

     “The last guard tried to stop me. The Prime… he looked amused. He raised his hand, and the others stepped back. They didn’t intervene. They just watched.”

     Her jaw clenched.

   “The last one was bigger. Stronger. But I was faster. I stabbed through his foot, and when he stumbled—I went for the face.”

     She didn’t describe it. She didn’t need to.

     Mai looked at the floor again. This time, her voice came low and distant. “The Prime was impressed. He brought me up to the First Floor and enrolled me in Ghost Operations.”

     Mai looked away.

     “So I trained. I learned. And I survived.”

     Praew stood still. The pieces began to arrange themselves, clicking together in the quiet.

     “So, The Prime does know about the Fourth Floor,” she said. “I knew it. I have to tell Haldric and—”

     Mai’s eyes widened. “No,” she said quickly. “Wait. There’s… something else I need to tell you.”

     Praew paused, mid-step. “What is it?”

     Mai hesitated. She shifted her weight, looking down at her hands as if the words were buried in her palms.

     “This all happened a long time ago,” she said. “Longer than you’d think.”

     Praew tilted her head. “It must have been traumatic. I’m so sorry, Mai, but I—”

     “No. That’s not it.” Her voice rose sharply, cutting through. “Praew, please listen to me.”

     Praew nodded once. Quiet. Waiting.

     Mai didn’t look up right away.

     “I don’t even know if the current Prime knows about the Fourth Floor,” she said. “It was the previous Prime who discovered me.”

     Praew blinked. “That’s… not possible, Mai. Maybe he used a disguise.”

     Mai’s voice softened. “No, it really was the last Prime. Because of something that happened… I don’t age.”

     “What?” Praew asked, mind reeling.

     For a second, she thought she’d misheard. Even for someone like Mai, Praew wasn’t prepared for levity in this conversation.

     But if it was a joke, Mai didn’t show it. Her face remained serious as she shifted uncomfortably beneath Praew’s piercing stare.

     “I don’t get older,” Mai said again, insistent. “I’ve looked like this since the day he found me.”

     Mai took a deep breath and shut her eyes, balling both hands into fists before forcing out one last sentence.

     “The truth is, I’m probably even older than—”

     “That bolting bag of bones is even older than me,” growled a voice behind them.

     Mai froze, then looked up with a tight smile. “Well slap my back with a BlastStone, I’m getting rusty.”

     Praew turned slowly. Silverwing Godric stood in the doorway, arms crossed.

     “What is with you bolting hatchlings and your obsession with classified information? I thought you’d learn a lesson after what happened to the others, Praew.”

     He started walking toward them.

     Mai stepped in front of her, calm and composed.

     “Godric Rivers,” she said, “you’re quite light on your feet. I didn’t even hear a footstep.”

     Godric gave a short nod. “Thanks. Years of practice. Though… I guess not as many as you.”

     Then his tone cooled.

     “You shouldn’t have told her about the Fourth Floor. No matter what she is to you… treason is treason.”

     He stopped a few paces away.

     “It’s kind of sick, you know—imitating a hatchling to experience your missed youth.”

     His eyes flicked to Praew.

     “What is she? Your next victim?”

     Mai stared daggers at Godric as she unsheathed a voice sharp enough to cut the air.

     “That’s rich coming from you.”

     She tilted her head, locking eyes with him.

     “Have you already forgotten what we Ghosts used to call you before your Hunter title?”

     She let the silence drag.

     “Madman Godric… the Deadly Scientist.”

     Godric stepped right up to her. Close. Looming.

     His fists clenched.

     “I thought I made it clear what would happen to you Ghosts if you ever called me that again.”

     Mai smiled her usual carefree grin, but her eyes told a different story. They were wide, fierce, and beastly—like the Ratrod-eating child from the Fourth Floor.

     “I think it’s best if you leave, Praew,” she chimed through the teeth of her stiff smile.

     “No,” Praew said calmly, though her heart was racing as fast as her thoughts. “You’re my best friend, and I don’t care about your age. I’m sorry for breaking my promises. So come on! Stay alive and scold me like before!” She turned to Godric with a sneer.

     “You were one of my heroes, Godric.”

     “I’m no bolting hero,” he growled back, eyes locked on Mai. “But I’ll do what I must. Go home, Praew. I’ll deal with you later.”

     Praew slowly backed toward the door. “If anything happens to me, Haldric will find you, Godric. Not even you can ignore the Heir!”

     Godric turned toward her, fury breaking through. “Do you really think that I—”

     Praew didn’t hear the rest, because Godric made a deadly mistake: he took his eyes off the best bolting assassin in the city.

     Mai dropped to a knee and slammed a concealed dagger through his foot.

     The monster didn’t even flinch. He just looked down at her.

     “Praew, run!” Mai shouted.

     Godric moved effortlessly. A casual kick launched Mai across the training hall like a child’s toy sphere.

     Praew ran. The hall blurred as the first flash signaled the start of a new day.

     You better be okay, Mai. You’re the best assassin. You must know how to escape that beast. You understood my hidden message, right? I’ll see you there.

     A plan? Yeah… she had a plan. Or part of one. She’d meet Mai on the Fourth Floor and ambush Godric together. But first, she’d need weapons. She’d need time.

     Haldric will expose Godric and clear our names… right? He has to.

     Godric’s echoing voice chased her down the hall, catching her the only way he still could—by seizing her attention with subtext that completely escaped her.

     “Drop the act, Zephyr’s Blade. Your prey already escaped. What’s the point in hiding what you really are now?”

     Zephyrs take me, what did he even mean by that?

     Pebbles began to scatter across the stone near the battle, clicking louder with each heartbeat. A low howl rose, and before she could look back, a sudden gust blasted Praew off her feet. The wind hurled her down the hall until she slammed face-first into the wall at the next bend.

     What in the bolting seven stars? That’s not what I meant when I said ‘Zephyrs take me.’ What kind of ZephyrStone can even do that!?

     Shaking off the daze, she pushed herself up and ran toward Haldric’s home. His family wouldn’t appreciate her barging in so late, but apologies would have to wait.

Sprinting without a breath to spare, Haldric’s home finally came into view. Just as Praew began to slow her pace, a shadow cut in front of her.

“Wait, how did you—“  





***



The second flicker ended, returning the street to a stillness that matched the silence. Not the kind of silence that invited peace, but the still, stone-deep hush that came with curfew. No voices or footfalls. Just the faint, distant dripping of stalactites hard at work to form stalagmites throughout the street. Its perseverance seemed to echo throughout the city, only to be sabotaged by the morning commute of hard-working citizens.

     Haldric paced in front of the administration building.

     What came over me? he thought. Why am I so impulsive? I could be in bed by now. What did I expect to accomplish by coming here instead? That bolting woman is making me lose my mind!

     He shouldn’t have cared this much. Shouldn’t have let that bolting conversation get under his skin. But the words wouldn’t leave. 

“You’re not as smart as you pretend to be.” His words to Praew words rang in his head.

     Praew was reckless. Always had been. But she wasn’t stupid.

     And Note. That smug bastard. The way he spoke, the way he looked at Haldric like he was naïve. Like he was blind. Like he didn’t understand how his own city worked.

     Haldric gritted his teeth and shoved the thought aside. He did understand. The Prime Archon had given him this position because he trusted him. Because he deserved it.

     So why couldn’t he stop thinking about the Fourth Floor?

     The bruises. The whip scars. The way those workers didn’t even scream when they were punished, like they had accepted it. Like it wasn’t new.

     That kid. Gnawing on a Ratrod like some starving animal.

     His stomach twisted. He wasn’t like Praew. He didn’t jump to conclusions. He needed proof that The Prime knew.

     If Note was lying, then fine, he’d throw it back in his face. But if he wasn’t…

     Haldric’s hands curled into fists. There was only one way to find out.

     And that meant breaking into The Prime Archon’s office.

     It wasn’t treason. 

     You’re proving them wrong, he told himself, shoving aside the unease curling in his chest. You’re proving you were right to trust him. And that he was, to trust you.

     Then why did it feel like he was about to cross a line he couldn’t uncross?

     His footsteps scraped softly against the carved stone floor as he walked toward the monolithic entrance. The building itself bled straight out of the cavern wall behind it, a symbol of smooth, chiseled order. The stone face rose two stories high, cut with sharp lines and recessed archways, everything symmetrical, exact. No guards stood at the gate. None were needed.

     The Prime’s key hung heavy on Haldric’s neck. He shifted the small book in his pocket so it would stop digging into his thigh.

     He could feel the objects’ discontent. Every step closer to the door made their presence feel unbearable. It was more than a book or key. It was a symbol of trust. The Prime’s trust. The city’s trust. And here he was, unable to give them the same.

     He stopped and ran a hand through his hair. No, he wasn’t breaking in. That’s what Note wanted him to think. That he was sneaking around, chasing conspiracies, doubting the one person who’d believed in him from the start. But that wasn’t it. That wasn’t it.

     He looked up at the sealed door. It was a massive slab, locked with a complex rotating seal embedded into the stone. It was a safe door. Every home held a miniature one that contained a copy of the family’s identification documents. No one could unlock this giant mechanism without the proper key. But his master key could open every lock in the city.

     This wasn’t betrayal. It was loyalty.

     Right?

     The second flash triggered, and Haldric’s breath caught as realization struck. Time was slipping through his fingers faster than he had anticipated. He knew that by the fifth flash, early risers would start filtering into the building, and The Prime had once confided that he never set foot inside before the fourth. That gave Haldric a narrow window. Two flashes at most to uncover the truth and vanish before anyone else arrived.

     Haldric took a breath. Pulled the key off his neck. He hesitated only once, then pressed it into the stone.

     The lock turned.

     The hall swallowed him in shadow.

     Haldric stepped inside, the heavy door sealing behind him with a dull grind. No light greeted him, just rows of benches stretching into dark silence. The benches were bare. The chambers, empty. Only the long stone aisle stretched ahead. This was where he’d normally worked, where others gathered for meetings, hearings, and reports. But now it felt hollow, like the air itself was waiting to catch him in the act.

     His footsteps echoed louder than they should have.

     As he neared the far end of the hall, a faint sliver of light cut across the stone floor. He froze. The Prime’s office door was shut, but underneath, a warm glow spilled from within. SolarStones. They were always triggered in The Prime’s chamber, whether occupied or not. But was he there? Haldric couldn’t tell.

     He moved closer, his heartbeat in his throat. The glow didn’t flicker. No shadows moved across the light. Still, he hesitated. Hand hovering over the latch. If The Prime was inside…

     He turned the handle.

     The office was empty.

     He exhaled sharply, the tension pouring from his shoulders as he stepped in and shut the door behind him.

     Light bathed the room in a soft, golden warmth. The Prime’s office was as he remembered, orderly, immense, carved into layered alcoves and shelves, papers neatly stacked, quills lined like weapons across the desk. It felt sacred. Untouchable.

     But he was here now. And there was no turning back.

     He took a steadying breath.

     Let’s begin, he thought. But still, he stood there.

     His hand twitched at his side.

     The light from the SolarStones glinted softly off the polished stone of the desk. The same desk he’d sat beside so many times. The same walls that had once made him feel respected and needed.

     What am I doing? He glanced at the door again. I shouldn’t be here. He treats me like his own. Gave me this key, this position…

     This is wrong but… if I’m going to be Heir, I have to lead. That means facing the truth, even if it hurts.

     He stepped toward the desk and reached for the first drawer.

Drawer by drawer. Sheet by sheet. Neatly stacked papers filled with daily notes, reports, and appointments. Training logs. Ledger summaries. Transport slips. He found his own name a few times, mostly evaluations, nothing unusual. Everything felt above board. Too above board.

     He moved to the shelves, scanning the spines of bound records. Storage rotations. Personnel files. Surface reports. Still, nothing stood out.

     The place was clean. Spotless. Like someone had swept the truth away before it could settle.

     He frowned. Stepped back.

     The light flickered once overhead.

     He glanced toward the hall. Still no footsteps yet.

     How many was that now? Five? Ten? Every flicker dragged him closer to exposure. But he couldn’t leave yet.

     There had to be more. Something hidden like the murals he’d seen.

     His eyes swept the bookshelves, stone-carved edges, orderly stacks, nothing out of place. It all looked clean. Neutral. But at the base of the lowest shelf, something interrupted the symmetry. A shallow groove, almost hidden in shadow.

     He crouched. Pressed his fingers against the unnatural line.

     The third flash triggered.

     Down to the last flash already?

     He broke out into a cold sweat.

     He traced along the edge of the slit, feeling for any give in the stone. A block of stone gave slightly. It shifted with a soft grating click. The shelf creaked, then pushed inward.

     A hidden panel.

     He exhaled and pulled it open.

     Inside, carefully stacked documents filled a shallow recess. Each bundle bound in string, labels written in firm, practiced hand. These weren’t discarded reports. They were current. Maintained.

     He lifted the top file and unfolded the first page.

     BoltStone Recorrection Program.

     The heading alone made his chest pull tight.

     Below it, a sketched outline of a human spine, annotated with clean lines and technical marks. A BoltStone embedded at the base of the neck. Margins filled with calm, efficient handwriting:

     “Sustained exposure reinforces impressionable behavior. Recommended in high-risk subjects.”

     “Scarring minimal. Long-term effects still in testing.”

     “Early-stage results promising. Further trials required.” —Godric Rivers

     He stared at the words. The paper barely crinkled under his grip, but his knuckles had gone pale.

     What was this?

     He set it aside. Pulled the next stack into the light.

     Breeding Ledgers.

     His brow drew low. That wasn’t a term he recognized from any official archive.

     He opened it.

     Inside, dense columns of tracking data. Hunter assignments. Population statistics. Breeding yards. Slaughter rotations. And then a final column—one that made his throat tighten.

     Meat distribution orders.

     Quantities requested. Approved. Then lines drawn through them. Decreased. Cut down.

     They weren’t just tracking the beasts. They were raising them.

     And The Prime Archon was holding back the food.

     He—

     Before he could think, a soft creak. A presence behind him. Haldric’s head snapped straight toward the door, but no one stood in the hallway.

     The Prime Archon walked out of a secret door directly behind Haldric. Intent on a stack of papers in his hands, it took The Prime a moment to register Haldric’s presence.

     Startled, The Prime stopped in place, turning around toward Haldric slowly. A brief flicker of surprise tightened his brow before the mask fell back into place. His hands clasped behind his back, rolling the papers he held into a tight cylinder. His gaze swept over the open drawers, the displaced books, the hidden compartment left ajar.

     His expression sank.

     Not into anger. But into disappointment. A heavy, quiet kind of disappointment. The kind that didn’t need raised voices or fury. The kind that sat deep in the chest, unsaid, unspoken, yet unmistakable.

     Haldric felt it like a blade to the gut.

     The Prime exhaled, slow.

     “Did you find what you were looking for?”

     Jaw agape, Haldric’s gaze flicked to the hidden door, to The Prime, then the papers in his own hands. No excuse would fix this.

     He had been caught.

     “I… I’m sorry,” Haldric stammered.

     The Prime’s grip tightened around the rolled papers. He sighed.

     “No. It’s my fault.” The Prime’s voice was steady, but there was something heavier beneath it. “My fault for believing in you.”

     Haldric clenched his jaw.

     The words struck harder than they should have. He wasn’t sure why.

     The Prime stepped forward, gripped the edge of the hidden passageway, and slammed it shut. Stone grinded against stone, sealing away whatever lay beyond.

     Haldric barely resisted the urge to step back.

     The Prime exhaled sharply.

     “Perhaps… if you’re satisfied,” his voice was calm, but patience thinned with each word, “you should put back the highly classified documents you just pilfered?”

     Haldric’s fingers twitched. Pilfered? The word lodged itself in his skull. Like he was some petty thief caught red-handed. Still, he forced his hands to move, placing the documents back where he found them, each motion slow and careful.

     The Prime watched. Didn’t move. Didn’t speak.

     A flicker later, he sighed. “I’m not mad, Haldric. I’m just disappointed.”

     Haldric clenched his jaw. That felt worse.

     “The whole city blames me for everything,” The Prime continued, his voice quiet but edged. “When food runs low, when water rations shrink, when a Hunter dies above ground, it’s always my fault. The people need someone to blame. And I accept that burden.”

     His gaze flicked toward Haldric, sharper now.

     “But I thought you, of all people, would be different. That you would look past the stories and rumors to see the real me.”

     Haldric’s throat tightened. A sharp, unsteady breath. “I—”

     The Prime didn’t stop. “To learn that even you think I’m a bad person?” A quiet scoff. “That’s frustrating.”

     Haldric’s stomach twisted. Because he had believed in The Prime. Still wanted to. But—

     “You are holding back food.”

     The words came before he could stop them. His hands curled into fists. “You’re giving more to the upper class and using people’s hunger to maintain control over everyone.”

     The Prime stilled. “Am I?”

     The words were measured and indifferent. But there was something there, something he couldn’t quite place.

      Haldric hesitated.

     The Prime didn’t wait for an answer. He turned toward the office door. “Come.”

     Haldric blinked.

     “What?”

     “You wanted answers.”

     The Prime didn’t glance back. “Walk with me.”

     Haldric hesitated. A test? A trap? But what choice did he have? He stepped forward, following.

     The doors shut behind them.

     And for the first time ever, The Prime instilled fear into Haldric.

     Fear for his life.





Chapter 14 - Answers



“Today, I saw the future.”



     Haldric followed, each step slow, uncertain. The office door groaned shut behind them, sealing away the confrontation like a weight pressing down on his chest.

     The Prime walked with purpose, a balance between hurried and idle. Every step measured, deliberate. The kind of stride belonging to a Hunter or a ruler.

     They walked to the now-open ration station near the administrative building.

     “If everything you said is true, have you not wondered why your rations hardly improved after becoming the Heir?”

     Haldric received his breakfast first. A bland grain bar. He moved to sit at one of the tables, expecting The Prime to follow suit. Haldric was surprised to see The Prime continuing to walk, ration bar in hand. Haldric’s eyes widened slightly. He hadn’t expected such simplicity from a leader of the city. 

     “No time for rest, Haldric,” The Prime said. “We need to keep moving.”

     Where was his fancy breakfast?

     The Prime took a bite of his grain bar. He walked with purpose, neither hurried nor idle. Every step was measured and deliberate, until the corridor opened into a winding, empty road.

     The Prime’s demeanor shifted. His shoulders slouched. His strides grew heavier, less smooth. The exhaustion on his face was impossible to miss.

     This was not the walk of a man who ruled Stormhaven.

     It was the walk of someone who carried it.

     The Prime glanced at Haldric.

     “Have you ever wondered why your ration slips didn’t change when you became the Heir Archon?”

     Haldric blinked, taken aback by the unexpected question. He glanced at the simple meal in his hand, identical to the one he’d always known.

     “I… I guess I never thought about it.”

     The Prime smiled, continuing down the winding road that led them deeper into the upper district.

     “A leader never asks of his people what he is not willing to do himself.”

     Haldric fell into step beside The Prime as the words settled over him.

     Isn’t this the way to the noble quarter? His thoughts began to spiral. He had expected anger or cold fury. Instead, he was met with this… rationality, a challenge to see things through The Prime’s eyes.

     What’s your angle? To manipulate me?

     Or was it proof that the man was truly worth believing in?

     They moved through the winding halls of roads, past guards who straightened at the sight of The Prime Archon. No words were exchanged. No questions asked. They all knew better.

     After the sixth flash, they reached their destination, and what Haldric saw made him stop.

     The Noble Quarter had always been described in whispers. Rumors spoke in abundance of soft beds and wide streets, and of citizens untouched by hunger. At least, according to some workers who had once been assigned a task inside. That’s the only time people were allowed to enter.

     But what is this? There was no feast laid out before him. No indulgence. Just order. Meticulous, suffocating order.

     The stone paths were swept to perfection. Every home looked identical, clean and polished, as if waiting for an inspection that never came. SolarStones lined the roads in symmetrical arcs, glowing brighter here than anywhere else in the city. There were no stalls. No loiterers. No signs of imprisonment or poverty. But none of that unsettled Haldric as much as the people.

     They weren’t what he expected. These weren’t bloated bureaucrats lounging in luxury. The adults were thin, tired, hungry, and… happy? They looked toward the children with a fondness in their eyes that seemed to heal the exhaustion.

     The children they looked after were strong and healthy. Full of life, they laughed and played with an energy that stood in stark contrast to the adults. They brandished makeshift swords, pretending to be Hunters, their voices ringing out with youthful determination.

     Haldric’s brows pulled together as the contradiction set in. His voice came out low.

     “Where’s the supposed luxury?”

     The Prime didn’t even slow.

     “This is your so-called upper class.

     “Have you ever wondered why I bring the families of Hunters here?” he asked, glancing at Haldric.

     Haldric hesitated.

     “I—I’m not sure,” he said, remembering Note’s accusations.

     “Your face tells a different story,” The Prime chuckled softly. “I know what the rumors say. That I hold them hostage, use them to control Hunters.”

     Haldric looked away, a flush of embarrassment creeping up his neck.

     The Prime chuckled faintly.

     “I don’t blame you. It’s not your fault.” A distant look grew on his face as he sneered.

     “Lies spread like dye. It seeps into the truth, twisting the perception of your true colors. Though it may fade with time, it’s certain to leave a permanent mark on your reputation.”

     It was difficult to follow The Prime’s words sometimes. But this time, Haldric felt he sort of understood the meaning.

     They turned into a final corridor.

     Children raced between the benches. Although chaotic, their games followed a kind of structure, watched over by a few older ones who sat nearby, giggling between whispers.

     They didn’t look like hostages.

     “The best hostages don’t even know they are one.” Note’s words rang in his head.

     “They’re not hostages,” The Prime assured. His watchful eyes never left Haldric. “The men and women above ground risk their lives to keep the city alive. Their families remain here, cared for. Their children, whether orphaned or not, are raised in safety and given the best education we can provide, which ensures higher-quality job assignments.” The Prime sighed. “Though I’m ashamed to admit it, none of this will fill the void in their hearts, but it is regretfully the best I could do.”

     Haldric scanned the rows of matching homes again, his mind catching on the details. The emptiness. The quiet grief beneath the stillness.

     “Hunters die, Haldric. More often than I wish to admit. A surface storm takes them. A beast strike. Or worse, other Hunters. Some never return because it’s safer that way. Their families could become a target if a jealous or defeated Hunter discovered a family connection.”

     The Prime paused, watching a little girl braid another’s hair.

     “Even when a Hunter lives, their absence becomes permanent. Some families wait five years… ten. Eventually, they grieve for them like the deceased.”

     A boy tripped and fell on the stones. Before he could cry, two patient adults came forward and helped him up gently, with practiced care.

     The Prime continued walking.

     “You already know about the two-child rule. But what happens to the orphans of Hunters?”

     That stopped him cold.

     What’s going on? It’s like he knows what Note told me.

     The Prime gestured toward the quarter without pausing.

     “They’re raised here. By the upper class. Everyone here experienced loss. Families of Hunters take in the children of the fallen, or those whose parents never returned. It isn’t luxury, it’s gratitude.”

     Haldric’s eyes narrowed as he considered the words.

     “You said ‘never returned.’ What happens to the family of a Hunter who abandoned the city?”

     The Prime sighed.  “A stone can only weather the storm for so long before it cracks. And though its task is to shield others, even stones can be overwhelmed, rolling away in search of shelter of their own. Who are we to blame the pebbles left behind, when we failed to be the shelter the stone needed?”

     It made a strange, compassionate kind of sense. Haldric felt his stomach twist. Why couldn’t he quiet the doubt in his heart? After all, everything made sense.

     What am I missing?

     Haldric was unconvinced.

     “So this whole upper class is just a glorified orphanage?” He just wanted reassurance, but the words that came out were sharper than he intended.

     The Prime let out a quiet breath. He didn’t appear offended, but he wasn’t amused either.

     “That’s an oversimplification, but not entirely wrong.”

     Haldric stayed quiet, waiting for The Prime to continue.

     “Though I never understood why they declined the relocation invitation, you had your Grayparents to look after you. Many are less fortunate. The family of Hunters… the orphans of miners…” The Prime’s voice softened. “This is the only part of the city where they’re allowed to be children. That’s what this district protects.”

     Haldric let the ration bar rest in his hand. He didn’t open it.

     “Then where is the bolting food going?” He lashed out, unsure what to believe anymore. “I saw the ledgers! So much food is allocated here! The only other location that gets that much is the general education program!”

     “Do you still not understand, Haldric? Everyone must have a role. Those who once raised children of their own, those who lost sons or daughters to the surface, they’re reassigned to care for the little ones who lost everything.”

     He stopped, turning to Haldric fully.

     “But what about a child’s role? To dream. To give others a sense of purpose. These are often the most difficult tasks, yet children accomplish them effortlessly.”

     The Prime gestured toward the surrounding homes.

     “This place isn’t about indulgence or privilege. It’s about healing and meaning. You asked where the food goes, but didn’t you answer that yourself? Why would the Noble Quarters, which you called a ‘glorified orphanage,’ receive a food allocation similar to the education program?”

     Haldric felt it finally click into place.

     “I’m a bolting idiot. It’s for the children.”

     “To both statements, yes,” The Prime said with a grin. “Though it’s true everyone gets a little more food here, they’ve earned at least that much. Still, the bulk of it goes to the children. We as leaders have a responsibility to keep them happy and alive. We must give them a chance to grow into something more than grief and loneliness.

     “The Noble Quarter was my first project as The Prime. I had believed I was so clever. The name refers to the noble sacrifice their loved ones made for this city. Not some bureaucratic nobility with an unjustifiable entitlement toward indulgence.”

     The Prime grit his teeth, face flushing red. Anger boiled behind his eyes, veins rising along his neck. But just as quickly as it came, the fury vanished without a trace.

     Haldric took a step back. He didn’t mean to but—stone me! What was that?

     “People will twist anything,” The Prime said quietly. “You give someone shelter, they call it a cage. You give someone food, they call it leverage. You try to build peace, and they’ll say you’re trying to break them. That’s why I don’t waste time defending myself anymore.”

     He began walking again.

     “Intentions don’t matter when no one’s listening. So I stopped talking and kept building. ”

     Haldric said nothing at first. For a moment, he almost believed it. Every word sounded right. Every justification felt earned. But something still clawed at the back of his mind. His stomach growled, a quiet betrayal that brought it all rushing back: the breeding ledgers, the intentionally reduced meat orders, his starving Grayparents.

     You say you care about these people. And I want to believe you. I want to believe your intentions are good. But if you truly care about all of us… why the lie?

     He looked at The Prime and spoke with a calm he didn’t feel.

     “There is no shortage,” Haldric said. “We both know that. So why do we all go hungry? Why force the city to starve if it’s not about control?”

     The Prime leaned back slightly, watching Haldric with a look that hovered between patience and quiet expectation.

     “You think I’m cruel,” he said. “Because I control how much food enters the city. Because I ensure there is no excess. But tell me, Haldric. Do you know what happens to a body when it’s overfed?”

     Haldric gave a dry look. “Let me guess. They’d be happier?”

     The Prime exhaled through his nose.

     “Chronic disease. That’s the first problem. Excess weight strains the heart, slows the body, increases the risk of illness. High blood pressure, failing organs—an early death.” His fingers tapped once against the table. “And in a world like ours, that is not a luxury we can afford.”

     Haldric’s stomach twisted.

     “There’s also the strain on medical supplies,” The Prime continued. “Every individual who deteriorates due to their own gluttony takes resources from those who suffer through no fault of their own. Every medicine, every ration, every breath wasted on a body that was compromised by choice.” His eyes flicked toward Haldric. “And in a city with limited means, every unnecessary loss weakens us.”

     Haldric swallowed hard.

     The Prime leaned forward.

     “And what happens to a society when its people grow heavy? They slow. Productivity collapses. They take more than they give. They demand comfort over discipline. And over time, they become incapable of standing when the world demands they fight.”

     Haldric clenched his fists.

     “You think I’m cruel. Perhaps I am.” The Prime’s gaze locked onto his. “But tell me, Haldric. Have you ever seen a single obese Hunter?”

     Haldric hesitated, but said nothing.

     The Prime gave a small nod, as if that silence was all the confirmation he needed.

     “Do you know what happens to a body when it reaches the surface?” he asked. “You’ve felt it. The way your lungs expand. The way your muscles adapt. The way your heart strains against itself until it can withstand the weight of the world above.”

     Haldric swallowed. He had felt it. The burning. The pressure. The way his bones seemed to shift inside him.

     The Prime exhaled slowly.

     “Now imagine if you carried even a fraction more weight than you should.”

     Haldric’s stomach turned.

     No. It couldn’t be—

     “The expansion would kill you,” The Prime said. His voice was grim. “Your lungs would collapse before they could stretch. Your heart would fail before it could strengthen. Your body would crush itself from the inside out.”

     Haldric’s hands curled tighter.

     “That’s not—”

     “It is true,” The Prime cut in. “Do you think we don’t have records? Do you think we don’t know exactly what happens when someone is unprepared for acclimation? We learned the hard way, Haldric. We lost lives. We watched people suffocate because their bodies weren’t fit to withstand the change.”

     The words landed like stone.

     The Prime pressed on, his voice even.

     “If I allowed overindulgence, if I permitted the city to gorge itself into lethargy, it wouldn’t just be a matter of laziness or inefficiency.” His eyes darkened. “It would be a death sentence.”

     Haldric’s breath caught in his throat.

     The Prime’s tone didn’t rise. It didn’t need to.

     “This city survives because I do not allow weakness,” he said. “You may hate me for it. You may think I'm a tyrant.” His jaw shifted slightly. “But I would rather be despised than watch my people choke to death in their own skin.”

     The silence that followed was total.

     Haldric didn’t move. Couldn’t speak.

     Because for the first time, he had no idea if The Prime was wrong.

     It all made sense. Too much sense. The puzzle was snapping together too quickly, every piece falling into place with brutal logic.

     But something still gnawed at him.

     The words slipped out before he could stop them.

     “This makes sense for Hunters. For those who will go to the surface. But most people in Stormhaven will never leave the city.” His throat felt tight. “Why must they suffer too?” He looked down. “Why must my Grayparents?”

     Something flickered in The Prime’s expression. A shift. Guilt, maybe. Or sorrow. It vanished too quickly to be certain.

     “Because they might have to.”

     He let the silence breathe for a moment, then stood.

     “Come.”



***



     Haldric followed in silence as The Prime led him down the hall toward the Second Floor. Aware of his own ignorance, he figured he’d get more answers by listening with an open mind rather than resisting every piece of information that challenged his assumptions. After all, the more he listened, the harder it became to defend his own conclusions.

     The air thickened with the faint echo of workers below, the clang of stone tools and the steady murmur of motion. The city continued around them, unaware of where they were going. 

     Then The Prime turned left, toward the Gate.

     Haldric’s pulse picked up. He knew this path.

     The guards at the checkpoint stepped aside without hesitation. A heavy groan shuddered through the chamber as the first set of doors unlocked, revealing the path to the surface.

     A long climb.

     Yet as they began, the steady pace through the old tunnel felt like an exercise Haldric’s body had long since surpassed. His muscles didn’t burn. His breath stayed even. But his heart still raced, as if it disagreed.

    The stone walls smoothed around them, no longer jagged but almost natural in shape. The ceiling stretched higher. The air thinned.

     As they approached the final gate, Haldric noticed subtle changes in the stonework. A faint seam ran horizontally across the massive barrier. The Prime’s lips curved into a subtle smile.

“You should appreciate this innovation,” he said, his voice carrying a hint of pride. “We’ve just completed my newest gate design. If anything manages to breach the lower half, the upper section will release and come crashing down, eliminating any intruders instantly. Another layer of protection to keep this city safe.”

Haldric blinked, trying to mask his surprise. It hadn’t been long since he last surfaced. When had The Prime found time to make such a drastic improvement?

     The Prime raised a fist and twisted it, giving a sharp signal.

     Four guards responded immediately, each gripping a separate crank embedded in the stone wall. With synchronized effort, they began to turn the mechanisms.

        The gate groaned open in two halves: the top half rising upward and the bottom half lowering into the ground, like the opening jaws of a giant stone mouth. A rush of dry wind surged through the widening gap, sizzling like the aftermath of a BoltStone. The scent of scorched rock choked Haldric’s nose, cutting off a deep inhale in its tracks.

        Morning light pierced the dim cave, though it lacked the strength and heat of Haldric’s last journey to the surface. He squinted as the stone lips parted further, the tunnel’s mouth drawing a breath. A gust of air rushed through, whistling as it curled around the stone teeth of stalactites and stalagmites before brushing against Haldric’s skin. 

     He froze as the world exhaled, gentle, like a whisper. The timid but curious breeze crawled up his arms and across his face. The city’s ZephyrStones, which circulated air, could never imitate this strange sensation. It felt real. It felt alive. It felt wrong.

      So wrong that Haldric missed what The Prime was looking up at.

      Above them, stretching wide and terrifyingly close, loomed another planet.

      The second world hung over Ravour like a ceiling, green continents swirling beneath thick clouds. Rivers shimmered like silver threads, glinting under a distant sun.

      The Prime inhaled slowly and said, “Ferta Mathair. One of the six planets that pass us each week.” His voice was quiet. “Isn’t it beautiful?”

      He didn’t look at Haldric. His gaze remained fixed upward. “A planet that doesn’t try to kill you.”

      Haldric forced his gaze away from the sky.

      “Why are we here?” he asked. “What did you want to show me?”

      The Prime breathed out. “We were never meant to be here.”

      “What do you mean?” Haldric squinted, rubbing his tired eyes as they adjusted to the giant SolarStone peeking above the horizon.

      The Prime didn’t answer right away. His eyes lingered on the planet above.

      “Ravour, our planet, is dying,” he said at last.

      Haldric stilled.

      The Prime’s voice remained emotionless. “Acclimation is getting harder.” His breath was even, controlled. “It once required only half a day of decreased food to guarantee safe acclimation. Then weeks. Then months. Now, a permanent decrease in food. The surface demands more from unacclimated people each year. And the more it demands, the fewer people survive. Their lungs collapse. Their hearts rupture. It’s unpleasant to witness.”

      Haldric’s throat tightened.

      “I… I didn’t know,” Haldric stammered.

      “That’s why the food shortage exists,” The Prime continued. “It isn’t a punishment. It’s a requirement. If we don’t keep people slim, they won’t be ready when the time comes.”

      Haldric’s chest felt tight.

      The Prime exhaled. “But even now, it’s not enough. Eventually, people will starve long before they’re thin enough to survive acclimation. At the rate we’re going, the human population will go extinct in a matter of years.”

      He paused, then added, “You’re already acclimated, Haldric. This doesn’t apply to you. Any acclimated could eat in excess, but would that be fair to the others?” His expression didn’t change. “This is why I eat the same rations that I ask them to eat.”

 “Something is wrong with that explanation,” Praew said in his mind.

Yeah, there must be more to it, he thought.

Haldric didn’t bother to hide his suspicion. “Then ration just the children until their labor roles are assigned. Only Hunters and soldiers who reach the surface need to worry about acclimation, right?”

The Prime regarded him in silence for a moment. Then his gaze shifted toward the horizon. “Ten noble beasts possess the strength to drive all humankind from the surface. Just ten.” He paused. “Twelve years ago, one of those foul creatures had finally been slain.”

Haldric perked up. “They… they can really be killed?” He couldn’t hold back his excitement.

“Yes,” The Prime said. “They can be killed. Now only ten remain.”

Haldric frowned. “You mean nine.”

The Prime looked at Haldric, face devoid of emotion. “No, I do not. The noble beasts… have since learned to reproduce.”

Haldric tasted blood. When had he clenched his jaw so tight?

The Prime looked back toward the imposing city gate, his face stoic. “A single addition to their ranks could spell the eradication of our species long before acclimation has a chance to.”

Something cold gripped the base of Haldric’s spine.

This explained so much, yet… Haldric looked down, jaw still clenched. 

 “The Prime dodged your question,” Praew pointed out in his head.

 True, he thought, why my Grayparents? Most citizens will never reach the surface. Why are they forced to starve as well?

      He looked up sharply.

      “Earlier, you said ‘ready when the time comes.’” His voice was rough. “Ready for what? How can we stop this?”

      The Prime didn’t answer.

      Haldric’s frustration flared, but before he could push further, something shifted in the corner of his vision.

      A figure made his throat clench.

      A white-haired girl, small and weightless in the wind.

     Haldric knew her but wished he didn’t. His pulse slammed in his ears as his mouth moved before he could stop it.

     “You again!”

     The Prime turned. “What?”

     Haldric went still. A sick realization twisted behind his ribs.

     The Prime still couldn’t see her. He showed no acknowledgment of the pale shape darting across the rocks. Hadn’t seen her hands clap together. Hadn’t heard her laugh, a surprisingly dainty tone, when the silver beetle with glowing wings flew through her hand.

     Haldric squeezed his eyes shut. She wasn’t real. Just like the voices in my head. He snapped his gaze back to her.

     Still there, pretending she could catch it again.

     Haldric cleared his throat. Ignore her.

     His fingers curled into his palms as he forced himself to face The Prime. “Nothing.”

     The Prime narrowed his eyes but didn’t press.

     Haldric inhaled, forcing the apparition from his mind. His voice came out rough. “What can we do?”

     The Prime’s answer was simple. “We leave.”

     For a moment, the word didn’t feel real. Leave? Haldric’s mind turned it over, searching for the shape of it. The idea felt so… unheroic. How could he even entertain the thought?

     His voice came quieter than he expected. “Leave… the planet?”

     The Prime nodded once. “Like I told you before, Ravour is not our home. There are ways out.”

     Something in Haldric’s stomach twisted. Way out, escape, running.

     “Coward.” Asher’s voice this time. 

     He wanted to argue, but information was more important.

     Haldric swallowed hard. “How?”

     The Prime exhaled. “There are three portals,” he said. “Hidden somewhere on Ravour. They were built long before our time, and each leads to a different fate.”

     Haldric stiffened. “Portals?”

     The Prime’s gaze didn’t waver. “The ruins describe them as doors, placed long ago. One leads to Morlorn, the planet of death. One leads to the Origin. And one… leads to Ferta Mathair, the planet of nature where we came from.”

     Haldric felt his pulse in his throat as he looked up. “Ferta Mathair… so that’s where we were taken from by the thief?”

     The Prime nodded. “Stormhaven is only one of three human cities still standing,” he said, his voice low, burdened. “Three. And yet we waste what little strength we have fighting each other over scraps.”

     Haldric stayed silent, watching him. 

     “I’ve reached out,” The Prime continued. “Tried to form alliances. But they either don’t believe the portals exist… or they think it’s a ploy to invade.” He grit his teeth. “Bolting fools.”

     He turned then, facing Haldric fully. “There are three kinds of Hunters. Beast Hunters, stone Hunters, and resource Hunters. But the world forgot the first kind.”

     Haldric frowned. “The first kind?”

     The Prime stepped forward, voice harder now. “Portal Hunters.”

     A beat of silence passed between them.

     “We need them again, Haldric.”

     Haldric’s brow furrowed. “But… how is it possible no one’s ever found one? Not a single portal?”

     The Prime’s eyes held a quiet, tired fire. “Who says no one has?”

     Haldric hesitated.

     “If you found a way out of this wretched world,” The Prime asked, “and barely survived the journey to reach it, would you come back? Would you risk everything to tell others? Or would you stay, live, and leave the rest behind?”

     “I would return,” Haldric said without hesitation. “Even if it cost me my life.”

     The Prime studied him for a long moment, then nodded, almost to himself. “That,” he said softly, “is why I chose you.”

     Silence stretched between them.

     What a strange man, Haldric looked toward The Prime. He had noticed long ago that in moments when anyone else might show a flicker of emotion, The Prime became as still and cold as stone. It was as if, in trying to hide his feelings, he revealed them all the more. His stone heart had been eroded by a storm of emotions beneath.

     The Prime broke the silence. “The world needs you, Haldric. The world doesn’t need another strong arm or cold strategist. It needs a kind heart willing to risk everything. I… will not suffice.”

     Haldric kept his focus on The Prime, but something was wrong. The wind didn’t stir. The world around them was still. Too still. Then a flicker at the edge of his vision.

     He tried not to acknowledge it, but his eyes betrayed him, glancing in her direction before snapping back to The Prime.

     The girl stepped forward without a sound. She was close now. Too close.

     Every muscle in Haldric’s body forgot how to move as she leaned in, tilting her head, studying him.

     His skin prickled. He willed himself not to react. 

     “Control your breathing,” Asher warned in his head.

     Her head moved slightly, face inches from his. The pale strands of her hair didn’t sway, as if untouched by the wind. Her gaze flicked over his features, tracing them like she was trying to remember.

     The breath of a soft whisper tickled his ear. “You look so much like him. It’s uncanny.”

     Haldric turned to face her, but a deep, instinctive tension curled in his spine, urging him to run. Without meaning to, his foot slid back. The retreat was small, barely a shift in weight, but it felt cowardly. He was acclimated. Why should some imaginary girl who couldn’t even hurt a beetle scare him?

     The air felt colder as a shudder ran through him. He glanced back at her, but she was gone.

     The silence stretched.

     The Prime looked confused as he stared at Haldric.

     Did he see her?

     “Uhh,” Haldric choked out, “how did you learn about these portals?”

     “The ruins I told you about earlier.” The Prime’s reply felt terse.

     No, he definitely didn’t see her. Great, I really am crazy…

     “The ruins speak of the portals’ existence,” The Prime continued, “but not their locations. We know only that they are here… somewhere. The world has not been very accommodating during our search.”

     Haldric remained focused. “And what if we don’t find the right one?”

     The Prime met his gaze. “We’ll go extinct.”

     This was it, wasn’t it? The truth behind the biggest mystery. The real reason they all had to starve. Hunter or civilian, The Prime Archon intended for everyone to leave this planet together. Everyone would have to acclimate.

     What was a little hunger compared to the preservation of our species? What’s a little hunger compared to never living underground again?

     Haldric swallowed. “I… I understand.” And he did. Truly, he did.

     He steadied himself. “What can I do? How can I help?”

     The Prime met his gaze. “You can start by putting a little faith in me.”

     Again, fear spiked through him. His shoulder jerked just slightly, but enough for the sweat gathering at his temple to slip, trailing cold down his cheek. He clenched his jaw, forcing himself still.

     The girl was perched on a boulder, legs swinging. Watching.

     His fingers curled at his sides. He could ignore her. He had to.

     Her head tilted, eyes locked on him.

     The Prime… did he say something? Haldric forced himself to look away, just in time to see The Prime gesture toward the gate.

     Haldric nodded. Without another word, he turned and stepped back through the gate, descending into the city.



***



     The Prime watched The Heir Archon go. The stone doors rumbled as they sealed shut behind him.

     He sighed. Then, without turning, he spoke. “Must you do that to him?”

     A soft laugh rang out behind him.

     “How disturbed must one be to find amusement in the torment of others?” Arthur asked.

     “You’re the one who pretends I don’t exist,” she teased.

     The voice was closer now, but he didn’t turn. He never did.

     She would be perched on a nearby boulder, legs swinging, weightless as ever. Watching. Always watching.

     The Prime’s voice remained steady. “Are you certain he’s the one? What made you choose him as my successor?”

     A smirk flickered at the edge of his vision.

     Her.

     The white-haired phantom that refused to be forgotten.

     She swayed on her heels, hands tucked behind her back, watching him with that insufferable amusement. Like she found it hysterical to linger where she didn’t belong.

     “Oh, no reason,” she murmured. “I just like his name. It reminds me of someone…”

     Of course it did.

     Slowly, deliberately, he turned toward her.

     His glare was sharp. A silent demand to stop playing games.

     She only smiled wider.

     Of course she did.

     Tilting her head, she hummed. “Oh, come on,” she teased. “You don’t trust me?”

     He folded his arms. “Trust you? Don’t presume we are allies, girl. I will never forgive you. I only care about saving my people.”

     She hummed. Not offended. If anything, entertained.

     “Oh, how noble…” she mused. “Yet you lie to the boy.”

     His jaw tightened. “When did I speak falsehoods?”

     Her smirk widened. “A lie of omission is worse than a lie of volition.”

     Compose yourself, Arthur.

     “Then how about you stop omitting what you really know, girl?”

     She snorted. Then, like mist in the wind, she was gone.

     The Prime let the silence settle. He uncrossed his arms.

     The passage was empty again.

     Good.

     Without another glance, he turned and walked back into the depths of the city.













Chapter 15 - Resolve and Reunion





“I saw it in the eyes of the next generation, those who will carry the spear after me.”





     The girl was still there. Not physically; Haldric hadn’t seen her since the gate. But she lingered. The image of her stare had seeped into his thoughts, staining the back of his mind in a way that refused to fade.

  He sighed, dragging a hand down his face. Get a grip. She wasn’t real.

  He focused on the real problem. The Prime.

  The man was ruthless, calculating, maybe even manipulative. But Haldric was not a fool. The Prime was no villain. He was a coward. Why do we have to leave the planet? This is our home too. We should learn from those disgusting creatures and unite our species. If we all acclimated, there’s no way we could lose. Then maybe he would finally have a chance to court—

  The realization settled like a stone in his gut.

  Praew had been right. Stone me, Praew was right! Stormhaven was suffering. People were barely surviving. The shortages were faked for their survival. But… The Prime Believed it was a necessity.

Haldric had been a complete Ratrod to Praew, but he needed to tell her everything. She needed to know the truth.

  What she really needed—no, what she deserved—was an apology.

  His pace quickened as he made his way through the winding corridors of the lower districts, each step pressing heavier than the last. He hadn’t seen her since yesterday.

  Guilt churned in his stomach.

  She was going to be smug about it, he was sure. She’d smirk and cross her arms. “Zephyrs take me! I told you so, you stone-headed boulder!” Praew chastised in his head.

  Haldric sighed, rubbing his temple. Yeah, I deserved that.



***



  Finally, he reached her family’s home. He took a breath, steeling himself, then rapped his knuckles against the stone door. It opened almost immediately.

  Praew’s father stood in the doorway, his lined face breaking into relief the moment he saw him.

  “By the stones,” he sighed, stepping forward as if to embrace him. “You’re safe. We were so worried.”

  Haldric blinked. Worried?

  He barely had time to register it before Praew’s mother appeared beside him, hands clasped tightly. “Where have you two been?”

  Two?

  Haldric hesitated. “We… two?” His voice came slower than he meant it to.

  Praew’s father frowned. “Isn’t she with you?” He poked his head past Haldric, looking behind him. Searching.

  Haldric felt his breath stall.

  “You were the last one to see her,” her mother added, her voice laced with quiet fear. “No one could find either of you since yesterday and… Hal?”

  Haldric took a step back. Yesterday. The words landed like a punch to the chest. Praew was missing.

  His skin went cold. He hadn’t seen her since—stone me.

  “Hal, Dear!” Praew’s mother called out.

  He left without a word. His mind was already racing. Why do I have such a bad feeling about this?

  “Wait, Hal!”

  Was someone calling out to him? No. Ignore that. Focus.

  He’d been with The Prime ever since separating from Praew last night. There wasn’t enough time for The Prime to grab her and catch him snooping. What if… someone else caught her committing treason?

  He headed toward the city jail, turning over every possibility. You better not be here, Praew. He wasn’t even sure if he had authority here, but he’d try anyway.

  The guard snapped to attention as Haldric approached. He straightened his posture and spoke in the most authoritative tone he could muster, just like he’d seen The Prime do.

  “I require your arrest reports and inmate ledger. Yesterday to present.”

  “Sir?” The guard blinked.

  Haldric’s voice dropped lower, colder. “I hope you don’t intend to make me repeat myself, soldier. It would be… unfortunate to learn you deliberately ignored the Heir Archon.”

  The poor man’s eyes nearly popped out of his skull. “N-no, Heir Archon, sir! I’ll get them right away!”

  He returned quickly with the documents. Haldric read through them more than once, taking brief, petty satisfaction in seeing War Claw Bresdin’s name in there. But ultimately, the documents were useless.

  Nothing. Back to square one.

  He went to The Prime’s office, hoping for help, but the man wasn’t there. Probably still on the surface. Maybe best not to disturb him right now…

  Haldric paused in the corridor. What next?

  Praew wasn’t easy to take down. Not after her Special Forces training. He absentmindedly rubbed his throat where she had hit him last night.

  She was probably more skilled than him and Asher in every form of combat by now. It would’ve taken a serious fight to capture her. Blood. Noise. Someone would’ve heard something. Right?

  He arrived at the infirmary and used his title to get immediate access to the medical records. But there was no record of her, nor any combat-related injuries among the wounded.

  Haldric slammed the infirmary’s thick booklet shut. She’s not here either? “Bolting stone me!” he yelled. Where in the ten stones are you? It’s not like you would vanish without a fight.

  That was not the Praew he knew.

  Unless the assailants were acclimated. But of course, that information wouldn’t be in just any accessible medical records.

  Startled by Haldric’s outburst, a chirurgeon cautiously approached. “Uhh… sir? Would you perhaps like to search through the classified records?”

  “The what?” Haldric snapped back.

  “Um—I mean…” The worker fidgeted uncomfortably. “The classified records, if it pleases you. Very few are authorized to review or edit them, but we all know they exist, sir. The Prime and Heir should have their own key to unlock the room.”

  I’m an idiot.

  ”You just figured that out?” Praew snorted in his head.

  Haldric pulled the key that hung beneath his shirt and unlocked an obscured room. Moments later, he cracked the classified check-in ledger open and flipped through the recent entries. If any acclimated Hunters or Special Forces operatives had been admitted, it would be noted.

  And there it was. Two notable admissions from early this morning.

  The first?

  Patient Name: [REDACTED]

       Superior Human Anatomy: Embedded

  Profession: [REDACTED]

  Injuries: Blunt force trauma, lacerations, coma

  Cause of Injury: [REDACTED]

  Haldric stared. He’d never seen this many redacted lines on a single document. That can’t be normal, right? 

      “Not likely.” Praew agreed in his head, “keep reading.” 

      He moved to the second entry. This one actually told him something.

  Patient Name: Godric Rivers

  Superior Human Anatomy: Acclimated

  Profession: Mythical Beast Hunter

  Injuries: Dislocated shoulder, impaled foot

  Cause of Injury: Combat training

  Haldric whistled. Combat training? In the middle of the night? Unlikely. Looks like Praew was right about you too.

  He had already taken down one Mythical Beast Hunter. Now it looked like he’d have to deal with the second.

  Silverwing Godric.

  Haldric’s jaw locked, his heart pounding. This time, he wouldn’t be caught unprepared. This time, Godric would be the prey.

  As he ran home, Haldric’s thoughts spun. Upstairs. Bedroom. Floorboards. He dropped to his knees and yanked a wooden box from beneath the bed.

  His father’s combat gloves.

  Each one had a single slot in the center of the palm for a Sifaralith Stone to be embedded.

  He had stared at these gloves for years. Obsessively. Unhealthily. He knew exactly what size of stone they needed. He pulled out a second box, which held pilfered goods from the previous night.

  He never should have taken them.
