  The Sifaralith Identification Program on the Fourth Floor was brutal. He’d seen it himself—how they made enslaved people handle stones, watching for a spark, a twitch, a scream. Waiting for a trigger. Knowing full well that an unknown trigger often meant death.

  Despite their origin, he had stolen a SurgeStone and a ForceStone of a Noble rank.

  He put the gloves on. His fists clenched, the gloves tightening against his skin. It was wrong. He knew that. But it was the only way to level the field. The only way to stand on even ground with men like Godric.

  His guilt didn’t matter now. Praew was missing.

  And he knew exactly where Godric would be.

  Haldric shoved the boxes back under the bed and was out the door before the guilt could catch up to him. His steps hit stone hard and fast.

  No more time to waste.

  It was time for answers.

  And time for Godric to face his wrath. 

  The training center was alive with movement. Six Special Forces recruits sparred in the open space, each clash of weapons and footwork controlled under the sharp eye of Silverwing Godric.

  Haldric didn’t slow until he set foot inside. The moment he did, everything changed.

  Combat froze to a halt as training partners hesitated mid-strike. A slow ripple of unease spread through the room.

  The look on Haldric’s face must have been frightening.

  His eyes locked onto Godric, who stood in the center of the ring, arms crossed. Unbothered.

  Haldric rolled back his sleeves. The gloves glinted, briefly exposing the stones embedded in his palms.

  A ForceStone in his right. A SurgeStone in his left.

  Haldric squeezed his left hand with as much force as his enhanced strength could muster. Triggered by force, the SurgeStone released a wave of power that washed over him. His muscles thirsted for action. 

  He could feel endless strength within every stride. He felt a sense of clarity as his pulse sharpened, his hearing improved, and time appeared to slow as his brain processed faster.

  Haldric smacked his chest with his left hand three times, a gesture that Hunters used to indicate their intent to fight.

  Silverwing Godric mirrored the gesture, but only twice.

  That’s odd. Shouldn’t it have been three to accept?

  Silence stretched between them, both knowing exactly what would happen next.

  Haldric clasped his palms together with all his strength. The contact made a surge link between his two stones.

  With a surged ForceStone, Haldric swung.

  CRACK.

       A violent, fist-sized pulse of concentrated kinetic energy hovered between them.

  Godric had casually caught the invisible force bare-handed, needing only a single step backward.

  Well… at least Haldric had thought he knew what would happen.

  He froze, too stunned to move. How did he even—

  “Your bolting form is all wrong,” Godric sighed, not even bothering to look toward Haldric. “Clench your teeth, kid.”

  Godric’s arm began to glow. A light blue symbol could be seen through his sleeve.

  What is he? Haldric braced himself, teeth gritting and eyes closed.

  Haldric felt a nudge on his outstretched hand. Then—nothing.

  He opened his eyes. “That’s it?”

  The suspended energy exploded backward.

  The shockwave rebounded, slamming into him twice as hard. His body launched backward, feet lifting off the ground.

  Then came the impact.

  Haldric’s back hit the stone wall, the force rattling his skull, air ripping from his lungs.

  His left palm slapped the wall behind him.

  The moment Haldric surged his body, the throbbing pain dulled instantly.

  Across the room, Godric straightened.

  Haldric pushed off the wall, rolling his shoulders.

  A slow brush of his sleeve. A breath.

  One step.

  Then another.

  Each movement deliberate, forcing himself forward, step by step, until he stood right in front of Godric.

  "Surprised you’re still standing," Godric said loud enough for the class to hear. "Guess you’ve grown some stones after all."

  Godric leaned in, lowering his voice to a warning. "Forfeit now. A surge crash after a head injury will drop you flat."

       “Don’t worry about me, Godric.” Haldric gave a slow, knowing grin. “How’s your foot holding up?”

  “You know too much.” Godric said in a smooth, emotionless tone. Now you’ve forced my hand.” 

  His demeanor had completely changed. He curled his fingers stiff, like the claws of a beast ready to tear flesh. His eyes widened, burning with an intent to kill.

  And he—stone me.

  Haldric’s legs began to shake uncontrollably.

  Silverwing Godric was smiling.

 Haldric’s instinct screamed to run, but no.

A prey that turns its back—

No. That wasn’t right.

A predator that flees—

Nothing. His mind went blank. 

Praew bolting needs me!

He clenched his fists and met Godric’s stare.

Godric never broke eye contact. Addressing his students, he spoke with a curt, bellowing voice. “Class dismissed.”

The trainees bolted without hesitation. Not a single one lingered, scrambling past each other, tripping over their own feet in their rush to flee.

”They probably sense danger," Praew suggested in his mind. “Stay alert!” 

  Godric cracked his knuckles. Slow. Deliberate. The sound echoed through the empty training hall. A smirk twitched at the edge of his lips.

  “A surged ForceStone? How cute. But I’m not Bresdin, kid. The Prime will need to send his Embedded if he plans to eliminate me!”

  Embedded? Haldric’s grip tightened around the stones. His body surged.

  “The Prime didn’t send me.” Haldric’s voice was sharp. “I wouldn’t want to bother him with your corpse.”

  He climbed to his feet, staggered, then charged. Fist raised, aiming straight for Godric’s face, he screamed,

  “Where is she!?”



***



       Praew found herself on the surface.

  The sky above was dust-swept and churning, dark with smoke. Distant cracks split the air like laughter from the gods. Before her stretched a cursed land, crater-pocked, waterlogged, and seething with hidden peril. Mud glistened under a colorless haze, masking the truth beneath its sheen. Great divots scarred the terrain where stones had fallen. They weren’t natural. 

     Monstrous chunks of blackened rock, shattered earth, and TerraStones rained intermittently from the volcano in the distance. One struck near the horizon. In an instant, a chunk of land silently vanished, swallowed by impact. The hole left behind steamed faintly. Water and sludge began to slither into its depths, disguising the wound beneath a false surface. The air shimmered with heat and danger.

  A middle-aged man, armored and weather-worn, stood a dozen paces ahead. His chest rose and fell with each breath, his blade enormous, jagged, and heavy, as if forged from the land itself. Rage warped his face. He pointed the weapon squarely at her.

  “I’ve been waiting to bolting kill you for a long time.”

  What surprised her most was that, instead of fear or anger, she only felt amusement.

  This little rodent dares?

  Then came the impact.

  A TerraStone struck without warning, swallowing the ground beneath her.

  The earth collapsed. Her body dropped into the mud, dragged down fast. Cold sludge closed around her limbs, locking them in place. She thrashed, but it didn’t matter. She was stuck. Only her neck and head remained above the surface.

  The man bellowed a laugh. “Even your god is on my side today.” He began approaching with his blade lifted, his silhouette growing as he neared.

  There was no mercy in his eyes. Only greed. And that sadistic, human satisfaction.

  “Praew,” came another voice.

  Who’s that?

  “Praew…”

  The voice didn’t come from the wind. It came from somewhere closer. Real.

  “Praew!”

  She gasped.

  Her eyes snapped open as she woke abruptly, finding herself somewhere else. That dream again?

  “Praew,” the voice said again, more clearly this time.

  She knew that voice.

  “Renwick?” she asked, sitting up fast. Her arm scraped against a wall. A sharp sting followed. She hissed, pulled back, and rubbed the scrape.

  The walls loomed close, their surface intentionally coarse. It was obviously designed to punish contact. The polished floor sloped slightly toward the door, slick with cold, sludge-tainted water. Her clothes had already drunk their fill, now clinging to her skin with the weight of filth. As she steadied herself with one hand, her palm slid against the smooth floor, the chill biting through her skin. She looked around, breath already tight in her chest.

  The room—no, the cell—was long and narrow. If she bent her elbows outward, they would graze both walls. Its length could fit two, maybe three people her size lying head to foot.

  A weak SolarStone glowed dimly from the ceiling near the gate, its light barely reaching the center. Shadows lingered at the edges, teasing her peripherals. Farther back, she spotted a second SolarStone embedded in the rear wall, unlit.

  I wonder what its trigger is.

  She tried to inhale deeper, but the air barely gave. No matter how she tried, she couldn’t seem to catch her breath.

  Her eyes fixed on Renwick, who gave her a weary smile. “You alright, Praew?”

       “Well, I was, before I saw your punchable face.”

     Renwick smiled and began to chuckle, but it broke off into a nasty coughing fit.

     Praew shifted toward him.

     Now that she looked closer, he barely resembled the boy she remembered. He was shaking, filthy, eyes wide and bruised. A long, tattered cloak hung off his frame like it had never been taken off. His hair clung to his face. His breathing was shallow.

  “Zepher’s take me! What happened?” she asked. “Where are we?”

  Renwick shook his head. “I don’t know.”

  “Alright... Do you remember how you got here?” Praew asked, determined to get answers.

  He rubbed his temples, voice low. “I was with Asher. We were about to head to the surface… then there was this blinding bright light. I couldn’t see a thing for nearly a day. The Hunters had to guide me around. When I finally got my vision back… I was here. Wherever here is.”

       Praew’s eyes widened. “Wait, Asher! Is he okay?”

  Renwick froze. His mouth parted, but no words came. His gaze dropped to the floor. A faint shake touched his lower lip. He gave a small, trembling nod.

  “Yeah. I… I think so.”

  He forced a brave smile.

       “Wait.” Her eyes widened. “The Hunter test was weeks ago! You’ve been here this whole time?”

  “Yeah. There’s no way out of our cell.” He looked away, voice quieter. “What about you? What’s the last thing you remember?”

  She closed her eyes, trying to force her way through the fog.

  “I was with… Mai Azadi. Then—” She winced, jaw tightening. “I don’t know. I can’t remember.”

  Renwick hugged his knees, his hands trembling at his sides. She studied him. He was trying so hard not to cry.

  “Renwick, did you see anyone else brought here?”

       He nodded.

     When? What happened to them? What did they look like? So many thoughts raced through her head. She was ready to blast him with questions but stone me, that look… Pure horror painted Renwick’s face.

     Praew didn’t hesitate. She scooted forward and wrapped her arms around him. “Hey, we’re going to be okay, Ren.”

  He didn’t answer at first. He just sat there, stunned. Then, slowly, he leaned forward and pressed his face against her torso, arms circling her waist. His body felt small like that, shaken, clinging, fragile.

  “I don’t think we are, Praew,” he whispered, voice muffled against her. “I think… we’re going to die here.”

  “No,” she said. “We’re going to be okay.”

  His voice cracked. “How do you know?”

  “Because.”  That was all she could think to say. It was hard to stay positive in a situation like this.

  Renwick was unconvinced. “Does anyone even know where we are? Is anyone coming to save us?” 

  She went quiet. “No,” she admitted, hoping he wouldn’t notice the tears beginning to run down her face and drip into his hair.

     Renwick’s shoulders began to convulse as he wept. She held him tighter. Praew felt his breathing grow shakier with each tremble. Her own breath broke into the same broken rhythm. Their sobs and sniffling filled the cell.

     They stayed in each other’s embrace for a full flicker.

 Renwick stopped abruptly, his shoulders freezing in place as his breathing snapped to an unnatural calm.

  He sighed. Praew’s arms loosened instinctively.

  Renwick stood, wiped his hand on his pants.

       Praew didn’t move. Her arms hung useless in the air, the warmth of the embrace still echoing in her chest

       What? 

  He stepped toward the cell door and knocked.

  Moments later, a pair of boots stopped on the other side.

  “Your orders, sir?” said the guard hidden from view.

     That voice… wait. Orders?

     Praew stared at Renwick’s back, confused. 

     Sir? 

     A chill ran down her spine. Her mind screamed.

     As if to confirm her fears, Renwick spoke. “No one else is coming to rescue her. We’re in the clear. Let’s prepare her for the doctor.”

     The cell door clicked. Only now did Praew notice the strange handle, bulbous and rounded like a giant toe, jutting out from the center. Renwick gripped it and gave it a twist. It turned with a stubborn, grinding resistance.

     Renwick looked back and gave Praew a wink before walking out of her cell.

     The cell door screeched shut.

  From behind the bars, Praew watched in silence. Renwick sniffed the air and grimaced.

  “Our guest reeks,” he said, shaking his head. “One might say we’re being unaccommodating.”

  The guard gave a short nod.

  That’s when she saw it. Some sort of weapon at the guard’s side. At first glance, it looked like a sword hilt, but instead of a blade, a rounded rod extended from it. Embedded near the base was something unmistakable: a BoltStone.

  Clink! 

     The guard struck the weapon against the outer handle of her cell. Electricity crackled across the shaft. Sparks hissed as the charge surged through the odd hoor handle.

  On her side of the handle, an embedded ZephyrStone flared to life. 

  A blast of wind shot out, catching her square in the chest. How did I miss that!? The SolarStone embedded in the far wall burst to life, lighting up the space behind her.

  The force alone wouldn’t have been enough to throw her, but the slick floor made it worse. Still kneeling, she began to slide backward, unable to stop herself as her knees scraped across the stone. The gust pushed her toward the back of the cell.

  “You wish!” she shouted, throwing her arms out, bracing both palms against the walls. I’m not playing your games, you bolting Ratrod!

  Rough stone bit into her palms, but she clenched her jaw and refused to budge. She planted her right foot forward, sole flat to the floor like a man about to propose. Only this vow was a promise to fight. 

     Another clink!

     Her shoulders and arms throbbed. She kept shoving against both walls, joints screaming, like she was all that prevented them from closing in.

  Renwick didn’t speak. He simply lifted a bucket and hurled its contents through the bars.

  The soapy water struck her with a sharp slap, the ZephyrStone amplifying the speed of the throw. Cold soaked her front, clinging to her clothes, trailing into her hair. Tears escaped from the corners of her eyes, not from pain, but from the wind lashing her face. She could have turned away, but she didn’t.

  She stared straight at Renwick.

  Coward.

  The gust died. The ZephyrStone dimmed. Her arms trembled. Her palms burned. She rubbed them briefly, glaring at Renwick through soaked strands of hair.

  Of course, he only smiled and gave the guard a nod.

  Clink.

  The weapon struck the handle again. Another burst of air slammed into her. This time, she lost her grip before she could catch herself. Her back hit the far wall, spine first. Knocking the air out of her. 

     Once the wind faded, the soapy water began to trickle towards the cell exit.

 Heat enveloped her from the SolarStone just above. It was the only comfort she found.

 She didn’t move. Right foot braced, left knee folded beneath her, arms crossed in front of her face. She stayed like that, anticipating another gust of wind. Praew was too tired to do anything else. So bolting tired.

 The cell door creaked open. Heavy cuffs clicked shut around her wrists, stone restraints embedded with a NullStone for some reason.

     The guard, dressed in all black with a familiar-looking mask, stalked toward her. That looks like Ghost Operations… but that can’t be. They’re not allowed to touch me. Not while I’m under Mai’s protection.

  “Mai Azadi is going to kill you,” Praew said.

  No response.

  The guard hauled her to her feet and guided her out.

     Praew was greeted by Renwick at the cell exit.

     The hallway was wide, flanked by stone cells. To her right, only a closed door. But to the left, she could make out at least two more cells on each side before the gloom swallowed the rest. Each one seemed separated by a thick slab of stone, maybe wide enough to fit five of her laid flat, head to toe. The spacing kept every prisoner isolated.

 “Did you have a pleasant bath?” Renwick asked, smirking.

 “Yes. Thank you for getting the stench of lying Ratrod scum off of me,” Praew shot back.

     Renwick gasped mockingly. He turned casually down the hall, arms laced behind his head as if they were out for a stroll. The guard shoved Praew forward to follow.

  “You wound me again, Praew. Only asking if Asher was okay, never asking about me? That’s true cruelty. When I heard you were coming, I put in quite the effort to make sure you received a warmer welcome than I ever did.”

  He shook his head, as if disappointed.

  Definitely still Renwick. He always loved to hear himself talk, that bolting Ratrod.

       A faint whimper drifted through the hall. Praew’s ears twitched.

  “Mommy?” a girl’s voice called. Fragile. Tear-soaked. “Mommy, where are you?”

     Praew’s blood chilled. She rushed to the cell opposite her own, pressing her face to the bars.

  Empty. Not even a shadow, only silence lingered. The echo of that cry lingered in her skull like a memory she didn’t own.

     Had she imagined it?

     Renwick hadn’t even paused.

  “You see,” he continued, “I never lied to you. I told you exactly how I arrived here. Almost died too. But after my recovery, well, let’s just say I found the doctor quite agreeable. Especially when I learned we shared similar tastes.”

     Praew’s eyes began to water. What is that smell? A charred, sickly scent crept up her nose, flaring as it climbed.

     Renwick chuckled. “Yeah, you’ll get used to the smell. The doctor’s work may be deadly, but it’s nothing short of brilliant. How could I not support such ambitious research?”

     A wet, hacking cough echoed from the next cell on her left. It rasped through the air, thick with phlegm, like whoever made it was barely clinging to life.

     Praew slowed instinctively to peer inside. No one was there. The cell was empty like before, yet the sound continued. It seemed to come from right behind the bars.

     Another cough followed. Then a low, guttural moan. Gasping breaths turned to choking and gagging.

     Is this really just in my head? But why do the cells look empty? What’s happening?

     The guard shoved her forward to keep moving. As she did, the choking ended in a long exhale. The cell went silent.

     That rotting scent had grown unbearable. A gush of salty saliva submerged her tongue. She spat and began breathing through her mouth instinctively to keep from retching.

     Renwick kept walking, unconcerned.

     He glanced back at her, voice laced with mockery. “I heard you made some new friends recently.”

  Praew snapped her focus toward him with a glare.

  “Aww, don’t look at me like that,” he said, giggling like a bolting moron.

  He stopped just before the final two cells. Turning toward her, he tilted his head and smiled faintly. “You should thank me. After all, I’m the reason your friends are still here.

  “Well… mostly.”

  Praew’s heart leapt. Her friends were here. They were okay.

  The guard opened the last cell and pushed her inside and locked it behind her. 

     Near the entrance, Junya sat with her hair matted, back hunched, arms tucked under her knees and legs arched in front of her. Her face was barely visible as she stared at the floor like a corpse.

    Zephyrs take me. She looks so broken.

    “Junya, are you okay?” Praew’s voice was a gentle whisper in the stale air.

    Without moving her head, Junya’s eyes flicked toward Praew and then snapped away.

    Is she angry? Praew feared an accusation might be hidden in her friend’s silence. Or perhaps it was her own buried guilt beginning to surface. Was training more important than protecting those you care for?

    Praew took a hesitant step forward.

    “Hey, Junya, I’m sorry it took so long to find you. I—”

    But Junya cut her off sharply, head snapping up to meet Praew’s eyes with a glare.

    “Now you pretend to be Praew? Get out of my head!” She clutched her hair at the roots, as if ready to rip out two fistfuls. “You’re not real!” she screamed. “Don’t you dare pretend to be her! She can’t be here. She can never come here!”

    Her voice trembled as she let go of her poor hair and bit down on her finger, trying to wake herself from a dream.

    “I’m real, Junya. I’m here.”

    Praew moved closer and tried to wrap her arms around her friend, but Junya flinched away.

    “No, no, no! You can’t be! Don’t be real, please!” Junya scooted backward until she neared the furthest wall of the cell. “If you’re real, then… then you’re as good as dead.” She broke off, gasping for breath. “And that will be my fault too. Please, don’t be real!”

    Praew followed slowly, her voice soft and reassuring.

    “Junya, it’s going to be okay. I’m going to get you—”

    She trailed off, the words dying on her lips as her eyes caught sight of something behind Junya.

    “What is that?”

    Stepping closer, Praew finally saw the source of the awful smell. A bloated, ruthlessly burnt corpse slumped in the shadows, the remnants of clothing identical to hers and Junya’s. Her stomach turned as the truth took shape in her mind.

    “Wannii!” Praew screamed.

    The cell door opened.

    “Ahh, what a tear-jerking reunion,” Renwick cackled behind her. “To be fair, I did say mostly.”

    She heard footsteps stalk toward her from the doorway.

    “You’re sick, Renwick! How… how could you?” Praew hissed through her tears.

    She spun and launched a fist at the approaching steps. The guard took the blow square in the mask, cracking it.

    “Ouch! And here I thought you were saving that kind of love for me,” Renwick called from the hallway, his voice thick with cruel amusement.

    Grabbing Praew with one hand, the guard lifted her with unnatural strength and threw her into Junya. Both slammed into Wannii’s corpse.

    Dazed, Praew watched as the guard yanked Junya out from behind and hurled her forward. In that cruel display of dominance, Junya’s hands and knees slammed into the floor with a slap.

     Sitting up to kneel, Junya’s hands came together, palms pressed tight like a desperate prayer. She then rubbed her hands back and forth frantically, her body trembling with each motion. “Please! Please don’t take me! Wannii needs me… I’m all she has!” she cried, voice breaking as tears streamed down her dirt-streaked cheeks.

     Ignoring her, the guard seized Junya by the arm, jerking her upward and began dragging her toward the cell’s exit. 

    Pushing herself up from the floor, Praew felt Wannii’s gooey, half-rotted skin smear along her arm and both her palms, leaving a cold, slimy trail, like blood tofu slithering down the throat.

     Staggering forward, she awkwardly caught Junya’s wrist with both her shackled hands and pulled. The slime on Praew’s hands coated Junya’s homemade protection bracelet, making it slick. As the guard pulled on Junya’s other arm, the bracelet rolled down her wrist and slipped over her hand. Praew’s grip finally broke when the bracelet slipped off Junya’s fingers and launched into the air.

    Praew’s feet slipped out from under her, and she landed on the ground hard.

    The guard dragged Junya toward the cell exit.

    Praew scrambled to get back up, but the door slammed shut in her face.

    “No, please! I’ll be good! Please don’t take me!” Junya’s voice strained with panic.

    A pillar blocked Praew’s view until something clattered into sight. The guard’s mask skidded across the floor and stopped just short of her cell.

    “Please, please—Wannii? You’re okay! But… but how? I thought—” Junya’s voice was cut off by the slam of a distant stone door.

    Praew stood there, shaking. She picked up the bracelet lying on the floor. A counterfeit BoltStone for protection. Yet it had done nothing for Junya when she needed it most.

    No… I failed to protect Junya when she needed me most. 

     Bracelet still in her hands, she slipped it on as a reminder of her own failure.

    Junya’s screams cut through the silence.

    I’m so sorry, Junya. I fear I’ll be joining you soon, and I hope that in another life, you find peace. You deserve it more than anyone.

    Mai, Haldric, you’ll take care of my family, right? Just… don’t come looking for me.







Chapter 16 - The Embedding



“I do not know the outcome, but I know one thing.”



“Let me get this straight," Godric said. "You used the Heir Archon master key to access the infirmary's classified medical records. That’s how you learned about my foot, and that’s all you actually know?”

     Haldric stared at the ceiling as blood pooled in his mouth, thick and bitter. He spat, the dark smear hitting the stone as he forced himself to sit upright. “Yeah,” he said weakly.

    His ribs burned, his jaw throbbed, and his entire body ached as if the stone itself had punished him.

    A boot nudged his leg. “Get up,” Godric said.

    Haldric blinked through the blur. Godric stood over him, arms crossed, back to that calm demeanor and the same maddening, persistent look of disappointment.

    Haldric hated that look. He tried to push himself up, ignoring the pulse of pain that followed. 

    Godric watched him struggle but made no move to help. “Bolting Archons,” he muttered, “they’re always the same. The way Praew spoke of you, I expected more. Bolting girl never did know how to trust the right people.”

    Haldric froze. 

   Godric shook his head. “She thought I was making my students disappear. Threatened me, actually. Said if anything happened to her, you’d be the one coming for her.”

    Haldric’s heart slammed against his ribs.

    Godric sighed. “I didn’t take the bolting girls, but I’ve been trying to track down who did.”

    “What?” Haldric coughed, his chest tightening. “Why didn’t you lead with that?”

    Godric scoffed. “Says the guy who hit first, asked questions later? I’ve seen a Tarnox in heat with more wisdom on a battlefield than you.”

    Sad thing is, he probably has, Haldric thought.

    Godric’s jaw tightened slightly. “Problem is, I can’t find them.” He shook his head. “I searched all of the Fourth Floor. No sign of them, except…” His voice lowered. “I could hear faint screaming from below it.”

      “Wait,” Haldric said slowly. “Who was the person you put into a coma last night? All their information was redacted.”

  Godric looked at him. “That,” he said, “was the deadliest person in the city. And I wasn’t the one who put her in that coma.”

  “Wait, then who did?”

  Godric shook his head. “I don’t know. And that’s what worries me.”

       Haldric felt a chill. Praew was caught up in something far more dangerous than he had ever realized. And if Godric had heard screams from below the Fourth Floor,

    Haldric stiffened. That could only mean…

    “There’s a Fifth Floor,” he breathed.

    Godric nodded. “I can’t find the entrance to it. There must be a hidden passageway somewhere.”

    Haldric’s pulse hammered. A secret passageway. An undisclosed location. Something clicked in his mind. A place. A possibility. A sickening certainty he didn’t want to have.

    He hoped he was wrong.

    He really, really hoped he was wrong. But, well, he wouldn’t be surprised at this point.

    He forced himself to his feet, pushing past the pain. There was no time for exhaustion. No time for hesitation. He had to move. He had to find her.

    Godric let out an exasperated sigh. “Yeah, yeah. I’ll go with you.”

    “You don’t have to—”

    “You’re only standing,” Godric cut in, “because I don’t enjoy beating up children. Do you think our kidnapper will show you the same mercy?” His towering figure looked down toward Haldric. “Don’t push your luck.” 

    Haldric didn’t argue. He just started moving.

    Please be okay, Praew!



***

The door slammed shut behind her.

The room was bright. Too bright. Praew squinted as she was shoved inside; the sudden shift from darkness made her vision blur. The room was a stark contrast to the cells outside. It was far too clean and organized. Trays lined the walls, holding neatly rowed instruments she didn’t recognize. A stairway wound along the wall to a location she couldn’t make out within the harsh lights. At first, Praew thought she was in a medical chamber, but no. It was far too quiet.

And then Praew saw her.

Junya lay, eyes wide open, strapped to a nearly vertical polished stone slab with her arms splayed out, wrists bound in thick restraints. Her ankles and neck were tied down as well, so not even her soul could run away.

But that wasn’t what made Praew’s blood run cold.

Something was sticking out of Junya’s forehead.

A Sifaralith, embedded deep, pulsing with a sickly glow. Junya herself was still.

Was she already—?

  Before Praew could finish the thought, Renwick led her to an identical stone table beside her motionless friend and strapped her down the same. Only able to turn her head side to side, Praew stared at her friend’s corpse.

  Junya was dead.

  Praew had known Junya wouldn’t leave this room alive. But knowing something and seeing it firsthand were two different things.

  She squeezed her eyes shut, but it made no difference. The sight was burned into Praew’s memory.

  And she was next.

  A voice slipped through the silence, drenched in quiet disdain.

  “Another aberration rendered useless. How profoundly unremarkable, no?”

  Praew didn’t move. She barely breathed. The voice wasn’t near her. It came from somewhere above, threading effortlessly through the haze of light streaks and rays.

  A sigh grew into a flicker of silence. Then, the silence was broken by the soft echo of descending footsteps. The sound circled her, each step closer than the last, like a predator closing in.

  “Feckless Ratrod,” the voice hissed, “this degree of incompetence is beyond untenable. What an inefficient and frivolous expenditure of my time, no?”

  A scientist came into view. He moved to the slab beside her and began unstrapping Junya’s body. With deliberate carelessness, he let the corpse drop to the floor with a thud.  

  Praew flinched as the scientist turned to her.

  “Do refrain from replicating this subject’s outcome. I’m disinclined to witness consecutive failings. You grasp that, no?”

  Praew looked away, only to find Renwick staring at her.

  “How could you do this, Renwick?” she shouted. “Do you really hate me that much? What did I ever do to you?! And Junya… she was innocent! She didn’t deserve this!”

  “Hate you?” Renwick asked incredulously. “I’m saving your life! I’m trying to make sure you don’t end up like—forget it.”

  He clenched his jaw, fists trembling at his sides, then turned and walked out of the room.

  She tried to move, but nothing gave. Each strap bit into her skin, cutting off circulation.

  Footsteps echoed again across the stone floor. Praew’s chest rose and fell too fast. She snapped her head the other way to look back at the approaching scientist.

  This was obviously a man who thought he was the smartest in the room. A man who enjoyed power over others. And right now, he was clearly enjoying this.

  Praew saw it in his smile. Too pleased.

  Once beside her, he drew in a slow breath, as if to relish the moment.

  “Predictable. The conscious mind clings to resistance even when statistical certainty dictates futility. Yet you persist. Fascinating, no?”

  His fingers trailed over her wrist, grazing the strap but not checking it. Testing.

  Praew didn’t flinch, but the scientist smirked, as if sensing her discomfort.

  “The tenacity of the lesser mind is its own tragic poetry. You will break, of course. They always do. The only variable is duration.”

  He leaned closer, eye to eye, just enough for her to feel the shift in the air. Just enough for her to smell the bitter tang of chemical oil on his skin.

  Then he grabbed her hair, inhaling like he was savoring something.

  Out of the corner of her eye, Praew caught a figure slip through the doorway behind him, clearly sneaking up on the scientist. Her pulse leapt.

  Renwick? He came back!

  The smirking madman raised an eyebrow and began turning to see what had stolen her attention.

  Trying to distract him, Praew locked eyes with the scientist and smiled. “You’re a coward.”

  The scientist froze, his smirk faltering slightly. Praew tilted her head as much as the straps allowed and gave him the most disgusted look imaginable.

  “I see you,” she murmured, voice steady and poisoned with amusement. “Men like you only touch women when they can’t fight back. I bet you don’t even know what to do with one otherwise.”

  He gripped her wrist tightly, his smile vanishing in an instant.

  “What a tedious display of defiance. The same primitive barbs, the same desperate bravado. Tiresome, no?”

  Her own grin widened. “Oh? Did I touch a nerve?”

  She saw it happen. The crack in his composure. The twitch in his jaw. The exact moment he lost control. He leaned closer, face just inches from her own.

  She smelled his pungent breath, never losing track of the shadowy figure creeping closer behind him.

  She spat directly into his eye.

  His curse barely left his lips before she slammed her head into his nose. The crack was satisfying.

  He staggered back, nose bleeding, teeth clenched.

  His face twisted as he spoke through gritted teeth, his voice full of raw contempt, absolute superiority, and final condemnation.

  “A squandered genetic anomaly, cursed with just enough sentience to be insufferable. No?”

  His hand wrapped around her throat.

  “You mistake toleration for limitation. I will rectify that.”

  “Any time now, Renwick!” she gasped.

  The scientist barely had time to react before his body went stiff.

  A splitting pressure pushed from within the creep’s throat, stretching the skin outward toward Praew. His eyes bulged, confused and panicked.

  A sword tip punched through the front of his throat, up through his jaw, and out his mouth.

  His grip went slack. His knees buckled.

  He slid off the blade, collapsing to the side, on top of Junya.

  Just as lifeless.

  Praew gasped for air, blinking up at the spot where Renwick stood.

  Only to find it wasn’t Renwick at all.

  The Prime Archon, sword in hand, sneered at the dying scientist.

  “It is absolutely deplorable monsters like you that give the rest of us a bad name,” The Prime said, his voice thick with disgust.

  Then, a breath. A shift in tone.

  “Are you alright, Praew?”

  She stared at him, swallowing hard. Her throat ached, her body was stiff and trembling, but the worst of it was over.

  She took a slow, shaky breath. “I… I am now.”

  The Prime? The Prime saved me?

  Her pulse hammered in her ears. She hesitated, then muttered, “Uh… thank you, sir.”

  He chuckled.

  “No need to thank me. How dare he think to sully the future heroes of this city?”

  “Uhh… right,” she mumbled, still trying to wrap her head around it. The Prime is saving me?

  She barely registered it before he stepped past her, moving out of sight, fiddling with something behind her head.

  “I… owe you an apology, sir.”

  “Hmm?” His voice sounded distant behind her.

  Praew took a deep breath. “I thought you were… well… an absolutely deplorable monster.”

  The Prime chuckled like someone only half-listening.

  What is he doing back there? Ohh, he must be looking for a way to release me!

  ”The straps have buckles here…”

  No response.

  She twiddled her fingers. “Uh… can you release me now?”

  A pause.

  “No, I cannot.”

  The words hung in the air—so matter-of-fact, so final.

  She swallowed hard. Alarm crept down her spine. “What…?”

  “Do you even know what’s happening here, girl?”

  His voice was measured, calm, like a man explaining basic facts to a child.

  She writhed against the straps, instinct clawing for escape, but nothing gave.

  The Prime continued without her response.

  “You probably only know about Common and Noble Stones.” His tone remained even. “But there are more. Prime. Sovereign. Mythical.”

  Another deliberate, dramatic pause before continuing.

  “There are humans who are, in essence, more beast than man. Some are born with a rare genetic mutation—a mutation that enables a particular Sifaralith Stone to be embedded into their body, much like the beasts who are born with theirs.”

  Praew’s body began to shake involuntarily. Her breathing grew difficult. She glanced at her friend’s corpse and the Sifaralith stone embedded in Junya’s skull. But… that… that wasn’t possible.

  The Prime and Renwick stepped into her line of sight. When did that bolting Ratrod return?

  She gritted her teeth. “I will kill you, Renwick! If I ever get out of here, I will make you pay! Both of you!”

  He refused to look at her as he handed a black pouch to The Prime, who then pulled out a BoltStone. Its polished surface gleamed under the dim light.

  The Prime lifted it between the three of them reverently, voice shaking as he spoke. “Exclusive to one of the ten beasts of legend. A Mythical BoltStone.”

  As if triggered by his voice, the stone began to glow bright red. The color flooded the room, overpowering the SolarStones within.

  A wave of fear and sadness overwhelmed Praew. “That glow!”

  “Yes.” The Prime nodded eagerly. His calm demeanor had begun to crack. “That red glow is proof of your compatibility with the stone. It recognizes you. But recognition is not enough.”

  He paused, staring at the tears that began running down her face.

  “You feel it, don’t you?” He continued with an excited smile. “Emotions that do not belong to you? That is the stone attempting to initiate the embedding process. The stone makes you feel the final emotions of the beast, just before its death. Do not fight it.”

  Praew stopped breathing. Her mind reeled.

  To a distant, buried memory. One she had assumed was just nerves.

  The Hunter Test. That same black bag.

  It had been glowing this same red.

  And she—she had felt this same raging storm of emotions.

  That test…

  It wasn’t about becoming a Hunter, was it?

  It wasn’t about skill at all.

  It was a screening process. A search for people. People like her.

  Her lips parted. A whisper. Barely a sound.

  “That’s impossible.”

  The Prime’s face softened. His expression became unnaturally paternal.

  “No, my dear. What’s impossible is the task you must face next.”

  He closed his hand around the Sifaralith and clasped his hands behind his back, pacing slowly before continuing.

  “You must synergize with the lingering will that the beast left within the stone. But the real trick is to match the intensity of those emotions. That is the only way to avoid ending up like your friends, who couldn’t even handle their Prime Sifaraliths.”

  The Prime shook his head in what could have been pity or disappointment.

  “You see, the rarer the stone, the slimmer the chance a human survives the embedding.

  “Common: forty percent. Noble: thirty. Prime: twenty. Sovereign: ten.”

  The Prime stopped just in front of her, gaze meeting hers.

  “Mythical, well, few believe the stones even exist.”

  Praew’s pulse roared in her ears. Her mind screamed at her to fight, to run, to do something.

  The Prime raised his arm above his head, eyes fixed upward as he admired the stone. The BoltStone glowed brighter now.

  He shook his head and sighed.

  “Yet, for the sake of our species, the risk must be taken.”

  The Prime slammed the BoltStone into her chest, just below the throat.

  Praew gasped.

  The BoltStone pulsed against her skin. Its red glow deepened to a bloody crimson that flooded the room with an intense heat. Praew tried to look down, but the straps locked her neck in place. She could just make out the stone’s new molten color between The Prime’s fingers while he continued to push.

  She screamed as the heat burned into her chest.

  Agony. Raw. Unrelenting. A shockwave of electricity ripped through her bones, searing through muscle, nerve, and skin.

  Everything faded to black.



***



  Praew found herself on the surface. The sky above was dust-swept and churning, dark with smoke.

  A middle-aged man, armored and weather-worn, stood a dozen paces ahead.

  “I’ve been waiting to bolting kill you for a long time, Boltrine!” he bellowed.

  This little rodent dares?

  Clack.

  A TerraStone struck without warning, swallowing the ground beneath her.

  The earth collapsed. Her body dropped into the mud, dragged down fast. Cold sludge closed around her limbs, locking them in place. She thrashed, but it didn’t matter. She was stuck. Only her neck and head remained above the surface.

  She cried in alarm.

  The surface faded.    



***



  Praew’s eyes snapped open.

  Even now, that dream torments me?

  But no, it was different this time. Praew had never heard the name Boltrine before, and she could finally make out the man’s face.

  Wasn’t that War—

  Lightning erupted, crackling, branching, arcing off the table, the walls, and the ceiling. The room flashed with searing light.

  The air filled with the sharp stench of burning flesh. She barely recognized that it was her own. Her vision blurred as fire seemed to melt through her.

  Nothing was spared. Electricity tore through every part of her. It started near her chest, racing down her limbs and spine until it burrowed into her very marrow.

  She continued to scream. It didn’t matter if she wanted to, it ripped out of her throat like it had been waiting.

  The glow dimmed, or was it her vision?

  Praew felt her consciousness slipping.

  This is it! I’m going to die! Zephyrs take me this is it!

  The world faded away.



***



  Boltrine found herself trapped on the surface. The wind ruffled against her exposed face and neck. She thrashed, but nothing gave.

  The man bellowed a laugh. “Even your god is on my side today.”

  What god? This dim-witted rodent thinks Haiyana’s hurl is the work of divinity?

  The man took a step forward.

  How could I be so careless?

  She thrashed more, with no luck.

  Love, where are you? I’m… I’m scared. I’m going to die aren’t I?

  She closed her eyes.

  The wind dispersed.



*** 



  When Praew came to, she kept her eyes shut tight.

  The Prime continued to push the stone against her as it melted her skin.

  “Don’t you dare give up now, Praew!” he seemed to call out from a distance.

  Wait, then who is—

  Praew opened her eyes to see no one touching the stone. It drove deeper into her chest on its own. The Prime and Renwick were climbing the stairway out of sight.

  They bolting left me! They left me to suffer and die alone!

  “I’m going to kill you bolting little rodents!” Praew screamed, her voice too loud to be her own.

  The BoltStone continued to discharge more fiercely than before.

  She thrashed and writhed, but the straps held firm. Her body arched off the slab, bones rattling from the force of the current.

  It felt like she was being peeled apart, piece by piece, burned from the inside out.

  Praew closed her eyes, fighting back tears.



***



  On the surface, the rodent began approaching with his blade lifted, his silhouette growing as he neared.

  There was no mercy in his eyes. Only greed. And that sadistic, human satisfaction.

  Boltrine shivered.

  My son, I can no longer fly with you. Please keep me close in your heart.

  My love, I fear I can no longer gaze upon your majesty, but I trust you will not forsake our pact. Blood for blood!

  Rodent, I’ll make sure he kills you!

  A majestic bird cry ripped from her throat and shot toward the sky. Cocking her head sideways to better eye the threat, she began to cast crackling streams of electricity toward him.

  The rodent dodged them effortlessly.

  But she knew he would. She was baiting him. He dodged again, but this time, his fate was sealed. A cry from above—her own echo—came crashing down on top of the rodent. The cry hit his exposed arm, successfully marking him. A yellow symbol appeared on his skin. She could sense her connection to it, and so would her mate, Boltrax.

  The man stopped just in front of her, sword raised, and without another word, he swung.

***

  Praew screamed again.

  The lightning surged more violently. It didn’t stop. It wouldn’t stop. Her body was unraveling, raw nerves singing in unbearable torment.

  She was going to die. Just like Boltrine.

  Boltrine… The thought cut through the pain.

  Every time Boltrine released electricity, she cried out first. Praew’s mind twisted, grasping at that moment.

  That was important, wasn’t it?

  That… wasn’t a coincidence.

  It was… triggered by sound!

  Her own voice was killing her, but she wanted to scream. She needed to scream.

  Her throat ached with it, her lungs desperate for release, but she clamped her jaw shut, grinding her teeth so hard she thought they’d shatter.

  The electricity weakened. Not gone entirely, but less.

  Her body still convulsed, her muscles still spasmed, her nerves still blazed with pain. But she had control now.

  She just had to survive it.

  Flickers. A flash. Or maybe forever. The pain dragged on. It never relented. It only wore her down, peeling at her will, digging in deeper, deeper, deeper. She clenched her teeth until she tasted blood.

  She held it in, silently enduring the greatest pain she had ever known.

  Then, at last, it stopped.

  All at once, the electricity died and the glow disappeared.

  A voice rang in her head, deep and ancient. “Who Dares To Desecrate Her Noble Stone? Answer This Exalted One, Or Await My Wrath.”

  Her body collapsed, limp, spent, shaking. Her chest rose. Fell. A breath. Then another.

  She was alive.

  She was ALIVE!

  And no one had come to save her.









Chapter 17 - Recorrection Program 



“Change is on the horizon. Who are we to stand in its way?”









Haldric couldn't believe his eyes. Carved out of the walls before him was a large balcony that circled a radiating chamber below.

And in that moment, he hated being right. 

The entrance to the Fifth Floor? It had been in The Prime’s bolting office this whole time.

When Haldric had been caught snooping before, it was only because The Prime had entered through a passage concealed behind some moving wall contraption in his office. At the time, Haldric had been too consumed by the shame of being discovered to question it. Too humiliated to notice what mattered.

Haldric and Godric crouched to sneak to the ledge of the balcony and peeked their heads just enough to look down. 

  Below, six masked statues ringed the chamber, half lost in the shadows. Near the center, in a spill of gore, blood, and bile, two bodies lay on the floor piled on one another beside a nearly upright stone slab, both bodies were twisted unnaturally.

  Haldric let out a silent sigh of relief to discover neither body was Praew’s.

  On the other side of the slab was the bolting Ratrod himself, The Prime Archon, completely unbothered by the gore before him.

  A wafting draft hit Haldric’s nose, causing his breakfast to surge up his throat and rest in his mouth. He shuddered and forced it back down.

  The Prime was a bolting sick man.

  He should have known. No, he had always known. Praew, Note, even The Prime himself had warned him in their own ways.

  Was the title of Heir truly worth it? Or, if he were honest, had he simply wanted a father so badly that he clung to denial? He’d wandered the city, pretending to search for Praew, while she needed him most. And for what? Just to avoid facing the truth?

  Stone me, The Prime could have just finished murdering people when he caught me snooping last night. 

  But those bodies below looked fresher. 

  If I’d suspected him sooner, could I have saved these two? One was a girl around his age. Praew will never forgive me if this was one of her missing friends.

  Haldric shook his head. Now is not the time for this! A true Hunter saves the living before mourning the dead. I need to find Praew!

  Asher’s voice popped into his head. “That bolting traitor! Please don’t let my father get away with this, Haldric!”

  Haldric gritted his teeth. Shut up, Asher! Are you really any better? Or did you forget the saying? ALL Rivers run cold.

  A figure entered the room below, pulling Haldric from… whatever that was.

  Was that Renwick?

  “What in the seven stones are you doing!?” Renwick yelled.

  Haldric flinched. How did he spot us?

  “Renwick,” The Prime said, voice flat, “I showed you the mural. When mankind grows fangs, claws, and scales, three will rise. Their hands will decide which force endures: the Beasts, the children of men, or the living breath of the world itself. The first life they choose to strike down will bind their fate. From that moment on, they will hunt the rest of that kin until none remain.

  “Now,” The Prime continued, “correct me if I’m wrong, but were we not threatened no more than a flash ago?”

  He pointed to something on the other side of the stone slab, just out of view. “Truly, would you risk our entire species over something as fleeting as unrequited feelings?”

  What unrequited feelings? Haldric didn’t know if he wanted his guess to be right or not.

  “Don’t you dare!” Renwick snapped, brandishing a sword toward The Prime.

  Where did he get the weapon from? Is he going to kill The Prime?

  The Prime didn’t seem to care, turning his back on Renwick to fiddle with whatever he’d pointed to earlier.

  Renwick raised the sword to swing.

  “Renwick,” The Prime said lazily, “drop the sword, stand down, and stand guard outside.”

  Renwick went unnaturally stiff, dropping the sword with a clang. “Yes, sir.” He turned and disappeared out the door below.

  Stone me, what just bolting happened?

  Godric started rounding the balcony to get a better view, so Haldric began to follow.

  “That bolting ratrod just doomed us all!” Godric hissed, staring at the stone slab before crouching below the railing a moment later. Haldric tried to peek his head up, but Godric pulled him back down. “Go home, Heir. Some things can not be unseen.”

  “No,” Haldric said. “I must… I have to see it for myself.”

  Godric nodded and let go of his shoulder.

  They found her.

  Praew was strapped against a slanted stone slab, nearly standing. Her wrists, ankles, and neck fixed in the same brutal restraints described in the medical records he had skimmed earlier. 

  Her head hung forward, lifeless. Blood streaked the front of her shirt, trailing down and pooling beneath her feet. Her skin looked colorless in the harsh light.

  Before Haldric could process what this meant, something else caught his eye.

  That…wasn’t a medallion, was it?

  Below her throat, something embedded in her chest reflected the light of the SolarStones.

  “Don’t panic,” Asher pleaded in his head. “If she’s bound, she might still be alive. Look for signs of life.”

   He’s right, Haldric thought.

  But she was way too still. Too limp. He was too high up to see whether she was breathing.

  And by the stones… all that blood.

  A rush of panic seized his chest.

  Praew’s head weakly turned to the side.

  His feet were already moving when Godric’s arm shot out, catching him before he could take another step. “Hold.”

  Haldric nearly shoved him off. “Move, stone you!” 



  “Look!” Godric hissed.

  Haldric followed his gaze, then stopped cold. Those six masked statues he had glanced over earlier… weren’t statues at all. Unnaturally still, masked figures stood watching the room below.

  They weren’t guards, nor were they Hunters. There was something else about them. Something wrong. 

  “Embedded Special Forces,” Godric growled quietly.

  Haldric’s pulse roared in his ears. He already knew what they were. Godric had told him earlier: some humans could fuse with Sifaraliths just like the beasts, and somehow that made them stronger.

  Godric growled. “No way through them.”

  Haldric’s teeth clenched. So what? Was he supposed to just stand here while Praew bled out? Every instinct screamed at him to go now, fight now, save her now — but Godric seemed certain they wouldn’t win.

  “Not yet, at least,” Godric whispered. “We need a distraction. Something big enough to lure them away.”

  Haldric’s mind raced. He shook his head in frustration. The Master Key around his neck brushed his chest.

  He grabbed his shirt, fingers wrapping around the key beneath it.

  Then it clicked.

  The Third Floor prisoners.

  Without hesitation, he turned to Godric. “I have an idea… if I can get to the Third Floor.”

  Godric nodded, but the fury on his face was… well, terrifying.

  I’ll take that as approval.

  “Then what are you waiting for?” Godric hissed. “I must reach her parents’ home to capture an Echo of their voices. So make your distraction right at the next flash. Do not let anyone see you. Then go home pretending like you know nothing.”

  His voice lowered.

  “Do you understand? Deny any involvement.”

  Haldric nodded. “I understand. But do you have another EchoStone I could use?”

  Godric rolled his eyes and pulled out both an EchoStone and a NullStone. “Here. These two are useless to me. They are both triggered by sound, so be careful.”

  Haldric nodded again, but before he ran off, he hesitated — looking back at Praew. “What will happen to her? It’s not like she can just hide in the city forever…”

  “I will take her to the surface,” said Godric. “I have connections with another city. She will be safe until you become the next Prime. Then she can return to her family.”

  Haldric nodded, only to hesitate again. “Please tell Praew… that… that I love her.”

  Godric stared at Haldric. “I’m not going to bolting say that, Heir. And would you get going? We don’t have all day!”



***



    Haldric moved quickly through the corridors of the city, footsteps light but urgent, the echo of them pressing at his ears. He kept brushing at his shirt as if to reassure himself the master key didn’t abandon him before becoming an accomplice in the highest level of treason.

     What if The Prime found the stones in the office? No. He’d hid them well. It would be impossible. And even if it wasn’t, it didn’t matter.

     The Prime had betrayed him first. Why Praew? Why would Arthur Rivers ever do that to her? No. Why would he do that to anyone?



     The image of Praew strapped against the slab clung to him like a weight around his ribs. Her head limp. Her clothes stained with blood. Her skin too pale in the harsh light. He swallowed hard. He couldn’t rid himself of what he saw.

     “This, Haldric, is your own fault,” The Prime accused in his head. “Foolish, you chose to believe my lies over the truth your friend made you witness. How unfitting of an Heir Archon.”

     He stumbled to a halt. Hearing Praew, Asher, and that woman in white was already unsettling. But The Prime’s voice invading his mind?

     Haldric shuddered before forcing himself back into motion.

     Praew found the truth, and I attacked her for it. Then when she needed me most, I wasted too much time pretending The Prime was innocent and unaware.

     He reached the stairwell leading to the Third Floor. The air was colder here, thicker. Empty. Perfect for what he needed. He descended with quick, controlled steps, gripping the railing once to steady himself before heading toward the prison hall. 

    His own words echoed in his head. “You’re not as smart as you pretend to be.”

    He tightened his hands until his palms ached. Praew’s voice, the way it cracked when he yelled at her, replayed with painful clarity. The hurt in her eyes. The way she flinched when he snapped.

     Haldric turned a corner sharply, nearly scraping a wall. His lungs were frantic by the time he spotted a guard up ahead, leaning on his spear, half asleep. Haldric slowed just enough to pass without drawing attention, keeping his head down like any restless Admin on an errand.

     He moved past the guard, heartbeat pounding, the rhythm matching each thought striking him one after another.

     He remembered Praew walking away, refusing to look back. That was the last moment he saw her before she vanished.

     The SolarStones along the prison walls flickered, dimming for the last time before the next flash.

     When has time ever passed so fast?

     The silence wrapped around him. No guards in sight. Luck or fate, he didn’t care.

     He stopped in front of the first cell.

     The flash began.

     Haldric reached beneath his shirt, pulled out the key, and hesitated.

     When will I see her again? What if I never do? What if I never get to say I’m sorry or tell her how I feel?

     Shaking his head, Haldric shoved the key into the first lock.

     He moved fast, with steady hands and a set mind. The Archon key fit every lock, just as promised. 

     Cell after cell swung open. Murderers. Thieves. Smugglers. Traitors. It didn’t matter. Some were infamous. Some were dangerous and… was that one Embedded?

     Haldric didn’t care.

     They poured into the hall in a frenzy, a mass of ragged bodies colliding with each other as they bolted for freedom. Shouts rose. Footsteps pounded. Someone laughed. Someone screamed. The guards didn’t stand a chance.

     Good.

     Cell after cell. Lock after lock. Hinges groaned. Bodies shoved past him, the air thick with heat and breath. The SolarStones flickered as the next flash crept closer.

     A voice called out from a cell he had already unlocked.

  “You think this changes anything? Some wounds never heal… and yours won’t either, not while you stand beside him.”

     Haldric froze. He looked back.

     War Claw Bresdin.

     Not so legendary now.

     The fallen Beast Hunter stood in the hall, unshaken amidst the chaos, rubbing the stump where his right hand used to be.

     No time for riddles.

     “I have no time for the cryptic nonsense of a crumbling relic,” Haldric called out.

     Without another word, Bresdin turned and walked away through the storm of bodies.

     Haldric shook his head, unlocking the last cell before pushing into the chaos as well.

     He kept his head low as he slipped out of the prison hall, the Archon key still warm against his chest. He didn’t look back. The echo of the last cell door creaking open rang in his ears like a private victory. That should be enough of a distraction.

     A muffled scream drifted from a path to his right.

     Let’s see how The Prime deals with this mess. The thought almost made him smile.

     He cut across the outer ring walkway, slipping past shadowed corridors and sidestreets as he headed toward the Third Floor’s central market. The heavy air felt lighter than it had all day. He imagined Praew exploring the surface like she had always dreamed of. He imagined seeing her again someday. Once he became Prime, he could welcome her back. But until then, he could relocate her family and keep them safe. When she learned of his consideration, he could finally make things right, fixing, and maybe even advancing, their relationship.

     Another shout echoed somewhere behind him. He was just glad the distraction was working.

     Someone rushed past him, brushing his shoulder hard enough to turn him half a step. He blinked, annoyed. If they knew how disrespectful they had been to their future Prime…

     Haldric continued walking until he stepped into the wide market square at the heart of the Floor. The stalls should have been active at this hour, crowded with shady vendors trying to sell questionable goods. Instead, several stalls had been knocked onto their sides. Supplies were scattered across the stone floor. A spilled jug left a wet streak that soaked the edge of Haldric’s boot.

     He slowed, confusion finally tugging him out of his thoughts.

     A prisoner stumbled out from between two fallen stalls, dragging a woman by her hair. A child clung to the woman’s shirt, crying out to her. The woman’s nails dug into the man’s hand as she tried to escape his grasp, voice breaking as she begged for mercy. The prisoner yanked harder. The child began to hit the prisoner’s leg. “Let go of my mommy!”

     Haldric’s feet stopped.

     “No,” the prisoner said. “This is your mother’s fault for reporting me, boy.”

     The prisoner backhanded the child. The boy fell to the floor, stunned silent. The woman twisted, desperate, reaching for him.

     “Hey!” Haldric yelled. “Let her—”

     The prisoner slit her throat before he finished the sentence.

     Blood sprayed across the stones in a sharp, violent burst. The woman collapsed in front of the child. The boy froze, covered in his mother’s blood, his small hands trembling as he clutched her clothes.

     The spreading pool reached Haldric’s boots. He couldn’t breathe. He couldn’t look away from the open eyes staring up at nothing.

     The prisoner laughed once and ran deeper into the chaos.

     Haldric didn’t move.

     The child’s sobs echoed across the entire square.

     Note’s voice swept through him. “The most dangerous thing to her right now is you, Haldric.”

     His oath to the Prime stabbed guilt into him sharper than any blade. “Every decision will be for the betterment of the city.”

     This is what I call betterment for the city? This… destruction?

     He looked at the woman’s vacant eyes. He looked at the child shaking above her.

     His voice barely rose above a whisper.

     “What have I done?”



***



Praew barely had the strength to lift her head, but she saw it—the tremor in The Prime’s fingers, breath, and shoulders. Even the way he looked at her changed. No longer a test subject, but as if she was something… divine.

     Praew felt nauseous. What?

     The Prime gasped a long, shuddering, almost reverent breath. “Finally,” his voice was soft. Fragile. “After fourteen years.”

     He was staring at the stone embedded in her chest.

     “Finally.”

     His hands hovered over it, close enough for Praew to feel a chill radiate from his palm. Or was it from the thing he’d forced into her? His disgusting fingers twitched. He almost touched it. Almost. Then he pulled back. Like he wasn’t worthy.

     The Prime sighed. His lips parted, whispering so softly she almost didn’t hear, “Our savior descends upon the unworthy to free us from our plight.”

     Praew’s heart raced. Zephyrs take me! He— he thinks I’m some kind of savior?

     His fingers curled into a tight fist, and for a moment she thought he was crying. Then he wiped his eyes, and the trembling vanished.

     The Prime reached into his coat.

     Praew swallowed hard as he pulled two stones free, a BoltStone and… an EchoStone?

      He used two fingers to slide them against each other in the palm of his hand. Slow. Deliberate. Like he was working a salve between his fingers

     “Ahh,” The Prime said, “you must be wondering what happens next.” He clasped his hands behind his back, the stones still in his grasp.

     “A BoltStone to your membrane in steady pulses makes the mind more impressionable,” he said.

     What?

     He wants— “to… control my thoughts?”

     The words slipped out before she could process them.

     “No, not exactly,” The Prime murmured, his tone maddeningly calm. “More like… guiding your emotions.”

     Guiding? My…emotions?

     She blinked. She wouldn’t believe something so ridiculous if it wasn’t for the Sifaralith stone sticking out of her bolting chest. She looked down at the stone and instantly found difficulty breathing. The stone rose and fell with her chest as she gasped for air. “You can’t do that. You can’t.”

     “I am sorry,” he said softly, “but I must.”

     Her heart raced. Her limbs strained against the restraints. No!

     “Please— don’t—”

     “Peace, Noble one. You will still be you,” The Prime continued, voice gentle like he was comforting a child.  As if he hadn’t already tortured her mentally and physically.  

     “I, unlike someone, do not believe in altering personalities. For I am no beast. I hold myself above such depravity.”

     He cast a sharp, disdainful glance at the scientist’s corpse sprawled over poor Junya’s limp body.

     Her pulse roared, breath shattering.

     This wasn’t happening.

     “You will just be more inclined to act as our guardian.”

     Guardian? Noble one? “Zephyrs take me! I’m not some guardian deity!” 

     Her body shook as her thoughts fractured. A fresh wave of fear surged up her throat.

     “Please,” she whispered. “Don’t do this, please—”

     “This,” The Prime began, “is a necessity.  I cannot have that much power go unchecked, now can I?”

     He moved behind her head, lifting the restraint.

     “Well,” he said, “this is for the best.”

     Cold pressed against her neck as something clicked into place.

     “Now,” The Prime said, completely emotionless. “You will feel a strong vibration within your head. Clear your mind and relax your body. Do not fight it. Resistance will only make the process … unpleasant.”

     “Why?” Praew gasped, her body jerking against the restraints. “Must everything be decided for me?!” Why can’t we choose anything in our own lives? Why are you so afraid of us?”

     “No, Noble one,” The Prime said. “I fear the story that begins with my end, and the end you are bound to begin.”

     The first pulse hit. A low, drumming thrummed inside her skull.

No!

Boom. Ka-boom. Boom. Ka-boom-boom.

Boom. Ka-boom. Boom. Ka-boom-boom. Boom. 

She clenched her jaw. 

I will not! 

Boom. Ka-boom. Boom. Ka-boom-boom.

Boom. Ka-boom. Boom. Ka-boom-boom. Boom. 

Let you! 

Boom. Ka-boom. Boom. Ka-boom-boom.

Boom. Ka-boom. Boom. Ka-boom-boom. Boom. 

Control… 

 Everything faded to black.



***



A distant voice called out to her.

Mother? Why was she here? Where was here?

She couldn’t shake the feeling that she was forgetting something… something important. Something more important than her own life. She needed to remember…

Her mother and father wrapped her in a much-needed hug. “Remember, little pebble,” her father said fondly. “You must leave the city for now. Go on that adventure you always dreamed of. Don’t worry about us. We’re fine.”

“Yes,” her mother agreed. “It sounds like a storm is brewing. Just never let the storm stop you. And only listen to others if your heart agrees.”

“We love you,” they said together.

“I love you too,” Praew said with a sniffle, burying her face in her parents— in her—

What was I doing again?

Ah. right. That’s what I needed to remember.

“Ratrod! You blasted Ratrod! You have doomed us all!”

Was that… The Prime?

“If we are, you can only blame your bolting self, Arthur.”

Strange. For a flicker, it almost sounded like… Godric. But it couldn’t be. Why would The Prime and Godric be in my home?









Chapter 18 - Picking Up the Spear 



“Change is on the horizon. Who are we to stand in its way?”









Her vision snapped back. The Prime was on the floor, tied up. Godric stood beside her with an embedded white Vitricite crystal spear pointed toward The Prime.

“Stone Face?” Praew called out.

“Good,” Godric said. “It worked.”

The Prime’s voice was raw and furious. “Godric, don’t tell me you truly believe that beast is still somehow human?”

Godric’s expression didn’t change. His grip didn’t waver. He simply sighed. “I don’t know about a beast. I just see how you’ve been treating my students. A crime punishable by death.”

The Prime choked in surprise. His eyes widened, his voice shaking. “You! You wouldn’t dare!”

Godric stabbed the spear blade down into the stone. While it stood upright, he released restraints and retrieved an EchoStone from behind her. “Come on,” he said. “We can’t stay here.”

Praew looked back at the stone slab. What happened to the BoltStone?

Her limbs felt weak, but she pushed herself up, forcing her body to move. They made their way out of the chamber, away from the awful stone slab.

Behind them, The Prime shouted, “Godric Rivers! You bring her back right now, or I will hunt you to the ends of the world!”

Godric didn’t stop. He exhaled. “Goodbye, brother.”

He pulled his Hunter’s cloak from his shoulders and draped it over Praew. “Wear this in case we get separated.”

She clutched the fabric, heart racing. He was taking her to the Second Floor? Toward the Gate to the surface?

Her mind reeled.

Godric’s voice snapped like a whip. “We need to leave the city, Praew. I know you’re worried about your parents, but—”

She gave an exhausted shrug. “There’s no reason to worry about them. They will live. If he kills them, I have no reason to return, right?”

Godric frowned, then nodded.

“We will get your parents out later,” he said. “If we can.”

Her breath was ragged. Don’t Hunters avoid traveling at night? No. Don’t think about that now. I must leave the city for now. And besides, I have Godric with me. I can finally go on that adventure I always dreamed of. I can handle this.

“You’ll be fine for now,” Godric said as they climbed toward the surface tunnels. “You’ll find it harder to breathe as you acclimate, but never stop running. You understand, girl?”

Praew swallowed hard and nodded.

Gate after gate, they climbed the slanted path. No guard dared question Silver Wing Godric. Each barrier opened the moment Godric approached.

Near the surface, something shifted. The air changed. She couldn’t tell if it was the BoltStone in her chest or the surface itself, but electricity surged. Her hair stood on end. A crackle hummed through the air.

Only one gate remained. A barred gate that teased Praew with a peek at her first sunrise. One more gate until freedom. A freedom that was now impossible.

Six Embedded warriors in identical masks stood between Praew and freedom. In front of them was Renwick with a smug grin on his face.

Praew’s heart sank.

It was over. At least I was able to breathe fresh air once. She tried to reassure herself, but—

Godric thrust his spear. The weapon launched toward the gate—then impact. A detonation of force. A surged ForceStone?

Praew barely had time to guess before the shockwave tore through the air. Renwick and the six Embedded warriors were scattered across the floor. And the gate? A human-sized hole gaped in its center.

Freedom.

Praew’s lungs gave out. She bent over, coughing. Couldn’t breathe.

Godric’s grip caught her arm, then flung her through the hole in the gate, up to the surface.

“Go!”

Praew staggered forward, then turned to look back at him.

She saw the back of her hero. The man she had idolized since childhood. Standing. Guarding the gate. Facing certain death, all to save her.

Something crushed her chest.

She had judged him so unfairly. Thought him emotionless. Though he showed the emotions of stone, he was truly a man who cared. He cared about his students, his city, and his people.

Why couldn’t you have been the Prime?

“Can’t you come with me?” she pleaded.

Godric didn’t turn. Didn’t hesitate. His voice was firm.

“Do you think you’re the only victim?”

The six Embedded were already rising to their feet.

Praew froze. She knew that quote. And finally, she understood.

Godric knew he might die here. He could probably escape if he left now.

But he wouldn’t. He couldn’t leave the city defenseless against The Prime’s cruelty.

He couldn’t live knowing he didn’t try.

Her vision blurred. Her feet moved. She ran.

But she turned back one last time. She had one thing left to say.

She cupped her hands and yelled, “Thank you for picking up your spear to face the storm!”

The words rang out over the battlefield. A quote from his book — Crescent Moon Srevlis. 

Godric’s stance didn’t falter. His grip on the spear tightened.

Then—

He raised it.

A silent goodbye.

Explosions erupted.

They followed her. No matter how far she ran.

Every step was a risk. Every breath a gamble.

Every sound, agony. Electricity crackled from the BoltStone in her chest, dancing across her skin, biting into her nerves. The ground was alive. The very act of running was testing fate.

She was triggering stones everywhere.

Her foot landed too hard on a ClamorStone, and thunder screamed back at her. A shockwave of sound exploded, ringing through her skull, reverberating in her bones. She gritted her teeth and kept moving.

A ZephyrStone howled as a gust slammed into her side. She skidded, nearly losing her footing, barely staying upright.

Her body felt stronger. She should’ve collapsed. Should’ve broken something.

She didn’t.

Her stamina was impossible. Her durability, inhuman. Did the BoltStone change me somehow?

Her electrical discharge grazed a ForceStone nearby. A pulse of pure force slammed into her ribs, launching her into the air. She tumbled, the impact knocking the breath from her lungs.

She landed gasping, limbs trembling, but nothing was broken.

A massive SolarStone filled the horizon. It radiated a pure, vengeful light, intensifying by the second. The glare pierced her vision, white-hot and searing. She squinted, but it was no use. She couldn’t see the stones anymore. Couldn’t see where she was running.

Splash.

An AquaStone. It shot a gush of water upward, catching her foot and launching her through the air. Limbs flailing, she crashed down hard onto a stone she had only heard about. A rush of gentle electricity stunned her in place, pinning her to the muddy ground. Her skin burned, her body screamed.

The ground split open as a TerraStone rumbled beneath her. A sinkhole attempted to swallow her, but she rolled out of the way. Rising to her feet, she continued to run.

Her lungs were failing. She was getting weaker.

Her limbs locked. Praew nearly collapsed.

She forced herself forward.

But the sun kept rising.

And with it, the world grew more violent.

The heat became unbearable. The glare blinding. She couldn’t see anything. She was stumbling blind, triggering stones she couldn’t even identify.

A ClamorStone shrieked. A BlastStone detonated. A ForceStone slammed her sideways.

Then — her BoltStone.

Zephyrs take me!

Electricity surged through her. Uncontrolled. Lightning arced from her skin. The pain paralyzed her. She couldn’t move.

She had become a walking storm.

The sun kept climbing, triggering more stones. More lightning. More agony.

She was going to die. Just like Junya and Wanni.

Here. After everything she’d gone through.

She had made it so far — and still, she was going to die.

At least I die free.

Praew closed her eyes, accepting her fate with a smile.



***



Back at his Grayparents’ home, Haldric knew this was coming. The explosions from the surface had made it clear: someone had made it to the surface. He just hoped it was Praew and Godric. Were they still alive? He didn’t have time to worry. The knock came moments later.

“Special Forces! Open up!”

Even expecting it, his heart raced. He didn’t resist. There was no point. He doubted The Prime would send two ordinary enforcers. Haldric guessed they were Embedded, their stones well-hidden, or acclimated at the very least. If he ran, he wouldn’t make it three steps.

As they led him through the corridors, his eyes flicked toward their necks. Burn scars. Similar to the documents in The Prime’s office. The recorrection program. Just how many people does The Prime control? Can I free them somehow?

 Haldric knew he was only trying to keep his mind busy to avoid drowning in the guilt for letting those violent prisoners free. That poor woman. This poor city.

After the familiar trek, Haldric and his escorts neared the Administration building.

He should’ve been afraid. But he wasn’t. He already knew how this would go. Haldric would deny everything. The Prime would see through it, of course, but he’d forgive him anyway. Just long enough to justify his own despicable actions. That bolting man.

Haldric clenched his fists as they reached The Prime’s office. The doors swung open.

The Prime Archon stood inside, hands clasped behind his back, not even looking at him yet. 	Haldric stepped forward, already forming his excuse.

“I—”

CRACK.

He lurched. The backhanded blow struck before he even processed it. He hit the floor, pain exploding through his cheek as the cold stone scraped against his side. Too fast.

A boot slammed into his ribs.

“You idiot!”

Another kick.

“Boy!”

Another.

“You doomed us all!”

Haldric gasped, curling in on himself as another vicious strike knocked the wind from his lungs. His ears rang, his body screamed, but the worst part wasn’t the pain—it was the tone in The Prime’s voice. Not just rage. Not just betrayal.

Pure disappointment.

Another kick, harder this time.

“Why?” Kick. “Must you?” Kick. “Ruin everything?”

Haldric pressed his hands to the floor, trying to rise. The Prime’s shadow loomed over him, breath rough, shoulders shaking. Then he stilled.

“Fool! You terrorized the whole city, releasing every criminal in our jail!?” His voice trembled with barely restrained fury. “Now I have to imprison my own brother! Why did you have to involve him!?”

Haldric barely had time to react before the next words struck harder than any blow.

“Bresdin, you even released Bresdin.”

Silence. The temperature in the room dropped.

The Prime wasn’t just furious anymore. He was afraid. For the first time since Haldric had met him, Arthur Rivers looked shaken. He stepped forward again, raising his foot for another blow.

Haldric tensed, bracing—

“Father, please!”

The words spilled out before he could stop them. 

He wasn’t even my father. Why did I call this monster that?

The Prime froze. The kick never came.

A long silence followed. Then he sighed, dragging a hand down his face. “I… lost my temper.” His voice was calm now, almost tired. Then he crouched down, voice soft.

“Son, are you alright?”

***



Godric’s breath burned, each pull of air sharp and shallow, ribs screaming with every movement. Blood ran warm beneath his armor, soaking into fabric and leather alike.

 He forced his arm to move and drove the butt of his spear into the stone to keep himself upright. The impact rang through his arms, draining what little strength remained. His knee struck the ground a heartbeat later. Scaled leather scraped against the wet stone as he knelt.

This was reckless. When did I pick up habits from those bolting kids?

Godric couldn’t move.

His body no longer listened to him.

How long had it been since he felt fear like this?

Do you think you’re the only victim?

The line echoed in his mind.

How would that bolting girl misinterpret my words this time?

Godric’s shoulder spasmed. His fingers twitched against the spearshaft before going still.
